HEADLINE: Beliefs

BYLINE: By PETER STEINFELS 

 BODY:
Basic to Congress's rewriting of Federal obligations to help the poor, disabled, sick and elderly are some assumptions about religion and religious organizations in the United States. The new wisdom in Washington is this: Religious institutions, along with other charitable organizations, can provide the nation with a social and economic safety net as well as a moral and spiritual one.
This conviction takes several forms. One envisions that, using voluntary contributions, religious congregations and existing religious and charitable organizations will fill in for much of the aid that, if Congress has its way, will not be provided by the Federal Government. Although the numbers shift a bit every day, that sum would add up to about $400 billion over the next seven years. 
 It is useful to get some perspective on what is involved here. According to Independent Sector, a coalition of not-for-profit foundations and charities, there are 258,000 religious congregations in the United States, from storefront churches to cathedrals, well-enough established to have telephone numbers. Divide that $400 billion among them over seven years. Each congregation would have to generate a little over $220,000 a year.
Remember that more than 70 percent of these congregations have fewer than 400 members (20 percent have fewer than 100) and that their average revenue is well under $100,000 a year.
What about private charities, religious ones included? Since many of them already use Federal funds along with private giving and money from state and local governments, they will probably be doing less rather than more under the new arrangement. Independent Sector issued a painstaking study last summer showing that the new Federal spending limits would mean that many charitable programs would have to increase their contributions by an unlikely 40, 80 or even several hundred percent -- or sharply reduce visits to the homebound, and other services.
But lobbyists for religious charities are being told in many Congressional offices that those charitable contributions would surge with the new budget's tax cuts, especially cuts for the wealthy.
Lobbyists, however, are skeptical. They know, for example, that between 1980 and 1993, when tax law changes produced dramatic growth in the number of people reporting more than a million dollars on their tax returns, the average charitable contribution per millionaire dropped from $207,000 to $108,000.
"Patterns of Generosity in America," a recent Twentieth Century Fund study, found that, through boom and bust, donations to charities have remained at a fairly constant 2 percent of personal income.
"If the level of giving could be raised to 5 percent -- an implausibly high figure," wrote the report's author, Julian Wolpert, a professor of public policy at Princeton, "that would still fall far short of matching" currently proposed cuts.
The study also challenged the notion that lower Government spending leads to higher personal giving.In reality, they follow the same curve: "Where state and local governments are generous," Professor Wolpert wrote in The Washington Post, "donations are highest."
But there is another approach to the role of religion and the safety net that dismisses all these figures as just so much charity-as-usual. Its basic principle was enunciated by Marvin Olasky, a scholar and journalist whose 1992 book, "The Tragedy of American Compassion" (Regnery) was hailed by Speaker Newt Gingrich.
What the author calls Olasky's Law says that "Bad charity drives out good" -- and by bad he means giving aid simply on the basis of need, unaccompanied by religious or moral instruction and without the personal involvement of the aid-giver in the life of the recipient.
Mr. Olasky and his allies see the current abolition of entitlements, reductions in Federal spending rates and the return of programs to the states as the first step toward a sweeping "charity revolution" that will eventually find churches adopting families in need; small groups of people forming religiously based "compassion circles" to shelter and counsel unwed mothers, and addicts and the mentally disturbed homeless being treated with "tough love" and appeals to religious conversion.
This approach anticipates two massive changes in American conduct. One is a drastic reduction in the number of single-parent households, which those in Mr. Olasky's camp tend to attribute almost entirely to illegitimacy. The other is a vast outpouring, from millions of Americans, of personal commitment, face-to-face involvement, out-of-pocket expenses, patient and skillful guidance and huge expenditures of time.
It is an inspiring vision, but is it realistic?
Mr. Olasky says that "journalistic and historical evidence" is on his side: His book argues that 19th century charity workers, armed with the Bible and the work ethic, succeeded where today's welfare fails. To this Mr. Olasky adds his own discovery of "a lot of effective community-based charities."
Representatives of existing Protestant, Catholic and Jewish social services say this is a slim reed on which to overhaul even a seriously dysfunctional welfare system. And one of Mr. Olasky's admirers, Doug Bandow of the libertarian Cato Institute, said, "Certainly there is a risk involved." It is probably the one statement in this debate that no one will challenge.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Benefits From Recoveries In Mexican Peso and the Franc

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The dollar ended higher yesterday, benefiting from recoveries in the Mexican peso and the French franc.
The dollar rose in early New York trading after a report showed that the United States economy grew more strongly than expected in the third quarter. The Commerce Department said gross domestic product expanded at a 4.2 percent annual rate in the third quarter, well above analysts' expectations of 2.6 percent. 
 Currency traders bid up the dollar on the view that strong economic growth would attract investors to American assets. The report was "dollar positive," said Earl Johnson, currency adviser at Bank of Montreal/Harris Bank.
But bond traders saw the report as evidence that the economy was too strong for the Federal Reserve to lower short-term interest rates again this year, and Treasury prices initially fell. The dollar followed the bond market lower, though both bounced back later.
The dollar's rebound was helped by the recoveries in the French franc and Mexican peso and speculation in the market that President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia would resign because of his health problems. Political uncertainty in Russia typically weakens the German mark, thus helping the dollar, because Germany is Russia's largest trading partner.
In New York late yesterday, the dollar rose to 1.4090 German marks from 1.3915 marks on Thursday. The dollar edged up to 101.93 Japanese yen from 101.43 yen. The British pound slipped to $1.5765 from $1.5831.
The French franc rose against the mark after President Jacques Chirac ruled out a devaluation of the franc and said cutting the budget deficit was his top priority.
When the mark weakens against European currencies, it usually falls against the dollar, too, as speculators seek all opportunities to sell the mark.
"The Chirac comments this morning helped the dollar stabilize," said Karl Halligan, currency trader with CIC Bank in New York.
The mark was last quoted at 3.4736 francs, down from 3.5180 francs late yesterday in New York.
The dollar benefited as the Mexican peso recovered from a tumble on Thursday. The peso was helped by higher Mexican interest rates, which make bank deposits denominated in the currency more attractive. The dollar, which rose to 7.2350 Mexican pesos from 6.7850 pesos on Thursday, retreated to 7.0650 pesos yesterday.
Changes in the peso's value affect the dollar because of the close ties between Mexico and the United States.

HEADLINE: Doctors Say Yeltsin Will Need At Least 6 Weeks to Recover

BYLINE: By STEVEN ERLANGER 

DATELINE: MOSCOW, Oct. 27

 BODY:
President Boris N. Yeltsin's doctors said today that the Russian leader, in the hospital after an episode of heart disease on Thursday, must remain under "close medical supervision" through the end of November but that he is conscious and his condition is stable.
Kremlin officials today canceled a meeting scheduled for Tuesday in Moscow with the Presidents of Serbia, Croatia and Bosnia before they travel to the United States to begin peace talks. Mr. Yeltsin has been pushing for a Moscow meeting on Bosnia to underline Russia's importance in the search for peace there. 
 The American and Russian defense ministers, William J. Perry and Gen. Pavel S. Grachev, today went into a second day of delicate talks in the United States about how Russian troops can help carry out a peace accord in Bosnia without being directly under NATO command.
But some Western diplomats and analysts suggested that the Bosnia peace plan is "a house of cards," in the words of Andrew Pierre, of the Carnegie Endowment for International Peace. They said Mr. Yeltsin's hospitalization, which has brought instability and uncertainty in Moscow, could complicate the Bosnia issue.
With decision-making so personalized in Russia, any serious incapacity by Mr. Yeltsin could prevent Moscow from making important decisions on Bosnia or erode the willingness of Serbia, a cultural ally of Russia, to agree to a deal. Mr. Yeltsin has undermined his Foreign Minister, Andrei V. Kozyrev, by talking of replacing him, and few Russian officials will be eager to take responsibility for any decision that will may be viewed as a compromise to Western or NATO interests.
There are also signs of increased warfare in Chechnya, the separatist southern republic.
Officials said today that Mr. Yeltsin had received no visitors in the hospital except for the doctors, his wife, Naina, and his longtime friend and chief bodyguard, Gen. Aleksandr V. Korzhakov.
There was little medical information available today, and it was all filtered through Yeltsin spokesmen. Doctors were quoted as saying that the supply of blood to Mr. Yeltsin's heart "is still unstable" but that "there have been no signs of heart insufficiency up until now."
As the chief spokesman, Sergei Medvedev, interpreted this, "That means the blood pump is working O.K." Mr. Medvedev then added, "I stress 'up to now.' "
Officials have already canceled state visits to China and Norway scheduled for November.
Mr. Yeltsin's second attack in the last four months of myocardial ischemia, an insufficiency of oxygenated blood to the heart, will also undermine any campaign plans he had for the parliamentary elections Dec. 17. Mr. Yeltsin had talked of marshaling forces against the challenge from the revived Communist Party.
The attack also puts his own chances of re-election into further doubt. Presidential elections are scheduled for June.
The December elections now take on added importance as an indicator of the strength of other presidential hopefuls, like Prime Minister Viktor S. Chernomyrdin; a former general, Aleksandr M. Lebed, and a liberal economist, Grigory A. Yavlinsky, all of whom are leading political parties into the parliamentary vote.
Diplomats and analysts suggested that despite Mr. Yeltsin's efforts to preserve his political clout by keeping open a bid for re-election, this second bout of heart trouble will be harder to shrug off politically.
"There are a lot of people today who will be recalculating their political positions," one diplomat said. "It's important to keep an eye on the relations between the Yeltsin entourage and the Chernomyrdin Government, to see if anyone's edging that way."
If Mr. Yeltsin suffers prolonged incapacity or dies, Mr. Chernomyrdin becomes Acting President and must call new presidential elections within three months.
When Mr. Yeltsin appears hearty, his loyalists have worked to undermine Mr. Chernomyrdin's political position. But if Mr. Yeltsin cannot run again, they may quietly edge toward Mr. Chernomyrdin -- or to another political heavyweight, like Mr. Lebed's ally, Yuri Skokov -- as a bet on the country's future stability.
Mr. Medvedev and the doctors, led by Sergei P. Mironov, the chief Kremlin doctor, gave no information about what kind of treatment Mr. Yeltsin is getting, other than calling it "active therapy." They did say Mr. Yeltsin had never lost consciousness and retains control of Russia's nuclear launch codes.
Diplomats from three countries said their private briefings from Russian officials had produced no further information on Mr. Yeltsin's condition. Doctors have never explained what kind of treatment they provided Mr. Yeltsin in July, when he had his first episode of ischemia (pronounced iss-KEE-mee-ah).
"Proceeding from the adopted measures of treatment, physicians believe that through the end of October and all through November, Yeltsin must remain under close medical supervision," said a statement from the President's press service.
Mr. Medvedev implied that medical supervision need not take place in the Central Clinical Hospital, which is supervised by the Kremlin. But it may mean that Mr. Yeltsin will go to work at a slower pace outside the Kremlin, perhaps at the Barvikha sanatorium, where he recuperated in late July.
Mr. Medvedev did complain about the abundance of "rumors and inventions" in the Western press about Mr. Yeltsin's condition. "I assume these rumors are used in both political and financial games," he said, referring to currency speculators.
Some legislators speculated that Mr. Yeltsin's illness might be used as a pretext to postpone the December and June elections, and others called for experts outside the Kremlim to examine the state of the President's health.

HEADLINE: Clinton Enters Battle Over Telecom Bill

BYLINE: By EDMUND L. ANDREWS 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 27

 BODY:
President Clinton personally entered the battle over telecommunications today, attacking numerous features in the bill now pending before a House-Senate conference committee. At the same time, his top economic adviser emphasized the Administration's threat to veto the measure.
In a letter to Senator Ernest F. Hollings, Democrat of South Carolina and ranking Democrat on the Senate Commerce Committee, Mr. Clinton attacked four major sections of the measure: 
 *Provisions that allow the regional Bell companies to enter the long-distance telephone business without needing specific approval from the Justice Department.
*The deregulation of cable television rates.
*Permission for telephone companies to buy up to 49 percent control of local cable television companies.
*Provisions that greatly relax restrictions on the ability of a company that already owns a newspaper to own television stations and radio stations in the same city.
Meanwhile, in an attempt to counter rumors that the Administration would limit its fight to issues about media concentration and cable television prices, the President's chief economic adviser, Joseph Stiglitz, said he would strongly urge a veto unless the bill that finally passes contained tighter restrictions on letting local telephone companies enter the long-distance market.
Both the House and Senate have passed roughly similar bills that would each overhaul the nation's telecommunications laws and are intended to deregulate the telephone, cable television and broadcasting industries.
In recent weeks, many lawmakers and lobbyists have speculated that the Administration would not pick a real fight over the issues of telephone deregulation, which are far more impenetrable to voters than provisions that allow companies to greatly expand the number of television and radio properties they own.
"I want to denounce the idea that telephone issues are not important," Mr. Stiglitz, the chairman of the White House Council of Economic Advisers, said in an interview today. "They are important -- so important that I would urge a veto unless they are fixed. In terms of long-term economic impact, this is where the action is."
Both bills would allow the regional Bell companies to enter the long-distance market, from which they have been barred since the breakup of the Bell System in 1984, once they meet a "checklist" of requirements to open their networks to competition in the local market.
The Bell companies argue that they must be freed, because their traditional monopolies are under increasing attack from cable television operators, long-distance carriers and other competitors. But more than 99 percent of all local telephone lines, particularly those for residential customers, still go through traditional telephone companies.
Mr. Stiglitz said he would recommend a veto even if the final bill resolves all the Administration's objections about media and cable television issues.
The broad goal of the legislation, which the Administration supports, is to overhaul the nation's communications laws and encourage greater competition among local telephone, long-distance and cable television industries.
But the bill, as it has passed both the House and Senate, goes further and greatly relaxes a host of other restrictions on the number of media properties a single company can own; it also would largely deregulate cable television prices.
The dispute between local and long-distance companies, although it generates far less interest among voters, almost certainly involves the greatest amount of money. The local telephone business represents roughly a $100 billion market, while the long-distance business generates about $70 billion a year.

HEADLINE: Life in Iowa May Not Have Changed, But the Political Turf Is Another Story

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE Jr. 

DATELINE: SIGOURNEY, Iowa, Oct. 25

 BODY:
Iowa is still flat and friendly, and people here still treasure their pork tenderloin sandwiches. But a lot of other things have changed since Senator Bob Dole of Kansas defeated George Bush, then the Vice President, in the 1988 Iowa caucuses -- the last time this often-influential state witnessed a keenly contested Presidential race among Republicans.
Madison County and the town of Winterset have become tourist attractions because of the book and film they inspired. The state's economy has gone from bust to boom, the religious right has seized control of the Iowa Republican Party, and the moderates who used to dominate it have lost most of their influence. 
 "The right has taken over Des Moines, they've taken over the party apparatus," said Michael Gartner, editor of The Ames Tribune and former president of NBC News. "They're not cheating or doing anything wrong. They're not anti-Semitic or racist. They just feel strongly about the issues. They're like Big Labor in the 1940's: they get out and work at it, while most other people stay home."
In 1988, Pat Robertson finished second with a quarter of the vote, well ahead of Mr. Bush, thanks to evangelical Christians and their allies. He ran especially well here in southeastern Iowa. Since then, Iowa has moved more rapidly to the right than the nation as a whole. Yet it is not clear what kind of effect the changes will have on the campaign for the 1996 Republican Presidential nomination, which opens with Iowa's idiosyncratic caucuses on Feb. 12.
Although neither of them is a true soulmate of the religious right, Mr. Dole by all accounts retains a sizable lead, and his potential rival, Colin L. Powell, the retired general, has better prospects than one might expect.
This time, no candidate has been able to monopolize the religious right's support the way Mr. Robertson did. If the conservative vote splits, that could give General Powell, the former chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff, an opening. In addition, some evangelicals rather surprisingly say that they want so desperately to beat President Clinton that they might end up supporting the general.
Senator Phil Gramm of Texas has gained ground since finishing in a virtual dead heat with Mr. Dole in a straw poll on Aug. 19 and adding appeals on abortion and school prayer to his original message of economic conservatism. Patrick J. Buchanan, the commentator, who finished a solid third in the straw poll, also has evangelical support, but his message is too "hot" for many other voters.
The other candidates seem not to have made much headway, though Lamar Alexander and Malcolm S. Forbes Jr. are already running television advertisements.
Because Iowa is viewed by many analysts as Mr. Dole's backyard, he would probably be deeply wounded by losing or winning unimpressively here. A strong second- or third-place showing by Mr. Gramm or another candidate, on the other hand, could propel that candidate into the thick of the battle.
Gov. Michael S. Dukakis of Massachusetts finished third here in 1988, close behind Representative Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri and Senator Paul Simon of Illinois, both from neighboring states, and went on to win the Democratic nomination; Senator George S. McGovern of South Dakota did the same in 1972 after running second to Senator Edmund S. Muskie of Maine.
Once considered a shoo-in, Mr. Dole has been slipping lately, dropping to 40 percent support in late September in the Iowa Poll from 57 percent last May. Mr. Gramm rose in that period to 18 percent from 11 percent.
Local experts think Mr. Dole could fall short of the 38 percent he attracted eight years ago, running against an incumbent Vice President who had held office for almost eight years, or perhaps even lose. Worries about the religious right in Iowa are one big reason the Senator, a pragmatist in the past, has been trying hard to grow ideological stripes.
"Dole's been around a long time," said Marlene Elwell, who organized the forces that defeated the proposed Equal Rights Amendment in Iowa, and who played a crucial role in Mr. Buchanan's campaign until quitting a few weeks ago in a strategy dispute.
"He's a compromiser," she said. "If he can avoid taking a stand on abortion and the other social issues we care about, he'll do it, and the conservatives know it."
But Mr. Dole has the backing of most of the Republican establishment, including Gov. Terry E. Branstad, who has not made a Presidential endorsement in his 13 years in office, and Senator Charles E. Grassley. He has recruited Steve Scheffler, a former executive director of the Christian Coalition, as a full-time organizer, helping to offset a coup by Mr. Gramm in hiring Marlys Popma, another highly regarded operative with close ties to the anti-abortion movement here.
"People in this state have known Bob Dole for a long time," Governor Branstad said in an interview in which he predicted a clear-cut Dole victory. "He's a small-town Midwesterner who has been our champion on agriculture, ethanol and other issues. He knows Washington, knows how to get things done, but he hasn't forgotten where he comes from."
After the straw poll, Mr. Dole dismissed his campaign manager and promised to change his schedule to spend much more time here. The race seems his to lose. But the Iowa Poll shows his backing to be perilously soft; three-quarters of his supporters said they could be persuaded to vote for another candidate.
For General Powell, who has emerged in recent weeks as a potentially powerful nominee, the state would seem at first glance to be an inauspicious place to begin his quest if he decides next month to seek the Republican Presidential nomination. Unlike primaries, the Iowa caucuses are mostly about organization, and it is already late in the day.
"Pete Wilson tried to put something together starting last May," said Brian Kennedy, the state Republican chairman, "and he couldn't do it. You need to line up the regional leaders and precinct organizers. No one's left."
Then there is the religious right, whose recent successes are hard to overstate. The evangelicals managed this year to drive a longtime member off the Des Moines school board after he acknowledged that he was a homosexual. Once outsiders, they have moved into many important party positions. For example, Sharon Rexroth of Burlington in southeastern Iowa, who eight years ago was just learning the rudiments of political organizing as a supporter of Mr. Robertson, is now a Republican county chairwoman and a member of the state party committee.
Some of the religious conservatives agree with Ms. Elwell, who said that General Powell "stands for almost nothing that we have stood for."
In Paris two weeks ago, the general said he shared many of the values of the religious right, like in the importance of the family, but he added, "I won't go so far as to advocate, as they do, amending the Constitution to allow prayer in the schools or ban abortion."
Hardly red meat for the religious right. But Ione Dilley, president of the Christian Coalition, said her view of General Powell was changing. On abortion, she said, "he's not as pro-choice as he sounded at first, and on other things dear to our hearts, like no homosexuals in the military, he's O.K., so I think we'll have to wait and see what happens."
"He needs to come out here and talk issues," she said.
Ms. Rexroth says her mind remains completely open.
"I like Colin Powell," she said. "I don't think any American can fail to admire what he's done for our country. I disagree with him on abortion, of course, but that's not the only issue. We know he's a leader. Above everything else, I want to beat Bill Clinton, and he may be the man who can do it."
If General Powell decides to run as a Republican and to campaign here, said Mr. Kennedy, the state chairman, he "doesn't want to be drawn into a detailed discussion about Medicare or the social issues, because he's not where the heart of the party is." But Mr. Kennedy added: "We need to go after Clinton on leadership and character in the fall. That's what Powell has to offer, and that's what he should talk about in Iowa."
The hope would be that General Powell's undoubted charisma would draw people to the caucuses who usually do not attend. About half of the 130,000 people identified by pollsters as likely participants in the Republican precinct meetings describe themselves as deeply conservative, and about a third, or 40,000, call themselves "born-again" Christians. But there is also a pool of at least 50,000 Republicans who, so far, say they see no compelling reason to take part.

HEADLINE: Treasuries Slide, Then Rebound

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Weighted down by concern over signs of a still-robust economy, but buoyed by expectations that a Federal budget agreement would eventually be reached, bond prices swung widely and ended mixed yesterday in heavy trading.
A morning selloff was prompted by the Commerce Department's report that gross domestic product was up at a 4.2 percent annual pace in the third quarter, far higher than the 2.6 percent that economists had expected. 
 "The number was a surprise," said Gary R. Thayer, senior economist at A. G. Edwards & Sons in St. Louis, "and therefore brought on a strong reaction from the market," which feared that the news might keep the Federal Reserve from lowering interest rates again.
But while the numbers showed widespread strength in the economy, there was nothing to suggest an immediate acceleration in inflation, Mr. Thayer said, so the market began to turn around as traders and investors refocused on budget matters in Washington.
In addition, a mini-rally occurred as traders who had misjudged and sold Treasury securities short -- that is, sold borrowed securities, hoping to buy them back later at lower prices and thereby pocket the difference -- began to cover their short positions by buying back the securities to limit their losses.
Traders said the short-cover rally caused a turnaround for the 30-year bond, which had been trading as much as three-quarters of a point lower. The bond registered a gain of just over half a point on the day. The 30-year bond rose 18/32 , to a price of 107, while the yield, which moves in the opposite direction, fell to 6.35 percent, from 6.39 percent on Thursday.
In futures trading, which affects the cash market, the December bond contract settled at 11628/32 . The high for the session was 11630/32 and the low was 1159/32 .
The economy's growth will remain moderate but still good, Mr. Thayer predicted, though growth will be slower this year than last. He said the risk of a recession was still low and that the Fed would probably hold to its current policy on interest rates. "If they cut rates on a budget accord, it is likely to be a token move," he said. "Going into the election next year, it is not clear whether the accord will hurt the economy in 1996; therefore, it's probably premature for the Fed to aggressively cut rates."
Other economic reports yesterday included the University of Michigan's final consumer confidence reading for October. The figure was revised slightly downward, to 90.2 from a preliminary figure of 90.4.
Donald Fine, chief market analyst at Chase Securities, said of the report on gross domestic product, "While the Fed cannot acknowledge anything publicly, I am sure that their feel about the numbers this morning is the same as mine -- that is, you don't take these numbers with a grain of salt; you take them with a ton of salt.
"The fact remains that the next time we see a G.D.P. release it will be on the new chain-weighted basis," he said, referring to a new method of measuring growth. Under that system, he said, G.D.P. growth was seven-tenths of 1 percent in the second quarter and 3 percent in the third quarter, or an average of 1.9 percent.
"My feeling is that the economy is very likely growing at, or near that pace in the fourth quarter as well," he said. "As a result, I continue to think that the Fed will ease at its December meeting, because not only does the nonthreatening inflation picture give them latitude, but the less-than-booming economy could prompt them as well."
Doug Burtnick, senior fixed-income analyst at Technical Data in Boston, said, "At first glance, the 4.2 percent rise in G.D.P. should have been enough to send the market sailing off a cliff, and most people tried to play it that way."
He said traders found it difficult to rationalize selling further, however, because the market fell so sharply out of the chute. "The late-day rally portrays the appearance of some larger investors making the play that bonds remain in a trading range," he added. "Clearly, the potential for further gains is limited by fears that the budget process could fail."

HEADLINE: BASEBALL;
Showalter's Chair Barely Cold as Torre Emerges

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS 

DATELINE: ATLANTA, Oct. 27

 BODY:
Bob Watson, only a few days into his new role as the Yankees' general manager, said today that he had a list of candidates for the club's newest vacancy, the manager's job, but the search may start and end with Joe Torre.
Watson, charged by George Steinbrenner with finding a successor to Buck Showalter, said he would look for a man with major league managing experience, "maybe someone who possibly has won a divisional title." 
 "There's some of them out there," Watson said, speaking by telephone from his home in Houston.
Sparky Anderson is one, and Gene Lamont is another. But in the Yankees' context, Torre fits that description best.
"I have not mentioned his name, but it's a good name," Watson said. "He's a friend. He's a manager who is unemployed. I don't know if he wants to work again. I haven't talked to him."
Torre, who was fired as the St. Louis Cardinals' manager in June, said he had not heard from Watson or anyone else with the Yankees, but he was prepared to listen when they called.
"I'd be interested in managing," Torre said from Palm Beach Gardens, Fla., where his scheduled round of golf was rained out. "After St. Louis, I said I wouldn't go around interviewing for managing jobs. But I would listen if someone wanted to talk to me. You can't say I wouldn't like to manage because you've done it your whole life. It's competitive."
Watson's search comes in the wake of conflicting versions of Showalter's departure. In a release issued by the Yankees on Thursday night, Steinbrenner said Showalter had rejected an offer for a new two-year, $1.05 million contract and had said he would not return next season.
Showalter, after hearing the owner's view, said he never resigned but only rejected the offer in the context of negotiations. Showalter reiterated his position today.
Asked if he thought that by rejecting the offer he was merely negotiating and not resigning, he said, "That's the way things are usually done, aren't they?"
Did he resign, or ever say the word? "Heavens, no," he said by telephone from his home in Pensacola, Fla. "I was hopeful to the end that something would work out."
Finally, the now former manager was asked, if this was a misunderstanding, did he think it was a door that could be reopened?
"Yes," he said, "but it's got to be by him. He made an offer, and I turned it down because of the way it was constructed. If he is willing to change it, I'm willing to listen."
But Steinbrenner, who has been busy with his son's wedding Saturday, has not appeared to be inclined to rekindle talks with the man who managed continuously for him longer than any of his 19 other managers.
Arthur Richman, a senior adviser to Steinbrenner, signaled that stand today by saying: "I put out that release. Why would I write it if I didn't hear it? He said he didn't want to negotiate with him, didn't want to meet with him, didn't want to return."
Watson appeared to be surprised upon hearing Showalter's version of his telephone conversation with Steinbrenner. But Watson said he would not attempt to contact Showalter.
"I'm honoring Mr. Steinbrenner's request to let him handle that, and that's what we're going to do," the new $350,000-a-year general manager said. "It wouldn't be fair to me or Buck or Mr. Steinbrenner to get in on the tail end of something. I was never involved in it."
But he will be involved in the selection of a new manager, Watson said. He said he could begin interviewing candidates as soon as this weekend.
"It is paramount to look for someone with experience," Watson said. "Just given the setting of the New York Yankees, if we were not in the mode of trying to win, if we were going to do a rebuilding job, I'd look for a different type manager. But the statement of 1996 is we want to win."
The Atlanta Braves won the National League West championship in 1982 under Torre. He also managed the Braves in 1983 and '84. Previously, he managed the Mets from 1977 through 1981 and subsequently managed the Cardinals from 1990 until last June 16.
Interestingly, Torre was approached about the Yankees' general manager's vacancy before Watson. He met with Gene Michael about the job last week in Tampa, Fla., but said, "It didn't seem to work."
"I interviewed a number of years ago in St. Louis when Dal Maxvill got the job," Torre said. "It never entered my mind then to be a general manager. Basically I'm not willing to do the job. If I manage, I always want to have a say about things. As general manager, you get paid to say things."
Torre and Watson are old friends. Watson played for Torre in Atlanta.
"We talked quite a bit," Torre said. "He was my unofficial hitting coach. Our views are similar. Then we talked a lot when I went into Houston."
Torre, who was a television commentator on baseball games between his last two managerial jobs, returned to that role after the Cardinals fired him. He said his agent has been exploring television possibilities for next season.
Torre cited an interview he once did with Hank Stram to explain his feelings about managing and broadcasting. Stram, a football coach turned announcer, said, "When you leave the broadcasting booth, you're not sure if you've won or lost."
Watson declined to discuss a wide range of possible candidates, but when asked specifically, he offered views on two:
*Sparky Anderson. "That's something we would talk about. We would consider Mr. Anderson."
*Chris Chambliss. "Chris would have an in because he was a Yankee. Chris has a lot of big moments in Yankee history. He's a guy who has stated he would like to manage. I'd have to sit down and interview him. I haven't talked to him about managing for a long time."
Anderson resigned from his job with the Detroit Tigers the day after the season ended and has been mentioned for the vacancy in Cincinnati. Chambliss has managed in the minor leagues and was a coach under Torre in St. Louis.
Showalter won't technically be unemployed until his contract expires Tuesday. The prevailing view in baseball is that he will wind up managing the Tigers.
Asked if he had heard from the Tigers or any other club, Showalter said: "No, that's illegal and unethical. I'm still under contract with the Yankees."

HEADLINE: Journal;
Good Night, David

BYLINE: By FRANK RICH 

 BODY:
To me, a fledgling news junkie growing up in Washington in the 50's and 60's, they were unmistakably giants. David Brinkley, who happened to live around the corner, turned TV news into a compulsory nightly ritual by bringing wit and skepticism to a trade in which most of the competitors sounded like ham actors auditioning for the role of Moses. Ben Bradlee, rising from the ashes of Camelot, refurbished a newspaper worth reading mainly for its comics with peppery new urban voices who finally woke up Washington's sleepy provincial culture. And Watergate was still to come.
Hard to believe that both these vital men have now reached the valedictory stage that requires full-dress memoirs. Their charming new books -- the eponymous "David Brinkley" and Mr. Bradlee's "A Good Life" -- are as idiosyncratic as their authors, especially when professional autobiography gives way to personal preoccupations (wives for Mr. Bradlee, taxes for Mr. Brinkley). The occasional overlaps are revealing, too, whether it's their mutual detestation of Joseph McCarthy, affection for Barry Goldwater or surprise at the posthumous revelations of President Kennedy's sex life. 
 But the most important congruity of the two books is their recipe for journalistic integrity and excellence. "There is no other way," Mr. Brinkley writes, than to rely on "the editor's personal judgment." Or, as Mr. Bradlee puts it, "Editors choose." The Washington Post would never have exposed a criminal White House if he had not made his then-lonely judgment that a "third-rate burglary" was a story worth pursuing, and if he had not received what he calls "state-of-the-art support" from his publisher, Katharine Graham, in the face of extraordinary official pressure to shut the investigation down.
What Mr. Bradlee and Mr. Brinkley don't talk about, however, is whether the independent journalistic spirit they celebrate and embody will continue to flourish. "It is no accident that the best newspapers in America are those controlled by families to whom newspapering is a sacred trust," Mr. Bradlee writes -- leaving mercifully unstated the fact that so many of the major papers fitting that description have folded (or been folded into chains) that the survivors can be counted on the fingers of one hand. For his part, Mr. Brinkley had the good fortune to complete his book before ABC News, his supportive journalistic home for the final chapter of his career, was betrothed to Disney.
The erosion in their old journalistic landscape doesn't end there. The proposed merger of Time Warner and Turner Broadcasting will further reduce the nation's store of truly independent news organizations. So will the telecommunications bill now emerging from Congress; in the House version of this deregulatory windfall, a single media company could end up controlling the TV, radio and print news outlets in the same market.
Even assuming that these new mega-media monsters are run by people of the caliber of, say, Katharine Graham -- a highly optimistic assumption -- they still may spell the end of journalism as Mr. Bradlee and Mr. Brinkley knew it. Media corporations this vast tend to speak in a bland, cautious voice that sounds more like "Headline News" than David Brinkley. And because these companies have so many more economic interests whose fate depends on the kindness of politicians -- whether in Washington or those ever-proliferating foreign markets -- they may be less likely to let their relatively puny (and low-profit) news divisions rock the boat. Indeed, such self-interest may explain why the mega-media's coverage of the telecommunications bill itself has been so spotty that few Americans understand its momentous implications.
Other journalistic conflicts of interest, meanwhile, pile up in exponential relation to media mergers. Will Rupert Murdoch's Fox News cover Newt Gingrich aggressively? Will Time Warner publications or CNN flush out Michael Milken's role in their proposed union? Will ABC take the investigative lead the next time its new parent company cozies up to state legislators to promote a scheme like Disney's America?
Don't hold your breath, especially after the current generation of executives at these corporations is gone, taking with it any institutional memory of journalistic independence. The end of the Bradlee-Brinkley era may be only the beginning of a spell of bad news.

HEADLINE: Observer;
Failure Under The Gun

BYLINE: By RUSSELL BAKER 

 BODY:
O.K., this was going to be a brilliant disquisition -- no, I'm not sure what disquisition really means -- and who cares? I'm too upset to care. Anyhow, disquisition feels right here.
Disquisition, inquisition, quisition -- whatever -- that's what this was to be. It was half written on Number One Computer. It's still in there somewhere. In Number One Computer, the big Number One, with beautiful colors, ringing bells, all that Windows junk. Useless. Useless, useless, useless. 
 "What's it all about, Alfie?"
It's about what it was going to be about. It was going to be about the new hard-nosed labor policy being promised by the new leader of the A.F.L.-C.I.O.
It was going to be wonderfully thoughtful, stuffed with trenchant economics thoughts, with highly doubtable generalizations, with poignant thoughts about the human heart.
There were going to be many delightfully constructed long sentences, demonstrating that just because you're a journalist it doesn't mean you can't write just as flowery as Henry James --
" 'Flowery' won't do, Alfie. Not if you're going up against Henry James. 'Flowery' is an adjective, and you need an adverb. The Master would never have misused an adjective as an adverb."
All right, all right already. Make it flowerly. Just because you're a journalist doesn't mean you can't write as flowerly as Henry James. So what happens? I'll tell you what happens. What happens is this idiotic, selfish, useless Number One Computer --
"Before lapsing into incoherent rage, perhaps you would tell us what your half-written column about the new labor militancy was going to say."
What are you, dense or something? What do you think it was going to say? It was going to say what a good column ought to say. Namely, that while new labor toughness might be a blessing for America, it might also be a disaster, but only time would tell.
"Surely you're joking. That's all it was going to say? That only time would tell?"
Look, it was only half finished. How do I know what it might have said at the end? It might have said something brilliant. How do I know what it was going to say when I couldn't even finish writing it? Just look at that computer. I can't even see the half that's already written.
"It certainly looks like a computer determined not to reveal any of its contents. Those 20 vertical white stripes, each with 25 pink little symbols that might be the letter 'L,' look less like a disquisition on labor than like a rather nasty piece of wallpaper."
I hate that computer.
"Please, please! Not another sneer at the computer. You know it generates all that hate mail from persons who adore their computers and point out that, fortunately, computer haters like you will soon all be dead."
I spit in the milk of the computers of such people.
"A joke rooted in Hemingway's 'For Whom the Bell Tolls' will not be grasped by many Americans today and will merely be taken by those who do grasp it as proof that you are too dull to make up a joke of your own."
O.K., I love my computers. But only when they work. If this disgracefully old Computer Number Two -- bright orange letters on a dull orange background -- were not working flawlessly right now, a deadline would be missed, and another vital communication would be lost to civilization.
I love Computer Number Two for working right now, for not letting me down like Computer Number One with its glorious colors and its amazing devices for putting me in touch with the whole darn universe if only it were working and somebody would write a manual explaining clearly how to use it to make contact with said universe. I also want to love Computer Number One, but cannot.
If I were a gun person I would shoot it right in its blank, staring, useless eye. Fortunately, I am not a gun person. This has saved many a car of mine from being shot when it broke down at some critical moment and sat idly on the highway snickering at my helplessness. This computer will live. In some remote future, archeologists may revive it and discover an astonishing disquisition on union labor back in the 20th century.

SECTION: Section 1; Page 1; Column 6; National Desk 

LENGTH: 1590 words

HEADLINE: BATTLE OVER THE BUDGET: THE OVERVIEW;
SENATE SOFTENS SOME EDGES IN PASSING, 52-47, ITS VERSION OF VAST BUDGET CUTBACKS

BYLINE: By JERRY GRAY 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Saturday, Oct. 28

 BODY:
After giving ground on welfare, Medicaid and several other issues important to moderate Republicans and the White House, the Senate early today followed the lead of the House and approved a bill designed to balance the Federal budget by 2002.
Included in the bill are provisions that would cut taxes by $245 billion and turn over to the states much more responsibility to care for the country's poor. 
 But along the way to the 52 to 47 vote on the tax and spending legislation, the more moderate Senate veered to the left of the House of Representatives on several measures. It voted to dropp a provision that would allow corporations to withdraw billions of dollars in surplus assets from their pension funds, retain the Federal guarantee of Medicaid coverage for a variety of poor people and keep Federal regulations on nursing homes.
Deploying a Senate rule that prohibits amendments to the budget if they have no effect on the deficit, Democrats forced the Republicans to strip from the bill 46 provisions, including several relating to welfare. Those proposals would have allowed states to deny cash assistance to children born to unmarried women under the age of 18 or to welfare mothers of any age who have additional children.
"It is a vote for real, meaningful and fundamental change," said Senator Bob Dole of Kansas, the Republican majority leader. "And, above all, it is a vote for America's future."
Nonetheless, Mr. Dole said he expected President Clinton to veto the final bill that will be negotiated by the Senate and the House. "President Clinton says he wants change," he said. "But his actions speak much louder than his words."
Senator Tom Daschle of South Dakota, the minority leader, said that the fight over the reconciliation bill had united Democrats and predicted that it would give them a political club to beat the Republicans.
"This budget is mean and extreme," Mr. Daschle said. "It rewards the rich and ravages the rest. It punishes families who need our help most to pay for tax breaks for those who need handouts the least."
"This budget is 'D.B.A.' -- dead before arrival -- and is certain to get the veto it so richly deserves," he said.
The final vote, which came after three days of debate, followed party lines with one exception: Senator William S. Cohen, a moderate Republican from Maine, voted with Democrats against the bill.
Despite an agreement that limited debate on most amendments to 30 seconds for each side, the Senate's effort to complete work on the budget legislation bogged down under the weight of dozens of proposals and a glut of procedural matters.
Between 9:15 A.M. Friday when the debate resumed and 12:09 A.M. today, when the last vote was cast, there were 39 roll call votes -- breaking the Senate's single-day voting record.
The previous record of 34 roll call votes was set on June 16, 1964, during the debate on the Civil Rights Act. But that mark fell shortly after 9 P.M. when the Senate, on a 53 to 46 roll call vote, the 35th of the day, tabled an amendment by Senate Robert C. Byrd, a Democrat from West Virginia, to remove all tax cuts from the bill.
For the better part of Friday, Mr. Dole tried to prod Democrats into dropping some of their amendments and to coax some of his own members back into the fold. Still, the proceedings dragged on well into the night.
The 1,900 pages of legislation -- known as the reconciliation bill because it reconciles Congress's broadly painted budget resolution, adopted months ago, with a detailed spending plan -- would bring the Federal budget into balance over seven years, cut taxes by $245 billion during that period and turn over to the states much more of the responsibility of caring for the nation's poor.
Over all, it is very similar to the version passed on Thursday in the House. Central to both versions are huge changes to Medicare, the health insurance program for the elderly, and Medicaid, the insurance program for the poor.
But the more moderate Senate pulled back from several provisions adopted by the House.
By 77 to 22, for instance, the Senate voted to protect the Federal guarantee of Medicaid coverage for pregnant women, children under 12 and the disabled.
And by a vote of 51 to 48, it agreed to preserve tough Federal standards for nursing homes. The House, by contrast, would eliminate most of the Federal regulations and allow states to set their own rules.
The White House was quick to praise those votes, but just as quick to make clear that the Administration thought the bill was still too extreme in many particulars.
"I want to balance the budget," President Clinton said. "I'm determined to secure the Medicare trust fund. I want a reasonable tax cut targeted to the middle class, child-rearing and education. But I have these other principles on health care, on education, on the environment, on technology."
"And one other thing," Mr. Clinton added, faulting the legislation for its provision scaling back the earned-income tax credit, "I cannot in good conscience support a budget that actually raises taxes on people with incomes under $30,000 a year."
The budget bill has became an especially contentious issue this year because Republicans have used it as the vehicle for most of the fiscal and social changes that they promised in adopting their campaign themes a year ago.
But Friday's debate in the Senate never rose to the pitched battle that those proposed changes have usually sparked in recent months.
The reason was that the real budget fight will not come for several weeks, not until Mr. Clinton vetoes whatever version of the bill emerges from a House-Senate conference and then engages in negotiations on a compromise with the Republican-controlled Congress.
Nevertheless, for every significant provision on which the Senate bill varies greatly from its House counterpart, there is a corresponding increase in the chances of a lively preliminary bout pitting Republican against Republican in the conference committee.
The differences that the negotiators from the two houses will have to reconcile involve not only money but also wide philosophical divides.
"There are a number of places in the two bills where we are going to be different, but we knew that going in," Speaker Newt Gingrich said. "Some are things the Senate can't accept but we have; some are things that probably we can't accept that the Senate has. But we will get it done."
Neither Mr. Gingrich nor Mr. Dole would dwell on any specific differences. "I don't see any real big thing," Mr. Dole said.
But there are plenty of points of friction, and one of the more significant is the issue of pensions.
Under current law, a company may dip into the surplus assets of employee pension funds only to finance other employee benefits. But under a provision of the House-passed budget bill, such a company would be permitted to do so for any reason -- including even financing the takeover of another company -- as long as the assets remaining in the fund totaled at least 125 percent of the company's current pension liability.
Democrats have maintained that despite the 25 percent cushion, the provision would leave employee pensions vulnerable in the event of a sharp downturn in the financial markets where the funds are invested.
Until Friday, though, Senate Republicans, like their counterparts in the House, had overwhelmingly supported the provision, in part, they said, because the money withdrawn would be immediately subject to taxation. Indeed, the Republicans had estimated that the taxes paid on this money by companies would bring $5 billion to the Treasury over the next seven years.
 
CONGRESS vs. CLINTON
 
Taxes
 
PASSED BY CONGRESS
Reduction of $245 billion over seven years; most families would get a $500 tax credit per child, but fewer low-income workers would be eligible for earned-income tax credit.
 
CLINTON'S VIEW
"I believe we ought to help working families raise their children and educate themselves and their children and give tax reductions for those purposes. But I do not favor funding them by raising the price of Medicare on the poorest elderly people." Sept. 15
 
Medicare and Medicaid
 
PASSED BY CONGRESS
Spending on each program over the next seven years would by $270 billion and $182 billion less, respecitvely, than it would be under current law. States would get more control over how remaining Medicaid funds would be spent.
 
CLINTON'S VIEW
"You can have reductions in cost inflation in Medicare and Medicaid, without these Draconian consequences....If these health care cuts come to my desk, of this size, I would have no choice but to veto it." Sept. 15
 
Welfare
 
PASSED BY CONGRESS
Federal subsistence guarantee to poor children would end. Instead, states would get a smaller amount of money to spend on support for poor families, but more contol over how to spend it.
 
CLINTON'S VIEW
"It really would end welfare as we know it....If we can make it a little better in conference I'll be happy to sign it. If they make it a lot worse, they could kill it." Sept. 21
 
Environment
 
PASSED BY CONGRESS
The Arctic National Wildlife Refugee in Alaska would be opened to oil exploration; mining would be expanded on Federal Lands.
 
CLINTON'S VIEW
"The President will veto any reconciliation bill that opens the Artic National Wildlife Refuge to oil and gas drilling."
Alice M. Rivlin, Director, Office of Management and Budget, Oct. 18 letter to the House

HEADLINE: Crack and Punishment: Is Race the Issue?

BYLINE: By CHARISSE JONES 

 BODY:
The drug-sentencing bill attracted little notice in Washington, compared with the attention focused on the pitched debate over the future of health care and welfare. But prisoner rights groups say word of its passage flashed through Federal prisons, and soon after there were rumblings of possible disturbances.
Prison officials will not say if they believe the uprisings that followed at five sites were related to the vote in Congress. But the disturbances have suddenly drawn attention to the debate over mandatory Federal sentencing laws for crack cocaine offenses. These laws, which Congress voted to maintain in the bill, are harsher than those for crimes involving powdered cocaine, and many lawyers and legislators say they have a disproportionate impact on poor black men. 
 "It is both unfair, impractical, and unwarranted," said Laura Murphy, director of the Washington, D.C., chapter of the American Civil Liberties Union, who called race issues in the criminal justice system "the new frontier" of civil rights. "How can you go to an inner-city family and tell them their son is given 20 years, while someone in the suburbs who's using powdered cocaine in greater quantities can get off with 90 days' probation? When people understand the truth about the way these laws are imposed, the fact they've had no deterrent, and the race-based nature of these prosecutions, then I think a sleeping giant is going to roar."
But others say the violence and addiction that lie in the crack epidemic's wake explain why such offenses are more harshly punished.
"What about the gangs, the rivalries, the shootings, the killings?" said Jim Shedd, an agent and spokesman for the Drug Enforcement Administration in Miami. "Everybody's forgotten about the amount of violence that goes with crack cocaine. It's not a racist thing, but unfortunately that's what it's turned into."
Last week, during the march on Washington by hundreds of thousands of black men, and later in an emotional debate on the floor of the House of Representatives, speakers decried Federal laws that say it takes 100 times more powdered cocaine to draw the same mandatory minimum sentence of five years in prison that awaits a person convicted of possessing five grams of crack.
The United States Sentencing Commission, created by Congress to draft, monitor and amend sentencing guidelines for the Federal courts, recommended that legislators scrap the 100-to-1 ratio, and make equal the amounts of crack and cocaine powder that draw the same base sentences. It suggested a number of enhancements that would more severely punish offenders who had also used a weapon, or committed other crimes. But instead lawmakers passed legislation that rejected those recommendations, and urged the body to re-examine its findings.
This week, the Congressional Black Caucus sent a letter to President Bill Clinton urging him to veto Congress's bill. Representative Maxine Waters, Democrat of California, who attacked the sentencing laws during the House debate, has written to prison wardens asking them to relay to Attorney General Janet Reno their concerns about the impact of the uneven penalties.
Challenges to the penalties have never been upheld in Federal courts. But in 1991, the Minnesota Supreme Court struck down a state mandatory sentencing law that mirrored the disparities in the Federal statutes. And in Miami, some Federal prosecutors say they have chosen not to charge some crack suspects because they believe the punishment they will face is unduly harsh.
With a set of mandatory minimum sentencing laws passed in 1986, Congress for the first time distinguished between crack and powder cocaine. Crack, which emerged in the mid-1980's, had been deemed a new drug plague, blamed for escalating gang violence and for a surge in the number of neglected children flooding the child welfare system.
Crack is the only drug that carries a mandatory prison term for possession, whether or not the intent is to distribute, said Paul Martin, deputy staff director for the sentencing commission. Possession of heroin or powdered cocaine, without intent to sell, is a misdemeanor carrying a maximum of one year in jail. The commission had also sought to make punishment for simple crack possession equal to that of other drugs.
Although Federal statistics find that half of crack users are white, the sale and use of the substance, a cheaper form of cocaine, is often concentrated in poor, urban, minority communities, experts say. Last year, 90 percent of those convicted of Federal crack offenses were black, and 3.5 percent were white, sentencing commission officials say. By contrast, 25.9 percent of those convicted on Federal powdered cocaine charges were white, 29.7 percent were black and 42.8 percent were Hispanic.
"When we saw those statistics," said Judge Richard P. Conaboy, the sentencing commission's chairman, "our theory was a law, no matter how well-intentioned it was, if it's causing such discrepant results, then the law has to be changed and a new method has to be installed."
The commission's recommendations grew out of a study requested by Congress, said the judge, who added he was not surprised by last week's prison unrest.
Commissioners often spoke with prison officials, he said, and "in the course of our dialogue with them, we learned that a lot of their prison inmates are serving time under drug sentences, and that many of the families of these young black men in particular are concerned and hopeful that there will be some change made in that particular statute. And if the change was not made it might lead to problems."
But others say crack deserves its harsher penalties. While most experts agree that cocaine, be it a crystallized pellet or powder, does not cause its user to become violent, many law-enforcement officials say crack's bustling street trade is particularly vicious.
"The closer we get to the crack dealers on the street," said Los Angeles Deputy District Attorney Curtis Hazell, who heads the major narcotics unit, "the more likely they are to have a past history of violence."
Of those convicted in 1993 of Federal offenses involving powdered cocaine, 15.1 percent were given additional time for using a weapon, as compared with 28 percent of crack offenders, commission officials said.
"Crack clearly causes more trouble than powdered cocaine," said Dr. Robert Byck, professor of psychiatry and pharmacology at the Yale University School of Medicine. "When rock was introduced, the number of hospitalizations for cocaine impairment went way up, and the number of people addicted went way up."
But some sociologists argue that many of the ills associated with crack have as much to do with poverty, unemployment and homelessness as the drug itself, and that society should focus on creating more jobs and treatment programs.
Others say there are negligible pharmacological differences between crack and powdered cocaine. Though smoking crack makes the drug's high quicker and stronger, some doctors note that powdered cocaine can also be smoked, when it is concentrated using the technique known as free-basing. Powdered cocaine can also be injected, they point out, which is more likely to lead to addiction.
"The potency, dangerousness and risk depends on multiple factors," said Dr. Mitchell S. Rosenthal, president of the Phoenix House Foundation, a national drug treatment organization. "A person who is selling cocaine in the powdered form or crack form will both bring about a great deal of harm in the user. So does it make sense for a sentencing disparity? Probably not."
The primary difference between crack and powdered cocaine, some say, is the public perception of the user and seller -- the white suburbanite usually linked with powdered cocaine, and the young, urban black man connected to crack.
"If these were young white men going to jail, this would not exist," said William Moffitt, a lawyer and treasurer of the National Association of Criminal Defense Lawyers, which has challenged the mandatory sentences in court. "So the war on drugs is essentially being borne by the black community."
The Sentencing Project, a national nonprofit organization that deals with criminal justice issues, recently released a report finding that one in three black men between the ages of 20 and 29 are in the grasp of the criminal justice system.
Marc Mauer, the project's assistant director, said the nation's drug policies were one of the primary reasons for this.
"Every middle-class parent knows that if their son or daughter started using drugs, the first response would be to find the best treatment program they could afford," Mr. Mauer said. "It would never occur to them to write out a check for $20,000 and reserve a space in the prison system for their kid's problem. We have the same problem, yet the responses are different -- one a public health response, the other a criminal justice response -- largely due to the individual who's affected by it."
Mr. Mauer and others said no one had proved that the stringent crack penalties have reduced other crimes or slowed addiction. And he and several others contended that the mandatory laws are too broad, punishing a small-time dealer caught with a few grams of crack as harshly as a drug kingpin.
But some law-enforcement officials argue that more blacks are being jailed for crack offenses because they are the ones committing the crimes.
"It's not disproportionate considering who's dealing," said Mr. Hazell, the prosecutor in Los Angeles. "It's like complaining that not enough Chinese are being prosecuted. But that's because it's not the Chinese that are doing that crime."

HEADLINE: Economy Surged 4.2% in Quarter And Inflation Rate Slipped Again

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr. 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 27

 BODY:
Surpassing nearly all expectations, the economy rebounded vigorously over the summer to grow at a 4.2 percent pace while inflation, already low, subsided further, the Commerce Department reported today.
The Clinton Administration sought to turn the combination of swelling output, steady prices and the likelihood of brighter prospects for job growth to its advantage as the White House enters the final stages of budget negotiations with the Republican-controlled Congress. 
 "We're on the right track," President Clinton declared, citing the sunny report on the nation's gross domestic product -- the Government's broadest measure of the economy -- as evidence that his domestic policies were working. He also noted that the budget deficit dropped substantially for a third consecutive year in the fiscal year that ended on Sept. 30. "We do not need to reverse our economic course. That's the message I have for the Congress."
Senator Bob Dole of Kansas, the majority leader and leading candidate for the Republican Presidential nomination, was not as enthusiastic as Mr. Clinton about the latest economic news. "One quarter of growth -- however welcome -- will not solve our problem," he said. "You can't erase 25 straight years of deficit spending with one quarter of growth in the economy."
Securities markets initially sputtered when the Government disclosed the strength of the economy in the last quarter, which actually represented the latest uptick in four and a half years of uninterrupted economic expansion. Traders sent bond prices down because of worries that robust growth might keep the Federal Reserve from cutting interest rates when it holds its next policy meeting on Nov. 15.
Upon reflection, however, the markets recovered. Most analysts have decided that the Fed will not embark on another round of interest-rate cuts based on the latest economic data, opting instead to wait until Congress and the White House agree on a deficit-reduction package.
Bonds made up much of their lost ground and stocks jumped sharply, recovering a good share of the market's big loss on Thursday. The Dow Jones industrial average closed at 4,741.75, up 37.93 points. [Page 37.]
A deeper look at the economic data apparently cheered investors. Analysts, noting that prices remained in check, predicted that the growth spurt was not likely to sustain a fast enough pace in the near future to rekindle inflationary embers.
Not only did the economy expand more than three times as fast as its lackluster 1.3 percent pace in the spring, but the growth was considerably better balanced. And the buildup of inventories, which most analysts had expected to slacken and thus cut into the growth of G.D.P., actually continued.
"There's plenty of evidence of a nice recovery from some temporary weakness earlier in the year," said David J. Greenlaw, an economist at Morgan Stanley.
An interest rate cut by the Fed in November has "close to zero probability," he said.
The housing sector, which is especially sensitive to changes in interest rates, was especially robust in the latest quarter, expanding at a 10.9 percent annual pace after a 13.9 percent decline in the spring. Exports of goods and services climbed at a 10.6 percent pace, and the trade sector, which had been a drag on growth earlier in the year, contributed to growth because exports outpaced imports. Purchases by the Federal Government, benefiting from military items bought at the end of the fiscal year, advanced at a 4.9 percent pace after declining for three straight quarters.
Spending by consumers, which accounts for two-thirds of all economic activity, moderated to a 2.9 percent annual rate of growth, from 3.4 percent in the spring quarter, the report also showed. An upturn for motor vehicles and parts lifted durable goods to a big gain after a flat first half. This was partly offset, however, by a flat quarter for production of nondurable goods like food and clothing and a cooling in demand for services.
Business investment in equipment, which has long soared at double-digit rates, slackened in the quarter to 8.3 percent. But that rise has been exaggerated, many economists contend, by a distortion in the way the figures price computers. The Government plans to start measuring the economy in a different way next year in an effort to more accurately reflect its output.
Looking at it in that light, "investment actually fell as a share of nominal G.D.P. for the first time in the recovery," said Dean Baker, an analyst at the liberal Economic Policy Institute.
As for inflation, what American residents pay for goods and services -- known as the fixed-weighted price index for gross domestic purchases -- rose at a 2.1 percent pace in the third quarter, compared with 2.8 percent in the previous period, which ended in June. A second gauge that takes account of changes in purchasing patterns, known as the deflator, rose only six-tenths of 1 percent, leading Commerce Secretary Ronald H. Brown to boast of "sustained low levels" of inflation not seen since the Kennedy Administration.
Still, some economists even found a cloud amid the clear skies of such price stability, contending that corporate profit margins were suffering because of an inability to raise prices.
David Orr, senior vice president of the investment banking subsidiary of the First Union Corporation of Charlotte, N.C., said that companies "are becoming less aggressive in their hiring and capital spending plans, which will then result in less new demand for goods and services."
In current dollars, the economy expanded at a 4.8 percent pace in the third quarter, and was running at a rate to produce more than $7 trillion in goods and services this year. Adjusted for inflation, the pace of growth was 4.2 percent. "Nearly every dollar of nominal spending contributed a dollar to real growth," Merrill Lynch analysts observed.
The savings rate -- personal savings as a percentage of personal disposable income -- edged up two-tenths of a point, to 4.2 percent, the report showed. At a briefing, Joseph E. Stiglitz, chairman of the President's Council of Economic Advisers, disputed those who earlier this week found cause for concern in data showing rising consumer debt.
He contended that the burden of servicing higher debt was manageable because of lower interest rates. He also noted that delinquencies remained low and that the rising stock market had generated large amounts of fresh wealth.
But he did express concern that political turmoil over the budget could raise interest rates, possibly affecting the millions of households with adjustable-rate mortgages.
Data included in the gross domestic product for the third quarter of 1995 follow, with all dollar amounts in billions at seasonally adjusted annual rates. Percentage changes are from the previous quarter at seasonally adjusted annual rates.
