LENGTH: 1882 words

HEADLINE: U.S. Cooling Ties to Croatia After Winking at Its Buildup

BYLINE: By ROGER COHEN 

DATELINE: ZAGREB, Croatia, Oct. 26

 BODY:
On March 24, 1994, Gojko Susak, the Croatian Defense Minister, wrote to the Pentagon appealing for assistance. "Our goal," he wrote, was the peaceful transition of the Croatian military "to one which follows the model of the United States."
The Croatian Army sorely needed help. Hammered by the Serbs in 1991, battered by the Muslim-led Bosnian Army in 1993, its brief history was one of failure. But the United Nations arms embargo imposed in 1991 on Yugoslavia and its former republics made direct military assistance illegal. 
 Mr. Susak's request was referred to a company called Military Professional Resources Inc., based in Alexandria, Va., and run by a group of retired American officers. "This," said Ed Soyster, a retired lieutenant-general and one the company's vice presidents, "is the greatest corporate assemblage of military expertise in the world."
Just what has been done with that American expertise, and whether any infringement of the arms embargo may be involved, is not entirely clear. The American generals say they have done no more than teach leadership skills and advise the Croats on the role of an army in a democracy.
Nonetheless, the Croatian Army, which has also made large clandestine purchases of heavy weapons, mainly in eastern Europe, was quickly transformed into the most effective proven fighting force in the region. And the role of the retired American generals in that transformation has become one element in the increasingly troubled web of relations between the United States and Croatia.
"Croatia became our de facto strategic ally," said a State Department official with intimate knowledge of the workings of the Zagreb embassy. "Arms flowed into Croatia despite the embargo, and this was not an issue. It was never raised. Not once. Now, we're starting to wonder: What have we unleashed in Croatia, and where it is going?"
Several factors have contributed to this new uneasiness about a country that has served until now as the Clinton Administration's pivotal Balkan ally in the search for a balance of power that could open the way to a peace settlement.
To this end, senior officials said, vast Croatian arms imports, in flagrant defiance of the embargo, have been systematically ignored while some top retired American generals have been allowed to advise the Croatian Army on modernizing.
Now, however, Croatian human-rights abuses during and since the recapture of the Serbian-held Krajina region in August are troubling the Administration. Apprehension has been growing over Croatian territorial ambitions in Bosnia and over persistent Croatian threats to retake Serbian-held eastern Slavonia.
More generally, an uneasy sense has taken hold among senior officials that Washington may have allowed itself to get too cozy with a Government whose rhetoric is often laced with thinly veiled racism toward Bosnia's Muslims.
"There are many marriages of convenience in the Balkans, and our relationship with Croatia has been one of them," said a senior Pentagon official. "Let's face it, you can't get to Bosnia without going through Croatia. You need their ports, you need their lines of communication. But right now we're not happy. We see the whole set-up there as overly militaristic."
These concerns have been forcefully conveyed to the Croatian President, Franjo Tudjman, over the last 10 days by the chief American envoy to the Balkans, Richard C. Holbrooke, and by President Clinton himself. Senior officials said the President had raised the threat of economic sanctions against Croatia in the event of an attack on oil-rich eastern Slavonia.
"Let's just say our relationship has entered a cooling-off period," said one senior American diplomat.
But troubling questions remain as the United States tries to recast its relationship with Croatia, especially as the possibility increases that more than 20,000 American peacekeepers will be sent to the region.
They center on whether American advice or intelligence was provided for the campaign in Krajina, which resulted in atrocities and the uprooting of more than 170,000 Serbs; how far a tacit alliance with Croatia has gone, and whether an American-led NATO mission to police any settlement is compatible with such close ties to Croatia.
Allowing Croatia to arm itself -- it spent an estimated $1 billion on arms in the last year -- has been one pillar of America's Balkan policy. The arms, including tanks and M1-24 armored assault helicopters, were acquired mainly in eastern Europe, Croatian officials said.
Croatia became the chief conduit for weapons to the Muslim-led Bosnian Army, but only after taking its cut, about 30 percent of all shipments, Western officials said.
The buildup has been complemented by the efforts of Military Professional Resources, , which has been active in Croatia this year.
The company occupies one floor of a small modern building in Alexandria. Conference rooms have names like the Infantry Room, where Mr. Soyster greets guests, the Cavalry room and the Artillery Room. A plaque on one wall says: "War is an ugly thing, but not the ugliest of things. The decayed and degraded state of moral and patriotic feeling which thinks that nothing is worth war is much worse."
In order to get a license from the State Department, the company had to undertake not to provide direct military planning or advice on strategy to the Croatian Army: This would violate the arms embargo.
Activities were to be limited to courses on leadership, the training of non-commissioned officers, the place of an army in an emergent democracy and other subjects unrelated to the obsession of the Croatian Army: the fact that, when the mission began, about 30 percent of Croatia was occupied by Serbs.
"It was kind of frustrating," Mr. Soyster said. "No military plans. No strategy. Those were our constraints."
For this mission, the company deployed some extremely high-powered people, including Gen. Carl E. Vuono, who was the Army chief of staff from 1987 to 1992, and Gen. Crosbie E. Saint, who was the commander of the United States Army in Europe from 1988 until 1992.
Some idea of the importance accorded the mission by the Croats can be seen in their attendance at a "leadership skills" course. Every Government minister turned up, and Mr. Soyster said President Tudjman also appeared.
The force behind the American presence was clearly the Defense Minister, Mr. Susak, a brisk and ruthless man with a successful career in the pizza business in Canada behind him and an obsession with remaking the Croatian Army to fight the Serbs. He became so confident about the arms buildup that he would joke about it with American officials, a State Department official said. The minister did not respond to a request for an interview.
Gen. Anton Tus, chief military adviser to Mr. Tudjman, said: "We are very satisfied by what the American generals have done for our top-level commanders and some lower levels. General Vuono has been helping us get closer to the standards of NATO. However, it is wrong to believe that he or other American officers have given any specific, operative military instructions."
But the nature of the attack on the Krajina, dubbed Uluja, or Storm, has led many Western military analysts to believe that American planning and advice played a role.
The lightning five-pronged offensive, integrating air power, artillery and rapid infantry movements, and relying on intense maneuvers to unhinge Serbian command and control networks, bore many hallmarks of United States Army doctrine.
An American officer working for United Nations forces called the attack a textbook campaign. He said: "It was carried out on an unsophisticated level, but for me the evidence of American instruction was unmistakable. You don't just stumble on what the Croats have achieved."
Mr. Soyster, who declined to disclose the value of the multi-year contract with the Croatian Government, denied that there had been infringement of the arms embargo. "The guys who run this company ran the American Army, and we are not going to do anything that is against United States policy" he said.
However, a Croatian liaison officer who handled contacts between the two generals and the Croatian army told Roman Majetic, a widely respected journalist in Croatia, that in the five days before the offensive, at least 10 meetings were held between General Vuono and senior officers involved in the Krajina campaign. This account could not be independently confirmed.
"According to this officer, there were a series of computer simulations," Mr. Majetic said. "These were not instructions but scenarios. It was pitched at the theoretical level, but it turned out to be true. What General Vuono and General Saint hypothesized was more or less what happened in reality during the Krajina offensive."
General Tus said: "It may be people came to that conclusion because of speed and strategy. I am not saying that these meetings did not take place. I'm just saying that I'm not aware of it. It is true that General Vuono was in the country at that time."
Mr. Soyster confirmed that General Vuono and General Saint were in Zagreb in the days leading up to the Krajina offensive, and they met with Mr. Susak and with the chief of the general staff of the Croatian Army. "But there was absolutely no wargaming," he added. "We were not even aware the attack was about to happen."
But it is clear that the officer class of the Croatian Army, particularly at the noncommissioned level, has gone through a radical transformation guided by retired American officers and that this has helped create the most potent military force in the area. Only 4 percent of Croatia is now in Serbian hands, and the Croatian Army has taken more than 20 percent of Bosnia.
Having helped in various ways to create this force, the United States is now concerned that it must be contained, especially after the Croats burned 70 percent of the Serbian homes in the southern Krajina, executed dozens of aged Serbs and evicted thousands more.
"You'd have to say we gave the green light for the Krajina offensive because our admonitions were at best half-hearted," said Charles Thomas, who retired recently from the State Department, where he worked on the Muslim-Croat federation in Bosnia. "But now we're worried by the Croatian military ascendancy. The balance between Muslims and Croats is too weighted to the Croats. It's skewed."
Mr. Soyster said the atrocities during the Krajina offensive were "a major area of concern." But he added that the courses were continuing.
The next target would clearly be eastern Slavonia. Mr. Susak has said repeatedly that by Christmas he will be drinking coffee in Vukovar, the town the Serbs leveled in 1991 when they took the area, slaughtering and evicting Croats. Last week Mr. Tudjman said the army would invade if the region were not returned by the end of November.
The Clinton Administration, switching signals, has told the Croats that it is determined to stop any attack. But the American officer working for the United Nations said: "With the money they have spent, and the training they have received, the Croatian Army is one that's going places. It is definitely not an army on the verge of demobilization."

HEADLINE: BATTLE OVER THE BUDGET: RAISING REVENUE;
Airwaves Plan Is Called Give-away to Broadcasters

BYLINE: By EDMUND L. ANDREWS 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 27

 BODY:
Buried in the huge budget bill emerging from Congress is a plan to pull $14 billion from the air -- literally -- by selling off a chunk of the nation's airwaves over the next seven years.
But in a move that has drawn criticism from liberals and conservatives alike, the same provision would give television broadcasters a package of licenses for "advanced television service," such as high-definition or interactive television, that Government economists say could be worth as much as $70 billion. 
 Though it has gone largely unnoticed, the tussle over selling off the nation's airwaves entails a huge and poorly understood scramble for money that significantly affects the budget deficit and the evolution of new communication services.
Everyone agrees that the nation's radio spectrum is worth tens of billions of dollars and offers one of the few seemingly painless ways in which the Government can reduce the deficit. Earlier this year, the Federal Communications Commission sold licenses for new wireless telephone services in an auction that raised $7.7 billion.
The real argument, largely behind the scenes, has been about which portion of the nation's airwaves should be put up for sale. Television broadcasters have waged a relentless lobbying campaign, arguing that every existing station must be given a second channel to carry out a transition to a new generation of digital broadcasting and high-definition television.
"Digital advanced television would never happen if there were auctions," said Lynn McReynolds, a spokeswoman for the National Association of Broadcasters.
But many experts and consumer advocates argue that the broadcasters are being handed a gift at taxpayers' expense. "It's a give-away if the broadcasters don't have to provide any public services in exchange," said Gigi Sohn, the deputy director of the Media Access Project, a nonprofit consumer advocacy group in Washington.
A number of conservative critics also maintain that the bill squanders a valuable public resource by limiting access to one of the best segments of radio spectrum available. "This is not going to help consumers get enhanced services and greater innovation, because that entails opening up the spectrum," said Thomas Hazlette, a telecommunications analyst at the American Enterprise Institute and a strong proponent of auctions.
The industry's arguments have been largely successful. After arduous bargaining over the summer -- mostly behind closed doors -- the House and Senate Commerce Committee came up with similar formulas: the Government must raise $14 billion from selling off a portion of the airwaves, but it would be prohibited from selling off channels that have been earmarked for advanced television services.
The numbers are huge. In a letter to Senator Joseph I. Lieberman, Democrat of Connecticut, the F.C.C. estimated that the television channels that are being reserved would have a market value of $10 billion to $70 billion. Using a separate approach, extrapolating from the recent sale of a New York City station, WNYC, for $208 million, commission officials said the nationwide value of the channels reserved for broadcasters would be $37 billion.
A handful of opponents in the Senate tried today to change the provision, but they are not expected to prevail. Senator John McCain, Republican of Arizona, offered an amendment on Thursday night that would have required the Government to auction off all new licenses of any type and estimated that the measure would bring in at least $35 billion. The amendment was defeated, 25 to 64.

CORRECTION-DATE: November 2, 1995, Thursday

 CORRECTION:
An article on Saturday about efforts by Congress to raise $14 billion by selling licenses for the nation's airwaves referred incorrectly to a Senate proposal on awarding channels for services like high-definition television. The bill would require the Federal Communications Commission to submit a plan for awarding the channels and then get Congressional approval; it is not like the House version, which would automatically give television broadcasters channels for those services.

HEADLINE: New Hope for the Innumerate

BYLINE: By Peter Passell; Peter Passell writes about economics for The New York Times.

 BODY:

A MATHEMATICIAN READS THE NEWSPAPER
By John Allen Paulos.
Illustrated. 212 pp. New York:
Basic Books. $18. 
 MATH -- or rather, Americans' fear and loathing thereof -- seems more the stuff of somber foundation reports than of best sellers. But six years ago John Allen Paulos, a professor of mathematics at Temple University, pulled off the impossible, writing a book so disarmingly charming that many who probably bought it out of guilt ended up reading it with pleasure.
Now the author of "Innumeracy" is back with what, on first glance, appears to be a gentle critique of the ways newspapers make hash of concepts that must be interpreted through the lens of mathematical reasoning. Mr. Paulos certainly does chide reporters for everything from the crime of failing to put new threats to health in context to the misdemeanor of offering impossibly precise breakdowns of calories, sodium and fat content for recipes that call for a pinch of this and a pinch of that. But his little book, "A Mathematician Reads the Newspaper," is mostly an excuse to do what Mr. Paulos does best -- namely, to explain the beauty (and relevance) of math to people who never got past the high school algebra problem that begins "two trains are speeding toward. . . ."
The book comprises some 52 bite-size essays. Among the highlights: an introduction to chaos theory in the context of changing fashions in clothing; the use of least-squares regression analysis to decide the outcome of a contested election in Pennsylvania; the utterly nonintuitive mathematics of affirmative action; Jeffrey Masson's defamation suit against Janet Malcolm as a segue to the theory of complexity; package labeling and the Central Limit theorem of statistics.
One essay guaranteed to startle is a lovely explanation of the elusive mathematics of coin flipping and its application to stock market forecasting. Picture a game in which I win on heads and you win on tails. On average, of course, each of us will win half the time. But Mr. Paulos notes that during a game consisting of 1,000 flips, it is far more likely that one or the other of us will have been ahead in cumulative scoring 90 percent of the time than, say, 55 percent of the time. The moral: Beware stock pickers whose claim to fame is to have been ahead of the Dow for most of the last decade. It's not as hard as it looks.
My own favorite illuminates the limits of voting as a means of expressing group preferences. He imagines a Senate debate over three health care plans in which 33 senators prefer plan A to plan B to plan C, 33 prefer B to C to A and 33 prefer C to A to B. (The 100th Senator, Bob Dole, "likes none of them.")
This implies that 66 senators prefer A to B, and 66 prefer B to C. Obviously, then, A is far better liked than C. Well, it isn't so obvious: go back to the blackboard, and you'll see that 66 senators prefer C to A. Maybe that's why Hillary Rodham Clinton is arranging flowers rather than writing legislation these days.
"A Mathematician Reads the Newspaper" won't help you balance your checkbook. It would even be stretching to claim that the book will improve your understanding of the ways reporters mangle body counts or explain inherently inexplicable changes in stock prices. At the very least, though, it will give the mathematically traumatized a glimpse of what they are missing.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Review

SECTION: Section 2; Page 1; Column 3; Arts and Leisure Desk 

LENGTH: 2186 words

HEADLINE: PHOTOGRAPHY VIEW;
Testing the Limits In a Culture of Excess

BYLINE: By VICKI GOLDBERG 

 BODY:
ONLY FRAGMENTS OF people are visible through a very dirty window, but what uncommon fragments they are: a naked back and one long, giraffe-print glove; hips and a star-spangled G-string with dollars tucked into it; the profile of a woman having sex with a man; a woman's arm outstretched in front of a pair of male hands. With a kind of ragged classicism, the window dividers gracefully section off clues to sex and drug transactions. Merry Alpern, a 40-year-old photojournalist, took these photographs unobserved from another window across an air shaft. She was looking into the bathroom of a sex club near Wall Street in Manhattan. So are we.
This project was selected to receive a grant by the peer-review panel of the National Endowment for the Arts in 1994, then vetoed by the National Council on the Arts, which reviews all recommendations. Collectors and museums (including the Museums of Modern Art in New York and San Francisco) nonetheless rushed to buy Alpern's compelling if unnerving pictures, which can currently be seen in a show called "Dirty Windows" at the Bonni Benrubi Gallery (52 East 76th Street, through Nov. 25) and in a book by the same name from Scalo. 
 Sexual imagery so permeates contemporary life that these photographs would hardly be surprising if they were studio performances rather than real-life documents recorded on the sly. Few people have seen what goes on in the bathroom of a sex club, but every week millions witness fictional sex and drug encounters -- some explicit, some merely suggestive -- in feature films, soap operas and advertisements.
Sex was kept pretty much under covers in popular culture until the 1960's (except, of course, in the sex trade -- pornography and burlesque). Titillation was a major staple of commerce but shock was not; it was assumed that an offended public would snap its purse shut. Only artists, who were outside the commercial loop, were entirely willing, and sometimes eager, to offend in sexual and other matters. Picasso and Dali were much more sexually explicit (if less naturalistic) than Warner Brothers or Vogue magazine.
It was and remains a prerogative of art to probe regions deemed off-limits, and art has long performed the function of finding new ways to look at familiar objects and new things to pay attention to. Such exploration was often transgressive and sometimes shocking. Manet's decidedly naked rather than nude "Olympia" shocked the French in 1865, and Duchamp's "Bottle Rack," a mass-produced object presented as a work of art, shocked them again in 1914. Dada artists tried not merely to loosen the tight laces of propriety but to undo the very order of things.
Commerce, however, was dependent on propriety and order. While advertising and entertainment teased, tantalized and were openly seductive -- think of cigar ads that purred, "Hey, Big Spender, spend a little time with me" -- they did not seriously try to breach the borders of the acceptable. That was the domain of art.
ONE OF THE BIGGEST stories in popular culture during the last third of this century (the work of the photographer Robert Mapplethorpe notwithstanding) is that the commercial sector has taken the lead away from art in the matter of breaking rules, abolishing taboos, pushing the envelope. Art, of course, challenges society's rules and norms at every level. Popular culture is mainly intent on widening the range of permissions for the display of sex and violence.
Everyone always knew sex and violence sold. What is new is the discovery that a mainstream audience would not only tolerate the casting off of a century's worth of restrictions and discretion but embrace enormous and rapid leaps from one broken taboo to the next.
What was once offensive is now a key to cash in the till. A mass audience today routinely spends money for the newest twist in sex and violence but gets generous daily helpings of the same free on billboards, newsstands and television: a passable description of decadence. This audience does sit up and complain sometimes, and very loudly too. Then it plunks down its money for more of the same.
The sensationalistic images that flood the visual environment appear far more frequently and get immeasurably greater distribution in the popular culture than they ever do in art. Yet art bears the brunt of public attacks.
In 1990, the Cincinnati Contemporary Arts Center was sued over its Mapplethorpe show despite displaying the most explicit pictures separately, as if rating them NC-17. In 1991, a female professor at Pennsylvania State University said that hanging Goya's "Naked Maja" in her classroom constituted sexual harassment because the picture prompted fantasies among her male students that made her workplace a hostile environment. In 1993, in response to another woman's sexual harassment charges, city officials in Menlo Park, Calif., removed two pleasant and rather innocent woodcuts of nude and seminude women from the walls of City Hall.
Yet no one stopped riding the buses in New York this summer when bus stops sported an ad for Versace jeans that showed a woman standing over and photographing a recumbent man whose foot was on her crotch, where her jeans were partly unbuttoned. Nor have women boycotted the parts of town where huge pictures of an apparently nude woman advertise pantyhose. It was a rare occasion this fall when complaints caused Calvin Klein to withdraw ads using very young models in scenes that looked like child pornography (and that maneuver simply did wonders for Klein's publicity).
Commercial images of all kinds are much less subject to public protest than art. Art, though central to culture, is considered elite, and many people resent it. But the business of America is business, and commercial images are protected by money -- lots of money -- and the mystique of private enterprise.
Life itself is so outrageous, what with tell-all talk shows, the O. J. Simpson and Menendez brothers murder trials and even the nightly news, that it is a wonder anyone can find anything to be shocked about, in art or anyplace else. Artists straggle on, sometimes straining after new transgressions, sometimes reduced to poking at the confines of art itself. Occasionally an artist stumbles onto something really forbidden, like the bathroom of a sex club.
WHEN THE INCREASINGLY blatant use of sexual imagery in the popular culture began some 30-odd years ago, it was partly a response to long-term repressions. From 1934 until the movie ratings system was introduced in 1968, the Hollywood Production Code kept the lid on, forbidding the display of kisses longer than 30 seconds, "intimate items of underwear," women's breasts (Howard Hughes had a huge row with the Hays Office, which administered the code, over Jane Russell's bosom in "The Outlaw") or illicit affairs ("For Whom the Bell Tolls" was another thorny problem).
Today, a Rip Van Winkle who had slept for 30 years might suppose that someone had inserted mandatory nakedness quotas into the commercial equation to make up for the old cover-up rules. (The more modest movie stars demand breast stand-ins, who might be called the cinema league's designated bodies.) Renoir, say, could paint only 60 nudes in a lifetime, but popular culture has the means to churn out nudes by the millions. It seems that once repressive and hypocritical barriers were knocked down, the result was less liberation than binge. We have achieved a stunning triumph of excess.
The media have probably broken more visual taboos in the last two decades than were overturned in the previous half century, not only raising the transgression ante but accelerating the pace of change. First underwear went public, then bodies, then simulated sex, frontal nudity, male nudity -- shifts so frequent and rapid that nakedness inexorably became cliched. Popular culture tends to repeat whatever looks financially successful; like films themselves, sexual images keep having sequels.
Every film of a certain ilk has a long panning shot of its sexpot star, starting at the foot and ascending slowly, the most recent being of Demi Moore standing for an unreasonably long and doubtless chilly time in a tub in "The Scarlet Letter." (John Donne once wrote a poem about the advantages of approaching his lover from the foot rather than the head; the only men who seem to have consistently followed his advice are cinematographers.)
Film makers use any old excuse to drag in frontal nudity or, having discovered from focus groups that women, too, have desires, male backsides (and a few discreet fronts). Kevin Costner appeared nude with his back to us in "Dances With Wolves." Gary Oldman skinny-dips in "The Scarlet Letter" so an admiring Demi Moore can spy on him, a neat, if obvious, reversal of the usual bathing scene.
Advertisers rack their brains to be equally original. In an ad for Valentino jeans, a woman wearing white jeans and nothing else is soberly embraced by four naked men. (Now you know what to wear the next time a similar occasion looms.) In an ad for Groves Brothers Fabrics in Elle Decor magazine, an artfully posed nude man holds up a long swath of damask that tactfully drapes his loins.
Sexual intercourse is simulated so often and so publicly that it seems repetitive, stereotyped, sometimes just plain fake and even -- you might have thought it impossible -- dull. Finding something more sensational to whet the appetite is not an easy task, but considerable creative talent has been devoted to it.
The plot of "Jade," a new sex-and-murder thriller directed by William Friedkin, depends on some grainy videos of a high-priced call girl in bed -- lite porn, you might call it. The sex is a bit more acrobatic than usual; she falls out of bed onto all fours and the man follows her. But, after all, we have already seen a woman pleasuring a man from behind with the aid of a stick of butter ("Last Tango in Paris"), a gang rape ("The Accused"), a woman ravishing and then killing a man she has tied up ("Basic Instinct") and a woman strangled during intercourse ("Rising Sun"), so what's new?
The problem is that shock goes stale faster than bread. It requires surprise or outrage, but once some level of the forbidden has been breached, it loses a good deal of its initial power. If repeated exposure to violence eventually renders viewers immune or indifferent, repeated exposure to sex does something similar. In a nudist colony, who pays attention to a flasher?
Thus, the sexual liberation of images has a built-in imperative to escalate. No doubt it has done us some good along the way. All the bedroom maneuvers on contemporary television soaps and in film may have helped a lot of people improve their sex lives. All those bodies in their undies or without them may have made others more comfortable with their own bodies, although the number of people dieting in this country suggests otherwise.
The constant focus on representations of sex and death looks less like hedonism than like the symptoms of a pervasive, free-floating anxiety and depression. Anxiety about economic problems, tensions between the sexes, broken families and fear of AIDS fuel a frantic search for pleasure, excitement, reassurance and connection, no matter how tenuous.
In a sense, we want popular imagery to substitute for lives we cannot achieve ourselves, as well as to soothe the lives we have. The down side is that however much good the entertainment media's sexual liberation has done or may do, it also burdens the audience with additional anxiety by holding up images, over and over, of beauty, nonstop pleasure and excitement that most lives cannot match.
Ads and movies and television have always purveyed fantasies. They used to emphasize the fantasy of the happy nuclear family eating cereal, merrily vacuuming the carpets or rooting for the Little Leaguer, but few people dare dream that one any more. The dominant fantasies are sexual or violent or both: pivotal matters embedded in the mind. Now the culture is calling on all good citizens to entertain them as often as possible and connect them to their wallets.
Major protests have been mounted against violent images and even hypersexuality, and not just from the religious right. "Showgirls," the movie that tried to bring the NC-17 rating into the mainstream; "Jade" and "The Scarlet Letter" did not fare as well as their producers hoped. Yet moviegoers who stayed away were protesting not the glut of sex but the poverty of invention. These movies were just plain boring, and the public, with plenty to choose from, prefers some kind of quality, even in titillation.
The protests over the resemblance of Calvin Klein's ads to pornography were especially loud because, right now, Americans are alarmed about child abuse, child pornography and the effects of media violence on children. We know how to shut down some levels of visual transgression. Although we are not always so eager to feed, school or tend to the health of all our children, at least we can rest assured that they will be safe from Calvin.

HEADLINE: Under Glass

BYLINE: By Bruce Bawer; Bruce Bawer is the author of "A Place at the Table: The Gay Individual in American Society" and "Prophets and Professors: Essays on the Lives and Works of Modern Poets."

 BODY:

VIRTUAL EQUALITY
The Mainstreaming of Gay and Lesbian Liberation.
By Urvashi Vaid.
440 pp. New York:
Anchor Books/
Doubleday. $24.95. 
 BEFORE Patrick J. Buchanan's anti-gay philippic at the 1992 Republican National Convention and the religious right's 1993 sabotaging of President Clinton's pledge to lift the ban on homosexuals in the military -- which propelled countless infuriated gay Americans out of the closet, many into activism -- the gay rights movement was essentially a creature of the political left. Between 1986 and 1992, Urvashi Vaid's successive roles as public information director and executive director of the National Gay and Lesbian Task Force put her, a young Vassar graduate and civil rights lawyer, at the center of the action. Noted for her belief that the movement should not only focus on equal rights for gay people but also seek "liberation" for women, people of color and the working class, Ms. Vaid responded to the influx of moderates into the gay rights movement with alarm, denouncing the "mainstreaming" of homosexuality as inimical to revolutionary change and calling for "a full-scale frontal assault" against "the coming of a racist, sexist gay and lesbian right." (There is, incidentally, no such right in sight.)
"Virtual Equality: The Mainstreaming of Gay and Lesbian Liberation" is the work of a woman in an unusual position. While remaining an ideological extremist (who, for example, still defends the stipulation that half of each state's steering committee members at the 1993 march on Washington had to be people of color), Ms. Vaid manifestly wishes to win over a general audience and re-establish her authority in a movement whose center has moved rapidly toward the nation's political center -- and away from her. To this end, she has moderated her formerly strident tone, cut down on the barn-burning rhetoric about the evils of religion, capitalism and the patriarchy, and embraced goals (like the acceptance of gay people by traditional religious institutions and the military) to which she was once at best indifferent. Instead of demanding "full-scale frontal assault" on dissident homosexuals, she speaks of working together.
At times, indeed, Ms. Vaid sounds strikingly like the Johnny-come-lately liberal, moderate and libertarian activists and writers (this reviewer included) whom she labels "gay conservatives." In stating that gay men and lesbians must "be willing . . . to expose the wildness of our subculture and be prepared to debate it honestly and question it thoroughly," that the gay ghetto can simply be "a more spacious closet" and that the movement should seek to educate straight people instead of ignoring or demonizing them, Ms. Vaid echoes core "gay conservative" concepts.
Yet "Virtual Equality" is, essentially, vintage Urvashi Vaid, proffering a vision of a "common, progressive movement" for social, economic and political revolution led by gay people, who, in her view (though not, it seems to me, in the view of most gay people themselves), embody a radical differentness that makes them the natural vanguard for revolution. And we need such revolution, she maintains, in order to transcend what she calls "virtual equality," the situation -- "at once marginal and mainstream, at once assimilated and irreconcilably queer," and at once accepted and vilified -- in which gay Americans now find themselves.
"Gay conservatives" consider the present state of affairs a transitional stage on the way to full social acceptance, which will be achieved only if the gay movement grows beyond extremism and confrontation and if gay people continue to come out of the closet and to give their straight friends and relatives courses, as it were, in Gay 101 (helping them to understand, among much else, that being gay does not necessarily mean being a revolutionary). According to Ms. Vaid, however, virtual equality has resulted from an excessive focus on "mainstreaming," and can only be overcome through "radical social change." Virtual equality, she says, will give way to real equality only if gay people "develop a movement courageous enough to articulate gay liberation's approaches" to issues like "the deficit, crime, public schools, housing, the role of government, national defense, welfare reform and the shrinkage of the work force." In other words, there are correct gay positions on every issue from Nafta to health care, and gay people need only to listen obediently when their leaders explain what those positions are.
Ms. Vaid, then, who at the National Gay and Lesbian Task Force could not even work out a coherent challenge to propaganda from the religious right (and who, in one of many errors in her book, confuses the Bill of Rights with the later amendments to the Constitution, which she dates at 1776), proposes to formulate both the goals and the strategy of a movement for comprehensive and well-nigh utopian "cultural transformation." She never gets very specific, however, about the nature of that transformation and the means by which she hopes to achieve it; she appears to think that in churning out vacuous sentences full of words like "organization," "mobilization" and "empowerment" she is actually saying something. Yet she is clear about who stands in her way: in straight America, her enemy is the white heterosexual overclass oppressor (she ignores the fact that it is in overclass communities that gay people are safest and most accepted); in gay America, her enemy is middle-class white males who, by insisting on "single-issue politics" -- that is, on a gay rights movement focused on gay rights -- fail to understand that for oppressed gay women, blacks and workers, sex, race and class are also gay issues. Issues, yes -- but gay issues?
 TO be sure, Ms. Vaid is aware enough of the recent sea change in gay politics to recognize that most readers will not cotton to her radical approach. Hence she proposes a compromise -- a coordinated two-tiered movement that would enable single-issue types to focus on gay rights while others pursue a "common movement" agenda. All this activity, she suggests, might be subsumed under an "umbrella organization" like Jesse Jackson's Rainbow Coalition. This is, of course, basically what the gay movement had in the Age of Vaid: many grass-roots people who did important and effective work toward understanding and acceptance, and a few national leaders who sent out a message of opposition to capitalism, religion and bourgeois values. Ms. Vaid is right to assert that "the gay movement needs a fundamental shift." But while more and more gay people realize that the required shift is one toward integration, education and conciliation, Ms. Vaid longs to return to the days of class struggle and liberation fronts. That her rhetoric has come to seem so old so fast is a measure of how gay political discourse -- outside the academy, anyway -- has been profoundly altered in a relatively brief time.
 
 
Green Mansion
 The last candle made of his bending face the ancient mask of a tyrant or a priest before it, too, winked out. Leon paused then as he always did, looking back on a world which only he ever saw. It spread away to every horizon, a tessellated land whose each plant was distinct yet thickly nested among its neighbors. Small water noises tinkled where voices had been muffled. Newt and terrapin nosed their way about invisible tanks while drops condensing inside an acre of cold glass pattered down on soil and leaves. Sometimes he could hear the tightly wound shoots of bamboos and lilies squeak as they thrust upwards. As his eyes adjusted, dabs and dots of green light marked patches of luminous fungi whose spores had arrived with the plants in their Wardian cases (along with mites and pests and noxious insects) and had thrived in the congenial warmth. And, as he waited still longer, a directionless radiance seeped into his eyes. It was so faint as to be without color, yet had an intensity of its own powerful enough to permeate the universe. This was starlight filtering through the thin layer of snow on the roof. From "Griefwork."

HEADLINE: YOUR HOME;
Lights! Camera! Auction!

BYLINE: By JAY ROMANO 

 BODY:
FEW things in life can make your palms sweat, your heart beat faster and your otherwise keen ability to calmly assess the potential impact of an action temporarily vanish. One of those few things is a good old-fashioned auction.
And when the item on the auction block happens to be a piece of the American dream, and the dream looks as though it just could come true, even the most even-tempered bidder may notice that restraint and resolve are "going, going, gone!" 
 "I've seen people get some great deals at real estate auctions," said Arthur Weinstein, a Manhattan lawyer who is a vice president of the Council of New York Cooperatives. "I've also seen people get burned. Auctions are not for the faint hearted. The guiding rule is to be thoroughly prepared."
Indeed, Mr. Weinstein said, anyone hoping to buy property at an auction should know the type of auction he or she is attending and the terms of the sale.
Some auction sales, he said, are inherently risky and should be avoided by amateurs. Auctions resulting from foreclosure, for example, come with few guarantees.
"The party auctioning the property typically makes very few representations concerning the status of the property," he said. "The terms and conditions could be deadly. They often do not even contain a guarantee of occupancy. You could buy an apartment occupied by a tenant and it could take years to get the tenant out."
In fact, if the property being auctioned is rent regulated, it may be impossible to remove the tenant at all. "Even the pros occasionally get stuck," Mr. Weinstein said.
For many people, real estate auctions also conjure up conflicting emotions. Since auctions are often perceived as the final public insult leveled at property owners already at the end of their economic rope, the idea of trafficking in someone else's misfortune may to some people be somewhat distasteful, not to mention risky.
Not all auctions, though, are distress sales. Rob Doyle, principal auctioneer for Absolute Auction & Realty in Beacon, N.Y., said that homeowners frustrated by the time it can take to find a buyer sometimes turn to auctions in the hope of making a faster sale.
Auctions used as an alternative to conventional marketing are usually less risky and can result in true bargains for buyers who are willing to do their homework.
Howard L. Michaels, chairman of the Carlton Group, said that when his company conducts an auction, potential bidders can usually inspect the properties beforehand.
"We have open houses on three consecutive Sundays prior to the sale," Mr. Michaels said. In addition, he said, the company provides fact sheets containing pertinent details on each property being auctioned.
"Still, we encourage everyone to do their own independent investigation," he said.
Mr. Doyle, said the job of an auctioneer is to set the ground rules for the sale, answer questions and eliminate as many contingencies as possible before the actual auction. "You want to get all the bidders on a level playing field before the sale," he said.
Accordingly, he said, all potential bidders should be satisfied that they know exactly what they are bidding on and exactly what their responsibilities will be if they are successful. Generally, Mr. Doyle said, properties sold at a public auction are almost always sold "as is," with no mortgage contingency. Properties sold with owner-financing, he said, while less typical, often results in higher bids.
Auction property, he said, is sold "absolute" or "with reserve." Some auctioneers also have absolute auctions with minimum starting bids. "Absolute is a very powerful word," he said, explaining that in such a sale, the property will go to the highest bidder no matter how low that bid may be. A sale "with reserve," on the other hand, means that the seller retains the right to reject the highest bid if he or she chooses. If that happens, there is no sale.
Successful bidders at a real estate auction, Mr. Doyle said, must leave a deposit of 10 percent of the sale price on the day of the sale -- certified check is usually the required method of payment -- and must pay the balance within 30 days.
On Nov. 16 and 19, the Carlton Group Ltd., a real estate auction company based in Manhattan, will be auctioning 190 commercial and residential properties scattered throughout New York, New Jersey and Pennsylvania.
Included in the two-day extravaganza are 138 co-op apartments, 10 condominiums, an 11-unit hotel/motel, 12.5 acres of vacant land and an 85-slip marina. Opening bids start as low as $5,000. Previous sales held by the company have drawn as many as 1,000 bidders.
And on Wednesday, Sheldon Good & Company Auctions, a firm based in Chicago, will be holding a decidedly more intimate gathering. On the auction block that day will be a condominium apartment on the 46th floor of the Bristol Plaza at 200 East 65th Street -- a 2,700-square-foot, three-bedroom apartment with 10-foot ceilings and a 270-degree view. Bidding will start at $1.6 million.
James Samson, a Manhattan lawyer who specializes in real estate, said that potential bidders for co-ops should be aware that the co-op board has a right to approve or reject a sale, even a sale made at auction. That presents less of a problem, he said, when it is the co-op itself that is selling the unit. "Unless there's something really wrong with the buyer, they're probably going to let the sale go through," he said.
In fact, Mr. Samson said, potential buyers who are willing to do their homework might find that co-ops being sold at auction can often be bought at bargain prices.
"If the reason an apartment is being auctioned is because people are scared of the building it could be that the building's problems are behind it," he said.
William Mannion, an auctioneer with the JP&R advertising agency in Manhattan, said that in many cases, potential bidders can find out much of what they need to know about a co-op apartment being auctioned by going to the building and asking questions. They should also obtain financial details, such as when the mortgage expires and whether the income from monthly maintenance is sufficient to keep the building in the black.
"The answers you get could make a deal that looked like a good one look bad," Mr. Mannion said. "But they can also make what looked like a bad deal look good."
In fact, most experts agree that the essential ingredients for making a smart buy at an auction are perseverance, knowledge and restraint.
"We went to auctions every weekend for a couple of years before we were successful," said Dean Bertolino, who, with his wife, Laura, recently purchased at auction a four-bedroom, two-and-a-half-bath colonial house in New City, N.Y.
Mr. Bertolino said that the house cost "a small fraction" of the about $300,000 that properties in the area are selling for.
"Anyone can do what we did," he said. "It means sacrificing a lot of weekends, going to see property after property, making notes, narrowing down the field and then being ready to walk away disappointed."

HEADLINE: After a Retreat, Gentrification Is Marching To the Fringes

BYLINE: By NICK RAVO 

 BODY:
FLORENCE NEAL fears her neighborhood is about to become an acronym.
"Please don't call us RedHo," she said, wincing at the thought of Red Hook becoming like SoHo (South of Houston Street) or TriBeCa (the Triangle Below Canal Street). 
 Ms. Neal, a sculptor, painter and graphic designer, was one of the first of a wave of artists who have moved in recent years to this bleak waterfront community of old warehouses, public housing projects and dilapidated town houses on the rump of southwestern Brooklyn.
She bought her four-story house at 353 Van Brunt Street in 1987 for $2,500 thanks to a city program that encourages artists to move into and, hopefully, improve low-income areas. She also estimates that she spent about $170,000 on labor and materials for the building, not to mention countless hours of sweat equity.
Today, Ms. Neal, 40, lives on the third and fourth floors of the building; she also has, from her roof, a million-dollar view of Lower Manhattan and the Statue of Liberty. She rents her second floor to other artists, charging about $800 a month for 1,100 square feet. She uses the first floor as a gallery, the Kentler International Drawing Space, and a studio.
"I can see the little espresso bars and cafes here someday," she said.
The process is gentrification. Young college-educated adults, often artists with the necessary construction skills, are priced out of the neighborhoods that had accommodated them in the past. So they buy or rent, and then renovate, cheap run-down housing, usually brownstones or lofts in unused industrial buildings.
Then come the bourgeoisie. Then the patisseries.
The gentrification phenomenon died, for the most part, in the late 80's in New York, largely because what few buyers there were for residential real estate narrowed their purchases to prime neighborhoods. For a while, what appeared to be a sort of degentrification even occurred in some areas, such as Manhattan Valley, above the Upper West Side on Central Park.
With the stabilization of the local economy in the last year or two, though, the buds of gentrification -- sometimes fertilized by less restrictive city zoning -- are again sprouting, to the chagrin of some, to be sure, on the city's rough-edged real estate frontiers, like the Bowery and Hell's Kitchen.
"There are islands in the city that had not gentrified that are now and others that are about to gentrify again," said Peter Marcuse, a professor of urban planning at Columbia University.
Red Hook, to be sure, is not about to become the next TriBeCa or even the next Williamsburg, a Brooklyn community where many artists, priced out of downtown Manhattan, settled in old warehouses in the 1980's
After all, the commercial activity along the Red Hook waterfront, from glass-blowing to breweries to apple-processing, is thriving and these warehouses are mostly one-story, low-density structures, as opposed to the multiareas like TriBeCa and Williamsburg.
"We get calls all the time for space," said Greg O'Connell, a developer and commercial landlord in Red Hook. "But the artists that are here are in one-family and two-family housing. And you don't even have that much of that. A lot of the housing here is in the low-income housing projects."
EVEN so, about 40 artists have taken root in recent years in Red Hook, where starting last summer there are now evening concerts on a barge at Pier 45.
"There is a bus that goes through Park Slope and Carroll Gardens and Brooklyn Heights and brings them down here," said Mr. O'Connell, who was a detective in SoHo during the 60's and saw the seeds of that neighborhood.
"It will be hard for many more artists to move here because there just isn't the kind of space they are looking for, and what is here isn't that cheap anymore," he said. "But you never know."
Red Hook -- a neighborhood so tough that local lore has it, apocryphally, perhaps, that it was the place where Al Capone got his scar -- isn't the sole frontier of gentrification these days. There is also Dumbo (Down Under the Manhattan Bridge Overpass), an area roughly bordered by York Street, the Brooklyn Navy Yard and Cadman Plaza East, where several hundred artists live in long-abandoned factories.
More established but still embryonic areas of gentrification in Brooklyn include Williamsburg -- still -- and, to a lesser degree, perhaps, Greenpoint, where some artists have moved in the last decade. Across the Hudson River, there is Jersey City.
"Artists have been in Williamsburg for a while, but now they actually have a restaurant over there and everything," said Ms. Neal, who lived in Dumbo before moving to Red Hook.
In Manhattan, there is the Bowery, a street long synonymous with skid row. For decades, artists have lived there as well. In the last year or two, though, the street has become almost chic. Witness the Bowery Bar, a trendy cabaret that opened in a former gas station a few blocks north of Houston Street last year, in the neighborhood sometimes referred to as NoHo.
Further south on the Bowery, a few storefronts in on Prince Street, in a tiny geographical interregnum between Little Italy, the Lower East Side and Chinatown -- an area sardonically referred to by some as Esso (East of SoHo) -- lies the Connecticut Muffin Shop, which also has branch in New Canaan, Conn.
"At least once or twice a day, we get people stopping by here asking if there are any living spaces for rent," said Gary Crissey, the owner of the bakery, which opened seven years ago. "It started up about a year or two ago, and we keep getting more and more people who are curious about living here."
"The Bowery is amazing when you find out about the people who live above the stores, the artists, the dancers," he said. "The Bowery has always had some of these people living down here, but its been really growing lately."
Another Manhattan neighborhood making the transition from derelict to desirable is Hell's Kitchen, sometimes referred to as Clinton. Gentrification has been occurring in the neighborhood for years, but the pace seems to be picking up in recent years, local residents and real estate brokers say.
Alexandr Neratoff, an architect, recently bought a loft for his mother, Rimma, an artist, in the Armory, at 529 West 42d Street, between 10th and 11th Avenues, in Hell's Kitchen. The 1,200-square-foot space, which has a view of the Hudson River, cost $70,000 with a monthly maintenance of $1,400, about two-thirds of which is tax-deductible.
"I moved to SoHo in 1976 and sort of identified it back then as a place where things would happen," Mr. Neratoff said. "A few years ago, I thought the West Side was going to happen, so buying there seems to make sense."
He noted that a lot of the residential development activity in the Hell's Kitchen area began in the 80's -- projects like Riverbank West, at 555 West 42d Street, and the Strand, at 500 West 43d Street. "Things kind of stalled out for a while, but now it seems to be coming around again," he said.
"For a century, New York has been turning away from the waterfront," Mr. Neratoff added. "And now, New York, and a lot of cities, are returning."
NOT all the gentrification in New York is inspired by migrating artists. One of the most significant neighborhood resurrections in recent years has taken place in the Chelsea section of Manhattan and was largely spurred by the northward emigration of gays and lesbians from the crowded and expensive West Village. Indeed, Chelsea and the neighboring Flatiron District may have been the only areas of the city that substantially gentrified during the economic downturn from 1989 to 1993.
"As the real estate market tightens up, people are increasingly looking at neighborhoods that have been previously overlooked," said Joseph B. Rose, chairman of the New York City Planning Commission.
He added that the city is also loosening its zoning restrictions in various commercial neighborhoods to help spark future gentrification. For example, the city recently changed the zoning on the Avenue of the Americas in Manhattan north of 23d Street to allow more residential devlopment.
"What you are seeing is a willingness of the real estate market to look at places that in the past have looked unappealing," Mr. Rose said.
Similar changes were recently made in TriBeCa. "The zoning was designed to eliminate high-rise construction and have development complement the existing physical scale and historic fabric in the area," said John Young, a senior city planner.
In the East Village, which has now expanded into Avenues A and B, in what was once the Lower East Side or Alphabet City, young Japanese immigrants, as well as the seemingly inexorable spread of the Village eastward, have been the catalysts for a lesser degree of gentrification.
"I had never been to Avenue A until a year ago, and now you have tour buses, and people think it is fun to look at the skinheads and kids with tattoos and body piercing," said Mary Hiatt, president of the Business of the East Village Association. "In '89, they didn't come near Avenue A. Now, they come and take pictures."
Proximity to a popular neighboring community is often a key factor in gentrification. Manhattan Valley, for example, became a spill-over for those priced out of the Upper West Side. And though the strip of apartments along Central Park above 100th Street to 110th Street and the blocks west to Broadway seemed to revert back to their crime and drug-infested past during the early 90's, regentrification now seems possible.
"Eventually, practically all of Manhattan, south of the northern edge of Central Park, will be gentrified, all the way down to the Battery, and that includes the Lower East Side," said Peter D. Salins, a professor of planning at Hunter College and a senior fellow at the Manhattan Institute.
"Of course eventually might mean the year 2025," he added.
Mr. Salins, who is known as the father of modern Hoboken, N.J., for his work with officials there to help gentrify that city in the late 1970's and early 1980's, said that gentrification usually never stops once it starts.
"It still isn't done in Park Slope, in Cobble Hill, in Carroll Gardens," he said of three Brooklyn neighborhoods that underwent widespread gentrification in the 1980's.
As for the mixed results in Hoboken, Mr. Salins said that town has remained a second-tier site of gentrification. "It didn't attract as many of the classic yuppie, two-income couples as we thought it would," he said. "Instead, there are more singles and people who earn less money, and that's one of the reasons why you have so many bars and pubs over here."
Moreover, he said that most gentrified areas tend to have been born from spillovers from other gentrified neighborhoods, and Hoboken, on the other side of the Hudson River, is isolated in that respect.
Mr. Salins also said that the major change between gentrification in most areas today and 20 years ago is that it tends to occur more in industrial areas and often involves non-artists. "It doesn't have to be a brownstone," he said. "Location is more important than building type."
As for the city's plan to restrict adult-oriented businesses to areas like the Hell's Kitchen and Brooklyn waterfronts, neighborhoods that are just beginning to gentrify, Mr. Rose said that these parts of New York are actually more protected from such businesses under the new ordinance than they have been.
"Even in areas where these businesses will be allowed, there will be serious restrictions," he said.
Not everyone, of course, is pleased with gentrification.
Renters in neighborhoods that are improved often suddenly find themselves priced out of communities where they have been raised. Even those who can afford to stay may feel culturally estranged from the newcomers, who are usually better educated and enjoy different types of leisure activities.
IN some neighborhoods, there is even organized resistance to gentrification. In the East Village, for instance, there is a local organization called RAGE-ON! -- Revolt Against Gentrification Erasing Our Neighborhood.
And Mr. Marcuse noted that the process of deterioration is continuing in many other neighborhoods in New York even as gentrification spreads in others.
"It adds to the polarization of the city," he said.
For the most part, though, the changes are welcome evidence of a recovery, albeit modest, in the city's residential real estate market.
"Gentrification absolutely lives," said Barbara Krongel, a broker with Harborview Realty in Brooklyn. "It slowed down after the stock market crash, but it is accelerating again now. We're even getting people taking fixer-uppers again. And if you saw Carroll Gardens now compared to 10 years ago, you wouldn't recognize it."
She noted that rents in many of these gentrified areas in Brooklyn have come back to their 1987 highs.
A two-bedroom floor-through apartment in Cobble Hill that rented for $1,400 to $1,600 a month in 1986 and fell to $900 or $1,000 a month in 1991 now rents for $1,400 to $1,600 again, Ms. Krongel said.
Sale prices have also rebounded. A two- to four-family four-story house in Cobble Hill that would probably have sold for $550,000 in 1987, but only $350,000 in 1991, might now be back up to $550,000, Ms. Krongel said.
"Sometimes, these things happen very slowly," she added. "You almost don't notice these changes until they are done."

HEADLINE: ON THE TOWNS

 BODY:

SECTION: Section 13NJ; Page 17; Column 1; New Jersey Weekly Desk 

LENGTH: 769 words

HEADLINE: SOAPBOX;
Savage Inequalities

 BODY:
Marilyn J. Morheuser, the lawyer at the center of a 13-year court struggle to make New Jersey's school-financing system more equitable for its poorest districts, died last Sunday at the age of 71 after a long bout with cancer. A tough-spoken former nun, she became a single-minded crusader for schoolchildren in what the State Supreme Court, in its first Abbott v. Burke decision in 1990, called the "special-needs districts."
What follows is an excerpt from her testimony before the Legislature in June 1992 on a proposed amendment to the State Constitution that would have wiped out the court's decision by nullifying the requirement that New Jersey provide a "thorough and efficient" education to every child. The lawmakers never put the amendment on the ballot. 
 In recent months, a message that exploded on all of us from Los Angeles has taken hold. National polls show a heightened public recognition that we can no longer afford to neglect the poor -- especially poor youth -- whom we have abandoned in the nation's cities.
New Jersey is now in a unique position to serve as a beacon for the nation. With the Supreme Court decision in Abbott II, this state stands on the brink of showing the nation how to bring hope back to the cities, how to provide little children with a way out of the cycle of poverty, how to provide trained employees who will help fuel the state's economy.
Rather than embarking on an enterprise aimed at nullifying it, we should be working together to devise a means to implement that decision and, at the same time, to assure excellent education to all New Jersey children.
As a prelude to the 21st century, nullification of the Abbott decree is not only counterintuitive, it is a rejection of the politics of inclusion, and it is contrary to basic self-interest. In New Jersey by the year 2000, 40.2 percent of all children and youth under age 18 will be what we now call minorities. That population is predominantly African-American and Hispanic. Two-thirds of those children comprise the resident enrollment of the special needs districts.
Today I ask you to consider the impact of the proposed amendment on 21 of the 30 special-needs districts, including the state's largest urban school districts. For nine districts, funding would increase by less than 5 percent, with 2 1/2 percent for New Brunswick and a little over 3 percent for Jersey City. But in 12 districts, including Newark, Paterson, Trenton, Long Branch, Asbury Park and Neptune, funding would decrease. Paterson, a state-operated district, would have $5.7 million less in state and local funding than it will have next year. Newark would lose $31.6 million; Trenton, nearly $5 million.
This kind of reduced spending level will decimate long-needed program improvements which are just getting started. Teachers by the hundreds, who have waited so long to see those improvements, will lose their jobs. Those teachers who aren't let go will have increases in the size of their classes, which are already overlarge. And what of the children?
What you should know about the Abbott decision is that it was not based on liberal, bleeding-heart appeals to morality. It was based on the record bullt during a nine-month trial, the largest record ever compiled in a school finance challenge. The proofs established who the poor children of New Jersey are, the educational needs they bring to school with them and the total incapacity of districts to meet those needs. Educators from both urban and suburban districts showed through testimony and documents what schools must offer to prepare children to compete in the marketplace and to fully function as citizens and what poor districts cannot offer. It was this record which convinced the court.
All of this, finally, is not to say that your only concern in financing public school education must be in meeting the Abbott mandates for poor districts. When I analyze the impact of proposed legislation or constitutional amendments, that must be my concern, for the children in the special needs districts are Education Law Center's clients. However, I too recognize that the Quality Education Act has visited hardships on other than the special-needs districts.
The amendment before you, however, is hardly the way to correct a bad statute. Indeed, amending the Constitution may visit on you more costly litigation. I will leave it to others to spell out for you the legal challenge you may be inviting. After all, I'm only a simple country lawyer. So I will leave you with some simple country wisdom: Look before you leap into that cow pasture.

SECTION: Section 3; Page 3; Column 1; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 1609 words

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT;
State Securities Chiefs Are Under the Gun

BYLINE: By KURT EICHENWALD 

 BODY:
THESE are tough times for state securities regulators. Many are facing tight budgets from legislatures. And in Washington, some lawmakers are supporting a bill proposed by Jack Fields, Republican of Texas, that would strip state regulators of some powers.
These facts should concern business executives and investors, according to Dee Harris, Arizona's chief securities regulator and the newly elected president of the regulators' umbrella group, the North American Securities Administrators Association. In an interview, Mr. Harris spoke about the importance of states in securities regulation and warned that cutbacks would leave many defrauded investors with few avenues of redress. KURT EICHENWALD
 
Q. What is it that the states do that's different from Federal regulators?
 
A. Let's take enforcement. In general, the Securities and Exchange Commission, because of its limited staff, tends to focus on large cases. They want at least $1 million in losses, usually. The states don't have any choice but to look at almost everything that comes in. We get complaints all the time by people just walking in and saying, "I think I've been ripped off."
If an elderly woman walks in and says, "I've just been cleaned out of my life's savings," we can't say, "Sorry, it was less than $1 million, we're not interested." The S.E.C. does have the luxury of saying that because they're not the only game in town. They can say, go to your friendly state regulator, and they know that's good advice because they know we will, by and large, take an interest.
Moreover, a higher proportion of our cases probably are criminal cases. The S.E.C. generally is an agency which, in accordance with the statutes, conducts civil cases and administrative cases. Occasionally, they will refer a matter to the United States Attorney, but, by and large, it is a civil enforcement agency.
The states also do administrative cases and civil cases, but we're much more likely to find that the things were so egregious that they merit criminal prosecution. So I would guess that a greater proportion are criminal cases, and in the aggregate, we bring about 10 times as many enforcement actions every year as the S.E.C.
Now that's also because we have more resources and because we are in a lot of places the S.E.C. seldom gets to. Most states do not have an office of the S.E.C. or an office of the National Association of Securities Dealers. So the complaints tend to come to us first.
 
Q. What about the state laws for registering securities? What significance do they have?
 
A. I think the state securities laws play a very important role. They are part of the fabric of securities regulation in the United States.
Our statutes tend to be somewhat stronger in the area of registration of securities offerings. Most large offerings, most national offerings, are exempt in almost every state. So we're tending to look at smaller offerings right on down to very tiny offerings of a few hundred thousand dollars. Many of those offerings are for emerging companies, which tend not to have much of a financial history, may not have yet achieved profitability and they require close scrutiny.
And our statutes tend to be stronger with respect to the discretion of the securities administrator than, for instance, the Securities Act of 1933 on the Federal level. We can say this appears to be a very severe conflict of interest involving an offer or that could harm investors.
 
 Q. So it's not just the disclosure standard they have on the Federal level. You ask issuers to justify what they do.
 
 A. Yes. Many states simply have a disclosure standard, but a good number of states also have a standard that allows them to ask issuers to justify their decisions. And we don't approach that process with hostility toward these companies unless, of course, they're being put together by people that we know are convicted securities swindlers.
 
 Q. What about the rules on licensing brokers and investment advisers? How do they differ from the Federal standards?
 
 A. In a given jurisdiction, there will be people who are not permitted to become licensed as registered representatives, or investment advisers who are in fact registered Federally or are members of the National Association of Securities Dealers.
It's not a criticism of those agencies, it's just that we have statutes which permit us to say that we believe that someone has a bad disciplinary history and should not be permitted to become licensed in our state. Day in and day out we make the decision that people who are active in the industry someplace should not be active in our states.
Most state statutes require investment advisers to meet some standards, require the passage of an examination. We have a state exam, called the Series 65, which is about ethical and legal requirements for investment advisers. So we require those examinations, we require financial statements to see if this person is solvent.
 
Q. There's a lot of talk in Washington about changing the way that this works. The bill from Congressman Fields has been particularly prominent. What is your organization's position on the Fields bill?
 
A. We believe that change, reasonable change, rational change is healthy. We're certainly willing and desirous of exploring the whole issue of what should be the proper role of the Federal Government in regulating securities activities and what should be the proper role of the states. We have appointed a task force to do just this kind of analysis.
 
 Q. Staying on the Fields bill again, what is it that the bill would do from the viewpoint of the states?
 
 A. It eliminates much of the state fee revenue. We get fees for registering investment advisers and we get fees for registering investment companies. All that revenue would be gone, and while that's more of an issue for governors and state legislatures, I think we can see that the appropriations to the states securities divisions are not likely to remain at their present levels if the legislatures figure out that they're no longer getting the fee revenue they were getting.
That means that the other things we do, the enforcement work, the inspections that we perform on investment advisers and broker dealers, the analysis of prospectuses and disclosure documents, all of that work would have a less likely funding source. So that's the first thing it does is to threaten the financial underpinnings of state securities regulation.
Secondly, it would prohibit state legislatures from setting standards for people in the industry. Remember I said before that we may decide that someone who is registered as a broker-dealer or a registered representative or investment adviser someplace in the country, should not be licensed in our jurisdiction because they may simply have such a long disciplinary history. Well, it would prohibit us from making those decisions. It would say basically that if you can manage to become licensed with the N.A.S.D. or the S.E.C., then the states have to take you.
Another troubling area is that it would require states, instead of enforcing state securities laws now in administrative forums, to go into Federal court to enforce Federal law. One of the problems is that we believe state legislatures are not likely for very long to keep in place state securities enforcement units for the purpose of enforcing Federal law. I think they're going to see that as an unfunded Federal mandate.
If you rip away that state enforcement capability, you really must think about replacing it with a much larger S.E.C., and I don't think that's about to happen. In fact, the S.E.C.'s budget looks like it will be cut. So we believe that the Fields bill needs considerable refinement to be workable.
 
 Q. If it does become law, what will that mean for the states and S.E.C., and second, for the investors?
 
 A. Let me take the investors first. I think what it means for investors is that they will have substantially less protection than they currently have, and I think that's bad for the country.
The United States enjoys the strongest capital markets in the world, and I think those markets are part of the reason we have such a strong economy. Those markets are based on investors knowing that there is a cop on the beat. Well, we constitute some of the cops and, in fact, we constitute a big proportion of the total number of cops. If we're gone, I think investors will find it's a lot harder to get someone to listen to their complaints unless they have very, very large losses.
I think there will be a lot less proactive work done, that is, there will be less effort to stop problems before they happen because there will be less resources. I think investors will find that they have to write to Washington or to some city in another state, to the S.E.C., and it may be a long time, if ever, before they get an answer. Even if they get an answer, it won't necessarily lead to any action. So I think both investors and the markets have a great deal at stake here.
In terms of the states, I guess what it means is that we'll do our best. If this legislation is passed and it says that we all have to learn all the nuances of Federal law and become familiar with the Federal court system, we'll do our best to do that. I hope the legislatures will continue to fund our activities. I seriously doubt it, so I think it means that maybe over a period of three to five years, state securities regulation will simply wither away. And virtually the only people who benefit from that are those who wish to commit securities fraud.
