HEADLINE: MUTUAL FUNDS;
Quebec Vote Creates Market Uncertainty

BYLINE: By EDWARD WYATT 

 BODY:
AMERICAN investors probably do not need any more reasons to stay out of Canada. The country's economy is shaky, unemployment remains stubbornly high and both consumers and the Government are carrying too much debt.
"Canada is undergoing the usual social tensions that occur when you try to cut the deficit while a recession is going on," said Don Cassidy, senior research analyst at Lipper Analytical Services, Summit, N.J. 
 Now, add the latest uncertainty: whether residents of Quebec, the largest province, want to remain part of Canada. In a referendum about sovereignty that remains too close to call, five million voters of the predominantly French province will go to the polls tomorrow. The outcome could affect the economy and financial markets of the entire country, if not the continent.
To what degree the impact of a separatist victory would spread beyond the provincial economy is unclear, said George D. Vasic, a strategist and economist at Bunting Warburg, a Toronto investment bank. Foreign investors hold about one-quarter of Canada's $550 billion debt, and Quebec contributes a like amount of Canada's gross domestic product. At issue is whether foreign investors will hold on to Canadian paper, and whether Quebec would assume its share of the nation's overall borrowings. Either variable could greatly influence the country's ability to continue to grow.
A final outcome will not be known even after Monday's votes are counted. The referendum simply calls for provincial politicians to negotiate a "new economic and political partnership" with Canada. If a satisfactory partnership is not achieved -- which might require another vote -- the province could move to separate from Canada, perhaps requiring yet another election.
Adding to the confusion, last week two groups that have native land claims in northern Quebec voted overwhelmingly to remain part of Canada. What makes the sentiment voiced by the Cree Indian and Inuit nations unique is that while they account for a mere 1 percent of the population, they lay claim to roughly half of the province's land area -- some 300,000 square miles of territory. Their claims are certain to be contested if Quebec tries to secede.
Investors dislike uncertainty, and voiced their feelings last week by punishing Canadian stocks. The Toronto Stock Exchange index of 300 companies, the most watched stock index in Canada, fell 103.03 points, or 2.3 percent, to 4335.30.
That leaves the benchmark 7.9 percent below its peak for the year, in July. After tracking the performance of the United States stock market for much of the year, Canada's equity markets took a downturn in midsummer, while American stocks surged to new highs through September and October.
"That might have been due to building concern about the Quebec situation," said Michael Smedley, chief executive of Maxwell Meighen & Associates, and co-manager of three Canadian closed-end funds, the largest of which is the $200 million Canadian General Investments fund. "But we've had a tremendous run in the natural resource sector."
The last couple of months, he said, represent "primarily a period of pause and correction" rather than a wholesale turn in the fortune of a market that has performed admirably this year and over the last three years. Two years ago, the Canadian market began far outperforming its neighbor to the south, and only this year did the American equity market catch up.
Much of last week's selling, Mr. Smedley said, came from international accounts rather than Canadian individual or institutional investors, suggesting simply "a short-term condition." Allowing that a "yes" vote in Monday's election would have negative consequences, particularly for international debt markets, he said that "it would also open up a tremendous investment opportunity."
In the end, though, Quebec will probably not separate from Canada, Mr. Smedley said. "The French Canadian who might have a separatist sentiment will have to ask himself if he really wants lower market values, lower job prospects, or if he's not concerned with the question of what passport and currency he will will use," he said. The block of 5 percent to 13 percent "undecided" voters in recent polls, which show the electorate rather evenly divided otherwise, is largely made up of "the fringe 'yes' man who will actually vote 'no,' " he said.
And Mr. Smedley still finds plenty of Canadian stocks that he says will prosper even if Quebec eventually separates. Among them is Bombardier Inc., a Montreal company that makes heavy equipment, including jet aircraft, motor bikes, snowmobiles and some of the subway cars used by New York City.
Canadian General Investments, which trades on the Toronto and London exchanges, but not in the United States, produced a total return in the first nine months of 1995 of 8.6 percent in American dollars, according to Lipper Analytical.
The largest open-end fund sold to Americans who want to invest in Canada, Fidelity Investments' Canada fund, with assets of $357.4 million at the end of September, underperformed the Canadian market in 1993 and 1994. But the fund has closely tracked the Toronto index this year, producing a total return, in American dollars, of 14.8 percent in the first nine months. Investors' gain have been trimmed this month, however, for a year-to-date gain of 5.6 percent.
A smaller option is the Ivy Canada fund, distributed through Mackenzie Funds of Boca Raton, Fla. With just $20 million in assets, Ivy Canada is too small to be included in this newspaper's mutual fund listings. The fund, which carries a 5.75 percent load and annual expenses of more than 2 percent, is down 7.4 percent so far in 1995.
In short, American investors who want to invest in Canada through mutual funds have limited options. Given the recent performance of the market there, and the uncertainty that lies ahead, that may not be all bad.

HEADLINE: DIARY

BYLINE: By JOSHUA MILLS 

 BODY:

THE BUDGET
Balancing Act at Center Stage
Ultimately, the Federal budget will be written in negotiations between the White House and Republican leaders in Congress. But leading to that denouement is a dramatic script. Act I: the House and Senate pass bills to balance the budget in seven years, largely by cutting taxes, sharply limiting spending on Medicare and turning much of welfare over to the states. Act II: President Clinton vetoes whatever the House-Senate conference comes up with, pronouncing it too extreme. Act III: Congress lacks the votes to overturn the veto, and talks begin.
The rhetoric flowed freely during the debates. The Republicans said the bill would "save the country and save the next generation." The Democrats said the plan was "a travesty on the American people" that was morally wrong and economically wrong. Stay tuned.
 
REGULATION
The Secret Sharers
Punctuating the S.E.C.'s desire to clean up municipal finance, Merrill Lynch and Lazard Freres agreed to pay more than $20 million to settle charges that they had agreed to split secret fees that the former paid for business provided by the latter. Mark S. Ferber, a former Lazard partner, was indicted on 63 charges, including wire fraud and attempted extortion, and Lazard said it was saddened by his "apparent violation of the firm's ethical standards."
 
NEWSLETTERS
Gazarelli Is Recruited
 Building its stable of newsletters, Phillips Publishing has recruited Elaine Gazarelli, the former Lehman Brothers strategist, to write a newsletter. Famous for calling the 1987 market crash, Ms. Gazarelli left Lehman a year ago and started her own firm in Boca Raton, Fla. Phillips recently bought The Independent Adviser for Vanguard Investors and The T. Rowe Price Adviser, and publishes nine other investment newsletters, including Ken and Daria Dolan's Straight Talk on Your Money.

LABOR
No More Road Kill
After telling union members that labor had become a patsy repeatedly victimized by management, John J. Sweeney won election as president of the A.F.L.-C.I.O., and promised to use aggressive tactics to make sure workers would no longer be treated like "so much road kill."
 
ON LEAVE
'I Have Nothing to Hide'
Dan Dorfman took a leave of absence from Money magazine after Business Week reported that Federal prosecutors were looking into his relationship with a stock promoter. Frank Lalli, Money's managing editor, said he believed Mr. Dorfman had not violated any laws; he also said he had asked the general counsel of Time Warner, which owns Money, to investigate. Mr. Dorfman, who has been writing a column for Money since January, said on his daily commentary on the CNBC cable network: "I have nothing to hide. I have done nothing wrong and I have nothing to be ashamed of."
 
CONGLOMERATES
Shamu Yes, LaRussa No
Yet another company has concluded that diversification, which seemed so right in the 80's, is wrong. Anheuser-Busch said it would sell the St. Louis Cardinals baseball team, which plays in Busch Stadium, as well as Eagle snack foods, in order to concentrate on brewing and its Sea World and Busch Gardens theme parks.
 
TAXES
Whew!
The I.R.S.'s plan to begin a round of extensive audits, including the returns of more than 92,000 individual taxpayers, appears to be dead in the face of public resentment and Republican opposition. The agency said the audits were needed to develop statistical profiles of likely cheaters but could not be done because its tax enforcement budget had been reduced.
 
INNKEEPING
Understaying Your Welcome
The Hyatt and Westin hotels said they would start charging a $50 fee at most of their hotels for guests who check out a day or more early. Hilton, Radisson and ITT Sheraton said guests would continue to pay only for the days and nights they stay.

LENGTH: 1815 words

HEADLINE: How to Succeed? Go to Wellesley

BYLINE: By JUDITH H. DOBRZYNSKI 

 BODY:
DETROIT and cars, Hollywood and films, Silicon Valley and chips. It's hard to think of one without the other. Here's a new link in the business landscape. Henceforth, when you hear Wellesley, Mass., the home of Wellesley College, think of top-level executive women.
More than any other college -- large or small -- Wellesley has groomed women who shatter the glass ceiling. Wellesley women take up far more seats in executive suites and corporate boardrooms than their numbers suggest they should have. They are overrepresented, too, at places like the Harvard business school, a fountainhead for occupants of the nation's corner offices. 
 To cite just a few of Wellesley's major achievers, there's Lois D. Juliber, '71, president of Colgate North America; Ellen R. Marram, '68, president of the Seagram Beverage Group; Shirley Young, '55, a vice president of General Motors, and Marion O. Sandler, '52, co-chief executive of Golden West Financial, a large savings and loan association.
Influencing business policy, there is Alicia Munnell, '64, a top Treasury Department official recently nominated to the Council of Economic Advisers. And of course there's Hillary Rodham Clinton, '69, the once and future corporate lawyer and director.
Just how does Wellesley, a school of only 2,200 students, do it?
This good fortune certainly wasn't planned, college officials said. Wellesley's outstanding record stems from -- in addition to bright students -- a strong, popular economics department; a dynamic professor, Carolyn Shaw Bell, who taught at the college from 1950 to 1989; and programs that encourage networking among alumnae and between alumnae and students, people said. Wellesley, in fact, could be an inspiration to other colleges that want to help their graduates go where few women have gone before, into the business world's upper echelons.
Evidence for the Wellesley edge abounds, starting with the number of companies that look to Wellesley for executive women. In 1994, 75 companies recruited at its leafy campus west of Boston, far exceeding the number at similar-sized elite colleges like Smith and Amherst.
Some executive recruiters say they sense that Wellesley women know how to really succeed in business. "I have always felt this was true, and I've always said I want my two daughters to go to Wellesley," said Julie Daum of Spencer Stuart, a big search firm.
Several studies lend credence to a "Wellesley factor." When Business Week magazine named the "50 Top Women in Business" in 1992, four went to the school. Only one other college claimed more than one spot on the roster -- the University of Wisconsin, which matched Wellesley's four but which also has six and a half times as many women students.
Of the 390 women who serve as directors of the Fortune 500 companies, at least 17 went to Wellesley -- more than any other college -- according to Catalyst, a women's advocacy group (headed, it happens, by a Wellesley graduate, Sheila Wellington).
And Carol A. Scott found evidence in her 1992 survey of America's senior executive women. Asked by The New York Times to look at the colleges attended by those women, she found 8 Wellesley graduates among the 439 respondents. That is 64 times Wellesley's share of the female college population and more than any other college except the University of California at Berkeley (with 9, but also five times as many women students).
"I thought you were out of your mind when you called, but I see that there's something to it," said Ms. Scott, a professor at the University of California at Los Angeles's Graduate School of Management.
And don't forget Wellesley's position as a dominant supplier of women to the Harvard business school. In 1975, for example, Wellesley sent more women to the Harvard business school than any other college. Twenty years later, Wellesley graduates still made up the biggest contingent from any women's college, though by then Harvard and Dartmouth were sending a few more proportionately.
Success, of course, breeds success. "There's a self-reinforcing mechanism at work in terms of student selection and faculty recruitment," explained Victor Fuchs, an economist at Stanford University who wrote "Women's Quest for Economic Equality."
Indeed, many Wellesley women are now feeding into the pipeline that leads up to the executive office. All told, about a third of Wellesley's graduates go into the business world, more than any other career.
As an all-female school, Wellesley starts with an edge. Numerous studies show that graduates of women's colleges, compared with women graduates of coeducational institutions, develop higher levels of self-esteem and do better in their careers.
But when it comes to business, Wellesley still outdoes its rivals -- a thought that raises hackles among some of them. "When you look around at the Seven Sisters, I think you'll find similar results," said Barbara Reinhold, director of the career development office at Smith.
True, of those elite traditionally women's colleges, Vassar can claim Geraldine Laybourne, president of the Nickelodeon network, while Smith can boast of Rochelle B. Lazarus, president of Ogilvy & Mather North America, and Laura D'Andrea Tyson, director of the White House's National Economic Council. Columbia's Barnard College has Phyllis Grann, chief executive of the Putnam Publishing Group. And Radcliffe College at Harvard has Joan T. Bok, chairman of the New England Electric System, and Lisa Henson, president of Columbia Pictures.
YET none of these colleges -- nor any other college or university -- has produced proportionately as many corporate women in high positions as Wellesley. Neither Smith nor Barnard nor Radcliffe produced a list that matched Wellesley's.
Vassar conceded the point. "Recruiters do say that Wellesley students are very business-oriented," said Clare D. Graham, director of career development. "Our alums have tended to have a focus in communications and the arts."
Wellesley's president, Diana Walsh, cannot explain precisely how Wellesley came to produce graduates who ascend to jobs like senior vice president of the Federal Reserve Bank of New York (Margaret Yeo, '64) and executive vice president of Bloomingdale's (Christine Miller, '66).
Ms. Walsh cited the school's past as the key ingredient. "Wellesley has a history of women's rights, a culture of women struggling to succeed in a man's world," she said.
Many graduates agreed that Wellesley's culture influenced them mightily. Many also credited the grounding they got at Wellesley's economics department, which was recently ranked No. 2 (according to the traditional standard, published citations of its members' academic work), behind Williams, among the liberal arts colleges in a study by Michael Robinson of Mount Holyoke College. Ms. Bell has retired, but the department still has widely respected teachers like Marshall Goldman.
Uncommonly for a women's school, economics is usually Wellesley's most popular major, or at least second. And that is a longstanding phenomenon, not a product of the go-go 1980's, which lured many bright students into business.
More important, "Wellesley has Carolyn Bell, who within the school set a model," said Margaret Hennig, dean of the Simmons Graduate School of Business. "She made it legitimate even during the antibusiness period, the hippie period, for Wellesley women to get involved with economics and business. She obviously influenced enormous numbers of women."
And they agree. Ms. Marram, of Seagram, remembered the first meeting, in 1989, of Wellesley's Business Leadership Council, a group of 93 high-powered alumnae. "A bunch of us sat around after dinner, and soon we were all talking about Carolyn Bell," she said. "She was a fierce promoter of Wellesley economics graduates. She used to tell us that we should brag, that we should let people know what we can do."
To that, Ms. Bell, a graduate of Mount Holyoke who at 75 still writes a monthly column for The Boston Globe, pleads guilty. But she says she "never pushed any particular field for people."
"It was a personal value that any woman should be able to support herself and her children, whether or not she has to," Ms. Bell said. "Wellesley produced women who were responsible for themselves, and that may have helped."
Whatever it was, it worked. Marsha Williams, for example, was majoring in archeology until she ran into Ms. Bell. "She made economics come alive," said Ms. Williams, now treasurer of the Amoco Corporation. "It made me not be afraid of being unable to hold my own in a business setting."
Wellesley also tries to expose students to the business world. Ten years ago, it started a program called "Wall Street Warm-Up." It takes 20 to 30 students a year to Manhattan, stationing each with a Wellesley graduate in a brokerage or investment bank.
Whether it is for their first job or years later, Wellesley graduates use these contacts. Some networking is formal, through the Business Leadership Council, which began as a forum for busy graduates to help the school, but quickly became a place where members compared notes about work.
BUT much more of this power-networking is informal, a matter of keeping in touch by women like Adele Morrissette, '77, a director at Cowen & Company, a Manhattan investment bank. She is not a member of the leadership council. Nor did she go to Wellesley with a business career in mind. By the time she was a junior or senior, however, "I knew there were a lot of Wellesley women in business and that they were breaking barriers," she said.
When it came time to look for a job, Ms. Morrissette met Donna Ecton, '69, now chief executive of Business Mail Express Inc., who was recruiting for the Chemical Bank. "She really championed me," said Ms. Morrissette, who took the job, then got an M.B.A. "I would not be a Wall Street investment banker, were it not for her."
Ms. Morrissette keeps up with several Wellesley women, calling on a former client, Robin Smith, '61, the president of Publishers Clearing House, as a mentor; trading information with an alumna employed by a venture capital firm, and steering Cowen to a search firm run by Patricia Morrill, a Wellesley graduate.
This kind of mutual support happens all the time. Ms. Juliber, for example, joined the board of the State Street Boston Corporation after Nannerl O. Keohane, a former Wellesley president who is now president of Duke University and a member of both the leadership council and State Street's board, recommended her.
No wonder, then, that Wellesley graduates keep turning up in corporate circles. And because few colleges have picked up on Wellesley's bottom-line success -- only a couple, like Simmons College, have started groups for alumnae in business -- Wellesley may well remain a mother lode of executive women for a while to come.

HEADLINE: FUNDS WATCH;
A Derivatives Caution, in Time For Halloween

BYLINE: By CAROLE GOULD 

 BODY:
THOUGH the derivatives debacle in Orange County, Calif., is receding from investors' minds, the crisis is not likely to be the last of its type, says a report by Ernst & Young, the accounting firm.
Derivatives have a sullied name, not only because they forced Orange County into bankruptcy, but also because they caused losses in bond funds and money market funds last year. Broadly speaking, derivatives are financial contracts whose value is derived from the value of an underlying financial asset or index. They can be simple or very complex and are used to hedge risks, to reduce borrowing costs or to speculate on the direction of markets. 
 To see if more problems lurk in the derivatives arena, Ernst distributed questionnaires to 400 investment management complexes in Australia, Britain, Canada, France, Hong Kong, Ireland and the United States. It received 143 responses.
Ernst's findings could give securities regulators a good scare before Halloween. The study, titled "Derivatives Usage by Investment Funds," found that nearly a third of the funds used some derivatives and that more than 60 percent lacked a supervisory board or risk management committee to set limits on derivatives usage. Moreover, few of the companies' boards were familiar with complex financial instruments or risk management.
"The growing reliance on derivative products, coupled with the lack of sophisticated monitoring techniques, should be a concern of many fund complexes," Ernst concluded.
 
Doing Good
The Devcap Shared Return Fund is a new fund that lets shareholders both invest in a portfolio of "socially responsible" companies and make a tax-deductible donation each year to charity.
Investors designate a portion of their returns for charities that provide money to the working poor who have fledgling enterprises in developing nations. An investor can specify that 50 percent to 100 percent of the return be donated.
Development Capital Fund, the sponsor, is a consortium of international development agencies formed in 1992. It includes Save the Children, Catholic Relief Services and the Seed Capital Development Fund.
The fund will build a portfolio that tracks the Domini Social Index, which mimics the sector weighting of the general stock market but screens out companies with a poor record on environmental issues, with labor practice problems or with business in the weapons industry. The index contains about 400 companies in the United States.
 
A Departure at Govett
Garrett R. Van Wagoner is leaving the top-performing Govett Smaller Companies fund at the end of the year. The star manager led the fund through a period of rapid growth to $550 million in assets, and his departure casts doubt on whether Govett & Company will follow through on its plan to sell Govett Asset Management, its United States subsidiary. Govett officials said that Mr. Van Wagoner would open his own money management firm and that they had yet to decide who would replace him.

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT;
Nintendo? Bogus. But Investing? Bonus!

BYLINE: By ANNA D. WILDE 

 BODY:
JARED DECOTEAU takes his investing seriously. He has called the Charles Schwab toll-free number so often for stock prices that some of the operators greet him by name. He reads Barron's, Forbes, Worth and Money and has spent hours daily hunched over computer screens glowing with company data.
But he had to cut back, hobbled by a demanding new day job: ninth grade. "High school started this year," the 14-year-old says, "so I've been doing a lot with high school." 
 Jared, whose diverse portfolio is worth about "two and a half years" at an Ivy League school, which now costs roughly $60,000, is one of a growing group of precocious investors who are playing the market at an age when most of their peers are more interested in playing Nintendo.
According to a survey by Teenage Research Unlimited, about 17 percent of youths aged 12 through 19 say they own stocks or bonds. That percentage hasn't changed much since the survey started in 1992, but experts in children's finance say they are seeing more youngsters who want to invest.
In a survey by James U. McNeal, a professor of marketing at Texas A & M University, the number of children aged 11 and 12 who said they were saving or investing jumped from almost none in 1990 to about 10 percent last year.
There are many resources and services for small-fry speculators, although experts caution that children shouldn't overdo it. They remind parents that, after all, their kids are still kids. There are books about money, summer investing camps, and special mutual funds for kids. A Stein Roe fund for young people, started in April 1994, has about 9,300 investors. Young Americans Bank, a subsidiary of the Young Americans Educational Foundation, is a Denver-based bank for children only, accepting no customers older than 21. It offers checking and savings accounts and has even lent money for cheerleader uniforms and bicycles. Last November, it started offering mutual fund seminars to its 18,000 customers and their parents.
"Kids are more and more conscious of the concept that you really have to think about what you're going to do with your money," says Gail Yumkas, who plans to expand her Baltimore-based money-management summer camp to sites around the country.
Among other factors, educators attribute the rise in investing by children to an increased classroom focus on market issues and to baby-boomers' worries that their children will live in a financially insecure world. A survey on children and money posted on the Prodigy on-line service last November got 34,000 responses from parents, more than 90 percent in favor of financial education for their children, says the children's finance author Neale S. Godfrey, who prepared the survey for Prodigy.
Today, parents feel "it's very different from the Donna Reed world we grew up in," Ms. Godfrey says. "The boomers are saying, 'We have to raise our kids to be financially responsible.' "
Some educators are responding to that need. About 30 states were requiring economic or financial education in 1993, according to the Investment Company Institute, a trade group for mutual funds. A growing number of schools are also offering higher-level classes in economics: 22,500 high school students took the College Board's advanced placement economics tests last year, up from 5,800 when they were introduced in 1989.
The Security Industry Association's 18-year-old Stock Market Game has grown to include about 650,000 students in 48 states, up from 550,000 just a year ago. The Chicago Board of Trade's eight-year-old Commodity Challenge, an investment game for high schoolers, now includes 13,600 students. Children are learning about mutual funds as early as the third grade in a new nine-city pilot program sponsored by the Investment Company Institute.
Already, the next generation has a few Masters of the Universe who are hitting the lecture circuit and authoring books to proffer their money-making tips. Arthur Berg Bochner, 13, started with a $1,700 stake in Blockbuster at the age of 9 and now has a $72,000 portfolio. He has written two personal finance books with his mother, and this year he toured the country lecturing to summer-camp audiences. After an appearance on "The Tonight Show" last year, he was given $1,000 to invest by Jay Leno. It has grown to about $4,500 with stakes in Home Depot and China Power and Light.
And 17-year-old Matt Seto, the subject of a front-page profile in The Wall Street Journal last year, has controlled his own $45,000 limited partnership, the Matt Seto Fund, since he was 14. That was no easy feat in high school, where he lost his bathroom privileges after the newspaper revealed that he used the breaks to call his broker.
Matt, who started with $700 accumulated from years of gifts, has a book, "The Whiz Kid of Wall Street's Investment Guide," due out in January, with a 10-city tour planned for his winter break. "I like the excitement and the research that goes before it," he says about why he started the fund, which he manages for his family and friends. "It's tough to put into words."
Most underage money managers work on a smaller scale. Blake Engel, a high school junior in Naperville, Ill., turned $500 into $6,000 over six years of investing, mostly through a purchase of stock in the Hewlett-Packard Company. Then he cashed out to splurge on a red Chrysler LeBaron convertible for his 16th birthday. "It was insane, it was great," he says of his gambles, but he acknowledges that he has no grand plans for his economic future.
Blake's relaxed attitude is the right one for a teen-age investor, psychologists say. Youngsters can become dangerously depressed or tense if they risk too much in a stock gamble, says Kenneth O. Doyle, a financial psychologist at the University of Minnesota.
"I'm seeing a tendency to turn kids into miniature accountants at a very young age," Mr. Doyle says. "If it becomes a source of anxiety, I don't advocate it."
The danger can be acute for small children, says Lawrence Kutner, who teaches child psychiatry at Harvard Medical School. "Very young kids have no idea what money is about," he says. "Kids should be out learning and playing and finding out how the world works."
Mr. Doyle and Mr. Kutner recommend close parental supervision for children investing in the stock market and a focus on the educational aspect, not the need for high returns. Minors cannot open their own brokerage accounts, though they can control them if their parents allow it.
A second-grader, Noah Miller, for instance, owns an account that is controlled by his parents. The boy watches for favorites Spiderman and the Human Torch in the annual report from one of his investments, the Marvel Entertainment Group. "I don't want to push it on him," says his father, Joel Miller, a psychologist in Burlington, Vt. "He just will ask, how are we doing?' "
After their interests are piqued, children may want to pick their own portfolios, often beginning with cash accumulated from gifts or allowances. A Northwestern University sophomore, Ben Koff, started investing at 13, with the booty from his bar mitzvah party, and still holds his stock as a nest egg. "It's all something I'll have when I get out of college," he says.
And Eric Lofgren of Denver, 11, cashed in thousands of pennies and $400 hoarded from his $5-a-week allowance to buy shares in a mutual fund. That augmented the 10 shares of the Walt Disney Company that his parents bought him when he was 9 years old. He tracks his stocks with hand-made dot graphs.
"It's kind of mystical to know I own some of a big business" Eric says. "I joke that I own all the nuts and bolts on one of the rides at Disneyland."
 
 
 
Finding Advice for Young Investors
 Here are some tools to help young people learn about investing:
* The Stock Market Game is offered by the Security Industry Association through local economics education clubs. The game requires students to team up and invest $100,000 in virtual "dollars." They read annual reports and follow the stock tables and see who has the richest portfolio after 10 weeks. Some students who play invest real money later, as did Blake Engel of Naperville, Ill. "It definitely gave me a perspective," he says of the game. Write to: Securities Industry Foundation for Economic Education, 120 Broadway, 35th floor, New York 10271-0080
* The Chicago Board of Trade sponsors the Commodity Challenge for high school students. Call (800) 843-2268, extension 97. Teen-agers pick a commodity, write a report and analyze the commodity's behavior over five weeks. The writers of the best reports go to Chicago to spend time in the pit. "It was absolutely fabulous," says Sujata Gosalia, a senior from Lexington, Ky., who turned in 150 pages on "The Deutsche Mark: Currency Mania!"
* The Investment Company Institute offers "Mutual Fundamentals," a four-hour course. The program includes nine cities. Write to: Investment Company Institute Education Foundation, 1401 H Street N.W., Washington 20005.
* Some mutual funds offer educational materials and funds geared to the young. The Stein Roe Mutual Funds Young Investor Fund invests in companies children know like Microsoft and Coca-Cola. Call (800) 403-5437.
* Investing books for the young include Adriane Berg's and Arthur Berg Bochner's "Totally Awesome Money Book for Kids and Their Parents" (Newmarket Press). Neale S. Godfrey's "Money Doesn't Grow on Trees," aimed at parents, includes a chapter on setting up a child's portfolio. And the National Center for Financial Education offers a $1 catalogue of books, board games and other aids. Call (619) 239-1401.

LENGTH: 1266 words

HEADLINE: MAKING IT WORK;
The Marxist Professor

BYLINE: By RAYMOND HERNANDEZ 

 BODY:
THERE he was, one of New York City's last old-time Marxists, propped in a lunchtime den of wealthy Wall Street types, shaking a forkful of Irish sausage as he delivered prophecies of doom.
"Capitalism is not a rational system," exclaimed Stanley Aronowitz, a sociology professor at the City University of New York Graduate Center. "The only way it turns around is through mass struggle." 
 But no one at the Beekman Pub was listening. Not the soberly suited young men leaning against the bar. Nor the smart-looking couple in a nearby booth.
"Is everything O.K.?" asked the cheery waitress asked, interrupting Mr. Aronowitz's rambling manifesto.
No! Everything isn't O.K.
That's exactly the point Mr. Aronowitz has been trying to make through four decades of union organizing, proselytizing and Marx-influenced writing and teaching. Whether we know it or not, he says, we're stuck in a class structure that doesn't let us (or enough of us) breathe.
But these are difficult times for Marxists like him. It's been decades since the studying of Marx was regarded as the radical chic of the academic world. The camp of professors that taught Marx in the 60's and 70's has retreated from the social activism that was once so closely associated with it.
What they're doing these days seems far more ivory-towerish. Dispersing into departments that range from economics to women's studies to cultural studies, they are content to explore the power games underlying sex, money and, in a truly abstract exercise, knowledge itself. And that can leave unrepentant radicals pretty lonely, even Mr. Aronowitz, who has tried to keep up with the academic times.
"They say I'm the last one," said the 62-year-old Mr. Aronowitz. "They say I'm the last of the working-class intellectuals. That's terrible."
But Mr. Aronowitz is not likely to fade away anytime soon. In fact, he made quite a splash with his latest contribution to the class struggle: "The Jobless Future: Sci-Tech and the Dogma of Work," a nearly 400-page book written with another sociologist, William DiFazio.
The authors argue that technological advances, like automated teller machines, are permanently dislodging legions of workers from their jobs each and every day in a reorganization of labor.
It's a dreary state of affairs, as Mr. Aronowitz is wont to point out any chance he gets.
"Look what's happening on Wall Street," Mr. Aronowitz said, sipping a very dry Bombay Gibson on ice. "They've laid off 80,000 people out of 400,000 since 1987. It's not a blip, either. It's a permanent displacement. If we don't begin to look at our social arrangements, New York is going to be like Bombay."
Or even, he said later, Detroit.
Ever since the "The Jobless Future" was published last fall, Mr. Aronowitz has been getting calls from unionists, politicians, quotation-hunting journalists and talk-show hosts. (He has been on National Public Radio more than once.) And traditionally liberal periodicals, like The Nation, published lengthy reviews, while major newspapers, like The Washington Post, quoted him in feature articles on joblessness.
It's all got Mr. Aronowitz gloating, if only a bit.
"Marx is starting to look pretty smart at a time when Marxism should have been buried with the Soviet Union," he said. "There was a lot of smugness before the stock market crash in '87, but things are changing. There are a lot more people who aren't so sure about their futures."
Still, he and Mr. DiFazio see a way out, albeit a utopian one. They envision a brave new world in which workers are given shorter workweeks, have guaranteed wages and can spend their leisure in the pursuit of happiness, whether in a museum, on a college campus or with their families at home.
Which is all in keeping with Marx.
The book only reinforces Mr. Aronowitz's reputation at CUNY. He has long been regarded as one of CUNY's most prominent crusaders, who attracts and nurtures some of the most radical students with his insights on the state of mass culture, from TV and movies to rock and roll.
A few years ago, he was right there, bullhorn in hand, calling for solidarity when student demonstrators occupied the CUNY Graduate Center, on 42d Street, to protest state cuts.
Mr. Aronowitz's class-conscious sensibilities run deep. He grew up in blue-collar East Tremont, the Bronx, where boys dreamed of landing jobs in steel mills and truck yards, just as their fathers had.
Mr. Aronowitz's father dropped out of New York University in his freshman year to take a job as a reporter for The New York World. His mother, who held a variety of jobs in department stores and an office, was an ardent unionist who eventually became a shop steward.
It was the early 50's when he joined the blue-collar work force himself as a teen-ager. He took a job loading a fur truck and then worked at steel plants in New Jersey. He also began to take labor-relations courses and became a union organizer.
By 1967, he was enrolled at the New School for Social Research in Manhattan, where he earned a degree in sociology. This set him on a lifelong academic course; he has published a dozen books and hundreds of articles for newspapers and academic journals.
Still, his passion has always been working in the trenches of the labor struggle. "I was always bored in classrooms," Mr. Aronowitz said. "Bored out of my skull."
But things aren't looking so good in the trenches these days, particularly not in New York City, which just a generation ago was a relatively nice place for a Marxist like Mr. Aronowitz to live. Now, he says, unions cave in routinely, as City Hall bows to the demands of corporate barons like George Steinbrenner and Donald J. Trump, who seek sweet deals.
"We're terrified that business will split," he said. "So we give them tax breaks and end up cutting education, culture, environmental protection and so on. It's an endless cycle of concessions. It's policy based on the bottom line."
His warnings come down to this: corporate mergers, downsizing and the like are leaving society -- including government -- beholden to the whim of the capitalist.
But don't think for one minute that his interest is purely theoretical.
"They can't afford to pick up the garbage on Wednesday now?" complained Mr. Aronowitz, sounding more like a frustrated New Yorker than a Marxist.
He's worried about the public schools, too, in no small part because he and his partner, Ellen Willis, the writer, send their daughter to an intermediate school in District 2 in lower Manhattan.
So what's the average proletarian to do?
"I think people have to decide if they want to continue to be held hostage," said the professor, who argues that a strong working-class movement is the only way to insure social progress. "No one has been willing to say, 'Enough already.' "
 
WORDS ON WORDS
 
Negotiating the Maze of Meaning
 
"The question What is to be done? has become tainted because it points to calumnies committed in the name of traditional ideologies of emancipation. Indeed, to speak of liberation, of the emancipation of labor, appears utopian, in the bad sense; in a period of the transformation of global politics by the breakup of the Soviet Union, the intellectual discrediting of Marxism, even by some of its erstwhile practitioners, and, on a larger canvas the steep decline of socialism, recalling these words conjures images of betrayal."
 
-- from "The Jobless Future: Sci-Tech and the Dogma of Work" (University of Minnesota Press, 1994) by Stanley Aronowitz and William DiFazio.

HEADLINE: PLAYING IN THE NEIGHBORHOOD

 BODY:

UPPER WEST SIDE
Children's Tales, as Sung By a Touring Octet
A St. Louis a cappella octet, Pieces of Eight, combines rich vocal harmonies with popular children's tales to produce jazzy renditions of such stories as "The Gingerbread Man" and "City Mouse, Country Mouse." Some selections are geared toward adult audiences. "As Time Goes By" and "Anything Goes" are included in the group's repertory. 
 Beginning next Saturday, Pieces of Eight (below) will start its first New York City tour, including performances at branches of the New York and Brooklyn Public Libraries. The group's music is written or arranged by it artistic director, Charles Mead, who weaves together elements of classical music with gospel, reggae, doo-wop and Cajun.
Pieces of Eight, Riverside Branch of the New York Public Library, 127 Amsterdam Avenue (at 65th Street); Saturday, 3:30 P.M.; free; (212) 870-1633.
 
BOWLING GREEN
 
Museum Demonstrations Of Latin American Weaving
Visitors to the program marking the first anniversary of the Museum of the American Indian will be able to watch female artists from Ecuador, Bolivia and Mexico weave, knit and embroider shawls, clothing and blankets. Interpreters will be on hand to help with questions and answers. The program, "The Market Place: A Latin American Women's Arts Program," will also feature slide shows and lectures on the development and significance of textile art forms in Latin America, and community-based collectives and art as engines of economic development for the continent and its people. Among the featured speakers will be an anthropologist, June Nash.
"The Market Place: A Latin American Women's Arts Program," National Museum of the American Indian, 1 Bowling Green; today and tomorrow, noon to 5 P.M.; free.
 
FLUSHING
 
Photos of Landmarks, And Memories of Vietnam
"I like to get to know my subject, whether it be a person or a building," said Tony Valez, a photographer and former adjunct professor in the art department at Queens College. "There is a time element you have to put in to get the right light or mood and personality."
Highlights of Mr. Valez's work over the last eight years, as well as some older photographs, will be featured in an exhibition at Queens College that opens on Thursday.
More than 50 photographs will be on display, including those of Brooklyn landmarks and historic buildings, like the Brooklyn Bridge and the Fulton Ferry historic district (above).
The show will also feature arresting photographs from Mr. Valez's tour of duty in Vietnam in 1966-67.

HEADLINE: Why Fisheries Drew Bipartisan Applause

 BODY:
To the Editor
"Moderates Soften G.O.P. Agenda on Environment" (front page, Oct. 24) was right to mention the recent 388-to-37 vote in the House to reauthorize and strengthen the Magnuson Fishery Conservation and Management Act as an example of Republicans' new approach to environmental measures. 
 The primary reasons for the large bipartisan majority for stronger fisheries conservation, however, concern economic necessity.
Foremost is the obvious need to protect fish populations so that commercial and recreational fisheries remain viable. Although the stronger act may mean smaller short-term profits for some fishing operations, it will help insure that there will always be ample fish, employment, recreation and profits from catching fish for generations to come.
Moreover, our overexploited, declining fisheries are producing far below their potential, at a cost of more than $3 billion a year. Government estimates show that managing our fisheries in an environmentally sustainable manner could generate 300,000 new jobs and cut our annual trade deficit by $2 billion.
If you add to this the social and cultural importance of conserving our fisheries and the traditional way of life of fishing communities, and the imperative of protecting our fragile marine ecosystems, strengthening the Magnuson Act is just common sense. Wise Republicans and Democrats alike agree.
The act also passed because scores of fishing and conservation organizations representing millions of Americans worked hard to raise public awareness and Congressional commitment to save our fisheries. The Senate should exhibit the same wise judgment by supporting the same strengthening amendments and reauthorizing the Magnuson Act this fall.
 
BILL MOTT
Campaign Director
Marine Fish Conservation Network
Washington, Oct. 25, 1995

HEADLINE: THE WORLD;
Powell Takes a Hit

BYLINE: By ERIC SCHMITT 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
WHEN Bosnia's three warring factions sit down to talk peace this week, it's only fitting that they will meet at a United States Air Force base.
NATO air power, dominated by United States fighter jets, bombed the Bosnian Serbs to the bargaining table. A two-week bombardment this summer demolished their ammunition dumps, air defenses and command bunkers. It wrecked their will to wage wide-scale war and broke the siege of Sarajevo. But it stopped short of breaking their army. 
 And in doing so, NATO's strikes also challenged the doctrine set forth by Gen. Colin L. Powell: If you use military force, use it overwhelmingly and use it to win.
 
The Wrong Time
 This comes at an inopportune time for General Powell. The former Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff conferred last week with top Republican strategists on his potential Presidential bid. As he approaches a decision, it seems that his most famous idea might have just been proved wrong.
In his autobiography, General Powell says he told President Bush and President Clinton that limited air strikes or heavy bombing of Serb positions would probably fail. "I emphasized that none of these actions was guaranteed to change Serb behavior," General Powell wrote. "Only troops on the ground could do that."
The General's critics say this thinking has encouraged an all-or-nothing approach that is ill-suited to conflicts like Bosnia.
"We did not set out to win a war," Kenneth Bacon, a Pentagon spokesman, said last month after the two-week bombing campaign. "We did not set out to inflict damage on the population of the Serbian sections of Bosnia, nor did we set out to destroy the Serbian army."
The specific goals were to force the Bosnian Serbs to stop shelling Sarajevo, withdraw many of their heavy weapons from around the Bosnian capital and push the parties into talks. And NATO did just that.
Air power has its limits. Bombing alone did not bring Nazi Germany to its knees. Waves of B-52's did not crush North Vietnam. And it was ground troops that finished off the Iraqi army in the Persian Gulf war.
Allied air power had a lot of help in bringing the Balkan peace talks to this point. The fall of Srebrenica and Zepa, two Muslim enclaves under United Nations protection, shamed the West into action. A ground offensive by Croatian and Bosnian Government troops rolled back the Bosnian Serb army. President Slobodan Milosevic of Serbia decided that ending the economic embargo against his country outweighed the temptation of continued combat.
Would the same bombing have achieved the same results two or three years ago, sparing many of the 200,000 people killed since fighting broke out in 1991? Many diplomats and military experts familiar with the Balkans answer, resoundingly, yes.
"Swift and disproportionate force is the only way to deal with bullies," said Charles H. Thomas 2d, who retired last month as the top American envoy in Bosnia. "The Serbs would have seen our use of air power as a symbol of our willingness to dish it to them. Diplomacy without force is like baseball without a bat."
While the warplanes cut off the Serbs' communications and supply lines and hampered their ability to wage wider war, they avoided most of the tanks and artillery guns that Bosnian Serb troops needed to defend themselves.
"We could have done the same thing two or three years ago," a senior NATO commander said ruefully. "We just didn't have the stomach for it then."

HEADLINE: THE WORLD;
Iraqis Are Angry but Not Just at You-Know-Who

BYLINE: By YOUSSEF M. IBRAHIM 

DATELINE: BAGHDAD, Iraq

 BODY:
A LAH MUKHTAR, a ranking member of the ruling Baath Party, glanced at television sets tuned in to CNN, BBC and (of all countries) Israel in his office on a recent evening, watching for commentary about the imminent referendum on whether to retain Saddam Hussein as President. Not far away, in a teahouse near Al Kazem mosque, Malek, an unemployed 25-year-old Iraqi Shiite, watched the Qatar-Iraq soccer game -- for him, a far more interesting diversion from the hardships of life under economic sanctions.
By nightfall, Iraq had won the match 3 to 2, but nobody was in a mood to celebrate. In the gathering dark, Abu Waleed, who drives clients between Baghdad and Jordan on the 18-hour road voyage made necessary by the embargo on air links, hooked a live electric wire to his GMC desert van to dissuade car thieves. And nightclubs in the suburb of Massbah were filling with visitors from Jordan, Yemen and Libya -- looking for the crop of girls from good Iraqi families now said to be selling their virtue for a few dollars. 
 This is what has become of the dreams of a comfortable modern life in Iraq, dreams on which Saddam Hussein built his ties to the middle class that he virtually created in the optimistic years before 1990.
Back then, the clients at the nightclubs were big-spending Iraqis seeking the company of foreign women. Malek was employed in a thriving business selling car parts. And Abu Waleed went to sleep without bothering to lock up his car at all.
Then came Iraq's calamitous invasion of Kuwait -- and, in its aftermath, the crushing United Nations sanctions intended to force Iraqi compliance with peace terms and, perhaps, to bring down Mr. Hussein.
The sanctions have, indeed, crushed hope that life will improve under Mr. Hussein. Today, the mood of Baghdad's inhabitants seems to alternate between apathy, hopelessness and anger.
But there is also an important way in which the sanctions have begun to work against their own purposes. For the anger these days is one of resignation and despair, not action -- focused on no one as it lashes out at everybody.
Two or three years ago, when the sanctions were relatively new and Mr. Hussein's staying power remained untested, many Iraqis here were expressing confidence that their problems would be over once the United States, working with Iraqi opposition groups, succeeded in overthrowing their leader.
But now that Mr. Hussein has proved he can survive, this clarity of view is gone.
 
Even Romania Looks Good
 "It is a case of total anger even against God himself," says Ghassan Attia, an opposition activist in exile in London. "People are at the end of their rope. We feel it in the opposition. You can measure the despair by the vast immigration movement. Two million Iraqis have left the country to places like Iran, England, Canada, New Zealand and Romania.
"Imagine," he said. "Romania. Just to get out."
To many Iraqis who speak candidly, Saddam Hussein remains a villain. But since nobody has succeeded in overthrowing him, he is far from the only villain. He is joined by the United States, because of its insistence on continuing sanctions; by Arab nations, because they have remained silent about the clear suffering of the populace, and by the many factions of the divided Iraqi opposition, who are widely seen here as interest groups doing someone else's dirty work.
This diffusion of blame has been used skillfully by the ruling clique in the past year to nail down two messages. One is this: As bad as things are, they could be a great deal worse, if the opposition gets control and Iraq is sundered into ethnic entities with Kurds in the north, Shiites in the south and Sunnis in the center.
 
A Past to Reckon With
 The other is a reminder that the regime that brought war and calamity to Iraq was also the one that raised Iraq's hope of modernity in the first place -- by greatly expanding the middle class, by pushing Iraq to become the fastest growing Arab economy (based on its oil, education and skills), by instituting a generous welfare and health system and a mighty army. Those things once made Iraq a country to reckon with, and the lesson of the sanctions is that its enemies are now simply out to destroy the country, the message goes.
"All Iraqis can see we have done everything that is required of us, yet there is no end to the suffering because of this fierce determination of the United States to wipe out this country as we used to know it," said Mr. Mukhtar, editor of the mass circulation newspaper Al Jumhuriya, in an interview in his office as he watched the television screens. "That is why we are not anymore under pressure from public opinion in Iraq." A true believer in Mr. Hussein, Mr. Mukhtar continues to think the Baath Party can bring Iraq out of this crisis by reaching out to angry Iraqis with the message that the embargo is a Western consipiracy to split up Iraq.
And he has reason for hope. While Iraq remains a society replete with ethnic groups, religious groups, subgroups, tribes and clans, many Iraqis say a strong sense of nationhood has emerged in the two decades of Saddam Hussein's rule -- and that many are wedded to it because if the nation fragments or descends into a power struggle, blood and anarchy would follow.
"I don't think of myself as a Shiite, but as an Iraqi," said a poet who opposes the regime and, like most of those interviewed, sought anonymity. "People forget there is something called an Iraqi identity that precedes Saddam. Right here in Baghdad there are hundreds of thousands of Shiites and Kurds. We are not in the south only and the Kurds are not in the north only."
It is safe to say that few Iraqis believe that President Hussein or his party is capable of shifting "from revolutionary legitimacy to democracy," as the Government promised last week. But that is, in a way, beside the point. The true believers can count on deep skepticism among the populace about the alternatives, and this has only increased in the years since sanctions were introduced. There are a half-dozen opposition groups, but the Kurds in the north fight each other and the hard-core Shiite opposition in the south is clearly under the control of Iran, the Persian enemy.
So more and more, Iraqis seem to believe that instead of pushing them to revolt against a ruthless regime, sanctions are simply undermining their society, altering its morality and social cohesion.
"If your arrows cannot reach the head, why do you keep aiming at the body?" asked Hamid, a once-successful Shiite businessman who claims to be one of the few Iraqis who had the nerve to vote no in the Oct. 15 referendum.
"Isn't it clear by now that Saddam is staying because he has no other place to go and no one strong enough to push him out? He is only leaving on a stretcher or not at all."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Saddam Hussein still rules, as this Baghdad scene shows, and many Iraqis have lost hope for a change soon. (Associated Press)

SECTION: Section 4; Page 1; Column 2; Week in Review Desk 

LENGTH: 1287 words

HEADLINE: THE WORLD;
Soldiering On, Without an Enemy

BYLINE: By ELAINE SCIOLINO 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
IN preparing to send 20,000 American troops to Bosnia, one of President Clinton's biggest problems is how to market the mission.
The polls explain why. 
 Only 37 percent of Americans favor sending in American ground troops to participate in a NATO-led force in Bosnia, while 57 percent are opposed, even though the troops would be going there to keep peace, not wage war, according to a New York Times-CBS Poll released last week.
Some of the reasons are familiar. Historically, Americans have always questioned the need for sending their soldiers to some new faraway place, even when the stakes were high and the risks low.
In this Administration, the television footage of Somalians dragging a dead American soldier through the streets of Mogadishu in 1993 had a chilling effect on future use of American troops in "peacekeeping" operations, particularly when there is no peace to keep. It's what Richard Holbrooke, the chief American negotiator, calls the "Vietmalia syndrome": the placing of constraints on policies by the memories of the worst cases rather than the successes.
But there is another problem as well. In arguing for the proposed Bosnia peace mission, the Administration finds itself describing something that sounds suspiciously like a war. It isn't supposed to be a war, of course -- American soldiers would be asked not to fight anyone, only to stand between adversaries who have already agreed to stop shooting at each other. But they still could be killed by mines or ambushed by trouble-makers, even if war doesn't resume.
So because of the scale of the overall NATO commitment (60,000 troops), the blood-soaked history of the Balkans and the risk to American soldiers, supporters of the mission find themselves facing the language of the battlefield.
The starkest -- but clearest -- moment in the debate so far came in a recent hearing on Capitol Hill, when Senator James Inhofe, the Oklahoma Republican, asked Administration witnesses point-blank whether the Bosnia mission justified "hundreds of young Americans dying over there."
Secretary of Defense William Perry: "Yes.
Secretary of State Warren Christopher: "Yes."
So the first question to Mr. Christopher on "Larry King Live" the other night from a caller in Henderson, Nevada, should have surprised no one. "Secretary of State, what is the difference of sending troops to Bosnia or to Vietnam?" she asked.
 
NATO Yes, Body Bags No
 To be sure, the decision of whether to commit troops is not going to be made by referendum, but by the President and Congress. And although lawmakers of both parties have raised objections to the mission, ultimately Congress is likely to be won over by the argument that the preservation of NATO is at stake. Still, there is great concern in Congress about the reaction from voters if body bags arrive home in the middle of re-election campaigns.
Indeed, American support for peacemaking in Bosnia falls dramatically when the hypothetical casualties rise. Americans favor the mission by more than a two-to-one margin if no Americans are killed, and oppose the mission by nearly the same margin if 25 Americans are killed, according to a USA Today/ CNN/Gallup poll released last week.
But the selling of wars, unlike peacekeeping missions, usually requires enemies. And usually these are not so much evil principles as the evil, larger-than-life actors who personify them -- a Hitler or a Saddam Hussein. Even little wars have people to hate: President Antonio Manuel Noriega in Panama in 1989, the specter of Fidel Castro looming over Grenada in 1983.
In his memoirs, former Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d acknowledged the folly of saying that what was at stake with Iraq's invasion of Kuwait was American jobs. ("I overreacted to the growing complaints that the only reason we were in the Gulf was because of oil," Mr. Baker wrote. "I still believe I was right, but it didn't work.")
It took the vilification of Mr. Hussein as a dictator "worse than Hitler" whose country was on the verge of becoming a nuclear power before President Bush had a message with resonance.
Bosnia has plenty of bad guys, but none of them make much of an enemy. It is simply too difficult for the average American to understand why the political leader and the military commander of the Bosnian Serbs have been indicted (but not arrested) by an international war crimes tribunal, but why the President of Serbia who speaks for them will be sitting at the peace table in Dayton, Ohio, this week.
"It is a hard argument to make," admits Michael McCurry, the White House spokesman. "Most Americans throw up their hands when they try to wrestle with the dazzling complexity that is Bosnia. Americans by nature would say, 'What does Bosnia have to do with me?' "
And by deciding not to declare Serbia an aggressive and genocidal enemy, the Administration has had to fall back on abstract and difficult-to-prove predictions to justify peacemaking: that the war will spread to the rest of Europe and the NATO alliance will collapse if American troops do not serve in the force.
But even Mr. Christopher resorted to war-like words in arguing for peacekeeping recently, when he suggested to a recent Congressional hearing that the stakes were nothing less than avoiding World War III. Asked what he would tell a mother whose son died for Sarajevo, he said: "I would recall that twice before this century the United States has had to send not a limited number of troops, but an almost unlimited number of troops to take part in a war that started from Central Europe -- once again from the very city that we're talking about here now. We have an opportunity to avoid being put in that situation again."
One problem with that argument is that it seems to overstate the risks of staying out, while shrinking from a more plausible, if less dramatic, explanation: If the United States wants a stable Europe with which to trade and share a democratic future, it's going to have to help police the danger zones. And if that requires troops, some will have to be Americans.
Another big problem with Mr. Christopher's argument is that this is the first time it's been used. For four years after the Yugoslav wars began, neither President Bush nor President Clinton told the American people that the United States, together with NATO, had to stop these wars because they could spread and because the future of NATO was at stake. On the contrary, with few exceptions, both Administrations argued that saving Bosnia was not in America's vital national interest.
But now that peace looks more likely, suddenly the argument is being put forward that it is a vital national interest for American troops to help keep a peace settlement because to fail to do so would risk a European-wide war and destroy NATO.
"All of a sudden, Clinton is trying to make the case that he is best friends with the heads of Europe and that he is doing this to solidify the Atlantic alliance," says Barbara Bardes, professor of political science at the University of Cincinnati and an expert on public opinion and foreign policy. "That's a big stretch. It's going to be a slippery sell."
Indeed, when asked on NBC's "Meet the Press" last week, "What's so sacred about NATO?" Mr. Perry found himself in the peculiar position of using the arguments of war -- the cold war, to be exact..
"There are still 20,000 nuclear weapons in the former Soviet Union," Mr. Perry said. "And the political and the economic recovery going on in those countries is extremely fragile. Extremely fragile. So we have a very strong interest in the security and stability of Europe. NATO, in my judgment, is the linchpin of maintaining that stability."

LENGTH: 1208 words

HEADLINE: IDEAS & TRENDS;
The Japan Bank Alarm

BYLINE: By SHERYL WuDUNN 

DATELINE: TOKYO

 BODY:
A DELICIOUS meal had been polished off, the Geisha girls had set down their musical instruments, the wine bottles had been emptied and the songs sung. Yoshio Nakajima, a top official at the Ministry of Finance, reportedly eyed his favorite hostess, who then accompanied him to his hotel.
The bill for that extravagance last year was paid, according to Japanese press reports, by Harunori Takahashi, who at the time was head of a credit union. The lavish entertainment was a factor in the credit union's bankruptcy and in Mr. Nakajima's resignation from the Finance Ministry in July. It was an extreme case, but such revelations are prompting people here to wonder whether those in banking officialdom are really the dour, restrained, middle-aged functionaries they are widely perceived to be. 
 Most likely many aren't, for regulatory oversight in Japanese banking is definitely not a dour affair -- as American officials are learning after Daiwa Bank and Japanese officials kept American regulators in the dark over the bank's huge losses in New York. Here banking oversight is a nod-and-wink business, and quite often a drunken affair in the bars -- and that may be one reason Japan's banking system is a mess today.
That mess is raising eyebrows and fears in America as well as Japan because international finance is like a huge swimming pool: if one corner springs a leak, the water level drops everywhere. If a major bank crisis forced the Japanese to sell their holdings of Treasury bonds to bring cash home, the price of bonds would slide. That would force interest rates to rise in America, and higher mortgage rates would make homes less affordable in Peoria.
This is partly why American financial officials are worried about the state of Japanese banking, and the uncertain role in the Daiwa scandal of the Finance Ministry, which has denied any wrongdoing. A certain trust in mutual cooperation has been breached, and with revelations that the Central Intelligence Agency spied on the Japanese during trade talks this year, the Americans are no less to blame. As Japanese and American officials meet for talks during President Clinton's visit to Japan next month, both sides will be asking: how much can we trust each other?
 
Chumminess
 In the case of these two huge trading partners, the need for financial integration and cooperation is being challenged by their extraordinarily different ways of doing business. While American bankers and their financial authorities have a distant, sometimes chilly relationship, their Japanese counterparts in contrast are hard-and-fast chums.
In every Japanese bank there is an elite group of promising young bankers whose full-time job is to dawdle in the offices of the Ministry of Finance. The real work, however, often begins in the evenings, when they and their fellow bank executives wine and dine ministry officials to gather the latest bits of news on regulatory moves, approvals of new products or anything that might help the bank's business.
They are the "MOF-tans," -- the handlers -- the bank's special liaison agents for the Ministry of Finance. Japanese bankers must consult the ministry for almost every conceivable matter related to their business, and so the job of the MOF-tan is to stop by the ministry, usually once in the morning and once in the afternoon. Sometimes, to deepen personal relationships, they send gifts to the officials' wives on their birthdays and wedding anniversaries.
"The MOF-tan people might just drop in the office, and that is why MOF people are quite busy -- we can do our work only after 5 P.M.," said Yukio Noguchi, an economics professor and a former ministry official. "They say, 'I happened to visit the MOF and just dropped in to see if you are in the office or not.' It's gradually changing, but the basics are there."
As these MOF-tans then scatter throughout the bank in their next assignments, the contacts they have built with financial officials help bind the banks to the Finance Ministry in a complex web of relations, a glue that helps the two sides stick together in a crisis.
"If you have a dinner with a business counterpart, with lots of drinks and so on -- it is a Japanese tradition -- you can know each other better so as to have a sense of trust to expand the business relationship," said a ministry official.
The Daiwa scandal, in which a rogue trader lost $1.1 billion over 11 years, illustrates how the cozy relationship between banker and bureaucrat can lead to misunderstandings with other countries. Daiwa executives informally told the Finance Ministry of the loss, saying they wanted to wait a while before publicly disclosing it together with satisfactory earnings, and the Ministry willingly obliged and kept silent.
When American regulators discovered that the ministry had known about the loss for six weeks, they were surprised and angry. But Japanese bankers were less astonished, for while in America bankers and regulators stand on opposite sides, in Japan they are sometimes partners.
 
Tip-Offs

Today Japan's sagging economy is trimming expense accounts, and public criticism has made ministry officials much more reluctant to be entertained by corporate executives. While extravagant entertaining has been toned down, meetings now occur over lunch or a leisurely drink, so that trust can still develop.
A conversation might go like this:
MOF-tan: "We believe there will be a surprise inspection at one of our branches. We wonder which one that could be?"
Ministry of Finance bureaucrat: "Somewhere in the west, I believe."
In 1991, for instance, the ministry conducted an inspection at Fuji Bank in the aftermath of an incident over falsified bank account certificates, according to the Asahi Shimbun, the Japanese daily. After the inspection, an executive at Fuji Bank reportedly said: "The timing and the target of the inspection were as we expected."
In fact, inspections in Japan are significantly scaled-down versions of the kind conducted by any of the numerous regulatory agencies in the United States. Japan has only 700 inspectors for all of its banks, far fewer (even in proportional terms) than the estimated 8,000 to 10,000 financial inspectors in the United States.
"Japanese believe that people are essentially good," said a senior Finance Ministry official. "But Americans believe that people are basically evil. For us, trust is very important."
Sometimes, the cultural divide between the world's two largest financial communities can even affect international financial stability. For several months earlier this year, the value of the yen hovered at 80 yen to the dollar, the yen's strongest level.
While it helped American exports, it was an embarrassment for Americans that the reserve currency of the world was falling through the floor. Americans blamed the Japanese, the Japanese blamed the Americans, and an impasse seemed unbridgeable until two old friends, Eisuke Sakakibara, a top Ministry official, and Lawrence Summers, a senior Treasury official, started talking.
They had met at Harvard University and had a common language -- Mr. Sakakibara speaks flawless English -- and with their coordination the dollar began to strengthen.

HEADLINE: THE NATION;
Bartering Inmate Futures

BYLINE: By IAN FISHER 

 BODY:
THE first 200 inmates from Colorado arrived at the Bowie County Correctional Center in Texarkana on the last, scorching week of July. Some were put to work making road signs, others picnic benches or toys for poor children. Still others agreed to duty in the sewing shop, where they learned a trade but worked on a more immediate goal: stitching shorts for themselves and other Coloradans unaccustomed to the deadly heat of Texas.
In the last 18 months, 500 inmates have been sent from Colorado's overcrowded prison system to the county prison in Texas, another 500 to a privately run prison in Minnesota. They are among a growing and hotly debated trade in prisoners across state borders, a movable byproduct of the drive to crack down on crime through tougher sentencing laws and an inability -- often because of voters' reluctance -- to build expensive new prisons quickly. 
 And while the numbers are small, probably fewer than 10,000 prisoners nationwide from less than a dozen states, few support the practice as anything more than a bad, short-term answer to overcrowding.
 
Prisoners in Oversupply
 "It's a last resort," said Liz McDonough, a spokeswoman for the Colorado Department of Corrections. "It's not something anybody wants to do. It's hard on the families. It's hard on the departments. It's not easy to manage. You have to, in essence, manage a facility long distance."
It has, however, turned prisoners into a commodity, as empty county jails and private prisons compete for excess inmates and the money they bring. There are prisoner placement consultants and rules for what kind of prisoner can travel (the healthy and tractable are preferred). One prison in Texas, where the county system houses by far the greatest number of out-of-staters in the country, advertises, its aim being to keep corrections employees employed and its local economy alive.
"I would never apply the word commodity to human beings," said James R. Roberts, vice president of Dominion, an Oklahoma company that locates private prisons and brokers transfers of prisoners for a fee. "I would say that they are becoming some kind of economic unit that represents a cost and, to the receiving facility or region, represents an economic benefit."
The practice has spurred lawsuits from prisoners' families and advocates. But in these days of limited sympathy for convicts, the economic argument against the practice appears the most compelling. Last summer officials in Tennessee decided against sending prisoners out of state largely out of concern that they would be filling another state's pot. Mr. Roberts said costs run from just under $40 to $70 a day per inmate.
States have sent prisoners to neighboring states for years, sometimes to relieve overcrowding and other times to fill a special need, like protective custody. But now, prison officials say, the difference is that the state prison systems are at or above capacity and the overflow is moved in greater numbers to county jails and private prisons.
Colorado, Oregon, Utah, Missouri, Virginia and Alaska have all transferred inmates out of state, and several other states, including Massachusetts, Idaho, Oklahoma and Hawaii, are considering it.
A tale of two states, Oregon and Texas, illustrates how and why this is happening.
In 1994 Oregon voters passed a ballot initiative that established a mandatory minimum sentence for 16 serious felonies at 5 2/3 years, with no chance of early release. Gary D. Weeber, a program manager at the Oregon Department of Corrections, said the measure, combined with tougher rules for parole, filled prisons to the point where the state's 7,600-bed system may soon grow by 4,000 prisoners.
In Texas a huge expansion of the state prison system emptied more than 200 county prisons of an overflow of state inmates, after many counties had expanded to accommodate the surplus. The counties scrambled to find prisoners from anywhere.
"Why let these facilities rust?" asked Bob Dearing, deputy director of the Texas Jail Commission, which regulates county jails.
So, starting Sept. 1, Oregon began sending what will be as many as 250 inmates toDenton County, north of Dallas. The cost is $36.50 a day per inmate, compared to about $52 a day in-state. Oregon saves money and Texas makes some. "We really have to choose between the lesser of two evils," Mr. Weeber said. "Right now we have more coming through the door than we can handle. And that being the case, our first priority has to be safety and security."
Advocates for inmates complain -- and prison officials agree -- that the practice defies sound theory: Inmates with ties to family and community tend to be better behaved and less likely to return to prison. They also complain that it is inhumane and unfair to separate prisoners from their families by hundreds of miles.
Jeffrey Bridges, a 39-year-old from Newport News, was among the first 150 inmates transferred from Virginia to Newton County, Tex., in February. He said he and other inmates had no idea where they were going until they sat, shackled, on a chartered plane. Many of the inmates incarcerated in Texas, he said, "were real angry. A lot of them acted up because they definitely didn't want to be there."
Mr. Bridges, convicted of stealing electronic diaries from a Wal-Mart, was visited once by his family, in a trip organized by families of prisoners sent to Texas. He was released in September.
"It was hard on me, but I think it was a lot harder on my family," Mr. Bridges said. "I had done a crime, but there was no need for them to suffer along with me."
