HEADLINE: Perot Commands Enthusiasm For New Jersey Conservatives

BYLINE: By IVER PETERSON 

DATELINE: NEW BRUNSWICK, N.J., Oct. 28

 BODY:
Ross Perot brought his twang and his pungent criticism of the major parties to New Jersey today, telling his United We Stand supporters to back New Jersey's infant Conservative Party in the General Assembly elections next week as a prelude to sweeping Congress and the Presidency with a new national political party next year.
The Texas industrialist uncorked a speech that was vintage Perot: the Democratic and Republican Parties, he said, are "bought and sold" by special interests; Federal bureaucrats are too stupid to manage Federal programs properly, and the United States' economy has fallen in thrall to the low wages and high technology of foreigners. 
 "Here is why we have to win the Presidency in '96," said Mr. Perot, whose political organization, renamed the National Reform Party, has won spots on the California, Ohio and Maine presidential ballots for next year. "Right now, this country is a patient on the table bleeding arterially, and we've got four broken fingers, and guess what? The doctor is the Republicans and the nurse is the Democrats, and they're fighting, and all they can agree to do is put a splint on those fingers."
The 500 people who attended the annual state conference of the New Jersey chapter of United We Stand America was a far smaller crowd than the nearly 3,000 people who jammed Mr. Perot's visit in 1993, a year after his presidential bid won 522,000 votes in the state, 14 percent of the total.
But if the crowd was smaller, the agenda at today's meeting was more complex than at Mr. Perot's other appearances in the state. In a bid to leapfrog the multiyear process of party-building in New Jersey, United We Stand has joined with the three-year-old New Jersey Conservative Party to field General Assembly candidates in 37 of the state's 40 legislative districts for next week's elections.
If they can win 10 percent of the General Assembly vote statewide, which party officials admit would be a stretch, the Conservatives would become the first official third party in New Jersey since World War II. That would create an automatic ballot spot for the new party, and for Mr. Perot's movement, in next year's presidential elections.
Some of the Conservative Party members were disappointed that Mr. Perot did not offer any money for a last-minute, get-out-the-vote effort on Nov. 7. But they were delighted to hear him bless the union of the two movements in his speech.
"I want every member of New Jersey United We Stand America to take a hard look at the Conservative Party effort here," he said. "They are fine, fine, first-class people, their principles are fine, first-class principles and our efforts are marching in lock step.
"But how are you going to get it done? You've got to get out on the streets, you've got to get on the phone, you've got to wake up all the people lying on the couch listening to Oprah and get them out to vote."
The leadership of the Conservative Party has agreed to consider changing its name to Mr. Perot's nascent Reform Party, which he has at different times called the American Independence Party, in time for next year's national voting.
Those attending today's event at the Hyatt Regency Hotel here reflected the coalition-building that has followed Mr. Perot into the state since last year. Besides the marriage to the state Conservative Party, United We Stand's pledge to change campaign spending and limit political fund raising has attracted support from two usually liberal groups, the New Jersey Public Interest Research Group and New Jersey Common Cause. But the campaign- reform effort, which is called the Citizens Agenda, is also embraced by the hard-right, antitax group Hands Across New Jersey, which helped defeat Jim Florio, the state's last Democratic governor, in 1993.
In addition to changes in campaign financing, the Conservative Party/United We Stand movement supports term limits for legislators, the adoption of new laws by referendum and the revocation of the North American Free Trade Agreement with Mexico and Canada, which the group blames for the loss of American jobs.

HEADLINE: Secessionist's Vision for Quebec Pleases the Crowd

BYLINE: By CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH 

DATELINE: MONTREAL, Oct. 28

 BODY:
He talks about the passport of a future independent Quebec as "a beautiful thing -- a symbol of what we are, the symbol of our people, the symbol of our state, the symbol of our identity -- and we'll be well received everywhere with a Quebec passport."
The cheers resound to the rafters. Hundreds of people, some of the younger ones with blue fleurs-de-lis painted on their faces, stomp their feet and mount a chorus of "Lucien, Lucien, Lucien." 
 Twelve hours a day, Lucien Bouchard, a truck driver's son from a remote Quebec village and the driving force of the campaign to pull the province out of Canada, plays to Quebec's pride and its sense of betrayal by English Canada.
Mr. Bouchard's appeals to Quebec's 4.8 million eligible voters -- of whom 82 percent are primarily French-speakers -- to back independence in a referendum on Monday could force Canada to confront the unthinkable: its own breakup. Quebec is Canada's largest province and its second most populous.
In the event of a separatist victory, Mr. Bouchard says, it could be up to a year before independence is proclaimed. He says he wants an economic union with the rest of Canada as part of an orderly transition, but threatens an immediate declaration of independence if Ottawa refuses to negotiate.
Prime Minister Jean Chretien has refused to be drawn out on how he would respond, but his options include a legal challenge and a second referendum to put the question of separation to all Canadians, not just Quebecers.
The separatist surge in the three weeks since Mr. Bouchard, the leader of the parliamentary Bloc Quebecois, took the reins has put victory within reach, alarming federalists and many of Canada's allies, and rattling Canadian financial markets.
The latest poll by Groupe Leger & Leger placed the contest in a dead heat. The poll, however, was conducted from last Monday to Thursday, just before an enormous rally on Friday brought tens of thousands of federalists from all over the country into the streets of Montreal.
While declaring a reluctance to interfere in the referendum, President Clinton has expressed puzzlement as to why such "a great model for the world" would want to break up.
Canadian federal officials have hailed the Americans' comments but the deputy premier of the pro-separatist Quebec Government, Bernard Landry, said bluntly that in the event of a victory for independence, the voters "will remember that the sovereignty of Quebec was achieved despite or even against the American will."
On Monday, prices on the Toronto Stock Exchange experienced their largest one-day drop in more than six years. The Canadian dollar has also weakened, forcing the central bank to raise interest rates, which could slow growth and increase mortgage rates for all Canadians.
"If you're going to lock in your mortgage, do it now," Mr. Chretien advised his countrymen last Monday.
Signs of capital flight were also manifest in the rush of inquiries to banks about transferring money out of Quebec or converting Quebec investments into cash.
The market reaction underscores one of the overriding issues of the battle. While Mr. Bouchard and his fellow separatists promote nationhood to preserve a distinct French cultural identity in a sea of English-speakers, federalists point to unacceptable costs, including higher taxes, increased unemployment and economic isolation should a split actually occur.
Separatists "ignore the costs of the new political structures," said Laurent Baudouin, chief executive of Bombardier Inc., a producer of planes, trains and skimobiles and one of Quebec's leading companies, "as well as the consequences of a devalued Quebec currency and the unavoidable rise in interest rates."
Fearful that market reactions could influence voters, the Quebec government ordered its financial institutions to buy hundreds of millions of Canadian dollars this week in an attempt to check the slide of the currency and reassure Quebecers.
Against the drumbeat of attacks on the economic front, Mr. Bouchard and his separatist followers promise that independence would shrivel deficits, restore pride, protect French culture and redress all wrongs.
Mr. Bouchard, who less than a year ago had his left leg amputated to check the advance of flesh-eating bacteria, took over the day-to-day management of the flagging campaign from the Quebec Premier, Jacques Parizeau, a former Quebec finance minister who lacks Mr. Bouchard's charisma.
On the campaign trail, Mr. Bouchard walks slowly with a cane, usually accompanied by his American-born wife, Audrey. Aides are steps away to shield him from over-enthusiastic crowds. When he speaks, he lays his cane aside and leans forward on the podium toward his audience, rarely consulting notes.
Although Mr. Bouchard advocates nationhood for Quebec, his notion of a newly negotiated political and economic partnership with Canada is at the heart of what has apparently proven to be a successful strategy. Many Quebecers -- including, pollsters say, about a third of those who would vote "yes" to sovereignty -- do not want a complete break with Canada but rather greater leverage for Quebec within the federation.
Working with Mr. Parizeau and Mario Dumont, leader of the fledgling Action Democratique du Quebec, an offshoot of the Quebec Liberal Party, Mr. Bouchard has outlined a plan for partnership that has little chance of being accepted by the rest of Canada but that seems to satisfy many Quebecers' desire for continued links.
He envisages, for example, negotiations with Ottawa to insure the free movement of people, goods, services, capital, money and labor and to establish such institutions as a partnership council, assembly and tribunal.
Federalist leaders denounce the plan, saying it is intended to confuse voters. They also warn there is no way Quebec would inherit membership of the North American Free Trade Agreement between United States, Canada and Mexico, as Mr. Bouchard and other separatists assert.
Since Confederation in 1867, one fifth of Canada's prime ministers have been Quebecers; a Quebecer has been Prime Minister in all but 3 of the past 27 years. And over the past quarter-century, the province has made great economic strides and solidified French as its working language. Yet this is the second time in 15 years that Quebec has voted on independence. Why?
Quebecers say that what they want most is a public acknowledgment of their distinctness within Canada to insure protection of their language and culture.
In a 1980 referendum, Quebec voted 60-40 to remain in Canada, but two years later, in constitutional changes, saw its provincial powers reduced and lost its coveted right to veto constitutional amendments.
Quebec sought redress through the "distinct society" clause of the 1987 Meech Lake agreement, which would have restored some of these powers. However, the agreement was not ratifed by all provinces by the 1990 deadline. So great was the sense of betrayal when the agreement fell through that polls taken in the immediate aftermath showed as many as 70 percent of Quebecers favoring independence.
Mr. Bouchard has deftly played into these resentments. "We will not be duped," he told a national television audience on Wednesday. "The broken promises from '80 and the odious blow in 1982 are too fresh in our memories."
Should Quebecers vote for independence on Monday a long period of uncertainty would be inevitable as Canadian leaders frantically try to buy time and calm and negotiate their way through uncharted waters.
"In many ways this is like our Civil War," said Robert W. Thacker, director of Canadian Studies at St. Lawrence University in Canton, N.Y. "There are provisions for admitting provinces into the federation, but none for the breaking up of the country."

HEADLINE: Redrawn Map Has Surprises For Nassau

BYLINE: By DOREEN CARVAJAL 

DATELINE: HEMPSTEAD, L.I., Oct. 27

 BODY:
With two beepers clipped to his belt, Lance D. Clark hunts for voters with a pocketful of plastic campaign pens, gently rattling picket gates to alert dogs and other household pets that a Republican is present.
He is striding through Democratic turf, a newly fashioned minority voting district, where -- in theory -- the party of Newt Gingrich is about as popular as speeding motorists and stray cats. But in fact, young black Republicans are running so well in Nassau County that the powerful Republican organization could utterly dominate the county's first legislature, which was fashioned to make government more open. 
 The political paradox is not lost on Democrats; it was black and Hispanic residents dissatisfied with the dominance of Republican and white candidates on the Board of Supervisors who brought a Federal lawsuit compelling Nassau County to scrap the board and create a legislature.
Campaigning for the Nov. 7 elections in the Second Legislative District, which includes Hempstead, Rockville Centre and Westbury, Mr. Clark, a lawyer, devotes little time to Republican ideology. He prefers to discuss more pressing issues: the proper height of a sidewalk curb, the replacement of diseased street trees.
In the neighboring First District, which covers largely black neighborhoods in Freeport, Roosevelt and Uniondale, the Republican candidate, Darlene Harris, campaigns on issues of job training and community revitalization.
When pressed about her Republican affiliation, she has a brief speech. "Frederick Douglass was a Republican," said Ms. Harris, 30, a lawyer whose father is a prominent black minister. "Booker T. Washington was a Republican and Abraham Lincoln was a Republican. The power structure in Nassau County happens to be Republican and I can work with them."
Nassau County has long been the base of one of the most powerful Republican Party organizations in the nation, producing New York's junior senator, Alfonse M. D'Amato, and a string of Republican county executives and other local officeholders, including the current County Executive, Thomas S. Gulotta.
The Board of Supervisors is made up of elected leaders of Nassau's three towns and two cities, who cast votes weighted to reflect their populations. But last year, after a court ruled that the board's voting system violated the constitutional principle of one person, one vote, Republicans and Democrats agreed to divide Nassau into 19 voting districts. The map makers envisioned a 12-to-7 split in seats, approximating the 3-to-2 advantage in Republican registration in the county. Two districts were fashioned so that minorities made up a majority.
But Democrats, who vigorously supported the new legislature, have already started to lower their expectations, with some saying the party will be grateful to win five seats on Nov. 7. And if they did win only five, Republicans would have a veto-proof majority.
"The notion that somehow a minority district is automatically going to go to the Democrats allowed a lot of people to be asleep at the switch," said Richard M. Kessel, former executive director of the State Consumer Protection Board, who began his career in Nassau Democratic politics in the 1970's as a gadfly and critic of the Board of Supervisors. "I certainly think the Republican Party is going to get the greater benefits from this."
Nowhere is this political boon more unexpected than the First Legislative District, where feuding Democrats could split the vote and permit Ms. Harris, the Republican, to win. So fractious is the campaign that the local chapter of the N.A.A.C.P. has requested help from the Justice Department to monitor the voting on Election Day.
Most Democrats had assumed that the district would support the Democratic organization's choice of Frederick Brewington, a young black civil rights lawyer who was considered a rising star in the Democratic Party. But in a three-candidate race, he lost the primary by a 54-vote margin to Seretta McKnight, an upstart who is president of the Roosevelt School Board.
Initially, Mr. Brewington tried to overturn the results, charging that more than 200 votes were tainted. He said he suspected Republicans of unfairly influencing the outcome of the election, particularly at the polling stations located on Roosevelt school property. There, for reasons still unclear, some Republicans were allowed to vote in the Democratic primary.
"The N.A.A.C.P. received telephone calls with regard to some irregularities, and we were the ones who brought this to the attention of the Brewington camp," said Robert Sumerville, president of the chapter and a former Roosevelt School Board member.
Mr. Brewington asked an appeals court judge to schedule a new primary, but when that tactic did not work, he started running under the banner of the Liberal Party.
From his vantage point as a newly minted independent candidate, Mr. Brewington watches with envy the support that Republicans offer to Ms. Harris. He complains that the Democrats never gave him any help in the primary, although he says they have promised him a judgeship if he ends his independent campaign.
"Just look at the plywood billboards she has, the posters in the windows," Mr. Brewington said. "The numbers are there in terms of money, but also the Republican structure and control."
His persistence has naturally irked Democratic Party leaders, who fear that the tarnished Democratic star has become the Republicans' secret weapon.
"Fred Brewington is being put up by the Republicans to act as a spoiler," said the Nassau County Democratic Chairman, Steve Sabbeth. "All of a sudden he's got finances now. These are people who will be excluded from the Democratic Party from now on. They are disloyal Democrats who are apparently acting in concert with the Republicans."
Not even Republicans are that nasty to the Democrats in the Second Legislative District, where the race between Lance Clark, the Republican, and Roger Corbin, the Democrat, is often characterized as a dogfight.
The odds should be against Mr. Clark. The district's registered Democrats outnumber Republicans by 13,500 to 9,000, and Mr. Clark is a longtime Republican who started as a party foot soldier and committeeman in the early 1980's.
But the Republicans have picked a candidate with mainstream appeal. A volunteer firefighter, Mr. Clark won a race for Deputy Mayor of the village of Hempstead under the Unity Party label. In that position, he cultivated a base of support by working regularly with local residents and community groups.
So when Mr. Clark started knocking on doors with his campaign pens and palm cards, residents associated his name with the bread and butter of government -- mundane calls about storm drains and new pavement, street-side trees and sidewalk curbs.
"I think he's going to run away with it," said Richard Burks, 39, after Mr. Clark had made a campaign stop on his porch in the Duncan Estates section of Hempstead. "He's extremely popular. If you have a complaint for the town, you get a call back from him the next day. If he can help you personally, he will."
While Mr. Clark makes his party affiliation seem as pleasant and unthreatening as a Hallmark greeting card, Mr. Corbin is not so saccharine when it comes to the G.O.P.
"Lance has approached this much like he was running for a nonpartisan village election," Mr. Corbin said. "He thought that once he got this nomination that this was going to be a breeze, but Lance Clark's party is the reason why we have the new county legislature. Their party has not represented our constituency in a rightful manner. Now he has to defend the party that houses Newt Gingrich, Governor Pataki and the all the rest of those knuckleheads destroying our country."
With just days until the election, the Republicans have already started to warm up their machine in the First and Second Districts. Lawn signs and bumper stickers have been distributed. The county Republicans will help with a districtwide mailing. Local committee members accompany the candidates, providing them with lists of residents likely to vote.
"We're spending some extra money," said Joseph Mondello, the Republican Party chairman, who declined to elaborate. "I think we're in very good shape in both races. I think we have a real opportunity. We're in a good position and we're poised and the organization is ready to work hard."

HEADLINE: PRESIDENT WARNS CONGRESS TO DROP SOME BUDGET CUTS

BYLINE: By TODD S. PURDUM 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 28

 BODY:
Hours after the Senate approved a sweeping budget bill, President Clinton today made his strongest warning yet to the Republican Congress, saying not only that he would veto the bill but also that "there is nothing for us to talk about" until money is restored for health, education and the environment.
By a vote of 52 to 47 shortly after midnight, the Senate approved the huge revenue and spending measure, which would cut taxes by $245 billion over seven years, sharply curb the growth of outlays for Medicare and Medicaid and turn over to the states broad new responsibility for caring for the poor. 
 Negotiators must now reconcile it, probably late next month, with a similar bill approved in the House by a vote of 227 to 203.
During a session of almost 15 hours beginning on Friday morning, the Senate adopted a number of proposals, demanded by moderate Republicans, that veered away from the more conservative House version.
But the President insisted today that he had no intention of negotiating a compromise until the Republicans moved his way, even if they tried to force his hand by refusing to extend the Government's borrowing authority so it could keep paying its bills during a stalemate -- a threat Mr. Clinton called "blackmail tactics."
"Hear this: Before or after a veto, I am not prepared to discuss the destruction of Medicare and Medicaid, the gutting of our commitment to education, the ravaging of our environment, or raising taxes on working people," Mr. Clinton said in his weekly radio address. "So I say to the Republican leaders: Back off your cuts in these vital areas. Until you do, there is nothing for us to talk about."
In the Republican response, Speaker Newt Gingrich warned Mr. Clinton to "think twice about vetoing the balanced budget and jeopardizing long-overdue revolutionary change" and said the President "promises to be a roadblock." The Senate majority leader, Bob Dole of Kansas, added, "America cannot afford to continue on this destructive course."
This morning's vote sets the stage for a new round of wrangling between Capitol Hill and the Administration that White House aides acknowledge could well result in a series of vetoes, not only of the budget package passed this week but also of some of the individual appropriations bills that accompany it, and a virtual stalemate through the end of the year. They say that is a risk Mr. Clinton is prepared to take to preserve what he can of his priorities and build a record for his re-election campaign next year.
Congressional Democrats have worried that Mr. Clinton, whose propensity for compromise is celebrated, will be too quick to bargain. His tough language today seemed intended to help allay those fears and to portray himself to the public as the true defender of middle-class interests and what he today called "national values."
"You say your principles are a balanced budget, a tax cut, extending the life of the Medicare trust fund," Mr. Clinton said, addressing the Republicans. "I want all those things. They are my principles too. But there are other important principles, the one that I have outlined. They are morally right for America, and they are good for our economy."
Repeating his refusal to let the Republicans pressure him into an agreement by holding up an extension of the Federal debt limit, which expires next month, Mr. Clinton warned, "I am not about to give in to that kind of blackmail."
"If they send me a budget bill that says simply, 'Take our cuts, or we'll let the country go into default,' I will still veto it," he added.
In the coming weeks, Republican negotiators from the House and Senate will not only have to iron out the differences between their bills but in the face of Mr. Clinton's veto threat might make accommodations to win White House support.
The Democrats' remaining strength in Congress virtually insures that Mr. Clinton's vetoes on budget bills will not be overridden. But what would happen after such a veto is far less clear. The consensus view in the White House is that a compromise will eventually be reached, but no one believes any longer that a compromise will happen soon.
"Don't assume that when Newt says this is all Kabuki theater that we're all just playing along to a compromise after a ritual veto," one White House aide said today. "We're sticking with these principles all the way through."
Both the President and the Republicans stand to lose in the end if the public perceives them as contributing to the Government gridlock that each, from different perspectives, has pledged to end.
Mr. Gingrich sought to make that argument in his response to the President's address: "Without a doubt, this week was a true crossroads for America's future. Down one road was the status quo, big government, rising deficits, high taxes, a bankrupt Medicare program and a destructive welfare system. Down the other road was historic change and a better future.
"This is the road Republicans chose for America," he added. "Unfortunately, President Clinton promises to be a roadblock."
Senator Dole sought to recast some of the issues on which Mr. Clinton criticized the bills, saying Republicans had "kept our promise to cut taxes so that you could keep more of your hard-earned money."
"And we kept our promise to preserve, protect and strengthen Medicare," he said.
On Friday morning, the Senate made a number of changes in the bill, voting to drop a provision that would allow corporations to withdraw billions of dollars in surplus assets from their pension funds, retain the Federal guarantee of Medicaid coverage for pregnant women, children under 12 and the disabled, and keep Federal regulations on nursing homes.
Making use of a Senate rule that prohibits amendments to the budget if they have no effect on the deficit, Democrats also forced the Republicans to strip from the bill 46 provisions, including several relating to welfare. Those proposals would have allowed states to deny cash assistance to children born to unmarried women under the age of 18 or to welfare mothers of any age who have additional children.
The White House was quick to praise those votes but just as quick to make clear that the Administration thought the bill was still too extreme in many particulars.
For months, the President has strived to walk a line between accommodating the reality of the new Republican majority in Congress and defending his own priorities. In an effort to keep the Republicans from seizing the moral high ground on balancing the budget by a fixed date, he dropped his previous reluctance to that idea early this summer and said he favored doing so in 10 years, instead of 7, as the Republicans wanted.
He has since suggested that improved economic forecasts now make that goal possible in 9 years or less, though he has resisted offering too many details of what spending cuts he would propose, lest the Republicans simply seize on his cuts and ignore the programs he wants to protect.
But wide differences remain, with little clear indication of just how either side might move. The Republicans propose cuts in taxes and in the growth in Medicare spending that are more than twice as deep as those Mr. Clinton wants and sharp restrictions in the Federal income tax credit for the working poor, which Mr. Clinton successfully expanded in 1993.
In recent weeks, the President has focused his attack on the Republican plans for curbing the growth of Medicare and Medicaid spending and proposals for overhauling welfare as cruel to children, even though an early analysis by his Administration showed that the Senate bill he supports could push more than a million additional children into poverty.
Because Mr. Clinton is so determined, on the advice of his new chief political strategist, Dick Morris, to portray himself as the one able to get things done, some of his own top aides have said they are worried that he will compromise too soon.
But today, several of them suggested that the President was now sharply aware that one of the greatest of Presidential powers is the power to say no and that the Republicans are equally aware that they cannot pass the sweeping agenda embodied in their Contract with America without Mr. Clinton's support.
The President himself embraced that notion in a telephone conference call with Democratic Congressional leaders on Friday.
"You know, this is one of those moments in our history when I'm grateful for the wisdom of our founding fathers," Mr. Clinton said then. "I mean, the Congress gets to propose, but the President has to sign or veto, and that Constitution gave me that authority and one of the reasons for the veto is to prevent excess."
He added: "They knew what they were doing, and we're going to use the Constitution they gave us to stand up for what's right."
"Just be of good cheer," Mr. Clinton told the House minority leader, Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri, in the conference call. "Just go out there and debate these things and tell them what we believe in, and it will all work out."


GRAPHIC: The main differences on the budget between President Clinton and Congress on the budget (dollar amounts are for a seven year period):
 
BALANCED BUDGET
 
Congress: Balanced budget after seven years using conservative economic forecasts.
 
Clinton: Balanced budget after nine years based on more favorable economic forecasts.
 
TAXES
 
Congress: Cut taxes by $245 billion with a $500 credit for most families with children, a lower capital gains rate and increased eligibility for I.R.A.'s.
 
Clinton: Cut taxes by $111 billion with fewer families eligible for a smaller per-child tax credit, no capital gains cut and similar provisions on I.R.A.'s. A deduction of up to $10,000 a year would be allowed for college tuition payments.
 
MEDICARE
 
Congress: Spend $240 billion less than under current law. Limit payments to doctors and hospitals, raise monthly premiums and offer inducements to beneficiaries who move into private insurance plans.
 
Clinton: Spend $124 billion less than current law. No specifics offered on how this money would be saved. Eliminate co-payments for mammograms.
 
MEDICAID
 
Congress: Turn program over to the states and spend about $170 billion less. House would eliminate most Federal standards for nursing homes.
 
Clinton: Retain Federal control, giving states more flexibility. Spend $55 billion less. Retain nursing homes standards.
 
WELFARE
 
Congress: End Federal guarantee of cash assistance to the poor. Allow each state to decide who is entitled to what benefits. Reduce Federal spending by about $100 billion. Require most recipients to work. Make most families ineligible for assistance after five years. Reduce spending for food stamps. House would deny welfare to unwed teen-age mothers and children born to mothers already on welfare. Senate would allow states to impose those restrictions.
 
Clinton: Support the general direction taken by Congress but insist that states be required to maintain a fixed level of spending on welfare, that more money be provided for child care and that mechanisms be strengthened to protect the poor in times of serious recession. Oppose cuts by Congress in foster care and child protective services, in benefits to legal immigrants and in assistance to disabled children.
 
EARNED-INCOME TAX CREDIT
 
Congress: Tighten eligibility to save between $23 billion (House) and $43 billion (Senate).
 
Clinton: Maintain existing program but crack down on fraud.
 
EDUCATION
 
Congress: House would save $10 billion in student loan costs by reducing assistance to students and parents, a proposal rejected by the Senate.
 
Clinton: Expand Federal aid to college students and increase spending for other education and job-training programs.
 
ENVIRONMENT
 
Congress: Open the Arctic National Wildlife Refuge to oil and gas exploration and take a variety of other steps opposed by environmentalists involving national parks, the Endangered Species Act, cattle grazing on Federal land and other matters.
 
Clinton: Maintain most existing laws and regulations on the environment. Mr. Clinton has said he would veto the entire legislation if it included the Congressional provision on the Arctic refuge.

HEADLINE: Easing Toward an Election in Philadelphia

BYLINE: By MICHAEL JANOFSKY 

DATELINE: PHILADELPHIA, Oct. 27

 BODY:
Mayor Edward G. Rendell has a zillion stories he likes to tell that reflect the changes in Philadelphia during his first term in office. One of his favorites:
Last year, Conde Nast Traveler magazine set out to find the friendliest of 10 major American cities. Among 12 test questions, residents in the cities were asked to change $1, police officers were asked to take a picture, shopkeepers were asked for a Yellow Pages directory and bartenders were asked for a glass of water. 
 "With one test to go, Philadelphia and San Francisco were tied," Mr. Rendell said, recounting the story for public school food-service workers at a state convention here this week. "The last task was to go up to a cabdriver and say: 'My car is broken down and I need some jumper cables. Can you help me?' "
All the cabdrivers in San Francisco refused to help, and so did all but one in Philadelphia. "The last one said yes," the Mayor said, beaming, and that's how Philadelphia won the title.
Given the time, Mr. Rendell could spend another four-year term spinning out ways to show Philadelphia as a city that feels good about itself, and judging from the changes since he won election in 1991 and the popularity he has achieved as a result, he will probably get it.
While Philadelphia is just one of 40 big cities with a mayoral election on Nov. 7, few candidates or incumbents anywhere are expected to win with the ease predicted for Mr. Rendell.
"It's not so much if he'll win, but by what margin," said Randall Miller, a political science professor at St. Joseph's University here. "This is a two-term guy. The only question is, Where does he go after this?"
A Democrat and former district attorney, Mr. Rendell, 51, is opposed by Joseph Rocks, a Republican former State Senator, and by two minor-party candidates. The son of a firefighter, Mr. Rocks, 48, has been endorsed by police and firefighter unions, but has not appeared to excite enough voters to bring about an upset even with the promise of a big wage-tax cut, the major thrust of his campaign. Democrats have held City Hall for 44 consecutive years.
Independent polls show Mr. Rendell far ahead, and much of the reason is the enormous energy and enthusiasm he brings to the job -- even when he is not campaigning. A natural schmoozer, he thrives on dashing about the city, visiting neighborhood groups, giving awards and making speeches. After an hourlong candidates' debate on Thursday night, he made three more stops before calling it a night, close to 11 o'clock. On weekends, his schedule includes as many as 20 events a day.
Not even Mr. Rocks has been immune to Rendell fever, acknowledging that last year, before he was a candidate, he asked Mr. Rendell for a job as a lobbyist.
But unlike big-city mayors who shake hands and kiss babies for show, Mr. Rendell has much to be proud of. During his administration, the city has raised its bond rating from junk status to investment grade, the last years have produced budget surpluses with no layoffs or tax increases, downtown is flush with new projects, libraries are open six days a week for the first time in memory, and voters say they have noticed a new spirit all about.
"There was always so much negative about Philadelphia," said Kathy Middleton, 39, a food-service worker who has lived in Philadelphia all her life. "You always saw things going down. There was no new life. But now, it's wonderful. He has brought the city back to life."
Philadelphia is not without its problems. Federal budget cuts could cost the city as much as $100 million by 1997, Mr. Rendell said, if the final legislation resembles the bill passed by the House this week. Also, police officials are investigating corruption in the department; the city's population, now 1.6 million, continues to shrink, and municipal workers, particularly police, are grousing because their salaries have remained stagnant for years.
In addition, Mr. Rocks has attacked Mr. Rendell with the criticism that he has focused too much money and energy on downtown commercial and institutional projects at the expense of neighborhood needs, an accusation Mr. Rendell dismisses. And some voters in low-income neighborhoods say Mr. Rendell ignores them.
"We have a new Marriott hotel," he said. "Who do you think works there? People from the neighborhoods, that's who.
"But you can't do everything," he said. "Look, it's what you expect in a campaign. If I found a cure for cancer, they'd criticize me for not finding a cure for AIDS."
The campaign has produced few fireworks. But even if Mr. Rendell wins with the large margin expected, he still faces some political risk if, as many voters believe, he has designs on running for governor or the United States Senate after his second-term as Mayor. Mr. Miller, the political science professor, said Mr. Rendell would need a big turnout and a big victory to help maintain his viability.
But both Mr. Rendell and his chief of staff, David Cohen, deny that the Mayor has made any plans beyond the next four years. Mr. Cohen was not even certain his boss would be suited for higher office.
"He only cares about one thing," Mr. Cohen said. "So for him, he's already found the perfect job."

SECTION: Section 1; Page 26; Column 1; National Desk 

LENGTH: 1436 words

HEADLINE: It's Not the Most Conventional Run, But Specter Asserts He's in It to Win

BYLINE: By ELIZABETH KOLBERT 

DATELINE: DES MOINES, Oct. 23

 BODY:
Senator Arlen Specter had just finished meeting with the editorial board of The Des Moines Register when, in the newspaper's lobby, he spied a young man who looked as if he might be an authentic Iowa voter. Naturally the Senator introduced himself. The young man responded by rolling his eyes.
"If it isn't Mr. Single-Bullet Theory himself," he said.
The young man ambled off, still smirking, and the Senator turned to a reporter. 
 "How would you like to not have a single day go by," he said, "without being asked about the single-bullet theory or Anita Hill?"
Mr. Specter, 65, has spent the better part of his life as a public figure, first as Philadelphia District Attorney, then as Senator from Pennsylvania and now as a candidate for the Republican Presidential nomination. But out of that long career, just two events seem to stand out in the public consciousness: first, as a young lawyer for the Warren Commission, his championing of the theory that a single bullet, fired by a gunman acting alone, killed President John F. Kennedy, and second, his fierce questioning of Professor Hill, who had accused Clarence Thomas of sexual harassment, during Senate hearings on Judge Thomas's nomination to the Supreme Court.
In the aftermath of the Thomas hearings, Mr. Specter, one of the Republican Party's most committed defenders of abortion rights and a longtime ally of women's organizations, seemed fated to go down in history as a monster of male insensitivity. Indeed, some political analysts have gone so far as to suggest that his campaign for President is less about getting elected than about altering the image he leaves to posterity.
Certainly Mr. Specter is a long shot for the Republican nomination, as he freely acknowledges. But while other candidates might be discouraged by the kind of showing he has made so far, he himself seems to derive a perverse satisfaction from demonstrating just how unpopular he is willing to be in clinging to his own principles.
For instance, while most of his rivals either call themselves opponents of abortion or have tried to shade their positions to appeal to crucial anti-abortion voters in the Republican primary elections, Mr. Specter has made his support for abortion rights the centerpiece of his campaign. And while the other candidates seem to have embraced the conventional wisdom that no Republican candidate can win the nomination without the help of the Christian right, Mr. Specter spends much of his time denouncing the movement as a destructive "fringe."
"If they control the party, they're going to lead us to certain disaster," he said in an interview during a flight on a chartered jet from Philadelphia to Des Moines. "Not possible disaster, not likely disaster, but certain disaster."
Still, Mr. Specter insists that he is in the race to win. Strategically, his campaign is built on the premise that there are enough abortion-rights Republicans that in a 10-person field, they could give one candidate -- him -- enough votes to be competitive.
In theory, at least, that is a plausible enough premise; after all, everyone's favorite noncandidate, Gen. Colin L. Powell, also favors a right to abortion. But Gov. Pete Wilson of California, another pro-choice candidate, recently dropped out of the race for lack lof support. And Senator Specter, short of cash and near the bottom of most polls, acknowledges that he too has yet to mobilize the constituency he maintains is out there.
The first Jew to seek the Republican nomination, Mr. Specter says he believes that the country is ready for a Jewish President. And if it is not, he quickly adds, "it should be." At the same time, though, he says he believes that his religion hurts.
"I think that there continues to be a lot of anti-Semitism in America -- not as much as there is racism, but still a fair amount," he said. "And I think being Jewish costs votes. Whether it's 5 percent or 8 percent or 11 percent, I don't know."
Mr. Specter is frequently classified as a member of an endangered species, "moderate Republican." But he dislikes the term, saying it has acquired too many negative connotations.
Instead, he prefers to call himself a "fiscal, economic conservative and a social libertarian." In contrast to most of his fellow Republican candidates, who invoke as their inspiration Ronald Reagan, Mr. Specter frequently compares himself to Barry Goldwater -- a curious choice, given the dismal outcome of Mr. Goldwater's own Presidential campaign after he won the Republican nomination in 1964.
"I think my views are really the conservative views of Barry Goldwater: get the government off our backs, out of our pocketbooks and out of our bedrooms," he said.
"The government is most intrusive when it gets into people's personal lives," he continued. "The true conservative is not Pat Buchanan, who wants to bring the government into everything. The true conservative is my view: the least government is the best government."
Although he has a reputation for abrasiveness, Mr. Specter endures the humiliations of the campaign trail with relatively good humor. He shrugs off sarcastic comments about the single-bullet theory, which he still actively defends, and even managed a wan smile when a student at a West Des Moines high school offered to sell him a tape of heavy-metal rock for $15. (He declined.)
In his campaign appearances, Mr. Specter stresses not only his stance on abortion but also his support for a flat tax to replace the graduated income tax. Wherever he goes, he carries around the postcard-sized form that he says is all a taxpayer would need to fill out if a flat tax were adopted. Under his plan, all of an individual's investment earnings would be exempt from taxation. All remaining income would be taxed at the constant rate of 20 percent, with deductions allowed only for interest on mortgages (up to $100,000) and for charitable contributions (up to $2,500).
On the trail, Mr. Specter is often asked about Professor Hill, and every time he gives the same obviously rehearsed but still heartfelt-sounding answer.
"The Hill-Thomas hearings were a learning experience for me, and I think really a learning experience for the country," Mr. Specter said in response to one recent day's first question on the topic. "I personally had no idea how much sexual harassment there was until after those hearings were over and practically every woman I met told me about the time she was sexually harassed and told me how painful those hearings were, almost as if they saw themselves in Anita Hill's position."
As for Justice Thomas, Mr. Specter said he was disappointed with the performance so far, judging the Justice little more than "a clone" of Antonin Scalia, the Supreme Court's most conservative member. But, he added, "I still think there's hope."
Unlike most Presidential candidates, who rely on finance chairmen with fat Rolodexes, Mr. Specter is financing his campaign largely through direct mail solicitations, singling out groups like Jewish and abortion-rights voters. His campaign chairman, Roger Stone, a veteran Republican strategist, maintains that the Senator will have "enough money to compete" in the early caucuses and primaries. But the campaign shows a net debt of almost $500,000 at present and should receive only $1 million or so in Federal matching funds early next year. In contrast, Senator Bob Dole's campaign has $6 million in the bank and expects at least $7 million in matching funds.
Because of his lack of money and of organizational support, Mr. Specter has adopted some unorthodox tactics for the early contests. Here in Iowa, for example, which will hold its caucuses on Feb. 12, he has been trying to enlist the support of labor unions, not an obvious constituency for a Republican candidate.
Mr. Specter estimates that one-quarter to one-third of the state's union members are Republicans and says that of all the Republican candidates, he has the record on labor issues that should be the most attractive to them. "I'm the only one who could walk into a union hall," he said, "and walk out alive."
Mr. Specter concedes that any Republican candidate relying on the support of labor unions will have to get pretty lucky to win the nomination. But, he maintains, "a lot of unexpected things have happened in this campaign."
"I can tell you this," he went on. "If I weren't in the race now, I'd really be kicking myself because of all the events that have occurred which leave me as the only candidate not off the board to the right. Everybody else is off the board to the right."

(Associated Press for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Murder a Growth Industry In Poor, Reeling Camden

BYLINE: By JON NORDHEIMER 

DATELINE: CAMDEN, N.J., Oct. 27

 BODY:
Clarence Jones, who had a record as a small-time drug dealer, was shot to death on Sunday. The police said the drive-by shooting was probably an act of revenge or a territorial grab by other drug dealers. His killing would be unremarkable by urban standards except that it was the 48th homicide here this year.
Early next month, at the current murder rate, the highest in New Jersey, the city will surpass its record of 51 slayings, set in 1992. Camden's homicide rate so far this year is nearly twice that of Newark. 
 It is another blow for a poor city of 87,000 across the Delaware River from Philadelphia that has known many defeats.
The latest rebuff came this week when the city's call for state troopers to help contain its soaring murder rate was turned down by state officials who wanted to know why the local 341-member police force could not handle the problem.
A task force of state troopers patrolled with city police for several months in 1993 after the previous record-setting outbreak of murders.
The State Attorney General, Deborah T. Poritz, after conferring Thursday with Bob Pugh, Camden's Police Chief, said that, before any troopers were dispatched to Camden this time, it needed to be understood why the city force was not more effective.
Getting that answer will take many weeks of study by outside experts. On Camden's dreary streets, where men lounge on street corners and young mothers push baby carriages past burned-out row houses, there were quicker judgments about the nature of the problem.
"Long as there's no jobs, people going to deal drugs," said Lonnie Watkins, 33, carrying a bag of groceries on Broadway in South Camden. "And if that's how you make your living, then you protect your business any way you can, know what I mean?" Mr. Watkins freed one hand from his grocery bag, cocked his thumb and took aim with a forefinger. "Ker-POW!" he said, and blew imaginary smoke from the end of his finger.
People used to measure prosperity here by the number of tomatoes that spilled from the trucks carrying produce to the Campbell Soup factory by the riverfront. But the factory closed several years ago, and while Campbell still maintains a white-collar force at its Camden headquarters, it no longer employs local workers in food production, and plans for a new 20-story corporate building have been shelved.
What jobs there were for Camden residents in the Philadelphia Navy Yard were lost with the recent closing of that base. Other fallout from the end of the Cold War and cutbacks in the space program eliminated aerospace and electronics plants that once employed thousands locally, leaving Camden the fifth-poorest city in the nation.
Unemployment hovers around 20 percent; two-thirds of the population receives public assistance, and more than half of the community is less than 25 years old. Middle-class white families long ago moved to suburbs like Cherry Hill, and black, Hispanic and Asian people now make up the great majority of the population. The flat-roofed brick row houses the whites left behind now house people mired in unemployment and the social ills that accompany economic decline.
"The difficulty with Camden is, people who can make it get away, and those who can't come here," said Monsignor Michael Doyle, an Irish-born priest at Sacred Heart Roman Catholic Church who has worked with the poor of South Camden for three decades.
New civic ornaments on the riverfront like the Thomas H. Kean state aquarium and a Blockbuster-Sony music entertainment center are attractive but offer a limited number of unskilled jobs "parking cars and picking up litter," he said.
Across town, Rosa Ramirez takes on the daunting task of driving the drug dealers and prostitutes from street corners across from St. Joseph's parish school, where she works as a secretary and lunch program director, and from locations a few blocks down Federal Street where Woodrow Wilson High School stands.
Ms. Ramirez, 53, is president of Camden Churches Organized for People, the city's largest grass-roots organization. On Sundays after Mass, hundreds of members drawn from more than 3,000 Spanish-speaking, black and Vietnamese families hold prayer vigils at what she describes as "hot corners," attempting to galvanize others in the neighborhoods to present a counterforce to the criminal elements.
"Two years ago, the people would not speak out about what was going on in their street," she said. "Now, they are more willing to talk to the police because they know sooner or later they or their children are going to get killed right in front of their homes." A drug ring called the Sons of Malcolm X was broken up last year and the streets became safer, she said, but the gang was soon replaced by others who fought murderously for the territory.
The police say they are doing the best they can with the resources and manpower available to them. In the 1970's, when the crime rate and murder rate were lower, the police department received Federal funds to add 30 percent more officers than are available today, said Inspector Albert Handy, a 28-year veteran of the Camden police force.
What makes selling drugs so profitable, and worth the danger to some, are steady streams of visitors from the suburbs who drive to Camden to buy marijuana, crack cocaine and, increasingly, heroin, said Lieut. Thomas Olson, head of the Police Department's criminal investigative division.
On Thursday evening, the two officers met in the fortress-like police headquarters with other city and community leaders to prepare for "Mischief Night," the Halloween observance that every year brings a rash of arsons and shootings. After hearing of the preparations, Ms. Ramirez said, "In Camden, every night is Mischief Night."

GRAPHIC: Photo: A debris-strewn lot in Camden, where joblessness is 20 percent and the murder rate, about double Newark's and New Jersey's highest, is rising. (Laura Pedrick for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Haitian-Americans Work to Rebuild Their Newly Democratic Homeland

BYLINE: By LYNETTE HOLLOWAY 

 BODY:
Max Delices's voice floats over the airwaves of a Haitian-American radio station in Brooklyn, painting enticing pictures of a Haiti that he urges listeners to go and see for themselves.
Roger Etienne, an accountant in East Flatbush, said he plans to help open trade centers in Haiti's jagged foothills to train high school dropouts to be paramedics, paralegals and word processors so they can work in "the Haiti of tomorrow." 
 And Dr. Jean Claude Compas ships donations of needles, bandages and intravenous bags from his Brooklyn office to Haiti's poorly equipped hospitals.
The three Brooklyn men are among scores of middle-class or affluent Haitian-Americans in New York City who are helping to rebuild their country after the restoration of democracy last year.
While few of the immigrants are moving back permanently, many are making frequent or long visits -- either to take advantage of new business opportunities or to offer humanitarian aid. Others simply wish to once again set foot on the soil they were forced to leave many years ago. They say that years of living abroad have only sharpened their passion for Haiti.
The way for return was cleared when President Jean-Bertrand Aristide was restored to power a year ago under the watch of United States military forces.
The country can already feel the effects of support from abroad; the money Haitian-Americans send to their families has increased by 10 percent to about $40 million a year, said Smarck Michel, who resigned earlier this month as Haiti's Prime Minister.
Many of the exiles equate the task of rebuilding in Haiti with development by blacks in South Africa. They say they hope to mirror what Jews from abroad have done to help Israel.
"In New York, the Jewish people helped rebuild Israel," said Mr. Delices, 36, a self-employed financial consultant in East Flatbush and a host on Radio Soleil. The station's signals are piggybacked on other radio stations' frequencies; radios must have special receivers or computer chips to receive the programs.
"One of the things they did was to set up a fund," Mr. Delices said. "It's brilliant. We should be doing that for Haiti to help build bridges, roads and hospitals." The current building boom in Haiti has caused a shortage of masons and electricians, Mr. Michel said.
Mr. Aristide's return ended the era of tyranny that began when the Duvalier family dictatorship took power in the 1950's. Haitians were taxed and robbed by successive governments that provided little in the way of roads, hospitals, schools and even potable water.
The Haitians who fled have settled largely in New York, Miami and Boston. Some 500,000 people in the New York metropolitan area are of Haitian descent, with enclaves in East Flatbush and Crown Heights, Brooklyn.
Mr. Delices arrived in East Flatbush 14 years ago after his oldest brother helped pack his suitcases and ushered him to a plane. The Port-au-Prince native was an outspoken radio broadcaster who blasted the Duvalier regime; his family feared his days were numbered. He stayed away until May 1995, when he returned to witness democracy in action, he said.
"If I hadn't left, I would be history by now," he said. "Now, it's time to go to do something for my country. It's free and open. You can say what you want and no one will punish you."
As a consultant for Haitian-owned travel agencies in Brooklyn, he prods owners to sell Haiti to customers. On his radio talk show, he discusses ways to revitalize the country and applauds Mr. Aristide's hotly contested move toward privatizing governmental services.
"We should go back and spend money to revive our home," he said. "I tell them: 'Hey, Haiti is open. You can dream about it, or make your dream come true.' "
Mr. Etienne, 56, also supports privatization, but his concerns revolve around education rather than business. At least two-thirds of the population is illiterate and less than 5 percent of the country's youths are enrolled in high schools.
He is a member of the Haitian Affairs Committee, a nonprofit group of professionals based in East Flatbush, which had worked vigorously to reinstate Mr. Aristide, but is now focused on education.
The group is busy trying to help find funding for trade schools that would provide classroom and on-the-job training for young adults in the countryside.
Born in the northern city of Cap-Haitien, Mr. Etienne left Haiti in 1968. "A lot of young people left because the situation was unbearable," he said. "There was no freedom. If you opened your mouth, you could have been shot on sight. The Duvalier law said: 'Any Communist thought in public or private was punishable by death.' Of course comments like getting food to the people was thought to be Communist."
The next time he went back was in 1986 after the last Duvalier was overthrown. He returned in 1990 when Mr. Aristide was elected.
"I was there," he said. "I saw the lines forming at 4 A.M. as people waited for the ballots to come. It was so important to the people that peasant women went to have their hair done so they could look nice. And if they had one good dress, it was ready after being laid out overnight."
Dr. Compas, a family practitioner in Crown Heights, speaks of his homeland with the same pride. Shortly after Mr. Aristide's return last year, Dr. Compas opened a medical office in Port-au-Prince, the capital, near the National Palace. He and other Haitian-American doctors, frequently sponsor medical and supply drives. He flies to Haiti once a month.
"There is a lot of technology that we don't have," Dr. Compas said. In Haiti, there are only 3 doctors per 100,000 people. But the doctors are concentrated in the capital, and even there, equipment and medicine are limited.
"At my clinic, I want to try to provide services that we don't have," Dr. Compas said, "but I don't want to be in competition with other Haitian doctors."
Dr. Compas left Haiti in his 20's to attend medical school in Lille, France, because his country lacked opportunities.
"My country is very important to me," he said. "I want to do what I can to make it grow."
With the presidential election scheduled for December, many Haitian-Americans are eager to spend Christmas in Haiti to relish the last days of Mr. Aristide, who is barred by the Constitution from succeeding himself.
American Airlines has added flights to Haiti from New York since Mr. Aristide's return. Flights in late December have been booked solid for months, the airline said.
Mr. Etienne remembers the "loving and festive" Christmases of his childhood. "If you heard caroling outside someone's house -- even if they didn't know you -- they would invite you in and offer you a soda.
"The very first Christmas I spent away from home I was sad, and every year I always say I'm dreaming of a green Christmas."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Scores of Haitian-Americans are pitching in to help rebuild their native country. At a Port-au-Prince hospital, Dr. Jean Claude Compas of Brooklyn examining a 10-year-old who had hepatitis and typhoid. (Reuters)

HEADLINE: Ailing Czar, Fragile State

BYLINE: By STEVEN ERLANGER 

DATELINE: MOSCOW, Oct. 28

 BODY:
The official secrecy and vagueness surrounding the condition of President Boris N. Yeltsin, hospitalized since Thursday with a second attack of heart disease, are beginning to have the usual counterproductive effects here.
For all of Russia's semi-democratic trappings, Mr. Yeltsin has followed Russian history in keeping the presidency a regal office, and everyone knows the czar is very ill. No one suggests he is about to die, but his chances of running for, let alone winning, a second term in June are growing slimmer. 
 As his loyal acolytes and retainers are seeing their hero and meal ticket falter, nerves are jangling, and assurances that all is well sound increasingly hollow. Big decisions cannot be made, whether about Bosnia or political strategy before December's parliamentary elections.
In sharp contrast to the American reaction to presidential illness -- charts and graphs and medical briefings -- Russia preserves a monarchic secrecy. There has been no photograph of Mr. Yeltsin, no television, no statements from his doctors except through official spokesmen.
For the third day, Mr. Yeltsin was allowed no non-family visitors. But clumsy efforts at reassurance continue, with claims that Government work is unaffected. The official Rossiskaya Gazeta said today that "the President will recover soon." The President's official paper, Rossikiye Vesti, asserted that "Boris Yeltsin's condition does not cause anxiety."
But a politician can survive only one heart attack, says Sergei Markov of the Carnegie Endowment for International Peace. "Who will vote for a sick man?" he asked. "Once, maybe, it could be explained away, but not twice."
Mr. Yeltsin's period of rule seems to be drawing to an end, with all its drama and accomplishment, but with the transition toward a new, democratic, market-oriented Russia inevitably incomplete. The system is still fragile, and moods about domestic and foreign policies are increasingly somber, defensive and isolationist. Even as the economy is finally stabilizing, Communists and ultranationalists who promise to undue some of Mr. Yeltsin's reforms are expected to do well in the elections scheduled for Dec. 17.
Mr. Yeltsin vowed to protect the essence of Russia's reforms against any resurgence of Communists and ultranationalists. Under the current Constitution, with nearly undefeatable veto powers, he could. But a different President -- Aleksandr Lebed, say, or Prime Minister Viktor S. Chernomyrdin -- would make different compromises with those unhappy about the pace and course of stop-and-go reforms.
Current opinion polls show that the Communists and the ultranationalist parties should do better in December. But they are still not likely to win a working majority in a new lower house, let alone command the two-thirds majority needed to override vetoes. And there is little expectation, judging from the last Parliament and other regional assemblies, that the Communists and ultranationalists can find common ground.
But the sense of Mr. Yeltsin as a bulwark against revisionism has been shaken. Questions are again being raised about whether he will allow himself the revolutionary act of voluntary retirement, or whether he will pursue the czarist and Communist model of trying to die in office, as the safest place to hide.
Some, like Mikhail Leontyev of the newspaper Sevodnya, think elections could be canceled and a state of emergency declared. Those around Mr. Yeltsin will seek to preserve him, and thus themselves, in power for as long as he draws breath, Mr. Leontyev suggests, saying: "Those in the executive branch are afraid of ending up in a jail cell."
As important, they are afraid of losing their offices, dachas, cars and business opportunities. That is why Mr. Yeltsin's aides had moved against Mr. Chernomyrdin, who appeared the natural, even anointed, successor after Mr. Yeltsin's first hospitalization in July.
But Mr. Yeltsin's new frailty casts the dice again. Russians afraid of instability may withhold their votes from Communists and ultranationalists in December and may look more favorably on the steady Mr. Chernomyrdin in June, rather than risk an untested figure as President.
Yeltsin aides, however, may move to Yuri Skokov, a quiet bureaucrat with good military and intelligence connections who nearly became Prime Minister in December 1992 instead of Mr. Chernomyrdin.
Now allied to Mr. Lebed, Mr. Skokov has promised Mr. Yeltsin and his aides security under a new presidency. Some Yeltsin aides suggested before his illness that Mr. Yeltsin might dump Mr. Chernomyrdin as Prime Minister for Mr. Skokov after December, in an effort to weaken the presidential bid of Mr. Lebed.
All these calculations are not idle. Whenever the czar weakens, everyone looks first to themselves.

HEADLINE: Syria Raises an Increasingly Lonely Voice Against Israeli Power

BYLINE: By DOUGLAS JEHL 

DATELINE: DAMASCUS, Syria

 BODY:
The Syrian Government is voicing new frustration at what it calls signs that Israel is being allowed to forge an increasingly strong role in the Middle East.
As other Arab delegations prepare to join Israeli officials at an American-sponsored economic conference in Amman, Jordan, the Damascus Government has decided to stay away, and it is pointing to the gathering to sound new alarms at what it regards as others' capitulation. 
 "There is no way to compel Syria to abandon her rights and dignity because if she did that, she would be like the one who places a bomb that permanently threatens the security of the region," Vice President Abdul Halim Khaddam warned in a recent address in Colombia.
After 47 years formally in a state of war, Syria remains firm in insisting that peace requires a complete Israeli withdrawal from occupied territory in the Golan Heights. But now that the Palestine Liberation Organization and Jordan have concluded accords with Israel, Damascus is portraying Israel as a dangerous beneficiary of a peace that is deeply flawed.
In announcing Syria's boycott of the economic-development conference, which is to open on Oct. 29, officials here have portrayed it as bound to serve Israeli interests.
And with Jordan, the P.L.O. and others plainly eager for the international aid from the Middle East Development Bank that the United States hopes the conference will establish, a Government newspaper used a recent front-page editorial to scorn the projects under review as likely only to "establish the so-called new Middle East under Israeli leadership."
Another fresh point of Syrian discontent lies in southern Lebanon, where requests from Washington and Jerusalem that Syria rein in the Party of God guerrillas responsible for the recent deaths of nine Israeli soldiers have been met with resentment.
As Syria sees it, those guerrillas are fighting a legitimate war of national resistance against Israeli occupiers. That vision, once a commonplace in the Arab world, is among the many that Syria, a powerful but increasingly lonely voice, warns are being trampled in the rush to ratify Middle East peace.
"I think it's fair to say that the Syrians are in a particularly sour mood," a Western diplomat said.
The sense of Syrian frustration has increased since June, when the abrupt collapse of the latest round of peace talks with Israel left Damascus to confront the first in a series of unwanted turns.
Syrian officials have said their side has been unduly blamed for the breakdown of the discussions, which were adjourned after two days by Syrian negotiators. They have said an Israeli proposal then to maintain ground monitoring stations in the Golan Heights so contradicted Syria's longtime insistence on total Israeli withdrawal that the two sides had nothing left to talk about.
Since then, Syria has had to watch as Washington embraced the two Arab leaders it regards as most misguided: King Hussein of Jordan and Yasir Arafat, the Palestinian leader.
In its response to the American and Israeli criticism over the Party of God attacks against Israeli soldiers in southern Lebanon, Syria has been more muted. But its official newspapers have suggested that those who have called on Syria to restrain the guerrilla strikes have swung too far toward an Israeli point of view.
"Operations against occupation are a kind of self-defense, no matter what the U.S. State Department thinks or says," The Syrian Times said in an editorial.

HEADLINE: WORLD SERIES '95: NOTEBOOK;
Players and Owners Take Lesson In Geography of Labor Problems

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS 

DATELINE: ATLANTA, Oct. 28

 BODY:
Fort Lauderdale and Homestead don't quite live up to Munich and Potsdam in the context of historical significance, where agreements affecting the world's powers took on the names of geographical locations.
But Fort Lauderdale and Homestead, cities in southeast Florida, are locations that have given their names to developments in major league baseball's long-running labor dispute. 
 Fort Lauderdale was the site of a meeting in January 1994, where the owners adopted a revenue sharing plan for the first time. Throughout the eight-month strike, when labor representatives of the owners and players talked about revenue sharing, they referred to "Fort Lauderdale" instead of a revenue sharing plan, as when the players said they would accept Fort Lauderdale.
Drive south from Fort Lauderdale, past Miami, and Homestead comes into view. No agreement was reached there, but when people in baseball say "Homestead," they mean the spring training camp operated there last spring by the players association for unemployed players.
"Homestead" is being used more and more these days as the free-agent period draws near. Players eligible for free agency, in fact, can begin filing tomorrow. But Homestead, as uttered by club officials, has a nasty connotation for players who plan to become free agents willingly or who will be set free by their clubs.
Homestead, in the view of one player agent, is a buzzword meant to intimidate players and agents, as in threatening them with unemployment. But, as one agent said: "They don't need any extra intimidation. Reality is enough."
There will be no Homestead next spring, at least not literally. Perhaps there will be figuratively, in the sense that many players could still be looking for jobs when spring training begins in February. But the union has no plans or reasons to duplicate the fine camp it ran last April.
The union created Homestead because most free agents had not found jobs by the time signed players were heading for spring training after the end of the strike. But the main reason so many players remained unsigned was the double signing freeze that existed through the winter.
First, the Players Association told players not to sign contracts because the clubs had changed the contracts and working rules unilaterally. Then the owners' labor relations committee placed a freeze on signings by delegating individual signing authority to itself.
With no work stoppage or signing freeze hampering operations this signing season, signings should proceed at a quicker pace. That is not to say all free agents will find jobs by the end of the year, or even by the start of spring training.
With the usual stars excepted, the coming signing season will be a buyer's market. Clubs needing a backup second baseman will have 8 or 10 or 12 players to choose from. If one doesn't want a $250,000 salary, four or five others will grab it.
That is the reality the agent referred to, and it will be the overriding economic factor at work in this free-agent market. Stars pursued by a collection of clubs will fare all right financially, but a growing number of players in the middle class will find it difficult to get jobs or salaries commensurate with what they believe their talents are.
The clubs have wanted a payroll cap or tax to curb their spending because they have never learned how to do it themselves. The heavy economic losses they have suffered, the half-billion dollars in lost revenue they have incurred this year, give the clubs a ready-made reason not to spend the usual amounts of money on players' salaries.
The clubs should be able to reduce their payrolls easily on that basis that the new economics should obviate the need for a cap or tax.
The market and the state of the baseball economy, though, will produce another development. So many players will be available that clubs could find enough players to transform themselves from a losing team to a winning team.
 
Free-Agent Potential
 These players would make up a pretty good All-Star team:
Fred McGriff at first base, Roberto Alomar or Craig Biggio at second, Shawon Dunston at shortstop, Ken Caminiti at third, Mike Stanley catching, Jose Canseco the designated hitter and Dante Bichette and Ron Gant flanking Devon White in the outfield.
Add starting pitchers Jack McDowell, Kenny Rogers, Ramon Martinez, Chuck Finley, Jim Abbott, Ken Hill and Andy Benes and a relief corps headed by Jeff Montgomery and Tom Henke.
Those are the more attractive players who are eligible to file for free agency beginning tomorrow. Other equally talented players could join them if their clubs don't exercise options in their contracts for 1996 or offer salary arbitration in the next five days.
The Seattle Mariners must let Edgar Martinez, the American League batting champion, know by Tuesday whether they will pick up an option for $3.5 million. Boston has to decide this week whether to pay Rick Aguilera, their No. 1 reliever, $4 million next season.
Players who face restrictions as repeat free agents will learn this week whether their options have been exercised. Those include Darryl Strawberry of the Yankees ($1.8 million), Dennis Eckersley of Oakland ($2.25 million), Paul Molitor of Toronto ($4 million) and Otis Nixon of Texas ($2.6 million). Eckersley missed by two games having his option take effect automatically.
In the group of players who can be free agents if they don't receive salary arbitration offers in the next five days are David Cone and Wade Boggs of the Yankees, Kevin Brown of Baltimore, Gary Gaetti of Kansas City, Todd Stottlemyre of Oakland, Mickey Tettleton and Bob Tewksbury of Texas, Randy Myers of the Chicago Cubs and Ozzie Smith of St. Louis.
 
Omar Vizquel Fan Club
 Jimy Williams, the Atlanta Braves' third-base coach, has added his name to the list of Omar Vizquel fans, but he has more than a fan's appreciation of the ability of the Cleveland Indians' shortstop.
"Vizquel is real active out there," said Williams, once a shortstop himself. "I read him because I played short. I respect him for the way he plays. He's sneaky in a good way. I think he watches me a lot to see if I'm watching him."
The activity Williams referred to is Vizquel's style of play when a runner is at second base. He often fakes a move to second for a pickoff throw and sometimes actually darts to the bag for a throw from the pitcher or at least to give the pitcher an opportunity to throw. The third-base coach is responsible for alerting the runner to the shortstop's whereabouts.
"He's trying to get an out; he's trying to do something," Williams said, flashing a smile of appreciation for Vizquel's efforts. "He does it from a distance, but his quickness makes him so good. I respect that. He's looking for a lean or a step. He has good instincts."
The only part of his game Vizquel hasn't exhibited in the World Series is the barehand grab of a bouncer to short. He did it a couple of times against Seattle in the American League playoff.
"This isn't turf," Williams pointed out. Vizquel makes the barehanded play only on artificial turf, always using his glove on less predictable grass.
 
Indians Look Ahead
 Even as they were trying to win the World Series, the Cleveland Indians were shopping some members of their starting lineup in trade talks with other clubs. Catcher Sandy Alomar Jr. and first baseman Paul Sorrento are the starters the Indians have offered.
The Indians, according to one executive, would like to find another catcher to replace the injury-prone Alomar. In seeking to trade Sorrento, the Indians are planning to platoon Herbert Perry and Eddie Murray, whom they want back as a free agent, at first base. They would add a left-handed hitter to be the designated hitter when Murray plays first.

HEADLINE: OCT. 22-28: U.S. Stands Out;
Inequalities in Income Are Reported Widening

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER 

 BODY:
The income gap between rich and poor keeps growing wider in the United States, according to a study issued last week. Some conservatives' definition of the middle class seems to be stretching along with the gap.
The study, commissioned by the Paris-based Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development, concluded that the difference between the best-paid 10 percent of Americans and the worst-paid 10 percent is wider than in any other large, industrialized nation.The study did not try to analyze the reasons for the high levels of inequality in the United States. It did say that inequality here had risen between 1980 and 1991. 
 Earlier in the week, the Raleigh News and Observer in North Carolina reported that one of the state's Republican Congressmen, Representative Frederick K. Heineman, had said that he considered himself lower-middle class even though he earns $183,500 a year from his Congressional pay and police pension.
"When I see someone who is making anywhere from $300,000 to $750,000 a year, that's middle class," said Mr. Heineman, a former New York City police officer and Raleigh police chief. "When I see anyone above that, that's upper middle class."
By comparison, the Census Bureau released figures this month showing that only one-fifth of all American households earn more than $62,841 a year, and only 5 percent earn more than $109,821. KEITH BRADSHER
