HEADLINE: INVESTING IT;
A Stock for Each Season

BYLINE: By ANTHONY RAMIREZ 

 BODY:
SECTOR analysis sounds like something you might flunk in high school. But with the stock market stagnant, technology stocks flying on vapor, and the economy in its 55th month of increasingly uncertain expansion, sector analysis may well pinpoint stocks to buy and stocks to avoid.
Sector strategies can be very dangerous, particularly when people chase today's "hot" sector. Often, these investors are Johnny-come-lately's who get in just as the industry cools down. Another tack, though also risky, takes a "top down" approach, looking for those sectors likely to do well at a given stage of the economy. 
 What does this economy-based theory say to today's investor? Here's one analyst's recommendation: "Buy Eat-'Em-Smoke-'Em-Drink-'Em-Then-Pay-for-It-by-Going-to-the-Doctor stocks." These are the consumer staples like Campbell's Soup, Philip Morris and Coca-Cola, along with health-care stocks like Merck. Stocks to avoid: those high-flying tech issues.
Such is the thinking of Sam Stovall, editor of Standard & Poor's Industry Reports, and his colleagues. Looking at economic and market data from 1969 through 1991, they concluded that the economy is in late expansion. That means, they believe, that the market will soon look kindly on health and consumer-staples companies -- the Eat-'Em-Smoke-'Em stocks -- because those enterprises tend to do better than others during economic downturns.
Other consumer-staple companies that may shine are Iowa Beef Processors and American Brands, the S.& P. analysts figure, and good health-care bets include Johnson & Johnson, Medtronic and Pfizer.
Sector investing gets its power from the fact that up to 60 cents of every dollar's move in a stock's price comes from trends in the overall market, and from whether a sector is in or out of favor.
Interested investors can turn to the 300 or so sector funds available. Fidelity Investments, the behemoth of the mutual fund business, offers 35 sector funds with some $5.5 billion in assets.
For Mr. Stovall and his colleagues, the economic cycle is the main guide for these investments. In their just-published "1995 Standard & Poor's Guide to Sector Investing," they posit that as economic expansion picks up, transportation stocks will thrive as more and more goods are delivered. As demand continues to rise,companies seek to improve productivity through technology, so computer and other high-tech stocks also prosper.
As the economy reaches the middle phase of expansion, service stocks, like providers of temporary workers, rise as companies struggle to meet increased demand but remain reluctant to add to permanent overhead costs.
Finally, when the expansion is in full throttle, companies hire a few more workers but spend more money on capital goods. That makes stocks in machine tools, energy, and basic materials like steel the way to go. Then -- now, Mr. Stovall would say -- the contraction begins.
Sector investing is not for everyone, warned Neal Litvack, Fidelity's executive vice president. A sector investor must stay alert to economic developments, be able to tolerate above-average volatility, and allocate no more than 10 percent of assets to such investing because of that volatility, he said. And watch out if you are chasing today's hot sector. On five of the seven occasions since 1985 when a sector was hot, many more investor dollars poured into funds after the sector delivered its best returns than before, according to a study by Morningstar, the mutual-fund research company.
Moreover, remember that all investing models cannot fit all of the facts. Mr. Stovall's model, for example, does not explain the remarkable 1995 performance of high-tech stocks. And it assumes that future economic performance will reflect past economic performance. But a few economists say that the economy is changing so quickly that the future ain't what it used to be.

HEADLINE: SPENDING IT;
Don't Spend Tax Breaks Before They've Hatched

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr. 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
THOUGH the final terms of the 1995 tax bill won't be known for another month or two, Congress has already taught some investors a costly lesson: a little knowledge is particularly dangerous when it comes to taxes.
After repeated statements from Republican leaders that taxes on capital gains would be cut retroactively to Jan. 1, 1995, legislators now appear inclined to make lower rates apply only to transactions made much later: probably after Oct. 13, but possibly not until early next year. 
 "They told everybody to go out and sell" with some assurance of getting the new, lower rate, said Bob Shapiro, national director of tax policy at Price Waterhouse and a former Capitol Hill tax aide. "I didn't think they'd do this," deny lower rates to most people who sold in 1995.
To be sure, accountants and other financial advisers routinely caution clients to be wary of Congressional whim and never to let tax considerations dictate investment decisions. But that is easier said than done.
Now, with the passage of its version of the budget bill, the Senate has put the main components of the pending tax legislation into useful focus. It's not necessarily time yet to take action. Still, it probably makes sense to refrain from a few actions, like putting money into an individual retirement account or buying an annuity, for the time being, since all the rules on savings and taxes are up for grabs. New types of accounts and new rules on existing ones could change your way of thinking by early next year.
In the category of things not worth delaying might be the sale of some financial assets to realize long-term gains, particularly if you're skittish about the financial markets and their increasing volatility. There are savvy ways to do such sales to make sure any future cut in capital gains tax will come your way.
On saving accounts, capital gains and other central tax issues, the final legislation will no doubt emerge only after a Senate-House conference, an anticipated veto by President Clinton and subsequent negotiations by all.
There is near unanimity on a few fronts. C. Clinton Stretch, of the accounting firm Deloitte & Touche, and other tax professionals consider a tax credit for each child a near certainty. The credit, probably $500 for each child under age 18, will probably apply to people under certain income limits and take effect in January 1996.
Next most likely, many tax advisers say, are a reduction in capital gains taxes and the creation of what the Republicans call American Dream savings accounts, which would provide a tax-advantaged way to save for something other than retirement, say a house or college tuition.
You will probably be able to exclude from taxation 50 percent of any gains on assets held more than a year. The top rate would drop from 28 percent now to an effective rate of 19.8 percent under both the House and Senate proposals. The provision is likely to kick in Oct. 14, though it might be later this year or Jan. 1, 1996. Indexing assets for future inflation, a provision in the House bill, will probably be dropped, while profits on baseball cards, coins and other collectibles are expected to remain subject to a 28 percent rate.
David M. Bradt Jr. of Arthur Andersen & Company predicted that the cut would take effect no later than mid-October, but suggested that investors who want to play it safe sell now in such a way that defers recognition of the profits until next year.
For stocks, which have generated hefty gains for many people this year, the tactic is called shorting against the box, a common way of locking in gains by borrowing and selling the same number of shares you already hold. Sometime next year after the legislation's effective date, you would deliver your stock to the party from whom you borrowed. Not only would your profit be taxed at the new, lower rate, but the tax would not be due until 1997. Why pay now when you can pay later?
Anyone who sold assets before mid-October of this year, on the other hand, may need to earmark some of the profit for taxes due next spring.
And anyone with losses on stocks or other investments will want to think about selling them before year's end. This is because both bills call for doubling the amount of losses needed to offset each dollar of ordinary income, up to the $3,000 maximum, effective Jan. 1.
For real estate, consider tailoring a transaction as an installment sale, with some or all of the proceeds to be paid next year. The risk is that the buyer might become unable to pay or, because of some real or imagined flaw in the property, unwilling to pay.
Before making such shifts wholesale, remember that under the Senate bill half of all capital gains would be added back to taxable income for purposes of calculating the alternative minimum tax, which trips up many taxpayers. "Watch out that you don't realize a lot of capital gains only to find out you don't capture it all because you're bumping up against the A.M.T.," counsels John A. McGraw, a vice president in the Washington office of Loomis, Sayles & Company, a large investment adviser.
In theory, a steep cut in the capital gains levy would make some other tax-advantaged products, like individual retirement accounts, less attractive in relative terms. But specialists dismiss the suggestion that taxpayers focus on capital gains instead of I.R.A.'s as wrongheaded. "Tax-free is always better than taxed at half rate," said Mr. Stretch of Deloitte & Touche.
I.R.A.'s will probably gain wider appeal in new legislation. Both the House and the Senate suggest that nonearning spouses be allowed the full $2,000 deduction for contributions, a perk now confined to the employed. If one spouse works and one does not, the maximum contribution is now $2,250 for the couple. More people are expected to qualify for the I.R.A. deduction because of higher income ceilings. Households could also gain financial flexibility, namely the ability to make penalty-free withdrawals for certain purposes, including medical and college expenses.
The American Dream savings account, probably taking effect in January, would have no initial tax advantage, but the contributions, unlimited in both House and Senate bills, would compound tax free and could be withdrawn tax free after five years. Earlier withdrawals would be permitted without penalty for designated expenses.
These accounts are considered so appealing, in fact, that advisers expect a surge of popularity that would come at the expense of the annuity business, which has boomed in recent years as Americans wake up to their long-term retirement needs.
Annuities, which merely defer taxes on growth until withdrawal and provide no tax benefit for contributions, would become less attractive, Mr. McGraw said, but he added, "I don't think it would be crippling."
Various other provisions of the House and Senate bills have a most uncertain fate. Some would not merit much financial attention even if the outcome of the legislative marathon were known, because taxpayers can do so little to prepare for them.
Among these items are a marriage credit of up to $145, credits for adoption and interest paid on student loans, repeal of the 1993 increase in taxation of Social Security benefits, creation of medical saving accounts and an increased exclusion from estate tax.
So the wisest course seems to be to keep an eye on the general direction of tax policy but avoid being paralyzed by uncertainty.
"If there's a valid business or investment reason to do a transaction now," said Mr. Bradt of Arthur Andersen, "I'd be very reluctant to defer doing it for tax reasons."

CORRECTION-DATE: November 5, 1995, Sunday

 CORRECTION:
A chart last Sunday with an article about proposed changes in Federal income tax laws referred incorrectly to current rules for contributions to individual retirement accounts. Contributions are generally not deductible if both of these conditions (not just one) apply: the taxpayer participates in a retirement plan at work, and adjusted gross income exceeds $50,000 for a married couple or $35,000 for a single taxpayer.

HEADLINE: MAKING IT WORK;
The Marxist Professor

BYLINE: By RAYMOND HERNANDEZ

 BODY:
THERE he was, one of New York City's last old-time Marxists, propped in a lunchtime den of wealthy Wall Street types, shaking a forkful of Irish sausage as he delivered prophecies of doom.
"Capitalism is not a rational system," exclaimed Stanley Aronowitz, a sociology professor at the City University of New York Graduate Center. "The only way it turns around is through mass struggle." 
 But no one at the Beekman Pub was listening. Not the soberly suited young men leaning against the bar. Nor the smart-looking couple in a nearby booth.
"Is everything O.K.?" asked the cheery waitress asked, interrupting Mr. Aronowitz's rambling manifesto.
No! Everything isn't O.K.
That's exactly the point Mr. Aronowitz has been trying to make through four decades of union organizing, proselytizing and Marx-influenced writing and teaching. Whether we know it or not, he says, we're stuck in a class structure that doesn't let us (or enough of us) breathe.
But these are difficult times for Marxists like him. It's been decades since the studying of Marx was regarded as the radical chic of the academic world. The camp of professors that taught Marx in the 60's and 70's has retreated from the social activism that was once so closely associated with it.
What they're doing these days seems far more ivory-towerish. Dispersing into departments that range from economics to women's studies to cultural studies, they are content to explore the power games underlying sex, money and, in a truly abstract exercise, knowledge itself. And that can leave unrepentant radicals pretty lonely, even Mr. Aronowitz, who has tried to keep up with the academic times.
"They say I'm the last one," said the 62-year-old Mr. Aronowitz. "They say I'm the last of the working-class intellectuals. That's terrible."
But Mr. Aronowitz is not likely to fade away anytime soon. In fact, he made quite a splash with his latest contribution to the class struggle: "The Jobless Future: Sci-Tech and the Dogma of Work," a nearly 400-page book written with another sociologist, William DiFazio.
The authors argue that technological advances, like automated teller machines, are permanently dislodging legions of workers from their jobs each and every day in a reorganization of labor.
It's a dreary state of affairs, as Mr. Aronowitz is wont to point out any chance he gets.
"Look what's happening on Wall Street," Mr. Aronowitz said, sipping a very dry Bombay Gibson on ice. "They've laid off 80,000 people out of 400,000 since 1987. It's not a blip, either. It's a permanent displacement. If we don't begin to look at our social arrangements, New York is going to be like Bombay."
Or even, he said later, Detroit.
Ever since the "The Jobless Future" was published last fall, Mr. Aronowitz has been getting calls from unionists, politicians, quotation-hunting journalists and talk-show hosts. (He has been on National Public Radio more than once.) And traditionally liberal periodicals, like The Nation, published lengthy reviews, while major newspapers, like The Washington Post, quoted him in feature articles on joblessness.
It's all got Mr. Aronowitz gloating, if only a bit.
"Marx is starting to look pretty smart at a time when Marxism should have been buried with the Soviet Union," he said. "There was a lot of smugness before the stock market crash in '87, but things are changing. There are a lot more people who aren't so sure about their futures."
Still, he and Mr. DiFazio see a way out, albeit a utopian one. They envision a brave new world in which workers are given shorter workweeks, have guaranteed wages and can spend their leisure in the pursuit of happiness, whether in a museum, on a college campus or with their families at home.
Which is all in keeping with Marx.
The book only reinforces Mr. Aronowitz's reputation at CUNY. He has long been regarded as one of CUNY's most prominent crusaders, who attracts and nurtures some of the most radical students with his insights on the state of mass culture, from TV and movies to rock and roll.
A few years ago, he was right there, bullhorn in hand, calling for solidarity when student demonstrators occupied the CUNY Graduate Center, on 42d Street, to protest state cuts.
Mr. Aronowitz's class-conscious sensibilities run deep. He grew up in blue-collar East Tremont, the Bronx, where boys dreamed of landing jobs in steel mills and truck yards, just as their fathers had.
Mr. Aronowitz's father dropped out of New York University in his freshman year to take a job as a reporter for The New York World. His mother, who held a variety of jobs in department stores and an office, was an ardent unionist who eventually became a shop steward.
It was the early 50's when he joined the blue-collar work force himself as a teen-ager. He took a job loading a fur truck and then worked at steel plants in New Jersey. He also began to take labor-relations courses and became a union organizer.
By 1967, he was enrolled at the New School for Social Research in Manhattan, where he earned a degree in sociology. This set him on a lifelong academic course; he has published a dozen books and hundreds of articles for newspapers and academic journals.
Still, his passion has always been working in the trenches of the labor struggle. "I was always bored in classrooms," Mr. Aronowitz said. "Bored out of my skull."
But things aren't looking so good in the trenches these days, particularly not in New York City, which just a generation ago was a relatively nice place for a Marxist like Mr. Aronowitz to live. Now, he says, unions cave in routinely, as City Hall bows to the demands of corporate barons like George Steinbrenner and Donald J. Trump, who seek sweet deals.
"We're terrified that business will split," he said. "So we give them tax breaks and end up cutting education, culture, environmental protection and so on. It's an endless cycle of concessions. It's policy based on the bottom line."
His warnings come down to this: corporate mergers, downsizing and the like are leaving society -- including government -- beholden to the whim of the capitalist.
But don't think for one minute that his interest is purely theoretical.
"They can't afford to pick up the garbage on Wednesday now?" complained Mr. Aronowitz, sounding more like a frustrated New Yorker than a Marxist.
He's worried about the public schools, too, in no small part because he and his partner, Ellen Willis, the writer, send their daughter to an intermediate school in District 2 in lower Manhattan.
So what's the average proletarian to do?
"I think people have to decide if they want to continue to be held hostage," said the professor, who argues that a strong working-class movement is the only way to insure social progress. "No one has been willing to say, 'Enough already.' "
 
WORDS ON WORDS
 
Negotiating the Maze of Meaning
 
"The question What is to be done? has become tainted because it points to calumnies committed in the name of traditional ideologies of emancipation. Indeed, to speak of liberation, of the emancipation of labor, appears utopian, in the bad sense; in a period of the transformation of global politics by the breakup of the Soviet Union, the intellectual discrediting of Marxism, even by some of its erstwhile practitioners, and, on a larger canvas the steep decline of socialism, recalling these words conjures images of betrayal."
 
-- from "The Jobless Future: Sci-Tech and the Dogma of Work" (University of Minnesota Press, 1994) by Stanley Aronowitz and William DiFazio.

HEADLINE: PLAYING IN THE NEIGHBORHOOD

 BODY:

UPPER WEST SIDE
Children's Tales, as Sung By a Touring Octet
A St. Louis a cappella octet, Pieces of Eight, combines rich vocal harmonies with popular children's tales to produce jazzy renditions of such stories as "The Gingerbread Man" and "City Mouse, Country Mouse." Some selections are geared toward adult audiences. "As Time Goes By" and "Anything Goes" are included in the group's repertory. 
 Beginning next Saturday, Pieces of Eight (below) will start its first New York City tour, including performances at branches of the New York and Brooklyn Public Libraries. The group's music is written or arranged by it artistic director, Charles Mead, who weaves together elements of classical music with gospel, reggae, doo-wop and Cajun.
Pieces of Eight, Riverside Branch of the New York Public Library, 127 Amsterdam Avenue (at 65th Street); Saturday, 3:30 P.M.; free; (212) 870-1633.
 
BOWLING GREEN
 
Museum Demonstrations Of Latin American Weaving
Visitors to the program marking the first anniversary of the Museum of the American Indian will be able to watch female artists from Ecuador, Bolivia and Mexico weave, knit and embroider shawls, clothing and blankets. Interpreters will be on hand to help with questions and answers. The program, "The Market Place: A Latin American Women's Arts Program," will also feature slide shows and lectures on the development and significance of textile art forms in Latin America, and community-based collectives and art as engines of economic development for the continent and its people. Among the featured speakers will be an anthropologist, June Nash.
"The Market Place: A Latin American Women's Arts Program," National Museum of the American Indian, 1 Bowling Green; today and tomorrow, noon to 5 P.M.; free.
 
FLUSHING
 
Photos of Landmarks, And Memories of Vietnam
"I like to get to know my subject, whether it be a person or a building," said Tony Valez, a photographer and former adjunct professor in the art department at Queens College. "There is a time element you have to put in to get the right light or mood and personality."
Highlights of Mr. Valez's work over the last eight years, as well as some older photographs, will be featured in an exhibition at Queens College that opens on Thursday.
More than 50 photographs will be on display, including those of Brooklyn landmarks and historic buildings, like the Brooklyn Bridge and the Fulton Ferry historic district (above).
The show will also feature arresting photographs from Mr. Valez's tour of duty in Vietnam in 1966-67.
Art Center, Rosenthal Library at Queens College, 65-30 Kissena Boulevard, Flushing; Thursday through Dec. 22; Monday to Thursday, 9 A.M. to 7 P.M., and Friday, 9 A.M. to 5 P.M.; free; (718) 997-3770.

SECTION: Section 7; Page 46; Column 1; Book Review Desk 

LENGTH: 2650 words

HEADLINE: UNIVERSITY PRESSES: ESSAY;
Keepers of the Tenure Track

BYLINE: By JUDITH SHULEVITZ; Judith Shulevitz, an editor at large at Lingua Franca, is a contributing editor at New York magazine.

 BODY:
IF you were a graduate student in the 80's and subject to the general delusion that held literary criticism to be the ne plus ultra of intellectual thrill, then you too probably owned one of these: an oversize paperback with an austere cover and small-type title that, grouped with three or more of its kind on your bookshelf, confirmed your status as an avatar of predoctoral chic. The object in question was a volume in the University of Minnesota series called "Theory and History of Literature" -- any volume, since they all looked alike and were each equally tantalizing, coming as they did in a wide variety of colors. There were Hans Robert Jauss's "Toward an Aesthetics of Reception" (pink), Paul de Man's "Blindness and Insight" (powder blue) and Jean-Francois Lyotard's "Postmodern Condition" (lurid aquamarine). There were Mikhail Bakhtin's "Problems of Dostoevsky's Poetics" (sunflower yellow) and Michel de Certeau's "Heterologies" (electric blue). No other books in all academia were quite so insistently chromatic, or had half as much cachet.
The tenets of scholarly fashion, like those of every other kind of fashion, derive in part from the rules of material culture; the fetish objects just happen to be books. Whatever the merits of the subject matter -- and in the case of Minnesota, they were not to be sneered at -- it is the rare academic trend that takes off without the aid of a magisterial series or a fashion-forward university press. Stanley Fish, a professor of English at Duke University, says that in the 80's Minnesota's "Theory and History of Literature" list -- more commonly known as the lipstick series -- "was the only series for which I put a standing order, so that I would just receive whatever book came out next." In the 90's, Routledge and Verso have edged out Minnesota; you can't even think the words "cultural studies" without the image of their bright, matte, pop-icon-laden book covers cropping up in your head. (There is even a bookstore called Verso in Manhattan, which, though technically unaffiliated with that left-wing British press, features a cultural studies section devoted almost exclusively to Verso and Routledge books.) Among the strictly academic publishers -- Routledge and Verso are not linked to universities -- Duke University Press's shrink-wrapped books probably enclose more fetish value per pound than those of any of their competitors, since their pages are hallowed by the imprimatur of no less a personage than Stanley Fish, the press's celebrity director, and such series editors as the queer-theory superstar Eve Kosofsky Sedgwick and the nonfiction prose stylist Jane Kramer. 
 Editors, of course, are eager to foster the impression that their presses reside at the cutting edge, since this is the perception that best moves books. But what about the ideals of what was once quaintly called "scholarly communication"? Ever since university presses began publishing in America in the late 19th century -- in fact, ever since the first academic presses were founded in Cambridge and Oxford in 1584 and 1585, respectively -- they have had as their lofty mission the unimpeded exchange of scholarly views and findings. Scholarship was never the only thing these presses published; in the 17th century, both Cambridge and Oxford stole away from several furious London printers the much-coveted royal charter to publish the Bible, thereby insuring themselves a subsidy for all time. But such revenue-generating tricks aside -- and the American university press has its equivalent of the Bible in the form of the glossy coffeetable book on state birds and other regional reference works -- academic publishing has always considered itself the handmaiden of intellectual labor. Indeed, by the turn of the century, American academic publishers generally saw their service in the most self-effacing terms possible -- printing dissertations and Festschriften, anthologies of essays celebrating the work of some beloved but usually unknown mentor. By 1919, one pundit had already observed that "the university press was an organization whose function was to publish works which no one would read."
In 1995, however, a better observation would be that the university press is an organization whose function is to publish works plenty of people want to read -- just not enough people to warrant an entry on the global megaconglomerates' bloated balance sheets. "We're moving into a market niche that has been less and less easy for major houses to occupy," Mr. Fish says. "Now we think in terms of books that sell between 5,000 and 40,000 copies. We no longer think in terms of the nine hundred to fifteen hundred print run that was for a long time the standard among university presses." This is not the sole prerogative of the more prestigious university presses, either; the smaller ones are following the bigger ones into the wider market, whether they want to or not. "We end up publishing in fields where we think sales will be good enough," says Sanford Thatcher, director of the Pennsylvania State University Press. "One of the ironies of all this is that a field like women's studies, which is still marginalized in the academy, which is not fully accepted there, is a gold mine for publishers."
 THERE'S another irony. It's that even as academic presses tend toward the broad and fashionable subjects for which tens of thousands of readers might be willing to put down credit cards, academics themselves are beginning to view scholarly publishing in the narrowest possible terms: It gets them jobs. Forget about advancing the intellect; for the academic, what scholarly communication is for is to advance the intellectual. With university presidents doing everything they can to balance their budgets by downsizing their faculties, all the stirring rhetoric about how teaching counts more than publishing has come to mean nothing. Last spring, The Cleveland Plain Dealer published a study of the records of tenuring committees at Ohio State University after a state court ruled that these documents, which are usually tightly sealed, were public information. The unsurprising finding: that tenure is awarded on the basis of a candidate's publication record, just as it always has been. Assistant professors don't need formal studies to know this; junior scholars coming up before a tenure committee are well aware that their potential employers will demand publication credentials with the implacability of the Victorian matron requesting references from a nanny. "There's a long and important tradition of a relationship between university presses and scholarship that results in tenure," says David Bartlett, director of Temple University Press. "But now we have the kind of absolutist tradition that dictates that you have to have one book or two books before you can get tenure, and that seems to me arbitrary and silly."
Editors sign up books they hope will sell; professors write books they hope will get them tenure. The unhappy result of these conflicting desires is an excess of unpublishable manuscripts and many desperate academics. "My sense is that there's a much higher level of anxiety among scholars because they recognize that it's harder to get that first monograph published," says Lisa Freeman, director of the University of Minnesota Press. "I hear that all the time: 'What do you mean you don't want to look at my book because it's too narrow! That's what you're supposed to do!' " The gap is increasingly filled by young professors trying to write crossover books. And while that might in many ways be for the good, it can also result in an intellectual conformity all the more distressing for being nonideological, the product neither of tenured radicalism nor of political correctness but of simple economics. For if books are the sine qua non of employability, then the judgments upon which academic hiring committees must ultimately rely are not those of experts, or of peers, or even of university press editors themselves. The people who determine what subjects up-and-coming academics get to write about -- and who in effect award tenure and the right to shape the next generation of scholars -- are Barnes & Noble customers. And what they want from university presses, as any editor will tell you, are women's studies, African-American studies, gay and lesbian studies, Asian-American studies, studies of popular culture and literary biographies, to say nothing of that inevitable publishing troika, Nazis, dogs and the Civil War.
 BUT doesn't such structured trendiness defeat the whole purpose of university presses? Aren't they subsidized by their host institutions specifically so that they can withstand the pressures of the marketplace? As it turns out, they aren't all that well subsidized. According to the American Association of University Presses, the average university underwrites about 10 percent of its press's cost. That figure hasn't changed much in the past 20 years, but other things have. Paper and printing costs are up. So is the practice of assembling photocopied course packs, which cuts severely into book sales. Foundation support, both for scholarship and for publication, is down; just last month, Congress's 37 percent cut in the National Endowment for the Humanities eliminated one of the most significant scholarly publications support programs in the country. And academic librarians -- perhaps the steadiest consumers of university press books -- are cutting their book budgets sharply. Not only have their general budgets shrunk, but the cost of commercially published scientific journals has soared by 115 percent in the past nine years; to keep up, research libraries are allotting more and more of their budgets to journal subscriptions. On the average, according to the Association of Research Libraries, its 119 member libraries buy almost a quarter fewer scholarly monographs than they did in 1986. "We're in a quandary," says Duane Webster, executive director of the research library group. "The practice of individual institutions has been to eliminate or cancel low-use, high-cost items, whether these are monographs or serials. The consequence is that, collectively, the corpus of available knowledge has shrunk."
Douglas Bennett, a vice president of the American Council of Learned Societies, an umbrella organization for a lot of very obscure specializations in the humanities, shares Mr. Webster's fears. "We think that scholarly research is something that to be healthy requires some active support from a variety of sources that are independent of what the marketplace will support," he says. Penn State's Sanford Thatcher tells the story of how he tried to publish some of the as yet untranslated works of Johann Gottfried Herder, an 18th-century German thinker best known for providing the first philosophical justification for modern nationalism. Despite good reviews, Mr. Thatcher had to abandon the project after the first volume for lack of funds. Colin Day, who is an economist as well as the director of the University of Michigan Press, illustrates the problem by way of example. "Say you want to publish a book on some very serious facet of Japanese studies," he begins. "What would your library sales be?" Since many academic libraries have begun limiting themselves to one particular branch of knowledge or another, Mr. Day says, "there have been discussions about having no more than, say, a dozen libraries in America collect books in Japanese studies." That would account for 12 copies, plus a few hundred more to those scholars of Japanese life interested in this topic. Given the cost of printing a book (Mr. Day says that when the print run drops from 1,000 to 900 the average cost of printing a volume rises by 10 percent, while the drop from 600 to 500 brings the cost up by 25 percent), no university press would take on the book. "Those trendy books on the Japanese threat to American industry will go on selling," Mr. Day says. "But not the serious books."
Not everybody thinks the market's bias against hyperseriousness is such a bad thing. "Me, I'm looking for people not to write books," says Lindsay Waters, the founder of the Minnesota series, who is now the executive editor for the humanities of Harvard University Press. "There are too many people writing books and sending them in who ought to be writing articles. People say to me, why don't you do nice books on Keats's image patterns? I won't buy 'em. What's so good about them? It's not like anybody is going to require their students to read them." At their best, university press editors can bring a generalist's rigor to their scrutiny of manuscripts, holding them up to standards of coherence and clarity. "The world of the humanities and social sciences could use more talk across disciplinary lines," Mr. Bennett says. "To the degree that the publication process results in works that, without any decline of sophistication or richness, are prepared in such a way that they're understandable by people in other disciplines, that seems like a positive effect."
One scholar of 18th-century English literature, David Ritchie, a professor at Queens College, recently explained in an article in Scholarly Publishing that the theory boom of the past decade may have been accelerated by the economics of publishing -- by presses looking for works that cut across subspecializations -- but that such pressures had had certain social benefits. "Why is it that I buy the latest books by Stephen Greenblatt, Stanley Fish, or Henry Louis Gates, since I don't specialize in Renaissance, 17th-century, or African-American literature?" he writes. "The answer is that theory has become common ground for most of us who take the profession seriously, and if I buy theory I will be able to discuss Greenblatt, Fish and Gates with a wide spectrum of my colleagues."
IN any case, for the most unmarketable pieces of scholarly research, there's always electronic publishing. In the sciences, any number of superspecialized subdisciplines now bypass the journal stage and transmit papers directly to subscribers on list servers that take advantage of the Internet. Why shouldn't the humanities follow suit? Eventually, they probably will, though at the moment nobody knows how to insure quality control, without which subscribers are likely to find themselves swamped with unedited articles of dubious value.
In the meantime, though, university press editors find themselves wielding ever more influence over the fate of academics and their departments, a degree of power that makes many of them uncomfortable. They'd like to see universities change the way they grant tenure -- athough they would not do away with it altogether, as some college presidents and administrators propose. What the publishing community wants to eliminate is the mindless reliance on books as material evidence of intellectual excellence.
"Tenure evaluation is based on the quantity rather than the quality of the publication," Lisa Freeman says. "We've got a system in which more is better." David Bartlett believes tenure committees should be required to read candidates' writing themselves, rather than tally up the number of publications and rank the presses at which they were published. Otherwise universities will someday find themselves staffed by academic equivalents of John Grisham -- by scholars who can sell.
"I think you're going to see a sea change in academic publishing in 1996 or 1997," Ms. Freeman says. "I think that in two years, there will be hardly any monographs on the market."
And lest that plaint sound wearisomely academic, consider that Harold Bloom's "Anxiety of Influence" and John Rawls's "Theory of Justice" both began life as relatively obscure monographs. It would be a bleak world indeed that could find no place for thinkers such as these.

HEADLINE: UNIVERSITY PRESSES;
Out in Front

BYLINE: By Jonathan Rauch; Jonathan Rauch is the author of "Demosclerosis: The Silent Killer of American Government."

 BODY:

BARRY GOLDWATER
By Robert Alan Goldberg.
Illustrated. 463 pp. New Haven:
Yale University Press. $27.50. 
 I GREW up in the shadow of Barry Goldwater's hilltop house in Phoenix, often thinking scornfully of the fossil inside. When my parents moved to Arizona in the 1950's, the Goldwaters had already been doing business in the state for a century; by the time I was old enough to notice, Barry Goldwater had been around forever and seemed little but an anachronism.
The joke was on me, and on the millions of other people who once saw Goldwaterism as the reactionary impulse of a dying ideology. There is not a candidate in the current Republican Presidential race who does not mouth themes that Mr. Goldwater pioneered in his own campaign of 1964. That campaign was the first to tell the country that government was the problem; the first to woo what Mr. Goldwater called the "forgotten American" (the precursor of Richard Nixon's "silent majority" and today's "angry white male"); the first to see an emerging Republican base in the South. Mr. Goldwater favored a 25 percent tax cut, space satellites to shield against nuclear attack, and school prayer; he opposed "inverse discrimination" and denounced "meaningless" sex and violence on television. Above all, he energized the conservative generation that is now in power.
Robert Alan Goldberg's admirable and refreshingly brisk biography of Mr. Goldwater could hardly be better timed. Those who want to understand the cycles of success and failure of the conservative movement need to know Goldwaterism. Mr. Goldberg, a historian at the University of Utah (who had Mr. Goldwater's cooperation), gives a splendidly clear-eyed view of it.
The author's verdict on Mr. Goldwater is split. On one side, Mr. Goldwater was a founding father of a familiar conservative hypocrisy. For decades, Republicans have been complaining about the evils of big government without really intending to do anything much about it. Mr. Goldwater detested the New Deal and its plethora of programs, but when the time came, in 1952, to make his first Senate race, he declared, "No responsible Republican has any intention or desire to abolish any one of them." He is heard to insist, time and again, that he came up the old-fashioned way, by hardscrabble individualist grit. "I suggest to you that government never did a thing for us," he says. In fact, as Mr. Goldberg shows, the economic boom in Arizona came on the back of untold billions of dollars in Federal subsidies -- defense subsidies, water subsidies, New Deal subsidies -- and they were subsidies that Mr. Goldwater fought to keep.
Too often, Mr. Goldwater was loyal to scoundrels. He protected his friends Joseph McCarthy and Richard Nixon long after others had abandoned them. In his private life, he unwaveringly opposed discrimination and racism; but in his political life, he was blinded to the moral call of civil rights by his reliance on white (and Southern) votes and his mistrust of liberal activism.
Yet one puts down this book feeling, as its avowedly liberal author appears to, that Mr. Goldwater is that rarity in public life: a man who swayed politics far more than it swayed him. Unlike Nixon and Lyndon Johnson, the author notes, Mr. Goldwater lacks a dark side. Unlike President Reagan (of whom Mr. Goldwater remarks, "I don't think he ever thought anything through"), he did much of his own thinking.
Moreover, he grew. Late in life, as the country moved to the right, he rose again to remonstrate against his own faction's intolerant streak. Get government out of the economy, yes, but out of private lives, too. Thus he attacked "political preachers" ("I will fight them every step of the way if they try and dictate their moral convictions to all Americans in the name of conservatism"), championed abortion rights and, in 1993, called for an end to the ban on homosexuals in the military ("I can't see any way in the world that being gay can cause damage to somebody else" expressed his philosophy). To this day, he is a gravelly iconoclast.
And there remains about him a certain gruff humility that seems genuine. "I just like to be remembered as an honest person who tried," he said in 1993. Mr. Goldberg is adept, if sometimes a touch dry, in tracing the trajectory of Mr. Goldwater's political career. But it is the man, much more than the politician, who finally earns Mr. Goldberg's admiration, and who lingers in the mind when the book is put away. To re-encounter Mr. Goldwater in the age of Newtbaugh is to hope that conservatives, above all, do not forget him.

HEADLINE: THEATER;
Spiffier Stage for 'Anything Goes'

BYLINE: By ALVIN KLEIN 

 BODY:
THE before (May) and after (September) pictures of the Downtown Cabaret Theater in Bridgeport tell the tale. The theater that closed its doors from June through August of this year, for "major physical, technical and cosmetic renovations," in the words of the 20th-anniversary program, reopened early this month, full of spiffy improvements, about $1.2 million worth.
In addition to a $900,000 grant from the state'e Department of Economic Development, $300,000 was raised from corporations, foundations and individuals -- and the goal is $1 million more. Or more than that, "to keep on going," said a theater spokesman. 
 The new lobby, a larger women's bathroom, an extended horseshoe-shaped balcony and backstage technology (fly space, lighting and sound) represent the beginning of a campaign to "keep this place alive and kicking," in the words of a speech given before a benefit performance of "Anything Goes" on Oct. 13.
The Cole Porter musical, a fitting choice for the reopening show, may well signal a more assured accent on big-time musicals for Bridgeport. At this point, any revival of "Anything Goes" is likely to be a mishmash of the original 1934 version, a 1960's revision and the musical's most successful adaptation yet, the rewritten 1988 Lincoln Center production.
In other words, there can be no musical comedy purists as such musicals go. Few contain so many inimitable -- and irrelevant -- songs, and few composers wrote so many sparkling ones for obscure shows. So it's easy to lift such sparklers as "It's DeLovely" (from "Red, Hot and Blue," 1936) -- so easy to lift when the setting is the deck of a ship in the moonlight.
And then there's "Friendship" (from "Du Barry Was a Lady," 1939), "Let's Misbehave" (from "Les Ambassadeurs in Paris" 1927) and "Take Me Back to Manhattan" (from "The New Yorkers," 1930). These are among the songs originally meant for "Anything Goes" -- songs like "You're the Top," "Blow, Gabriel, Blow" and "All Through the Night," not that there are other songs like those songs.
"Anything Goes" is now more a scintillating hit parade than it could ever have been in the first place, when Ethel Merman and Victor Moore headed the cast. And since the book, originally written by P. G. Wodehouse and Guy Bolton, had to be revised, even in 1934 by Howard Lindsay and Russel Crouse, because of news events of the day.
The sinking of the S. S. Morro Castle, for example, with 135 people going under, made a story about a jolly shipwreck cry out for rewrites. Then there was the Lindbergh kidnapping, which caused Porter to delete two lines, suddenly in questionable taste, from one song and substitute "flying too high with some guy in the sky/is my idea of nothing to do."
What remains of a daffy book still includes the ever effervescent Reno Sweeney, "her high-stepping holiness," who says: "It's not easy being New York's most notorious evangelist" and the debutante Hope Harcourt "who came out in a Zeppelin." Add the stowaways, Snake Eyes Johnson (public enemy No. 1) and Moonface Martin (public enemy No. 13), gangsters dressed as ministers. One of them is a "broken down stockbroker" while the other likes to quote Dillinger, as in: "It's always darkest just before they turn the lights on."
"Let's Misbehave" is an unbeatable seduction song for anyone with class, "All Through the Night," in a nice up tempo, is one of the most intoxicating songs, well, ever. If you don't get the line, "You're the pants on a Roxy usher," don't worry; you'll get another one.
In the Bridgeport production, Janet Aldrich effervesces as Reno Sweeney. The title song brings on an all-out tap number, choreographed by the show's director, Scott Thompson. It could easily fit into the company's next scheduled show: "42d Street."
 "Anything Goes," Downtown Cabaret Theater, 263 Golden Hill Street, Bridgeport, 576-1636. Performances through Nov. 25.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Janet Aldrich, foreground, and, from left, Lili Calahan, Ashley Ryan Byler and Michelle Aravena in "Anything Goes."

LENGTH: 1236 words

HEADLINE: THEATER;
Adding a Line to a Distinguished Resume

BYLINE: By ALVIN KLEIN 

DATELINE: PRINCETON

 BODY:
WHERE do you find the strength to trust your vision and instinct?"
Now, that is the question for the artist to muse upon, but first, Athol Fugard acknowledged it, humbled himself before it. 
 "What a huge, beautiful question," he replied to Sarah Angelini, a Princeton senior from Northboro, Mass. He grasped for an answer. "It's all I've got. I'm not schooled. I'm not trained." He paused. "But I'm not answering your question. It relates to my passion for the craft."
This South African Renaissance man of the theater is currently carrying out the missions of playwright, director and actor in "Valley Song," at the McCarter Theater here through Nov. 12. On Monday afternoon, he was playing yet another role, that of teacher, in the theater and dance department of Princeton University.
Mr. Fugard might argue with being called a teacher. "I get enormous pleasure from sharing my craft," he said after class. "I don't think of it as teaching." The 16 students in Theater 330, "Fugard on Theater," might disagree.
"There are no hard and fast rules; there are as many ways to write a play as there are people who want to write one," Mr. Fugard said. "This is a sharing experience. The selfish aspect is that I get an enormous energy feed, an adrenaline rush. I'm 63. The theater has given me my life, my identity. I try helplessly to repay it. I find the students very challenging. Young people replenish my work."
Yes. But are those not the words of a master teacher? If Mr. Fugard's commitment to six seminars, three hours each, is not an exaltation of teaching, what is it?
"They are venturing in there, gaining confidence," Mr. Fugard said of his students, who are majoring, variously, in politics, in anthropology, in religion, in English. "I spent the first three hours talking nonstop. Now we have a genuine dialogue in that room. I love that word, dialogue."
It is Mr. Fugard's first such experience, "having a sit-down dialogue with students," he said. It's also a first for Princeton, having a playwright of power and glory take over a class -- not for just an hour, but a whole course.
"I've stood up and delivered orthodox talks and lectures and addresses," he said, most recently at the Governor's Conference on the Arts in Trenton last month. "But here I insisted on a venue that doesn't feel like a classroom, that invites openness."
In a semicircle, Mr. Fugard sits next to Prof. Michael Cadden, the director of the university's theater and dance program, who initiated the course. The six sessions span 12 weeks and include an overview of Mr. Fugard's career, as well as the customary reading assignments, journals and conferences.
In addition to his own works -- "Blood Knot," "Hello and Goodbye," "My Children! My Africa!" -- Mr. Fugard included Brecht and Grotowski, who "used the theater to do something about the world in which they lived."
In this session, the fourth of six in which Mr. Fugard is participating, he wore a black sweatshirt and gray sweatpants and held a mug of tea or sipped bottled water. He began with a question: "Any un answered questions?" And the students' hands flew.
For three whirling hours, he shared a life in the theater. He recalled an improvisational theater piece in Johannesburg last year: "My Life," taken from the words of young women. "We sat around and developed a beautiful piece of theater," he said. He would later say that, yes, he would welcome a similar opportunity in Princeton.
He commented on the ages of most theatergoers, 40 plus. (How does one get younger people into the theater? he wondered.) He reflected on the problems of the previous night's performance "in the naked reality of the rehearsal room." He referred to the theater of Shakespeare, of Machiavelli, of Tennessee Williams. He recalled the time he assisted black South African actors who came to him for help in forming a theater troupe "to break the conspiracy of silence that had gagged their mouths."
He thanked a student for asking about lighting and admired the lighting designer's palette of colors. He answered a question about his most memorable theater experience. It was a performance of "Sizwe Bansi Is Dead" in the black ghetto of New Brighton near Johannesburg after the play's award-winning American premiere, and audience members were emboldened to interrupt, take sides and otherwise throw themselves into the action. He relived the groundbreaking premiere of "Blood Knot" -- "when I knew I had already found my voice." Throughout, his eyes searched for more questions, his hands dancing, gesticulating, building sets and images in the air.
"You sit here as young Americans," he told the group, "and you take for granted that you can speak, that you have freedom of movement, that you can enjoy the essential freedoms -- of the press, of the university, of an artistic environment -- that are the hallmark, the guarantee of a civilization.
"It is harder for Americans being a member of a minority group. There is more anger, more bitterness, more frustration, and rightly so, here -- because in South Africa, the oppressed are in their own majority group. They are not part of the white world, so the world is theirs, though not economically. In this country, the Constitution promises one thing and society practices another."
He wanted to temper an image of Fugard as polemicist. "Fun is such an important word in theater," he said. "I haven't the slightest problem with using the word entertain. It's an important, useful, beautiful word, and a good start."
Even though the racial separatism and political persecution that informed his seminal plays have ended, his notion of theater, as expressed in a 1986 interview in The New York Times, remains un altered. "I think it is almost impossible to have a piece of meaningful literature that does not involve some form of social criticism," he said then. Now, he added: "It's amusing and it's scary. People say now that it's all changed, well, that's the end of him."
The student who asked, "What is the most precarious role you play in the theater?" invited a speculation: "If I was never to act again, that would be no big deal. If I was never to direct again, that would be a more significant loss; it teaches me a lot about my craft as a writer. But my life wouldn't end. If I was never to write again, I wouldn't know what to do with my life. That is the fact of my life. The only truly safe place in my life is in the middle of a story, as its teller. Then I know who I am and why I am."
AFTER the McCarter's acclaimed production of Mr. Fugard's early play "Hello and Goodbye" last year, it is unavoidable to wonder if he intends to forge an association with the McCarter comparable to the one he had with the Yale Repertory Theater in the 1980's.
"Because of its size, the McCarter is a hard venue," he responded. "But I'm so comfortable, so at home with Emily Mann's vision of theater and the amicable warm environment here, that there's an enormous agreement between us. And yes, I'm prepared to come back a third time. But three Yale Rep theaters can fit into the McCarter, and what they need here is a second smaller theater."
By the time Monday's class ended, Mr. Fugard had not let go of the question about vision and instinct. "I can psychically feel the truth of a story, feel its generative power, its pulse, its throb," he said. "I plant words, and they grow."

GRAPHIC: Photo: "If I was never to write again, I wouldn't know what to do with my life," said Athol Fugard in his seminar at Princeton. (Norman Y. Lono for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: ATLANTIC CITY;
The Slot With Something Extra

BYLINE: By BILL KENT 

 BODY:
MAC SEELIG has seen the future of casino gambling, and he says it will be "more comfortable and a lot more fun."
The big, barrel-chested Mr. Seelig, 52, is president of the A.C. Coin and Slot Service Company, New Jersey's largest slot machine distributor. Having just returned from the World Gaming Conference, an annual gambling industry trade show held in Las Vegas last weekend, Mr. Seelig could barely contain his enthusiasm for new products that could transform his privately held Pleasantville company, which started as a vending machine service, into an international concern. In 18 years, Mr. Seelig estimates, he has sold nearly 100,000 slot machines to casinos in Atlantic City, Puerto Rico and the Caribbean, and to cruise ships. 
 At the three-day trade show, Mr. Seelig said, he signed 50 contracts with casino operators for the Bonus Road Rally "interactive slot environment," a $300,000 row of 10 slot machines with a Corvette race car theme. Mr. Seelig's company developed the game in partnership with International Gaming Technology, a slot machine manufacturer, and the Trump Taj Mahal Casino.
Bonus Road Rally is a marriage of slot machines and slot cars, vaguely reminiscent of a Boardwalk arcade skill game in which contestants use water pistols to move cars across a track. Above the slot machines, a mural trimmed in neon light is divided into 10 horizontal tracks. Scale models of Corvettes, representing the gamblers playing individual slot machines, move across the tracks. The faster gamblers feed the machines, the faster the cars move. The machines can be customized to provide rewards like extra payouts or noncash prizes as the cars near the finish line.
"This is a business in which you make money by listening to your customers and giving them exactly what they want," Mr. Seelig said. "My customers are casino operators. For years, they've been telling me they haven't seen anything new. Variations, sure, but nothing really exciting. At all the trade shows, they would say to me, 'Mac, give us something new.' This year, I did just that, and when they sat down to play Bonus Road Rally, they went berserk."
"What really blew everybody away is that an idea for something like this could come out of Atlantic City," he added. "Up until now, all the creative gaming innovations come out of Nevada and work their way from west to east. This time, we sort of reversed it."
The Bonus Road Rally is undergoing regulatory testing by the State Division of Gaming Enforcement, and the first game is expected to be operating at the Trump Taj Mahal by January, with others in casinos around the country and in Europe possibly by the end of 1996.
Although Atlantic City has shown the gambling world a few new tricks, like Tropworld's indoor theme park, the state's long, tedious and often adversarial regulatory process made introducing such tricks an expensive, risky proposition. It took more than a decade for Las Vegas standards like poker, keno and racetrack simulcasting to appear here.
In the past, the smallest items used in an Atlantic City casino, from cocktail stirrers to hotel soap, were subject to intense and costly regulatory examination. But the economic climate of the last five years, and increased competition from other gambling meccas, have made state officials move a little faster.
Obtaining approval for even minor variations in slot machine technology "used to be a nightmare," said Thomas McCormick, a lawyer for Mr. Seelig's company. "Now it can be a five- or three-day turnaround."
Mr. Seelig said the current regulatory attitude had encouraged entrepreneurs to dream up innovations that are changing the gambling industry "from a grind type of operation to something that's exciting and fun." He cited a smaller, sit-down version of a craps game recently developed by three Harrah's employees, and a $1,500 casino chair invented by a New York furniture manufacturer. In addition to vibrating when the customer flips a switch, the chair can be adjusted up, down, backward and forward for more comfortable seating.
"When you look at what the chair does for the customer," Mr. Seelig said, "the issue is not the price of it."

HEADLINE: UNIVERSITY PRESSES;
Why Buy American?

BYLINE: By Michael Lind; Michael Lind, a senior editor of The New Republic, is the author of "The Next American Nation: The New Nationalism and the Fourth American Revolution."

 BODY:

OPENING AMERICA'S MARKET
U.S. Foreign Trade Policy Since 1776.
By Alfred E. Eckes Jr.
Illustrated. 402 pp. Chapel Hill:
The University of North Carolina Press. $34.95. 
 THE cold war consensus in favor of free trade is now under attack in the United States from economic nationalists on both the right and the left. In debates like those over the Nafta and GATT treaties, both sides have claimed that history supports their position. Alfred E. Eckes Jr. has provided a dispassionate, informed and accessible account of the history of United States trade policy that can help Americans evaluate the arguments of both free-traders and protectionists. Mr. Eckes, a former chairman and commissioner of the United States International Trade Commission during the Reagan and Bush Administrations who now teaches contemporary history at Ohio University, unites scholarly rigor with a policy maker's sensitivity to the political factors influencing trade.
The commitment of the United States to free trade, Mr. Eckes shows, is a relatively recent phenomenon. George Washington not only supported the protection of infant American industries, but set an example by "buying American." "I use no porter [ale] or cheese in my family, but such as is made in America," he said. Alexander Hamilton's arguments in favor of a national industrial policy not only inspired early American economic nationalists like Daniel Raymond but influenced the German-American economic thinker Friedrich List, whose vision of a united Germany with a protected market was realized later in the 19th century.
In the period before the Civil War, the Democrats, representing the agricultural exporters of the South, tended to favor lower tariffs; Northern manufacturers sought protection from British and European imports. Northern industrial interests prevailed during the period of Republican dominance from the Civil War to the Great Depression. Theodore Roosevelt summed up the prevailing Republican view when he declared, "Thank God I am not a free-trader. In this country pernicious indulgence in the doctrine of free trade seems inevitably to produce fatty degeneration of the moral fibre." Republicans, accused of exploiting American consumers on behalf of industry, claimed that high tariffs protected American workers from low-wage competition abroad; Herbert Hoover, for example, sounded like many of the opponents of Nafta when he argued that free trade would "place our farmers and workers in competition with peasant and sweated labor products." Mr. Eckes, while cautious about drawing conclusions, notes the trouble caused for doctrinaire free-trade theory by the fact that the economy grew more rapidly during the high-tariff era than during the post-World War II period of tariff reduction.
During Franklin D. Roosevelt's Administration, the traditional Southern Democratic goal of free trade was promoted by Secretary of State Cordell Hull (himself a Southerner, a former Senator from Tennessee). After World War II, Hull's vision was realized as the United States, formerly the world's leading protectionist nation, led successive rounds of trade liberalization negotiations under the auspices of the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade (GATT). In doing so the United States, as the superpower leader of a global anti-Soviet alliance, had geopolitical as well as economic goals. "With the executive, not Congress, managing trade policy," Mr. Eckes writes, "tariff policy became another instrument of cold war foreign relations." He notes that "time after time during the cold war years, when policy makers confronted a conflict between foreign policy and domestic interests, the diplomatic imperative prevailed."
With the end of the cold war, the national security rationale for tolerating deviations from free trade by allies like Japan has diminished. At the same time, many Americans whose wages are threatened by low-wage producers abroad are giving their votes to economic nationalists like Ross Perot and Patrick Buchanan. "In an open world economy with no common standards governing working and environmental conditions," Mr. Eckes writes, "many believed that the transfer of high-paying factory jobs to developing countries would bring about the downward harmonization of wages and incomes that 19th-century Republicans had foreseen."
The most controversial section of "Opening America's Market" may be Mr. Eckes's revisionist account of the Smoot-Hawley Tariff Act of 1930, which is often blamed today for causing everything from the Great Depression to World War II. The author shows that while Smoot-Hawley raised duties on some items, it lowered or eliminated them on many others. "Except under the Underwood schedules during World War I, no tariff before or after Smoot-Hawley permitted such a large percentage of U.S. imports, by value, to enter duty-free." By a careful examination of American diplomatic correspondence, Mr. Eckes destroys the often-repeated myth that Smoot-Hawley provoked a wave of discrimination against American exports. "Why have textbook authors, editorial writers and public officials beat up on the bogy of Smoot-Hawley for three generations?" he asks, and concludes: "Committed to this goal" of free trade, "Wall Street, Washington, corporate America, academic economists and the media elite found it expedient to thrash Smoot-Hawley at each opportunity."
At the moment, Mr. Eckes observes, the post-World War II free-trade orthodoxy still reigns supreme within the American political and academic establishment. "With international contacts proliferating and transnational corporations allocating resources on a world scale, the 'global village' had become an operational reality," he writes. "As the 20th century moved to a close," it was not clear whether the free trade millennium "had finally arrived or was another mirage. Historians of the future would have the perspective to decide." One hopes that future historians will provide their readers with a perspective on the past as helpful as the one Mr. Eckes has given us in "Opening America's Market."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Mexicans Reach New Pact On Wages and Prices
The Mexican Government signed a new agreement with labor and business intended to control prices, limit salaries and cut public spending, in an attempt to restore stability and economic growth. The pact does not include any controls on exchange rates, an issue of concern to both foreign and domestic investors who have been hurt severely by instability in the peso-dollar exchange rate. [Page D2.]
 
$2 Billion Takeover Offer
Praxair, a large producer of industrial gases, offered $2.1 billion for CBI Industries, the world's largest producer of carbon dioxide. Praxair, which was spun off from Union Carbide in 1992, said it would pay $32 a share, a 59 percent premium over the $20.125 closing price of CBI shares on Friday. Praxair made public its offer after six months of unsuccessful talks with CBI, which said it would review the offer. [D2.]
 
Budget Conferees Gather
Republican leaders in the House and Senate said they would meet today to start ironing out differences in the budget bills passed last week. President Clinton has said he will veto the bill, and Senator Bob Kerrey of Nebraska said the Republicans should negotiate with Congressional Democrats instead of focusing on Mr. Clinton. [A1.]
 
Leeson to Return to Singapore
Nicholas W. Leeson, the futures trader accused of ruining Barings of London, said he would return to Singapore to face forgery and fraud charges, ending his fight to prevent extradition from Germany, where he was arrested in March. [D2.]
 
Good News for Insurers
The cost of environmental cleanups is not quite the disaster for insurance companies that was once feared, a number of studies have found, in part because claims for restoration of natural resources, like lakes polluted by acid rain, have not materialized. [D2.]
 
Cable Prices Climb
Prices for cable television service have resumed their rise, even as Congress is completing legislation that would unfetter the industry. [D7.]
 
All the Nostalgia, All the Time
Hoping to capitalize on the success of its Nick at Nite cable channel, MTV Networks plans to offer a cable channel early next year that is devoted to nostalgic television shows. [D7.]
 
Sybase Seeks a Turnaround
Sybase is hoping that its latest version of its SQL Server will restore its competitive edge -- and its earnings growth. [D4.]
 
Forget the Aston Martin
BMW of North America and its new advertising agency have developed an aggressive campaign that features Pierce Brosnan, who as James Bond drives a BMW in the latest 007 film. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D10.]
 
Turning On the Lights
Experiments are under way to demonstrate that chunks of silicon can be used to run the nation's electrical power network much the way a computer is operated. [D5.]
 
When Systems Crash
The Food and Drug Administration has spent $13 million on a computer system to help it identify tainted food and medicine from abroad, but the General Accounting Office said that the system doesn't work. [A13.]

HEADLINE: A State Tries to Rein In a Prison Awash in Drugs

BYLINE: By MATTHEW PURDY 

DATELINE: GRATERFORD, Pa., Oct. 26

 BODY:
In February, the Pennsylvania authorities paroled Robert Simon, despite his being a convicted killer and known prison drug user who had killed another inmate in self-defense.
Three months later, back on the street, Mr. Simon was accused of another killing, this time the shooting of a New Jersey police officer who had stopped him and a fellow motorcycle gang member in Gloucester County for a traffic violation. 
 When the authorities in Pennsylvania began looking into why Mr. Simon was set free, the trail led back to the state prison here, where officials say the case is another sign of the widespread drug trade, mismanagement and possible corruption at the crowded maximum-security prison outside of Philadelphia.
In the view of state corrections officials, it was as if the prison had fallen into enemy hands. Last Monday, 650 state troopers and corrections officers from other prisons deployed in a nighttime surprise assault on Graterford. During the next 72 hours, they searched all 3,500 inmates, the prison's staff and its 1,700-acre grounds, looking for drugs and weapons. As a result, nine ranking officers at the prison retired or were transferred, and 21 inmates suspected of drug trafficking were moved to other prisons.
The shakedown, in which about 200 homemade weapons and more than 60 caches of drugs were seized, was unusual in scope. But the extreme action was an indication of the frustration many corrections officials and politicians feel at the constant battle to keep drugs out of prisons, especially as tougher laws are sending drug-addicted criminals away for longer sentences.
The question that prompted the raid -- "Who runs the prison?" in the words of Martin F. Horn, Pennsylvania's Corrections Commissioner -- is a common one for prison administrators. Corrections officials across the country say controlling prisons is becoming more difficult because of increasing inmate populations, harder-core prisoners and tighter budgets. Earlier this month, inmates were ordered confined to their cells at Federal prisons after uprisings at five sites.
The drug economy in prisons not only threatens peace among inmates but also dangles temptation before guards. In the last year at the Federal prison in Atlanta, for example, several employees have been arrested on drug charges and there has been a spate of violence including the first killing of a corrections officer at the prison since 1987.
[On Friday, Federal prosecutors announced the indictments of four corrections officers, four inmates and two others on charges of trying to smuggle marijuana, cocaine and heroin into the Atlanta prison.]
"In large systems, it's almost a truism that you're going to have some drug trafficking," said a former Texas prison official and a consultant on prison conditions, Steve Martin. "But when it seeps into your security staff you have serious problems."
Gov. Tom Ridge of Pennsylvania, who approved the search at Graterford, said, "The first thing we need to do is control the prisons, and we weren't in control of that one."
Officials said that in Mr. Simon's case, a complete record of his prison misdeeds was not in his file when the parole decision was made. Inmates told a recent State Senate hearing that it was common practice among prisoners at Graterford to avoid punishment for infractions and to cover up evidence of drug use by paying officers to alter records.
At the hearing, an inmate who was a clerk at Graterford from 1992 to this January, Jonathan Brown, told the State Senate Judiciary Committee about officers' selling furloughs and other privileges, and about drug trafficking involving officers. He said Mr. Simon paid officers to cover up his disciplinary infractions, though the allegations have not been proved and are under investigation.
"The fact that he was allegedly selling and using dope was not in his file," said the committee chairman, Senator Stewart J. Greenleaf.
Mr. Simon's case was just more evidence of trouble at Graterford, one of the largest state prisons in the country. Since 1989, 13 staff members have been arrested on charges of trying to smuggle drugs, 11 inmates have died of drug overdoses and about 20 percent of the urine tests done on prisoners each month show signs of drug use.
In March, three inmates were found in a cell, all with hypodermic needles and all unconscious from drug overdoses, Mr. Horn said. They recovered, and one took another nonfatal overdose the next night.
Calling the drug trade in prison "enormously frustrating," Mr. Horn said it "gives the lie to everything we're trying to do in terms of rehabilitation."
The search had been planned since April by Mr. Horn and his staff without the knowledge of Graterford officials. In the raid, officers with drug-sniffing dogs removed inmates from their cells one at a time, searching the cells, strip-searching the inmates and then moving to the next cell.
Officials say they might have found more drugs but many inmates flushed them down toilets after hearing about the search.
"I suspect all of the bass and trout in the streams connected to that sewage system are jumping far out of the water," Mr. Ridge said.
Mr. Horn was realistic about the search's effect. "The minute we walk away from here the inmates are going to start trying to figure out how to get drugs," he said.
Although few deny that Graterford has a drug problem, some prisoner advocates say the search, with all of its drama, had political motivations. The Simon case, which led to an overhaul of the state parole board, came less than a year after the release of a Pennsylvania inmate, Reginald McFadden, who went on to kill and rape on Long Island and in Rockland County, N.Y.
In last year's election, Mr. Ridge criticized his opponent, Lieut. Gov. Mark S. Singel, for voting in favor of the McFadden parole as a member of the parole board.
Drug use at Graterford is both long running and well known.
"There was a period of time at Graterford that I smoked a joint when I woke up, at lunch, at dinner and when I went to bed," said Kenneth Tervalon, 49, who was released in 1993 and says he has stopped using drugs.
Although just 10 percent of Pennsylvania's state prisoners are at Graterford, the prison accounts for 25 percent of inmate attacks on staff members and a third of inmate drug infractions, said Mr. Horn, who headed the parole system in New York State before becoming Commissioner in March.
Donald T. Vaughn, the superintendent at Graterford, said the drug problem was caused largely by its proximity to Philadelphia, which is 30 miles to the southeast. The drug market behind Graterford's 30-foot wall mirrors the city's.
Mr. Vaughn acknowledged that his staff might not have provided the "intense supervision" required for the prison. He said he had asked the corrections department for help in the past but not yet received it.
Mr. Horn said he would leave Mr. Vaughn in charge.
With 85 percent of the inmates from Philadelphia or neighboring counties, there are frequent crowds in the visiting room, a key entry point of drugs in most prisons. Drugs packed in small balloons are passed mouth-to-mouth when inmates kiss female visitors and inmates swallow them and pass them later. Although visitors are searched before coming in, they have been known to hide drugs in babies' diapers or in their own body cavities.
Inmates and others say Graterford has a more casual atmosphere than other prisons, which may contribute to the drug problem.
Prisoners are allowed to walk down the halls freely, not in straight lines under close supervision as is required in other prisons. And the number of volunteer workers -- almost 400 -- gives inmates many chances to make contact with people from the outside.
"If you don't want to go to work, you just go out to the yard," said Steven Blackburn, an inmate for 12 years there until 1991. "Compared to a lot of other institutions around the state, Graterford is still considered loose."
Whatever that buys in inmate peace, it has its downside. On Aug. 4, an inmate serving a life sentence for murder hid on a bread truck leaving the prison. He was recaptured 18 days later, but his escape was made possible because no one reported that he failed to show up for his job that day or that he was in the delivery area without authorization, Mr. Horn said.
Some inmates have been allowed to acquire enormous power. Ameen Jabbar, a gang leader in Philadelphia, has led Graterford's Muslim movement, officials said, controlling part of the prison's drug trade.
A prison guard, Lieut. Cynthia Link, said that when the lights in the prison's mosque suddenly went out one night two years ago the 100 inmates ignored her order to leave. Finally, one of them said, "Ms. Link, these people aren't going to move until Ameen tells them to."
"I said, 'Well, go get Ameen, tell him I need him,' " Lieutenant Link recounted. He came, told the prisoners to leave and they did.
Last week, Mr. Jabbar was one of the inmates transferred out of Graterford.
