HEADLINE: Detroit Wages All-Out Fight To Stop Devil's Night Arson

BYLINE: By JAMES BENNET 

DATELINE: DETROIT, Oct. 29

 BODY:
Once more, with new depth of feeling, Detroit's leaders are pleading with its residents not to try to burn their city down on the night before Halloween.
After being surprised and embarrassed by a surge in arson last year, the city is hoping to snuff the bizarre, heartbreaking ritual known as Devil's Night and substitute Angel's Night, using curfews, dances for teen-agers, bans on containers of gasoline and neighborhood patrols. 
 Prompted, they say, by reawakening pride in a reawakening city and by the lessons of the Million Man March, some 25,000 volunteers have responded to the city's call for help, and the list is expected to grow. Officials say the outpouring -- from churches, businesses and at least one motorcycle gang -- has been so strong that this year they are not even praying for rain.
"I'm looking for good weather," said the executive assistant to Mayor Dennis W. Archer who is director of public safety, Emmett R. Baylor Jr. "If we get 20 degrees and rain, we won't know if we can conquer this thing."
Detroit has been watching the urban revival across Lake Erie in Cleveland with a blend of envy and hope, as it plots its own economic renaissance. Residents fret that a fiery Devil's Night would set back the city's fragile recovery.
On Friday, an important piece of Mayor Archer's turnaround plan fell in place as the city announced that it had secured public and private financing for a $235 million baseball stadium downtown. Mr. Archer is betting that the open-air, natural turf park, for the Tigers, will do for the city what similar stadiums have done for Cleveland and Baltimore.
The stadium is the largest but only the latest in a series of new developments planned for the city. Just 10 days ago, Standard Federal Bank said it wanted to build 400 houses for middle-class families along the Detroit River on the west side, the biggest housing development here since World War II.
But residents fear that Devil's Night could scare off businesses and developers by provoking another bruising of the city's image by news organizations.
"It's almost how they get their kicks," Loretta Williams, who has signed up to help patrol her west side neighborhood, said about reporters. Detroit is treated "almost like we're a foreign country," she said.
For years, Detroit appeared to have doused Devil's Night. But the city relaxed its vigilance last year, notwithstanding another official attempt at a nomenclature change, to "Halloween Eve." It was rewarded with the biggest outbreak of fires since 1986, as the number reached 182, almost triple the level in 1993. "The citizens didn't come out," said the executive fire commissioner, Harold D. Watkins Sr. "They felt we had it under control."
Abandoned and occupied homes, parked cars, and piles of garbage and brush were torched. One blaze killed an 18-month-old girl, Destiny Wilson.
The fires were a blow to Mayor Archer, who had swept into office in 1993 pledging to revive moribund city services. This year he is not taking chances. The city has imposed a curfew on children and teen-agers under 17 from 6 P.M. Monday to 6 A.M. Tuesday. Violators will be immediately prosecuted, the city says, facing $100 fines or 40 hours of community service.
Another emergency measure forbids dispensing gasoline into portable containers from Saturday through Halloween. Although Devil's Night peaks on the night before Halloween, the arson tends to spread over three nights, through Oct. 31.
But the backbone of the efforts are the volunteers, who have promised to watch empty homes and to walk the streets.
"We want to put an end to the self-destructive behavior," said the Rev. Lawrence T. Foster of the Calvary Baptist Church, on Detroit's lower east side, adding that the Million Man March -- "like spending a day in heaven," he called it -- had helped encourage 25 members of his congregation to volunteer so far.
"There's no doubt in my mind that we're going to create a new Detroit and we're going to give Halloween Eve a new name," Mr. Foster said. "We're going to change it from a night of mischief to a night of constructive energy and fellowship."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Firefighters hosed down abandoned buildings in Detroit yesterday to try to prevent serious fires on the night before Halloween. The city is making a new effort to stop the self-destructive Devil's Night tradition. (Peter Yates for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Mideast Leaders Talk, This Time of Business

BYLINE: By SERGE SCHMEMANN 

DATELINE: AMMAN, Jordan, Oct. 29

 BODY:
More than 1,000 officials and business leaders from around the world gathered here today to explore ways of moving from Arab-Israeli peace agreements to business deals.
Greeting a packed house of monarchs, sheiks, government ministers and executives in the modern Palace of Culture, King Hussein of Jordan set the theme of the largest such gathering ever held in the Middle East when he declared that people in the region must "realize the importance of peace through tangible and positive change in their standards of living." 
 But for all the glossy proposals and optimistic talk, there were also reminders that this is still the Middle East.
Syria and Lebanon, the two Arab neighbors that have yet to make a peace deal with the Israelis, were conspicuously absent, as were Libya, Iraq and Iran.
An army of soldiers and police ringed the conference center and hotels. Rivalry between Jordan and Egypt, and between Israel and the Palestinians, burst briefly into the open, and one major project, a Middle East development bank, failed to generate much support from European or Persian Gulf countries.
And although neither Israeli nor Arab officials made any public comment, the news of the assassination in Malta last week of Fathi Shiqaqi, the leader of the militant Islamic Jihad -- and the expectation of retaliation against Israel -- was a reminder that the threat of violence still hovers over the region.
But for all that, the mood was distinctly upbeat and pragmatic as representatives of 60 countries mingled in the lobbies or attended seminars on regional development and doing business.
"For too long, this has been a region of warriors and widows," said Secretary of State Warren Christopher, who represented the United States along with Commerce Secretary Ronald H. Brown. "Let it again become a region of builders and traders."
Prime Minister Yitzhak Rabin of Israel, who led a team of 5 government ministers, 60 other officials and 100 Israeli businessmen, echoed the call to do business.
"No one will come here because of our winning smiles," he said. "They all want to make money."
The Israeli Government arrived with a package of regional development proposals, many involving Jordan and the new Palestinian homeland that has begun taking shape in the West Bank and Gaza Strip.
These included the construction of new bridges over the Jordan River, the development of tourism around the Dead Sea and the Mediterranean coast, the creation of special commercial regions and a program to link the electrical systems of Israel, Egypt, Jordan and the Palestinian lands.
The greater hope of the conference organizers was on private business. The Amman conference came a year after the first such regional summit, in Casablanca, where the stress was on the very novelty of Arab and Israeli businessmen getting together. The intention this time was to produce actual business contacts.
Accordingly, the emphasis of the conference was on small workshops on specific subjects like "textiles and apparel" or "information superhighway and telecommunications," where participants could meet with executives and officials in their field.
"There's a lot of people here looking for specific deals," said Christopher Keen, the London-based general manager of the United Bank of Kuwait. "The list of organizations and the level of representatives is definitely higher than in Casablanca."
But there was also ample evidence that the rivalries and tensions that have plagued the Middle East were far from overcome.
The absence of Syria and Lebanon was the most conspicuous. Mr. Christopher planned to fly on Monday to Damascus on his way home to meet with President Hafez al-Assad, but he held out little hope of breaking the stalemate in Syrian-Israeli negotiations.
"I don't expect any major developments to come out of that meeting," Mr. Christopher acknowledged. "Really it's just to bring me up to date on their own thinking."
One of the major projects before the conference had been the establishment of a regional development bank, and Mr. Christopher announced that one would be set up with headquarters in Cairo.
But resistance from Western European governments, which see the bank as an extension of American influence at the expense of existing development banks, and from Arab oil states, which thought they were being asked to finance an institution that would primarily benefit Israel, left the proposed bank with little capital and limited backing.
Two exchanges in the opening session also revealed the rivalries that are never far below the surface in the Middle East.
One erupted when Foreign Minister Amre Moussa of Egypt, which has looked with annoyance at the rapid development of Israeli-Jordanian cooperation, cautioned in his speech against "chasing" after peace.
The Arab word he used implied an irresponsible rush, and King Hussein unexpectedly asked for the floor.
There were some who have had peace for 17 years, the King said with an edge in his voice, referring to the Egyptian-Israeli agreement, and it was necessary for others to catch up.
Another exchange ensued when Yasir Arafat, the chairman of the Palestine Liberation Organization, assailed the vote in the United States Congress mandating the move of the American Embassy from Tel Aviv to Jerusalem, calling it a "very dangerous precedent" and a "violent violation" of United Nations Security Council resolutions.
Mr. Rabin was the next to speak, and he noted that it took him only 20 minutes to fly from Jerusalem -- "the capital of Israel" -- to Amman.

HEADLINE: CALI DRUG CARTEL USING U.S. BUSINESS TO LAUNDER CASH

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD KRAUSS and DOUGLAS FRANTZ 

 BODY:
Frustrated by Government crackdowns on banks that once helped Colombian drug traffickers funnel their profits back home, the leaders of the Cali cartel are processing the billions of dollars they reap in cocaine sales in the United States through a wide variety of American companies.
In recent months, investigators in New York, Los Angeles, Houston and Miami have uncovered a range of intricate schemes in which Colombian drug kingpins are using scores of American companies -- large and small, witting and unwitting -- to help launder their illicit profits. 
 "The cartels have been moving into trade because we have been working to tighten up the financial sector," said Robert S. Gelbard, Assistant Secretary of State for International Narcotics and Law Enforcement Affairs. "It's become increasingly important that companies know who they are doing business with."
In a single continuing New York investigation, Federal Customs Service officials said, they have identified 105 American companies that accepted drug money for electronics, auto parts and other goods shipped to Colombia. They will not name the companies, they said, until the investigation is completed.
United States officials estimate that Cali traffickers are using such trade-related schemes to process about half of the $6 billion a year in cocaine revenue that goes from the United States to Colombia, with billions more being sent through third countries like Mexico.
In addition, the Manhattan District Attorney's office is just beginning an investigation into more than a dozen small costume-jewelry exporters operating along Broadway below midtown and in the Times Square area, as well as several other electronic and furniture companies around the borough that are suspected of knowingly or unknowingly accepting drug cash.
And Scotland Yard, working with Federal and New York prosecutors, has been looking into still more money laundering schemes in which cartel operatives buy and sell paintings at art galleries in London and New York, a senior American law-enforcement official said.
While the schemes can be complex, the concept is simple. Just as drug merchants need to move their product into the United States, they need to move their earnings south and convert them into pesos.
In the early days of the drug trade, profits were small and easily transported in suitcases. But when America's appetite for drugs exploded in the 1970's and the volume of business reached into the hundreds of millions of dollars, the drug exporters needed more efficient means of sending the money home.
In the new schemes, cartel intermediaries typically contact companies in Colombia that import goods from the United States. The intermediaries offer to pay for the imported goods with dollars that go to the American exporters. In return, the importers pay intermediaries an equivalent amount inside Colombia. For participating in the scheme, the importers pay intermediaries slightly less than the true exchange rate.
In other cases, the cartels have turned to old-fashioned methods similar to those used to smuggle drugs into the United States -- concealing dollars inside real products or in false bottoms of containers.
Officials say the increase in the trade-related schemes is so great, they have helped improve the United States' balance of trade with Colombia. Ten years ago, the country had a $5 billion trade deficit; last year it had a $5 billion surplus. Clinton Administration officials estimate that a major portion of the increase in exports is paid for with tainted money.
"What makes them so difficult to detect is that these operations are cloaked within what appear to be legitimate businesses, with legitimate inventories, sales and customers," said Eric Friedberg, the chief of narcotics prosecution in the United States Attorney's Office in Brooklyn. "The drug money can be easily mixed with legal sales of the company."
Last week, the Clinton Administration singled out 33 Colombian companies -- from drug store chains to automobile dealers -- that President Clinton said were fronts for the Cali cartel, and ordered United States companies to stop trading with them.
Explaining the decision in a speech to the United Nations, he said, "I directed our Government to identify the front companies to freeze the assets of the largest drug ring in the world, the Cali cartel, to cut off its economic lifelines and stop our own people from dealing unknowingly with its companies."
The Drug Enforcment Administration compiled the list of Colombian front companies after law-enforcement agents interrogated a former cartel accountant and inspected computer logs that were captured along with six cartel leaders recently arrested by the Colombian police. The agency has refused to divulge the names of the American companies, which have been ordered to change their trading partners.
 
Trafficking
The Cash Flow Of Cocaine
But Greg Passic, a special agent at the Treasury Department's Financial Crimes Enforcement Network, said that wholesale representatives of many American companies, including General Electric, Microsoft, Apple Computer and General Motors, sold goods to Colombian importers they did not know were Cali fronts. In other instances, officials said, smaller private American firms or franchises knowingly accepted cash generated by drug sales.
Cocaine trafficking is widely regarded as the most lucrative criminal enterprise, and the cascade of cash constantly requires new means of moving money to Colombia.
Through the early 1980's, the task was relatively easy as many banks turned a blind eye toward cash transactions while law enforcement concentrated on interrupting the flow of drugs into the country. The first modest crackdown came in 1985, when dozens of American banks paid fines after admitting they had not filed reports on millions of dollars worth of cash deposits.
Cali cartel leaders like Gilberto Rodriguez Orejuela set up vast money laundering networks in Jackson Heights, Elmhurst and Corona, Queens, in the late 1970's and 1980's, according to Drug Enforcement Administration intelligence reports.
 
How It Moves
Faxes, Beepers, Brown Paper Bags
A routine traffic stop on the Sprain Brook Parkway last year led to a break in one cartel trade scheme.
Inside a black 1985 Chevrolet Camaro a state police trooper discovered $40,000 in small bills, postal money orders worth $16,000 and records of extensive financial transactions with Colombia. The driver, Hector Fabio Gualteros, told the police the money and records were part of his work as an independent distributor of Herbalife International health-care products.
Agents with the State Organized Crime Task Force and the Federal Customs Service placed Mr. Gualteros under surveillance, eventually monitoring 22 telephone lines, 8 beepers and 13 fax lines. In weeks, their suspicions were confirmed.
As described in court papers and interviews, the ring collected drug money for the Cali cartel and paid it to small New York City companies exporting costume jewelry and watches. Cash was often left in brown paper bags.
Seven companies that received the drug money were identified in court papers as High Top Enterprises, Almar Sales, Axelrod Inc., Proyectos Z Corp., Ruby International, AAA Watch and Royal Watch Company, but none was charged with wrongdoing because officials concluded they were unaware of the scheme. Some of the companies were in nondescript offices on Broadway and Seventh Avenue, while four other companies receiving such tainted cash were in Miami and Los Angeles.
The investigation ended earlier this year with the arrest of six people, including Mr. Gualteros and Libardo Moncado, a Colombian importer, who was arrested on a trip to Miami. All pleaded guilty in Federal Court in Brooklyn. Prosecutors said Herbalife was not implicated.
Records show that payments were directed to the companies by coded fax and beeper messages from Cali bosses in Colombia. The authorities say the operation laundered about $4 million in six months.
It also had a violent side.
On Sept. 1, 1994, midway through the inquiry, state investigators arrested two men and seized what they suspected was $157,000 in drug cash. To keep the investigation alive, the woman who delivered the money, Cleofe Ojeda, was not arrested.
Two weeks later, she was found dead in an apartment in Forest Hills, Queens. Investigators think Ms. Ojeda may well have been executed for losing the money.
 
How It Works
Taking a Trip To a Restaurant
Besides employing innovative trade schemes, authorities say drug traffickers are shipping cash in everything from used cars and dolls to television sets and refrigerated containers of bull semen. When a forklift accidentally punctured a shipping pallet in a warehouse on West 32d Street, workers were shocked to see $100 bills tumble out.
A sharp jump in seizures of drug currency -- to $314.4 million in the last fiscal year from $250.4 million in fiscal 1993 -- suggests that the amount of cash shipments is rising.
"We're seeing an increase in the amount of physical transportation of illicit drug money as Government investigations and regulations close loopholes in money laundering vehicles such as casinos, real estate brokers, attorneys, banks and even stockbrokers," said Robert E. Van Etten, the special agent in charge of the Customs Service in New York.
One of the most elaborate operations uncovered involved small warehouses and sham export businesses in suburban industrial parks in New Jersey, Houston and Miami, Customs officials say.
On the New Jersey end, money from drug sales in New York was collected at safe houses after which a coded call to a beeper set up a meeting at a McDonald's on Route 22 in Union, N.J. In a hallmark of the cartels, the operation was carefully compartmentalized so no single arrest would bring down the operation.
A driver arrived at the restaurant in a Mazda pickup truck, cash concealed in compartments beneath the liner of the bed. Another person met him and drove the truck to an apartment in Westfield, leaving the driver to wait. After the cash was unloaded, the truck was returned to the McDonald's. A third person counted and packaged the cash and yet a fourth person transferred it to a warehouse in neighboring Springfield.
Inside the small warehouse was Reno International, what appeared to be a normal business. Machinery had been installed to wrap thin sheets of fiberglass around large spools, and a handful of workers showed up daily. But Reno was a front. Inside each roll of fiberglass were compartments to conceal $700,000 in small bills; officials said the operation could launder $2 million a day.
The organizer prepared so carefully that he first researched the fiberglass industry down to the finest detail and then made two test shipments to Colombia and the Caribbean island of Aruba to see whether it would attract attention.
"They did their research," said Martin D. Ficke, assistant special agent in charge of the Customs Service office in Newark.
With the help of an informant, the authorities uncovered the operation and arrested nine people last year. Among them was Juan B. Munera, 41, a Colombian living in Miami.
Mr. Ficke said Mr. Munera had set up similar warehouses in Miami and Houston for the Cali and Medellin cartels. He described them as money-laundering franchises which were turned over to the cartels once operational. Mr. Munera, who has pleaded guilty to money laundering, collected $1 million for each franchise.

HEADLINE: Fish Dealers Tell of Slump In Business

BYLINE: By SELWYN RAAB 

 BODY:
In the dense darkness before dawn, Herbie Ackerman peered gloomily at the mounds of 100-pound crates of unsold perch and salmon stacked in front of his Fulton Fish Market stall.
'It's not a pretty picture, and it's a difficult time for us," he said. 
 The 68-year-old Mr. Ackerman, owner of the Messing Fish Company, has hawked seafood for 42 years, and other dealers value him as one of the market's best judges of trends in the risky art of selling fish wholesale.
On Friday, he was uneasy about the market's prospects. Dealers like him, he said, are having to cope with a new system of unloading seafood that New York City officials imposed this month in a campaign to end reported mob corruption.
Wholesale prices are sharply higher, he said, and seafood supplies and sales are down. Consumers in the New York area should expect prices to keep climbing, at least in the short run, Mr. Ackerman warned.
He said that his normal sales of 35,000 pounds of seafood on a busy day had dipped 30 percent in the last two weeks. Many of the market's 58 other dealers say their businesses also have nose-dived since a wildcat strike brought chaos briefly to the market on Oct. 16, the first day of the new unloading system.
On Oct. 13, the city abruptly ejected six companies that had unloaded supplies for decades, saying that they had been operating a cartel with an economic choke hold over the flow of 125 million pounds of seafood into the market every year. Some truckers had long complained that the companies demanded payoffs for speedy unloading of their highly perishable products.
The ousted companies were replaced by the Laro Maintenance Company of Bay Shore, L.I., which had no experience in handling seafood. Under Laro's system, trucks are unloaded on a first-come, first-served basis and wholesalers' fees were cut by 20 percent.
Some dealers blame the city's unloading plan and Laro's inexperience for their sluggish business. They say that the new crews misplace and damage shipments, create congestion and deter shippers and buyers from using the market.
But most merchants say it is more than that. They blame a combination of factors that coincided with the introduction of the new unloading system for reducing supplies and raising wholesale costs: catches in the Gulf of Mexico and in the North Atlantic have been substantially cut by bad weather, they said, and many suppliers in Southern states and in New England have bypassed New York in the last two weeks or sent smaller cargoes to avoid possible losses from new labor disruptions.
Dealers say their problems have been compounded by dropping retail sales, apparently caused by consumer fears that the market is gripped by disorders and is selling stale products at inflated prices.
And the dealers claim that their incomes depend mainly on volume, not on sales. Thus, with less seafood available and with demand dropping, all of them are suffering.
The market, mainly on city property next to the South Street Seaport, generates about $1 billion a year in sales, salaries and related businesses. It is the nation's largest wholesale seafood distribution center.
City officials insist that the kinks in the new unloading system have been smoothed out, and that it is more efficient. According to city records, 329 refrigerated tractor-trailers were unloaded last week, compared with 298 in the final week of the old system.
"You can quibble over the amounts being brought in, but the market is operating well and there have been no incidents since the first night," said Randy M. Mastro, chief of staff in the Giuliani administration. "We have cut unloading fees by 20 percent and the carting costs by 40 percent. Eventually, that has to reduce or hold down costs for consumers."
Joseph Niece, the owner of the Sweetwater Sea Food Corporation, a major supplier of shellfish based in in Carlstadt, N.J., said that the high retail prices should level off in a few weeks. "If there are no more unloading debacles," he added, "the shippers will have to come back."
In Miami, Sonny Kravitz, the vice president of Solymar Fisheries International, said he had reduced his weekly shipments of grouper, mahi-mahi and red snapper to 8,000 pounds from 30,000 pounds.
"With catches in the Gulf and Central America improving, prices in New York should drop in a week or two," Mr. Kravitz said.
The Laro company offered jobs to the 40 employees of the ousted companies, but none applied. On Thursday, Gennaro Prisinzano, the former president of a dismissed company, PAS&G Rapid Unloading, was arrested at the market on charges of trying to intimidate Laro employees.
The rejected unloaders, meanwhile, have sued in State Supreme Court to regain their contracts. "Giuliani's people are cooking the books about the level of shipments into the market," said Gerald J. McMahon, a lawyer for the unloaders. "City Hall may appear to be winning but the war is not over, and I fully expect the old unloaders will be returning before Christmas."
To protect Laro's crews, the Police Department has assigned more than 100 officers to the market.
Some dealers said the first-come, first-served unloading system does not allow for a variety of fish and shellfish to be quickly available.
On Friday, with a fishhook dangling from his belt and a Yankees cap on his head, Mr. Ackerman complained that sloppy unloading by Laro crews had forced him to hire two more helpers, on Mondays and Thursdays, the busiest days.
"They're getting the fish unloaded, but not in an orderly manner," he said of the Laro employees. "It's costing me about $800 a week in extra salaries."
Under a law that went into effect last month, the city can license all market businesses and workers. So far, the city has licensed 46 wholesale dealers and is reviewing the backgrounds of 13 companies that are still operating in the market.
The next priority for the administration is a decision on licensing or removing 10 companies, known as loaders, that provide parking spaces and guard services for buyers.
Most dealers say a change in loaders could lead to slowdowns in loading and more problems.
On Oct. 17, at a hearing in Federal District Court in Manhattan on the unloading dispute, Judge Thomas P. Griesa encouraged city officials to heed the dealers' concerns.
"If you don't," Judge Griesa cautioned, "bingo -- $1 billion in business in this city is gone."

HEADLINE: As Deregulation Is Debated, Cable Prices Outpace Inflation

BYLINE: By GERALDINE FABRIKANT 

 BODY:
Prices for cable television service have begun to creep back up, even as a House-Senate conference committee negotiates final details of telecommunications legislation that would unfetter the cable industry.
Cable prices rose 4.1 percent during the 12-month period that ended in September, according to the most recent statistics released by the Department of Labor, compared with a 2.5 percent increase in the Consumer Price Index during that time. 
 The cable increase came after more than a year of declining cable costs, resulting from the 1992 legislation that sought to tighten control on cable rates.
But while the White House indicated on Friday that it would take an even harder look at the proposed telecommunications reform package, the rate increases have failed to attract the aggressive consumer criticism that was a major motivation behind the 1992 law.
To the contrary, cable is luring new subscribers at impressive rates. The industry's subscriber rosters increased 4.26 percent, to 62.2 million, in the 12-month period that ended in July. The number of subscribers increased 2.6 percent in the 12 months that ended in July 1994.
Larry Haverty, a senior vice president at State Street Research in Boston who follows the cable industry, said that cable companies promise rich returns in the not too distant future.
"We are now expecting double-digit growth in cable cash flow from every major cable operator," he said. "Subscriber rates are creeping up, growth has been vigorous and the companies have done well at controlling costs."
Cable operators have also taken a more sophisticated approach to marketing. As an example, Viacom Inc. is promoting its Nickelodeon children's cable service at its Blockbuster video stores. Viacom has also teamed up with MCA Inc. to open a Nickelodeon studio at MCA's Universal Theme Park in Orlando, Fla.
Federal rules adopted in the wake of the 1992 law held rate increases for basic cable services in line with inflation, starting last January. But cable operators were permitted to raise rates by as much as $1.50 a month for newly added advertiser-supported services, up to a maximum of six channels.
Some critics of the system, like Barry Orton, a professor of telecommunications at the University of Wisconsin, believe that prices are also rising because the Federal Communications Commission has been relaxing the rules in recent months. He cited a provision in the regulations that gives certain small cable systems far more flexibility to raise prices than their larger counterparts.
But supporters of the new trends argue that consumers are relatively docile because they are receiving additional benefits in return for the higher rates.
A spot check of consumers found little if any revolt, though not everyone was pleased with the current combination of prices and service.
Scott Martell, a Detroit writer who pays $22 a month for basic, no-frills service from Comcast Cable, said he paid $1 a month less now than he did a year ago.
But Rene Graham-Buckner of Rochester Hills, a Detriot suburb, said that she now pays TCI Cablevision $48 a month for the same service that cost her $40 a year ago. The bulk of the increase came from a $4.95 monthly charge for two services, Stars and Encore, that were free a year ago.
Asked about the increases, a company spokesman said: "We are not obligated to discuss rates."
Both Mr. Orton and Gene Kimmelman, a co-director of the Consumers Union, which publishes Consumer Reports, said they expect rates to soar again if the main provisions of the pending legislation become law. "There is a treasure at the end of the road for the cable industry if the bill passes. Naturally, they will curb their greed a bit anticipating that event," Mr. Orton said.
Mr. Kimmelman predicted that if the legislation passes, rates will go up $5 a month. "The language of the new bill gives operators the flexibility to do that," he said.
But Mr. Haverty dismissed such consumer pessimism. "The cable industry has had a good sniff of regulation, and they don't like it," he said. "The result is there is likely to be pricing prudence, even if there is deregulation. Rates will go up 3 or 4 percent, but a lot of the increase will mean better product. At the very end of the day, the people who run major businesses are not world-class stupid."

HEADLINE: New Rules on Ownership for German TV

BYLINE: By NATHANIEL C. NASH 

DATELINE: FRANKFURT, Oct. 25

 BODY:
Almost two years ago, German broadcasters began complaining to regulators that they were in danger of losing control of German television to foreigners with deep pockets.
International media giants like Capital Cities/ABC, the News Corporation and Walt Disney were quietly coming into Germany, the world's largest television market after the United States, and buying pieces of existing channels or starting small, specialized channels. 
 German media groups feared that the competition could eventually wrest away control of big nationwide channels. The broadcasters did not get the urgent measures they sought, but finally, earlier this month, they were pleasantly surprised by government action that in the short run will give them the chance to consolidate their own entrenched positions in Europe's largest and arguably most coveted television market.
But the short-term gains may have a price, as the law would also give their foreign competition greater opportunities to move into the market over the medium and long term.
At a recent meeting, the governments of the country's 16 states, which regulate broadcasting, agreed to change the country's ownership rules, which had sharply limited the ability of individual companies to acquire substantial stakes in German channels.
The states' leaders agreed to allow investors in general interest and all-news channels to own 100 percent of one channel, compared with 49.9 percent before, and as much as 50 percent of subsequent outlets, provided the overall market penetration of an owner's channels does not exceed 30 percent.
"Practically speaking, if you can't control the voting stock in a channel you can't control programming content or editorial content," said Dirk Reiter, head of the media group at Roland Berger & Partners, a management consulting firm in Frankfurt. "It's an issue not only of management control over content, but also of being able to compete with outside investors."
The new law is likely to open the German market to a host of new competitors, but at first the leading German media companies, like the Kirch Group of Bavaria and the giant conglomerate Bertelsmann A.G., will probably move to lock in their control of the country's biggest channels.
Because foreigners will compete under the same ownership rules, however, American companies will be able to build a larger presence and compete for dominance. "Takeovers and new alliances will be the outcome," said Roland Berger, head of the Berger consulting firm, in the weekly magazine Der Spiegel. "The competition will be merciless."
Nonetheless, the law should insure that the $4.4 billion broadcasting business, one of the country's fastest-growing industries, will continue to generate jobs inside Germany, which because of its high wages has seen unemployment rise sharply and manufacturing jobs move abroad.
"We have to be careful that this growth creates jobs in Germany and that jobs are not exported abroad," said Bavaria's Premier, Edmund Stoiber, a key backer of the new law.
The interest of foreign companies is not just in the advertising revenue generated by the nationwide channels. The companies are investing at a time when Germany, which has Europe's wealthiest viewership, is deregulating its entire telecommunications industry, clearing the way for highly lucrative businesses like telemarketing, pay-per-view and on-line services.
The law is scheduled to be made final on Dec. 14.
The old regulations were put in place in the early 1980's, when commercial broadcasting first began in Germany with a handful of private channels and regulators feared the effects of one owner's dominating the business. Now, with 25 nationwide channels, those fears have diminished.
But concern about foreign control of German broadcasting companies has increased. Outsiders own large stakes in almost 40 percent of the German broadcasting properties not owned by the state, according to some media experts. (The state owns two networks, ARD and ZDF, which together control almost 40 percent of the television market.)
For example, the Walt Disney Company, after its acquisition of Capital Cities/ABC Inc. is completed, will have a 16.5 percent ownership in RTL2, a general interest channel; a 50 percent stake in Super RTL, an entertainment channel, and stakes in Eurosport, a European sport channel, and in Frauenkanal, a specialized channel aimed at women.
Time Warner Inc.'s planned merger with Turner Broadcasting System Inc. will mean that the combined company will own more than 50 percent of Germany's only all-news channel, N-TV. Time Warner also has holdings in two local channels near Hamburg and Berlin, and two successful popular music channels, Viva 1 and Viva 2. Analysts say the new law probably means that the combined company will be allowed to keep the larger stake in the news channel.
The News Corporation, which is controlled by Rupert Murdoch, owns almost 50 percent of VOX, the country's sixth-largest commercial channel.
By far the largest foreign presence in German television is that of a Luxembourg media consortium called CLT, which owns almost half of RTL, Germany's largest commercial channel. CLT also has stakes in RTL2 and Super RTL, giving it a market penetration of about 24 percent.
Ralf Beke-Bramkamp, a spokesman for Bertelsmann, which also owns a stake in RTL, said: "We do not complain about this foreign presence in the German market, because we are also operating in more than 40 other countries. But those countries do not have the same strict regulations that we have in Germany. So we see this change as very important."
Still, German companies dominate the largest private channels, and the new law should help them consolidate their grip on these cash cows.
To begin with, if the two Government-controlled networks are privatized, as many argue they should be, they are sure to be sold into German hands.
In addition, the Kirch family already controls almost 30 percent of the German market. Leo Kirch has major stakes in SAT.1, the second-largest commercial station in the country, and DSF, the most popular sports channel. His son, Thomas, controls Pro 7 and Kabel 1, the third- and fifth-largest commercial channels. It is still unclear whether regulators will treat the two men as separate investors or -- because of their relationship -- as one enterprise.
Bertelsmann will also benefit from the rule change, because it holds large stakes in RTL, RTL2 and Vox. It is already seeking to wrest control of RTL from its non-German partner, CLT.
Since the summer, Bertelsmann has struck a deal with another German shareholder in the channel, the WAZ Group. Pooling their ownership in RTL, they now control as many votes on the RLT board as CLT. The two German groups now have 49.1 percent ownership, and CLT has 49.9 percent.
CLT has filed suit against Bertelsmann, saying the agreement violates German media rules, but experts say that given the planned changes in ownership rules, such a challenge will be in vain.

HEADLINE: MEDIA: PUBLISHING;
Discount books in Britain could reshape the industry.

BYLINE: By Sarah Lyall 

DATELINE: LONDON

 BODY:
"TWENTY-FIVE percent off!" boasts the sign on the High Street bookshop, above an enormous pile of books. It is a common sight in the United States, which is why it is so startling to learn that before Oct. 1, bookstores in Britain were forbidden to sell books at reduced prices.
For close to 100 years, publishers and booksellers had been bound by a gentleman's pact called the Net Book Agreement, which allowed publishers to establish fixed minimum retail prices for their books. 
 If the system stifled competition based on price, the thinking went, then at least it encouraged literary competition: the company that published the best (rather than the cheapest) books would win. But the system fell apart this fall when most of Britain's largest book publishers, dismayed at recent deplorable sales, said they were pulling out of the Net Book Agreement, freeing up most of the country's biggest-selling titles for American-style deals.
It happened just as the industry was entering its busiest period of the year, leading up to Christmas, and retailers braced themselves for a round of price wars in an industry whose margins are notoriously low. "It's a fairly crazy period being led by frenzied retail competition, which is what you would expect when the rules change overnight," said Simon Master, chairman of the General Books division of Random House U.K.
The biggest bookseller chains -- W. H. Smith, Waterstones and the Asda Group supermarkets -- immediately cut prices up to 50 percent on their most popular current titles, including "The Moor's Last Sigh," by Salman Rushdie, and "Enigma," by Robert Harris. Publishers began printing more copies of their best sellers, knowing that because of their new discount deals with bookstores, they would have to increase sales to turn a profit.
Afterward, book sales for a few discounted titles skyrocketed. The most impressive example was "Delia Smith's Winter Collection," a book of recipes by Britain's top television chef. The book was discounted by more than 15 percent in mid-October by chains and independent stores alike. It sold a reported 500,000 copies in one week -- as many as its publisher, BBC Books, had expected to sell in the entire holiday season.
Britain's 2,000 or so independent bookstores (about 1,000 other stores belong to chains) have reacted with fear and in, some cases, plucky promotions. In the town of Bicester, Coles Book Store instituted a special weeklong deal: every customer who bought a nondiscounted book received a free can of Heinz baked beans.
"When the N.B.A. fell, we decided we would have to compete in the local price wars," said Jamie Cole, a salesperson at the store, which is next door to a W. H. Smith's and near three big supermarkets that have begun discounting the best sellers on their racks. "We decided that since they would be selling discounted books, our customers should be able to get baked beans."
Forcing books to answer to the same market forces that govern other retail products sounds like a boon to consumers, who in Britain used to pay upwards of $25 for a hardcover book. But booksellers who oppose the new discounting policy say it could drive up prices, if publishers and booksellers charge more for their nondiscounted titles to help pay for lower profits on discounted books.
"The economics of discounting are merciless," said Kevin Ramage, who owns the Owl Bookshop in Kentish Town, London. If he was to reduce the price of some hardcover books by 25 percent, he said, he would have to sell four times as many books to make the same profit he would have had without the discount.
So far, chain stores are faring better under the new system because they can place bigger orders and get better deals from publishers, although some publishers are offering special price-promotion deals to independent stores. And while no one knows what will happen in the long run, England's booksellers are braced for an American-style battle between the small and the large retailer, the idiosyncratic store and the large conglomerate.
Mr. Ramage at Owl said he hoped that a book's price would prove less important than its content and the personal service his store offers. "In the long term, customers will become more price conscious," he said. "But so far, customers don't seem particularly motivated by price on an individual title. Buying books is not like buying milk."

HEADLINE: Unlikely Allies Join Forces To Save a Neighborhood

BYLINE: By JOE SEXTON 

 BODY:
The landscape of East New York is still dominated by damage, with decaying buildings pockmarking blocks where murder remains much too frequent. But behind the scars is a vibrant neighborhood, one where children are led by the hand through the streets to school, buildings are rebuilt and gardens are grown where there once was debris.
The mix of devastation and determination makes East New York ripe for urban experimentation. Enter, then, one of the more unusual sets of lab partners: police officers and professors, residents and corporate executives, housing experts and welfare recipients, all banding together to create the latest model for community policing. 
 Starting this week, 20 blocks of East New York will become the laboratory for this effort, benefiting from an intensive deployment of police and other resources. The program will use private money, national and local community development corporations, criminal justice experts from Harvard University and the officers of the 75th Precinct.
Two sites have been chosen so far for the three-year national pilot program: this area of Brooklyn -- the center of what has long been one of the most murderous neighborhoods in the city -- and Seattle's Chinatown.Projects in three other cities, including Boston and San Diego, are expected to be announced soon.
"I don't believe East New York is the Wild West anymore," said Rosa Serna, 42, a mother of two who lives there. "And the working people have always been here waiting for change."
Under what will be known as the Community Security Initiative, to be announced by Police Commissioner William J. Bratton at a news conference today, the 75th Precinct, reorganizing its community policing unit, will assign four officers to the target blocks of East New York. Those officers will be directly supervised by a sergeant, who will have the authority to request any or all of the department's tactical drug and street crime units.
The precinct's liaison to the neighborhood's residents will be the East New York Urban Youth Corps, a community development corporation that has helped rehabilitate scores of buildings in East New York in the last several years. Using their contacts with tenant organizers and residents, the development corporation will introduce the officers to a network of people willing to provide information on drug dealing and desperate for a relationship with beat officers that goes beyond staring at them as they drive by in a car.
There will be monthly meetings; committees of residents from each of the 20 blocks will be set up, and professional surveys and polls will be conducted. And all of the data collected will be studied, with refinements proposed, by professors from the criminal justice and management program at the John F. Kennedy School of Government at Harvard.
 "This is a long-term commitment, not just about two cops on a corner for a couple of weeks," said Inspector Edward Mezzadri, commanding officer of the 75th Precinct. "This is community policing turned up to the highest possible volume."
 The history of community policing in the city has not been sterling. Former Mayor David N. Dinkins initiated his version of the concept -- broadly understood to be a way of fostering working relationships between the police and the residents of individual precincts to prevent crime -- only to have his own officials conclude that the effort had been plagued by flawed strategy, a lack of interest among officers, high turnover rates among community policing officers, and outright dishonesty. Officers were found to have lied about how often they actually walked their beats.
In East New York, there has been a gap in trust between the residents and the 75th Precinct. For officers, an assignment to work in the 75th Precinct, which two years ago recorded 129 murders over 12 months, was viewed as punishment. There was also corruption among some officers, and plain fear about having to serve in such a troubled area.
But the 75th Precinct has enjoyed a rebirth of sorts over the last several years. Under the aggressive tactics endorsed by Mr. Bratton, violent crime has fallen by 30 percent across the board in the precinct, with murders down by more than 60 percent over the last two years.
And while no one in the neighborhood is willing to concede that the police have completely accounted for the drop in crime, there is a sense that the moment is right to reclaim East New York's streets permanently. More than 7,000 units of affordable housing are to be built in the coming years, and more modest rehabilitation efforts are going on along whole blocks of the neighborhood.
Program organizers, who have been working with the Brooklyn District Attorney's office, are ambitious enough to be considering having people who plead guilty to buying drugs or soliciting prostitutes work on the block on which they were arrested, during which they would be forced to wear clothing identifying them as having committed a crime in the neighborhood.
To Paul Grogan, the president of the Local Initiatives Support Corporation, which has coordinated the investment of millions of dollars in revitalizing East New York and put together the community policing program, the program makes practical financial sense.
 Millie Wilkerson, though, doesn't look so deep for rationales. Mrs. Wilkerson, who has lived in East New York for 20 years, knows she wants to feel safe again.
 "Who knows why people are doing it or how long it will stick for?"' she said. "But how can it not be worth trying?"

HEADLINE: PULSE;
Higher Education Funds

 BODY:
New York is one of only four states putting less state money into higher education this year than two years ago, according to an annual study by the Center for Higher Education at Illinois State University. A sluggish economy and budget cutting by the Pataki administration caused 1995-96 higher education appropriations to drop 4 percent this year compared with 1993-94. This year's expenditures are only 12 percent higher than 10 years ago. For both the 2-year and 10-year period. New York ranks 48th in the nation in growth of state funding for higher education. In both New York and New Jersey, state financing for student aid was down 4 percent from two years ago.

HEADLINE: Baseball Ends With a Flourish

 BODY:
The baseball season, which could not have started more badly, redeemed itself at the end. The playoffs -- a bit too drawn-out for our tastes -- nevertheless produced magnetic moments. Not the least of these was the series between the New York Yankees and the Seattle Mariners, an unforgettable duel that gave Yankee fans a welcome respite from the tiresome grievances of George Steinbrenner and gave Seattle fans hope that their troubled franchise would stay put.
At last came the World Series. Behind some extraordinary pitching, including Tom Glavine's masterpiece in the final game, the Atlanta Braves neutralized Cleveland's powerful hitters to capture a prize they had come so close to winning before. There was justice and symmetry in this. The playoff schedule was so arranged that a mediocre club could have lucked its way into the Series. The two best teams in baseball actually survived, and the better team won the championship. 
 The biggest winner, however, may have been baseball itself. The season started late because of a demoralizing labor dispute that began in 1994 and did not end until April. The fans, furious with both players and owners, stayed home. Yet over the last month there have been enough dramatic nights and enough packed stadiums to suggest that the game of baseball might well survive the business of baseball -- if only the players and owners will get their heads together. There is still no collective bargaining contract, no real bargaining and no commissioner of baseball. There could well be another strike.
It is hard to summon great sympathy for either side in a dispute over riches that most Americans can only dream about. That includes the players, especially those who charge children $7 for an autograph. In the end, though, the responsibility for stabilizing the sport lies mainly with the owners. The players are justified in fighting for the freedom to sell their skills in an open market. The salary inflation that imperils teams in weaker markets -- teams like the Pittsburgh Pirates, an old and honorable franchise -- was propelled by the owners themselves. If some redistribution of wealth is required to keep these franchises competitive, the owners should finance it.
For now, let us savor a fine Series and join with the disappointed Cleveland fans in baseball's most hopeful refrain: Wait till next year. Let us also join with fans everywhere in hoping that there will be a next year.

HEADLINE: Sports of The Times;
Message to the Fans: Baseball Isn't Back Yet

BYLINE: By DAVE ANDERSON 

DATELINE: ATLANTA

 BODY:
WHENEVER they weren't bundled in warm-up jackets during the World Series, the Atlanta Braves and Cleveland Indians could be seen wearing a patch on the left sleeve of their uniforms that read, "Welcome to the Show." And the late-but-unlamented Baseball Network also blared that message across its television openings.
Welcome to another example of baseball not understanding itself. 
 Baseball isn't supposed to be "the show" or even "a show." That's a phrase out of the movie "Bull Durham." A show is a Broadway play or a Hollywood movie, something with a script. Baseball is a game, a competition without a script. But baseball is also big business, an industry. It is not a show.
Baseball's public discovered that a year ago and now it is about to be reminded.
Just because the World Series was compelling, just because the earlier post-season games had their moments, don't think that baseball is back. As good as the games were, as glittering as Tom Glavine, Mark Wohlers and Dave Justice were in the Braves' epic 1-0 one-hit clincher on Saturday night, games alone won't put baseball back on the public pedestal it once enjoyed.
Since the owners and the players union knocked baseball off that pedestal, they're the only ones who can put it back there.
When last year's World Series was canceled, the owners and the union hung a dark cloud of public distrust over baseball. That cloud is not only still up there, but more clouds are on the horizon.
Now that the World Series is over, now that stadiums are empty, labor negotiations are due to resume soon. If a settlement and a multiyear agreement develop, that distrust will disappear.
But if the dispute over a salary cap and revenue sharing lingers, if it threatens or delays the 1996 season, the patch on those World Series uniforms should have read, "Welcome to the Woe."
Talking baseball soon will mean discussing the moves of the acting commissioner Bud Selig and the union chief Donald Fehr instead of the decisions of the on-the-field managers Bobby Cox and Mike Hargrove.
Talking baseball soon will mean arguing Congress' debate over baseball's antitrust exemption and recalling a Federal judge's injunction last April that prevented the owners from imposing a new economic system on the union.
That injunction, remember, saved the recent season. Without it, the union would not have ended its 234-day strike against the owners.
And for all the glitz of the post-season, for all the boasting by some that baseball is back, remember that the excitement involved only 8, then 4, then 2 of the 28 franchises. Many people, if their team wasn't in it, weren't that interested.
Only hours after Atlanta celebrated the first championship professional sports team in its history, many of its citizens seemed more concerned yesterday with Deion Sanders's return to the Georgia Dome as the Dallas Cowboys' designer cornerback.
Unlike other eras, baseball has a short shelf life now. Too much else is happening. Pro football is halfway to the playoffs. Pro basketball starts dribbling this weekend. Hockey has been skating for nearly a month. Mike Tyson will swagger into a ring again Saturday night.
Baseball likes to think that its franchises are stable. Many are. But the future of the Houston Astros and the Pittsburgh Pirates is uncertain. Even the St. Louis Cardinals are up for sale.
After more than four decades, the Busch beer barons decided that the Cardinals, one of baseball's flagship franchises, were no longer cost-efficient. Another brewery, Molson, wants to sell the Blue Jays. That's always the risk under corporate ownership. To the bean counters, the bottom line is always more important than the farm system.
As if baseball didn't have enough problems, Albert Belle and George Steinbrenner tarnished the World Series with their bullying.
Belle, the Indians' sneering slugger, has apologized to Hannah Storm for insulting the NBC broadcaster in the dugout before the third game, but he deserves a stiff fine of at least $100,000. And when will he apologize to all the others he has insulted in recent seasons?
Steinbrenner stepped on the World Series by announcing Buck Showalter's "resignation" as Yankee manager an hour before Thursday night's fifth game was to begin. The principal owner's explanation was that he had to announce it because it had been leaked. But who leaked it?
Steinbrenner was fined $50,000 for his derogatory remarks about the umpires during the first two games of the divisional series with the Seattle Mariners. And he should be fined $100,000 for upstaging the World Series, at least in New York, with his Showalter announcement.
Because these incidents occurred during the World Series, the fines of both Belle and Steinbrenner must come down from what is known as "the commissioner's office."
But there's no commissioner in that office. And none is in sight. Welcome to the Woe.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Braves' Mark Wohlers getting wrapped up in the celebration with catcher Javier Lopez as Chipper Jones moves in to join the fun. (Barton Silverman/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: A Talent Cradled in London And Nourished by New York

BYLINE: By PETER MARKS 

 BODY:
In 1993, Patrick Stewart went to London to perform his one-man version of "A Christmas Carol" for the hometown crowd. In New York, his performances of the work over the two previous Christmas holidays had been sold-out sensations, the audiences so thrilled at watching him act out an entire Dickens novel that they laughed even before the punch lines.
But at the Old Vic theater in London, there were no waves of love or laughter radiating from the audience on opening night. The crowd sat stone-faced as Mr. Stewart anxiously went through his paces as Tiny Tim and Bob Cratchit and Scrooge, material that had seemed sure-fire on the other side of the Atlantic. 
 "I played to almost total silence," said Mr. Stewart, who is to star in "The Tempest," which opens on Wednesday at the Broadhurst Theater. "I was in a total state of panic. There was no indulgence, no overt generosity. I felt I was on trial."
And of course, he was. Theater is to London what fine red burgundy is to, well, Burgundy. So when Mr. Stewart returned to the British stage after years in the United States as a performer on "Star Trek: The Next Generation," he was instantly reminded of how much more demanding audiences there could be. The only thing to do, he said, was to raise the level of his performance and win over the crowd.
"In London, they make you work for it," Mr. Stewart said the other day as he sat in his Manhattan apartment high above Third Avenue. "I just think that acting here in New York is more fun. When I think of London, I think of drama. When I think of New York, I think of show biz."
Few actors working today have bridged the worlds of drama and show biz more successfully than the 55-year-old Mr. Stewart, who spent years playing classical roles with the Royal Shakespeare Company in England before finding huge commercial success in the United States as Capt. Jean-Luc Picard on "Star Trek." Now, armed with the technique acquired in London and the money and fame earned in Hollywood, Mr. Stewart has the skill and the following to bring the two worlds together. Which is precisely what he is doing this season, as the star of a Shakespeare play on Broadway.
And not one of Shakespeare's traditional crowd-pleasers. Not a zippy "As You Like It" or a bloody "Macbeth" or a raucous "Taming of the Shrew." Mr. Stewart is Prospero in the New York Shakespeare Festival's production of "The Tempest," an esoteric work that scholars describe as one of Shakespeare's "problem" plays. It was such a critical and popular success during its run in Central Park last summer, however, that George C. Wolfe, who heads the festival and directed the play, decided to move it to Broadway.
"Watching 1,900 people rise to applaud and cheer 'The Tempest'!" Mr. Stewart marveled as he recalled his summer in the park. "It's not 'Hamlet.' And they were reacting like it was 'Sunset Boulevard.' "
That the festival finds itself on Broadway with Shakespeare for the first time since 1972, when Sam Waterston and Kathleen Widdoes starred in "Much Ado About Nothing," is in no small measure due to Mr. Stewart. The transfer, in fact, was predicated on his participation. It is a strange and gratifying turn of events for an actor who was unknown in this country a few years ago. And odder still that an interlude on television should be a major factor in how a classically trained English actor gets to Broadway in a classical role.
Mr. Stewart is a bit ambivalent about his television success: pleased at the economic freedom it has provided ("That's 'Star Trek,' " he said, showing a picture of a 315-year-old farmhouse in the north of England that he bought with earnings from the show) but worried that the impression he made in seven seasons as the Star Fleet captain will obscure his stage accomplishments. Because Mr. Stewart, despite his television experience and a growing film career -- he managed to steal a scene or two in the recent "Jeffrey" -- still thinks of himself as a stage actor. And it is on the stage, always the stage, where English actors prove themselves.
He has been doing the classics since his boyhood in Mirfield, a village in Yorkshire where theater was as ubiquitous as pick-up rugby matches. "In my small town, there were 9,000 people and 11 fully active drama societies," Mr. Stewart said. By the age of 15 he had played Richard III, Leontes and Prospero. "Every church, every chapel, you had plays," he added. "Those were possibly the most idyllic days of acting, and it was all just for fun."
After training at the Bristol Old Vic, Mr. Stewart joined the Royal Shakespeare Company. In 1968 he came to New York for the first time, appearing with the renowned company in "As You Like It" and "The Taming of the Shrew." Two years later, he returned to the United States in another Shakespeare production that some theatergoers regard as one of the most memorable theater nights in their lives: Peter Brook's legendary 1970 staging of "A Midsummer Night's Dream."
Mr. Stewart had a small part in "Dream," that of Snout, the tinker. He would work again with Mr. Brook in the mid-1970's, playing Enobarbus in "Antony and Cleopatra," a production he remembers better for what happened during rehearsals than for the performances.
"The aura of mystique and magic around Brook I found to be unfounded," Mr. Stewart said. "I felt his technique was based on absolute common sense." He recalled a rehearsal period with the five other principal actors, including Alan Howard and Glenda Jackson, in which they were called on to perform all the parts in the play. Mr. Brook had placed a script on a carpet; anyone could pick it up and start to read. "It was one of the most thrilling experiences of a play that I've ever seen," he said. "It was as if I was experiencing the play for the first time."
But little in Mr. Stewart's theatrical training had prepared him for Hollywood. Having come from a society obsessed with status by birthright, he was stunned to discover that he was entering a subculture with almost as rigid a hierarchy. "A New York producer once said to me, 'In Hollywood, people define themselves by what they won't do,' " Mr. Stewart said. "Like, 'I don't do TV daytime.' " As an actor in a syndicated series, Mr. Stewart learned that his rung on the ladder was lower than that of feature film and prime-time network television stars.
But in New York, Mr. Stewart has never felt anything less than a celebrity with a permanent key to the city. Just as New Yorkers seem to have embraced his performances, so has Mr. Stewart embraced New York.
"I feel, as a performer, for the first time in my life I'm understood," he said, gazing out the kitchen window at the rooftops of the Upper East Side. "I think America is good for me."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Patrick Stewart, star of "The Tempest," which opens Wednesday. (Sara Krulwich/The New York Times)

SECTION: Section D; Page 5; Column 1; Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 753 words

HEADLINE: Trying to Put the Power Grid On a More or Less Even Keel

BYLINE: By MATTHEW L. WALD 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 29

 BODY:
These days, small wafers of silicon precisely control tens of millions of personal computers. But the electric system that powers those computers -- along with the lights, the telephones and most of the rest of the physical components of the economy -- still lumbers along with technology from the days of manual typewriters and mechanical adding machines.
Now, though, experiments are under way in the power industry to demonstrate that chunks of silicon could help run the nation's electrical power network in a manner similar to the inner workings of a computer -- by rapidly and precisely switching current on and off. 
 Why silicon? Because it is a "semiconductor" of electricity, silicon can be instantly switched from a conductor to an insulator and back again, using the electricity itself to make this change.
Silicon is a particularly effective medium for the on-off switching of the tiny pulses of electricity that represent the ones and zeros of information in a computer's binary code. Engineers hope that the same principle can be applied to an electric utility system, in which silicon could rapidly switch huge jolts of electricity on and off -- to better stabilize the current's flow and to open new possibilities in the productivity and reliability of transporting electricity over the nation's power grid.
The Tennessee Valley Authority will begin testing such technology in mid-November at a substation near Johnson City. And in eastern Kentucky, American Electric Power is working on its second installation of a silicon-control system.
This is the dawn of "the other silicon revolution," said Karl E. Stahlkopf, vice president of the Electric Power Research Institute, a utility consortium in Palo Alto, Calif.
Dawn may be breaking not a moment too soon. After years of public discussion about the generating side of the electricity business -- whether to build nuclear plants, how to control acid emissions from coal plants, new ways to avoid using oil -- attention is now shifting to the transmission system. About 40 percent of all electricity produced in the country is sold on the wholesale market, transmitted from one utility to another -- a proportion that has quadrupled in the last 15 years, according to the research institute.
 And the percentage could rise sharply again as the industry is de-regulated and customers are allowed to shop among producers, rather than being forced to buy electricity from the local monopoly.
The problem with the existing power transport network is that it is largely uncontrolled, resembling a network of canals, in which the electricity is like water, flowing down the course of least resistance.
The new silicon technology can be thought of as converting the system to something more controllable, like water mains outfitted with adjustable valves. These silicon valves are called thyristors (pronounced thigh-RIS-ters) -- thick disks of silicon slightly larger than hockey pucks, capable of switching millions of watts of electricity on and off in rapid bursts.
 Because the existing power grid was built primarily for reliability, not bulk power transfers, when too much electricity is pushed through it, it is prone to collapse.
The Tennessee Valley Authority installation, scheduled to go into use on Nov. 15, will use 240 thyristors to stabilize voltage at a Johnson City substation with a growing number of industrial customers.
 Electricity at the substation, as in most major electric operations, is moving in three phases. Each phase is 60-cycle alternating current, in which the electrons switch directions 60 times a second. But the three phases are staggered by exactly one third, like three people singing "row, row, row your boat" in a round.
A generator produces equal amounts of energy in all three phases. But miles away, where customers are drawing power, the energy in each phase can vary significantly. By opening and closing electrical gates at precise intervals, the thyristors can shunt energy between the phases to stabilize the system, according to T. W. Cease, the principal project specialist for the Tennessee Valley Authority.
Dr. Stahlkopf sees future applications as well. Today, some industrial or computer sites that need high reliability are wired with feeders to two different power substations, in case one fails. A thyristor switch, he said, could probably accomplish the switch in well under one cycle, or one-sixtieth of a second -- so fast the computers would not even hiccup.


GRAPHIC: The power systems operated by electric companies often suffer irregularities too small to produce even a noticable flicker in the lights, but large enough to hurt sensitive equipment. Engineers are using silicon devices, similar to computer chips but thousands of times larger, to stablize the current that flows over electric-power transmission lines. Current alternates 60 times a second. Even though an outage or voltage sag may last only two or three cycles, that is long enough to cause trouble.
Chart/Diagram: "A Computer Chip Model for Energy"
 As a precaution against interruptions, some commercial buildings are hooked up to feeder cables from two different elecrical substations. Old-style power equipment cannot make a switch fast enough to keep a comptuer running. However, silicon switches called thyristors can sense a failure or a voltage sag and accomplish the switch in well under one cycle.
 Sometimes the 60-cycle system developes unwanted variations. The amount of energy may increase or decrease, like a series of big waves on the ocean followed by a series of small waves. A thyristor can chop off the tops of the big waves to keep the power steady.
 
(Source: Scientific American)

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 1; Metropolitan Desk
