HEADLINE: In Flat Market, Co-op Life Has Steep Ups and Downs

SERIES: THE HOUSE RULES: First of three articles.

BYLINE: By N. R. KLEINFIELD with TRACIE ROZHON 

 BODY:
The actor was so enraged at his co-op board president that one morning he dumped a mountain of dirt outside the man's apartment door. When the board president died of cancer, another exasperated resident of the Upper West Side building held a little party to celebrate.
The board of a Sutton Place co-op despised a tenant who had spent three years noisily renovating his apartment. When he decided to sell the place for $2.7 million, the board, to spite him, rejected an amply qualified buyer. Word circulated among brokers that this was a problem apartment. Not until years later did it sell -- for almost $1 million less. 
 The rambunctious 4-year-old threw tantrums unless she was fed in the elevator of her family's Queens co-op. Thus the girl's nanny dutifully nourished her in the elevator -- messily. Neighbors went off to work with food remnants speckling their shoes. They complained to the parents, who pointed out that they were apartment owners and could do as they pleased. The dispute boiled until the board formally prohibited dining in the elevator.
Co-op life, of course, has always been difficult. But in the last few years, for the hundreds of thousands of New Yorkers who bought into the co-op market only to see it collapse, this often fractious communal living arrangement has become even more so.
Always powerful and subject to precious little oversight, co-op boards are now increasingly vigilant about their buildings' finances and thus are becoming more controlling -- or, many residents and brokers would say, more intrusive, autocratic and quixotic. Several brokers say boards appear to be turning down more people than ever -- even applicants painstakingly screened by brokers well-versed as to which buildings consider which sorts of people "compatible." Prospective buyers, in general, are being forced to reveal more and more. Often acerbic co-op litigation is escalating as well.
A co-op is very much an insulated private club, and many of them impose a thicket of house rules: "Poorly dressed guests must ride service elevator." "Residents cannot sit in lobby chairs when dressed in flippers." You may be penalized if you flout them.
At 860-870 United Nations Plaza, you are not permitted to cook in a wok (people don't like the smell), and all draperies must be lined in white. At 77 Seventh Avenue, pianos are prohibited. At the Dakota, you need board permission to be interviewed by television crews in your apartment. At 301 East 64th Street, you can be fined $250 for skating inside the building. At 53 East 66th Street, if there are two written complaints about a dog creating a nuisance, "said dog would be removed."
The result of all this is that many co-op residents aren't just routinely fed up with one another these days; they're starting to get fed up with the co-op enterprise itself.
"A lot of people bought into co-ops not knowing what the life would be like," said Vicki Chesler, executive editor of The New York Cooperator, a publication devoted to the field. "We did a seminar and one woman said, 'When are you going to do a book about how to overthrow your board?' We get a lot of calls from people unhappy with their boards. We get, 'My neighbor makes a lot of noise, but he's the board president.' We get a lot of complaints about boards rigging elections to keep new members off. There is no question that some people have become disillusioned about co-op life."
This souring of the cooperative mood has come as co-ops, once identified largely with the affluent, spread throughout New York's geographic and economic interstices in the 1980's to become available to virtually all socio-economic groups. Relatively rare outside New York, co-op apartments are home to more than a million New Yorkers. Four times more co-ops exist today than in 1980, and they sell for $15,000 as well as $15 million.
Many of the people who bought these co-ops did so not out of any abiding belief in the system itself, if they even understood it, but for financial motives. Co-ops made more sense than renting, and as real-estate values rode the frenzied boom, they became investment gold mines.
Now, though, with the gold rush done and prices generally stagnant, the more misunderstood and distracting elements of co-op life are asserting themselves with a grouchy vengeance: the potentially stringent limitations on tenant freedom, capriciousness about who can move in, malicious boards, crooked managing agents, malevolent neighbors and the fact that tenants are not simply homeowners but partners in a multimillion-dollar corporation they have not been trained to run.
"It's a form of living under dictatorship," said Arthur Cantor, a producer who owns a co-op in the Dakota. "Anyone who lives in a co-op has to have mixed emotions. In a sense, you're half-slave and half-free."
 
Realization
Shareholders Live Above the C.E.O.
There are 8,080 co-op buildings in New York City, containing about 416,000 apartments, more than half the co-ops in the nation. In contrast, there are just 1,040 condominium complexes, the less restrictive form of communal ownership that is more popular elsewhere in the country. Half of the city's co-op apartments are in Manhattan, with the largest and most expensive enclave lining the Upper East Side. And while New York remains emphatically a city of renters, roughly 15 percent of the population now lives in co-ops, though about 45 percent of those people rent the apartments from their owners.
It is only after actually buying a co-op that many people truly realize what it is: a synthesis of home, kibbutz and Hewlett-Packard. Living in a co-op means agreeing to share responsibility for everything from a building's finances to its decor.
Tenants do not own the space they live in; they own shares in the corporation that owns the building. The corporation then leases an apartment to each shareholder, who pays a monthly maintenance that covers a proportional share of the expenses of the building.
Co-ops operate like businesses. Rules and governance are the province of an elected board of directors. In no other business, however, do shareholders and board members all live together in the same building.
People who rent apartments share a common enemy in the landlord, who may live in New Jersey. In a co-op, all the tenants are also the landlord, and the boy playing the tuba next door belongs to the board president. But since many people migrate from rental buildings to co-ops, they often take a long time to acclimate themselves.
"There's a real rental mentality among many co-op residents," said Naomi Karp, who is working on a book about the history of co-ops in New York. "They still think the landlord is going to come to fix the broken water pipe."
One of the aspects least appreciated by co-op owners, until something goes awry, is that the business they own may well boast a multimillion-dollar budget. In their spare time, not necessarily with any managerial grooming, they are expected to run it. If something goes dreadfully wrong with the building, they have to pay for it. One day, as happened to residents of 740 Park Avenue, you can receive a bill for a special assessment of $258,000 to repair the facade of your building.
"You have invested in not only a home but a corporation," said Carol J. Ott, editor and publisher of Habitat magazine, which covers co-op issues. "What's in your face every day is your home. But the fact that there's a corporation, too, is becoming more obvious to many people. And that realization has made people unsettled. In no other industry do you have neophytes spending this kind of money."
In the last couple of years, many boards, neophyte or not, seem to be seizing even greater control of their buildings' affairs. To some extent, this is a reaction to revelations last year by the Manhattan District Attorney that scores of managing agents, who as a rule mind the day-to-day details, had extorted millions of dollars in kickbacks from vendors.
And since most co-ops are no longer appreciating in value, boards making such mundane decisions are very anxious about how each one -- from approving buyers to deciding the wattage of the new lighting in the elevators -- will affect the value of the apartments.
"It used to be that people would be elected to the board and complete trust would be put in the managing agent to do everything," Ms. Ott said. "Boards today are in a much tougher spot. Expectations and their sense of responsibility are much greater than members have been trained for. I don't know if it is fair to ask volunteers to do this. An enormous amount of people have moved into this form of ownership, and they've been left out to dry."
There has been a certain amount of agitation to have agents licensed -- though, as some point out, even licensed agents might steal if no one watched them. Some owners have broached the more radical notion of having board members licensed. Others argue that it is difficult enough to persuade owners to serve as board members without requiring training and licensing.
On occasion, this shortage of attention and expertise can lead to rude shocks. For years, residents of a midtown co-op allowed a wily and obstinate board president to run their building as if he personally owned it. In fact, he told business associates that he did own it. A few tenants finally started poking around. They discovered that he had been convicted of embezzlement in the late 1970's and served two years in prison. Several other embezzlement cases were pending against him, and there were suspicions that he had defrauded the co-op. A few weeks ago, he was forced to resign. Both the Attorney General and the Manhattan District Attorney have been alerted.
"What I learned is that people living in co-ops don't know anything about co-ops and they run away from responsibilities," said the leader of the movement to expel the president. "I think everybody who lives in a co-op needs to take courses to learn what their rights are. We sit around and grouse, but we don't do anything."
Confusion has been especially rampant in the hundreds of buildings that tumbled into financial distress in recent years, often because of crushing mortgages that had been attached to the buildings by their former owners (some of whom subsequently defaulted on their obligations.) Numerous troubled co-ops are selling at bargain prices and in many cases shares must be bought in cash, because no bank will make loans against them.
The prices of the preponderance of co-ops continue to lag below their peaks of the late 1980's, though Lawrence Sicular, a co-op appraiser, said that the city's elite apartments -- those selling for between $3 million and $10 million -- have attained peak selling prices.
Many disaffected owners have begun looking with greater favor on condominiums, where buyers own their apartments outright and hold joint title to common spaces. The only control boards have over buyers is the right of first refusal, meaning that if they oppose someone the building has to buy the apartment, something that rarely happens. Condominium boards also tend to impose less control over subletting and over how people live within their apartments.
Except at the highest end of the market, brokers say condos sell for appreciably more than comparable co-ops, largely because the co-op board system deters people.
"Co-op boards have such incredible, sweeping power," said I. Dolly Lenz, a senior broker at Sotheby's International Realty. "They have the sort of power that they wouldn't have at I.B.M. They can say someone with green eyes shouldn't get in their building."
 
Control
Courts Backed Boards' Power
Many people, of course, like being able to control who moves in beside them. To some, an inherent allure of a co-op is the opportunity to mold an environment in which everyone is a mirror reflection of themselves. If a resident strays from the fold, they want to be able to curb his behavior, and even punish him. And, indeed, the courts have empowered boards with broad latitude to make their homes as they see fit. Two cases are especially notable.
The first was the 1959 ruling by the Court of Appeals, New York's highest court, in Sidney Weisner v. 791 Park Avenue Corporation. Mr. Weisner, a lawyer, sued after he was rejected by the co-op board at 791 Park Avenue without being given a reason. The court said that as long as members of a co-op do not violate civil rights law, they have total discretion in who they admit "to share their elevators, their common halls and facilities, their stockholders' meetings, their management problems and responsibilities for their homes." Furthermore, the court said that a co-op need not furnish a reason for rejecting someone.
The second key ruling came in a 1990 Appeals Court case, Levandusky v. One Fifth Avenue Apartment Corporation.
Dr. Ronald Levandusky, a plastic surgeon and board member, had received board approval to renovate his apartment. His plans, however, did not show that a two-inch steam pipe in the kitchen was being moved four feet. The board told him that he could not relocate the steam pipe. He did it anyway. When the co-op board halted the work, Dr. Levandusky sued. He argued that the restriction was unreasonable, precipitated by a personal vendetta by a board member whom he had criticized for hooking up a sink in her rooftop greenhouse.
The court, reversing a lower court decision, held that the co-op board was within its powers. It said that co-op boards should be afforded "the greatest possible degree of deference." Furthermore, it said that boards should be subject only to the "business judgment" rule applied by courts to corporate directors, meaning that a co-op board can do as it pleases if it acts in good faith and has the benefit of the entire co-op as its goal.
The court acknowledged that governing boards "may significantly restrict the bundle of rights a property owner normally enjoys." But it added that people always have the freedom not to buy an apartment.
As it happened, the One Fifth Avenue board, whose composition had by now changed, never did require Dr. Levandusky to restore the pipe to its original position. In exchange for agreeing to pay $181,000 of the building's legal fees, he was allowed to keep the pipe where it was.
Dr. Levandusky still does not speak to the board members who fought him. But three years ago, he ran for the board again and was voted in handily. He served until this past April. "It completed the healing process," he said. "It made me feel again that I was respected and worthy as a tenant."
 
Battlegrounds
Who's Making All That Noise?
Now that so much of the city's choicest housing stock is co-ops, the admissions dance between buyer and board has become one of New York's most traumatic and dreaded rituals. Many people would rather face an I.R.S. auditor, or, as one spurned applicant said, "I'd take a firing squad."
Co-op boards can pose any conceivable question to applicants, and do. Marc Luxemburg, a lawyer and president of the Council of New York Cooperatives, an organization that offers guidance to co-ops, says his three favorite questions are those routinely asked by a co-op on West 85th Street: What is the wattage of your stereo? How many C.D.'s and tapes do you own? When were the last three times you saw a lawyer, and why?
A number of real-estate brokers report that boards have gotten far fussier about whom they bless. The Corcoran Group, a large broker, said that it found a rejection rate of 2.5 percent among the 1,010 apartments it sold this year, compared withto 1.1 percent during the depths of the market in 1991 and 1.7 percent during the height in 1988. While those percentages seem tiny, brokers agree that rejections would be much higher without their own meticulous screening to shepherd buyers away from what brokers call a "tough building."
Barbara Corcoran, the head of the Corcoran Group, ascribed the rise in turndowns largely to board queasiness, since the stock market crash, about purchasers reliant on Wall Street and self-employed people.
Subletting, always severely limited, has become an especially raw issue recently. Since the market is slow and boards may not approve buyers, many owners who are in financial trouble desperately need to rent their apartments. While a lot of buildings have relaxed their rules, others have stood firm. "Some people have declared bankruptcy and abandoned apartments because they weren't allowed to sublet," said Vicki Chesler of the New York Cooperator.
No one has taken a tally of the cases, but several lawyers agreed that in recent years there had been a pronounced surge in lawsuits affecting co-ops.
"Twenty years ago, it would be six to eight months between when you saw a co-op decision," said Mr. Luxemburg, of the Council of New York Cooperatives. "Now there are two a week."
During the summer, a member of the board of 1045 Park Avenue went so far as to sue Charles Millard, a city councilman, to compel him to name the shareholders who had complained to him about building code violations in their co-op. The board member wished to inform all the co-op's tenants of their identities. Two weeks ago, the State Supreme Court rebuffed the co-op, declaring that the suit was an "invidious" attempt to make it difficult for the tenants to live peacefully in the building.
In another recently decided lawsuit, a Greenwich Village co-op was granted an injunction forcing a shareholder to give it access to the roof to make repairs, even though the tenant's roof garden would be destroyed. In yet another, a woman was forbidden to let her grandson live in her co-op while she was away, because the board had not approved him. The board noted that he threw a loud party and had a pit bull.
Most often, tenants battle over noise and renovations. It could be any noise. A resident at a Sutton Place co-op declared the whooshing sound of the toilet flushing in the apartment above his unacceptable to his comfort. Attempts at a peacable solution failed. The man recently went to court and asked a judge to actually enjoin the neighbor from flushing his toilet. So far, the judge has demurred and the case has been postponed while the building tries to find a less astringent way to address the bathroom.
NEXT: Getting in.

CORRECTION-DATE: November 1, 1995, Wednesday

 CORRECTION:
A chart on Monday with an article about the co-op apartment market in New York City misstated the number of co-ops in the city in 1984. There were 247,000, not 211,000.


GRAPHIC: In a co-op, tenants don't own their apartments, but shares in the corporation that owns the building. The corporation then leases an apartment to each shareholder, who pays a monthly maintenance that covers a proportional share of the expenses of the building. The building is governed by an elected board of directors, made up of shareholders.
 
THE EARLY YEARS
Chart: "The Co-op: History of a New York Insitution"
The first co-op in New York is thought to be the Tenth Street Studio at 15 West 10th Street, built in 1857 to contain studios, galleries and living space for artists. In the 1880's, Philip Gingembre Hubert, an architect and inventor, conceived of the Hubert Home Clubs as co-ops for those of moderate income, but not enough working class people had the money, and so the home clubs were snapped up by the affluent. In the early 1900's, members of the Finnish community erected two dozen co-ops in Sunset Park, Brooklyn, and various labor unions also got into the co-op market.
 
THE 20's
 During the Roaring 20's, the "white glove" co-ops for the wealthy sprang up along Park Avenue and Fifth Avenue. Promoted by Douglas Elliman, a real estate broker, the buildings became the province of the wealthy WASP community, who were intent on keeping out "undesirables" -- namely blacks, Jews and Catholics. The onset of the Depression, however, toppled many co-ops and brought the movement to a standstill.
 
THE POSTWAR YEARS
 The acute need for housing for veterans returning from World War II rejuvenated the concept in the form of government-subsidized complexes. In time, thousands of units aimed at moderate-income tenants were also created under the state Mitchell-Lama program.
 
THE BOOM YEARS
The pivotal moment was the 1980's, when co-ops went mainstream. The easing of rules for conversions in 1982, when the real-estate market was surging, stimulated an explosion in rental buildings being transformed into co-ops. Driving the conversions was the desire of building owners to evade rent control. Moreover, they discovered that they could attach a mortgage to a building and buyers of apartments paid scant heed to it, paying as much for their apartments whether the mortgage was small or large. After all, co-op apartments were appreciating almost by the hour and buyers were untutored in their economics. Some people bought and sold co-ops in a day, gilding their lives with profits in the tens of thousands of dollars.
 
THE LEAN YEARS
When the stock market crashed in October of 1987, the real-estate market began to come apart, and it has remained stagnant in the current queasy economy. In the last few years, the number of co-ops has remained virtually unchanged. Many troubled co-ops are selling at bargain prices and must be bought in cash because no bank will give loans against them.
 
Where the Co-ops Are
Density of co-op apartments in the five boroughs of New York City. Over all, the number of co-op apartments grew from 211,000 in 1984 to 416,000 this year; the growth was especially dramatic in the boroughs outside Manhattan. (pg. B2)

PUB-DATE: October 31, 1995

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 2; Metropolitan Desk 

LENGTH: 3081 words

HEADLINE: Getting Into Co-ops: The Money Bias

SERIES: THE HOUSE RULES -- Second of three articles.

BYLINE: By TRACIE ROZHON with N. R. KLEINFIELD 

 BODY:
They knew they were going to be rejected the second they walked through the door.
"Our other friends had gone before co-op boards, and they said the meeting was just a formality, just a little tete-a-tete to go over the house rules," said the woman, for whom the board interview was supposed to be the last step to acquiring a $275,000 co-op apartment on East 72d Street -- a process that had already cost her and her husband thousands of dollars in bank and legal fees. 
 "But when they opened the door to us," she recalled, "there was a chill. It was businesslike, polite, but I knew: we'd never get out of there alive. They sat us down and started right in on my husband. They said they had a problem with his income."
Before a New Yorker can experience the ambiguous joys of co-op life, there is the matter of getting in -- an exercise that has always tended to be unpleasant, intrusive and exclusionary. But in these days of co-op angst, as the market struggles warily in the wake of the bankruptcies and defaults of the early 1990's, there is widespread agreement that the admissions process has become more onerous than ever before.
Nervous co-op boards, several big brokers report, are rejecting a growing number of applicants outright, though the numbers remain small. But brokers acknowledge that without newly rigorous methods of "screening" applicants, far more would be turned down. And many, many more people are having to wait longer, and to reveal more, as boards probe deeper and deeper into the recesses of their lives, expecially their financial lives. All of which adds up to a new, and increasingly economic, system of discrimination.
To be sure, the traditional "social" strictures -- the right connections, the right religion or ethnicity, the right, or at least not the wrong, way of making a living -- haven't exactly disappeared, especially in the grand old buildings lining Central Park West and Fifth and Park Avenues. (A number of celebrities -- always a dicey group in the high-end co-op world -- can attest to that, including Sean Lennon, son of John, recently turned down at 88 Central Park West, and Steve Wynn, the casino entrepreneur, rejected at 825 Fifth.)
Nor has the socioeconomic structure of the co-op world changed much. Hundreds of thousands of New Yorkers, of virtually all economic groups, have bought co-op apartments in the last 15 years, but co-op residents remain generally wealthier, better educated and -- even accounting for differences in income -- whiter than the city as a whole, according to an analysis of housing data by The New York Times.
But many brokers and other industry experts agree that the old prejudices have lost some of their sting. More than ever, money -- solid, secure money -- is the ultimate arbiter.
"You used to turn in a financial statement and sign it, period," said Joanne Kennedy, co-president of Hunt-Kennedy Real Estate. "Now, the board members add up all the lines to the last decimal, and if it doesn't add up, they impugn the person as either a liar or reckless. It's ridiculous."
Cooperative buildings like to see more and more money; 20 years ago, one building held its board interviews at tea time -- in French. If the prospective shareholder managed that with elan, entrance was taken for granted.
These days, agents say, some buildings (4 East 66th Street; 2 East 67th Street, 820, 834 and 960 Fifth Avenue; 740 and 778 Park Avenue) prefer a net worth of $100 million. Boards for other buildings (720, 730 and 770 Park; 950, 998, 1020, 1030 and 1040 Fifth) like to see $50 million. The run-of-the-mill posh Fifth or Park Avenue co-op looks for a buyer's net worth to total between three and five times the price of the apartment. Off these streets, known as the Gold Coast, buyers usually must show that their long-term debt does not total more than 25 percent of their gross income.
Some buildings do not mind if the money is crisply new; some want it old, with socially prominent ancestors lined up behind it. But no matter if it is a 12-room palace on Park Avenue or a one-bedroom in Park Slope, Brooklyn, boards now want to see the assets already piled up, not merely flowing in. Often, it is the co-op boards for lower-end buildings, without hefty reserve funds, that worry the most: one defaulting shareholder can tip a building into the red.
According to a statistician at the Corcoran Group, which reported a turndown rate of 2.5 percent among the deals it handled this year, compared with 1.1 percent during the depths of the market in 1991, the majority of board turndowns on apartments that cost more than $1 million are for "social" reasons. The vast majority of the rest are financial; since the stock market crash in 1987, boards have become increasingly queasy about stockbrokers and entrepreneurs.
Scott Warshaw, a managing agent with Midboro Management, agreed that board turndowns were rising, at least partly because boards felt an increasing number of apartments were selling too cheaply and would therefore lower the value of others.
All this talk about money and apartment values particularly annoys brokers in the business long enough to know when and how certain board members came to buy their own apartments.
"It really galls me to see these guys, who bought their own apartments for $35,000 or $40,000 decades ago, who are sitting there clipping food coupons so they can afford to pay the monthly maintenance bills," one broker said, requesting anonymity out of fear of reprisals by co-op boards. "They're passing judgment on lovely families who can afford a $3 million apartment -- and finding them wanting. It's a joke."
 
A Private Club
Screening Process And Possible Bias
 A co-op, essentially, is a private club. And so the courts have ruled that boards may turn down anyone they want, as long as no one can prove they have broken laws barring discrimination on the basis of things like race, sex, sexual orientation, age, religion, disability or lawful occupation. And since boards also do not have to give reasons for their decisions, illegal discrimination is almost impossible to prove.
What this system produces is a world that is considerably more homogeneous than the city at large. The most striking finding of the housing data, based on the New York City Housing and Vacancy Survey for 1993, is the racial imbalance. While 48 percent of New Yorkers are white, 77 percent of co-op owners are white. Although blacks make up 23 percent of the city's population, they account for just 6 percent of co-op owners. In Manhattan, the percentage drops to less than 2 percent. On the Upper East Side, a negligible number of blacks live in co-ops.
Even among New Yorkers of similar means, the analysis indicated, whites are more likely to own co-ops than nonwhites, especially at the upper income levels. Among homeowners with annual incomes of between $99,999 and $199,999, 44 percent owned co-ops, compared with 11 percent of blacks, 27 percent of Asian-Americans and 18 percent of Hispanic people.
Given the secrecy allowed co-op boards, the precise anatomy of each board's widely differing admissions process cannot be known. Still, most brokers agree that while an applicant's bottom lines have gained more importance than ever, old-fashioned discrimination still comes into play in some buildings. Often, though, it is camouflaged in more socially acceptable ways.
A young Jewish family may be welcomed in a Park Avenue building one week, and the next month, a remarkably similar family is rejected -- because of an unwritten quota. Blacks seldom apply to the poshest buildings -- and when they do, are almost never rejected. Some buildings have no problem with openly gay couples; another may prefer to see a man and woman married before they approve them.
In most cases, applicants are "screened" by brokers, not only for money but frequently also for religion, ethnicity and sexual preference. (The term "steering," which is illegal, is never used.)
"Absolutely, there are buildings that don't want kids, and there are buildings with clear ethnic preferences and clear racial preferences, and the brokers know where to take people," said Mary Ann Rothman, executive director of the Council of New York Cooperatives, a group that represents co-op boards.
As part of this screening, applicants are handed a "reverse directory," a list of all occupants of the desired building, and asked if they recognize any friends -- or perhaps more important, any enemies. If there is doubt, board members are sounded out.
"If there are any questions, it's always better to lay your deck on the table," said Roger Erickson, a senior vice president at William B. May Real Estate. "We sometimes ask the seller to 'run it by' a board member -- nothing formal. If I get a question mark, I'll go to the customer and tell him it's not a green light."
Still, there is a consensus that, for whatever reason, things have improved. Though blacks simply do not apply to many buildings, brokers point to co-ops that accepted their first blacks in the last five years -- 834 Fifth, which welcomed Reginald Lewis, who until his death owned Beatrice Foods, and 1158 Fifth, home to Sidney Poitier.
Abraham Foxman, national director of the Anti-Defamation League of B'nai B'rith, said that there are still anti-Semitic buildings but Jews avoid them. "The community is comfortable enough that they can say: 'They don't want me. I won't go there.' "
Brokers point to buildings that were once all-white Anglo-Saxon Protestant and are now run by a Jewish board of directors -- and they add that those can be some of the worst anti-Semitic buildings. "It's often surprising to brokers when a predominantly Jewish building turns down a Jewish applicant," said Barbara Corcoran, president of the Corcoran Group. "There is a sense that the board doesn't wish to be labeled a 'Jewish building' or that they have already reached their quota."
One broker even refers to himself as a Kremlinologist as he tries to keep track of who's in and who's not.
Mike Wallace, the "60 Minutes" host, was rejected at 770 Park. Ron Perelman, the financier, was turned down at 820 Fifth, 834 Fifth and 4 East 66th Street. Madonna and Barbra Streisand were rejected, too. The biggest surprise, according to brokers, was Mr. Lennon, who was rejected from his mother's building.
"Garmentos," brokers' slang for people in the clothing business, including the dress designer Carolyne Roehm (rejected from 131 East 66th Street), are out in certain buildings. "Loud" entertainers are out. Politicians are frowned upon. Those with bodyguards need not apply.
A co-op board's scrutiny can extend to mundane minutiae. Charles Rappaport, president of a co-op in Flushing, Queens, said his building's interview committee wants to see everyone who is going to live there, "including babes in arms." The board uses an outside investigator and insists on a home visit. "We want to know," he said, "is it dirty? Is it sloppy?"
In Manhattan, some boards, including the one at 870 United Nations Plaza, will commission an investigation known as the Bishop's Report, which costs between $1,500 and $2,000, agents say; a Pinkerton's investigation costs $500.
Each board has its own unwritten rules: no big dogs at 336 West End Avenue; no shopping carts in the lobby of the Majestic on Central Park West; applicants must provide a letter detailing community service at 870 United Nations Plaza.
At 431 West 54th Street, tenants had become perturbed by smoke that seeped into their apartments from smokers who were subletting. When a five-pack-a-day smoker applied last year, the board rejected him and decided to go smoke-free.
 
Rights and Death
Gay Residents And Succession
 One day last spring, a 36-year-old gay man with a growing reputation for designing glossy magazines, was forced to listen to a discussion of his life -- and his death -- through the paper-thin walls of a managing agent's office.
"They stupidly put me in a conference room next to where they were meeting," said the man, who asked that his name not be used for fear of alienating his neighbors. "They began what I can only term a very homophobic discussion. The whole thing was based upon what would happen if I were going to die. It's a very sensitive subject -- like all gay people have AIDS, all people with AIDS are going to die.
"It was fascinating, listening to them through those walls, how cavalier they were with my life. It felt incredibly un-American."
The door opened, and the board members shook his hand, "like I was coming to an interview at Harvard or Princeton." If he died, they asked, what would happen to his apartment? Would his companion of 11 years want to live there? Would he be able to afford it?
He said he was bequeathing his apartment -- and his considerable wealth -- to his friend. How, he was asked, was the board to know his relatives would not contest the will? In the end, both men signed a letter, agreeing that if the wealthier man died, the other man would reapply, as if he were a new applicant.
Some rejected candidates threaten to sue, then find out there is almost no chance of winning. A few go to the state's Division of Human Rights or the city's Human Rights Commission. In the last fiscal year, only 13 complaints of co-op board discrimination were filed with the city; most are still under investigation. The state does not break down cases geographically.
 
Rejections
Getting Through Board Interviews
 The couple who were rejected from the East 72d Street co-op said their broker had assured them that they were "perfect for the building." They walked into the interview on a Friday last February -- she in her "beautiful red jacket," he in a sport coat and tie, "like lambs to the slaughter," recalled the woman, who, for fear of being rejected again, asked that she be identified only as Paula B.
"There were only two of them, and it was held in the woman's apartment," she said. "She's there with another, older gentlemen. They started off with the question about David's salary."
Her husband owns a sizable piece of his family's construction business. The year before last, he made only $32,000. She makes more than $100,000 as an advertising executive at a woman's magazine. They had enough cash in the bank to buy the apartment three times over, they said.
"We wouldn't have had a problem if it was the other way around," she said. "If he had made $100,000 and I had been the little housewife, they would have praised me for being so noble for me to work and contribute."
The interrogation lurched onward. "You say you spent $10,000 a year on meals in New York City -- I find that unrealistically low," the woman said she was asked.
"I replied that we don't have kids, and it doesn't cost that much to live if you're not eating at Lutece every night," Mrs. B. said, adding, "At this point, I could tell she really didn't like me."
The board member then asked what synagogue the couple belonged to, they said. "We didn't want to answer the question," Mr. B said. "I thought it was inappropriate. I knew they couldn't legally ask that, but we were in a vulnerable position. I knew if I didn't answer, we wouldn't get the apartment."
He muttered something about going with his mother to the family synagogue in Westchester, and the two interviewers moved to a new subject: the couple's current apartment. "Oh, we have a lovely apartment," the wife answered enthusiastically. At last, she said she thought, she was on safe ground. "We love it."
"Then why do you want to leave it?" the female board member snapped.
The interview lasted an hour. On Monday, the broker called to give them the bad news.
The couple appealed to the State Human Rights Division, which held a preliminary conference based on the question about the synagogue. But when the female board member volunteered that she herself was Jewish -- despite her looks and Irish-sounding name -- "the case just kind of evaporated," the couple said. The state found no probable cause for their complaint.
That was in mid-March. The couple have just started looking for another apartment. They are looking at co-ops. "What else?" Mrs. B. said, shrugging. "All the buildings we want to live in are co-ops."
 
Stalling Tactics
Black Applicants And Qualifications
 Hal Harris is a black real estate broker who sold co-ops from 1987 to 1993, when he became so discouraged he switched to commercial spaces. When he was selling co-ops, he said, he saw a half-dozen fully qualified black applicants rejected or stalled by co-op boards until they gave up.
"They delay until your bank commitment expires," Mr. Harris explained. "They know you have 90 days on your loan commitment. So they say the integral board member is out of town. Next month, they say the recording secretary is out. They stall and stall until it's too late."
Confronted with months of the same delays, Abbey Lincoln, the singer, who is black, gave up, feeling herself a victim of racism.
Ms. Lincoln had kept asking for an interview at the building on Riverside Drive so she could explain her finances. Singers, like models and artists, have complex income tax documents, with many, many deductions, giving them a relatively small taxable income. But the board would not budge.
"I just said, 'That's it -- I'm through,' " she said, tossing her many braids with spirit. "They never did reject me exactly, but they let it be known they weren't going to accept me. They kept coming back and back, asking me for more information."
A year and a half later, by coincidence, the president of the building's board happened to visit Judy Maysles, the real estate agent who had represented Ms. Lincoln.
"We started chatting, and I told him I was mad at him for what he did to Abby," Ms. Maysles said. "He admitted he'd been against her, but he said it wasn't because she was black; it was because of her income. It turned out the apartment was still available, and we were able to write a cover letter stating her total income and reorganize the financials, and she was accepted."
Ms. Lincoln no longer thinks it was racism. Or does she? "Everybody discriminates against someone, don't they?" she said. "At my old building on St. Nicholas and 157th Street, they discriminated against Hispanics.
"Isn't that just life?"

HEADLINE: MERGERS FOR YEAR APPROACH RECORD

BYLINE: By STEPHANIE STROM 

 BODY:
This year's wave of mergers and acquisitions in the United States is poised to set a record, continuing a feverish pace of global business consolidation that during 1994 and 1995 could see as many companies swallowed up as during the last three years of the 1980's, the decade of the takeover.
According to the Securities Data Corporation, American mergers and acquisitions thus far this year were near $346 billion yesterday, just shy of the record $347.1 billion completed during all of last year. 
 D. Scott Lindsay, the co-head of mergers and acquisitions at CS First Boston, estimated that worldwide mergers and acquisitions could reach $800 billion this year, with a two-year total of $1.3 trillion. The $653.4 billion in global deals done so far this year is already a record.
In many ways, the deals announced so far this week are typical of many transactions this year. Yesterday, Softkey International, a software company, announced it was making an $800 million bid for two makers of education software. On Sunday, Praxair, an industrial gases spinoff from Union Carbide, said it planned a hostile $2.1 billion bid for a rival. And Comcast agreed to buy the cable television operations of E. W. Scripps for $1.58 billion.
While the entertainment, drug, banking and media industries have dominated center stage this year, the range of companies involved in acquisitions has touched all sectors of the economy, even the once somnolent utilities.
What many of the acquirers have in common is a new willingness, even by blue-chip companies, to engage in hostile takeovers, making this year the biggest for such deals since 1988. There is also a desire to capitalize on the strong performance of the stock market by financing deals with shares that are steadily rising in price.
"So long as the stock market stays at record highs, it's a great time for companies to do stock deals," said Samuel C. Butler, the managing partner at Cravath, Swaine & Moore, a law firm that has one of the leading practices in advising on mergers and acquisitions.
Other factors contributing to the deal-making boom include a perception of a looser regulatory environment and a sense that in a period of a slow-growth economy, business expansion can best be achieved by acquiring competitors. Deals also can be a way to satisfy pushy investors who want the reduced costs and better earnings that can be achieved in some mergers.
"Strategic, not financial, issues are behind most deals that have been done over the last two years," said Willard J. Overlock Jr., the head of global mergers and acquisitions at Goldman, Sachs, which brokered $93.7 billion of deals through the first three quarters of this year.
Wall Street is benefiting from the fees for the deals, even if more competition means the business is not quite as lucrative as it was in the 1980's. The broader economic benefit from the consolidation, however, is still being debated.
"There are people who like to read a variety of sinister implications into this sort of activity, but I just don't see it," said Robert Crandall, an economist at the Brookings Institute in Washington. "If in fact combined firms become more efficient by paring labor in the short term so that in the long term they do better, then I would think that's good for everyone in the long run."
This year's burst of activity is changing the corporate landscape. With the $7.5 billion acquisition of CBS, Westinghouse will metamorphose into a media company. The pending $488 million acquisition of the Washington Electric Company by Puget Sound Power and Light weds an electric company and a provider of natural gas.
The Southern Pacific Railroad Company's name will disappear when it is absorbed in the $5.3 billion merger with Union Pacific. Chemical Banking will give up its name in its $10 billion merger with Chase Manhattan. And with these and other corporate disappearances, thousands of jobs will also vanish as costs are cut to make the deals pay off.
It is too early to say whether the deals of the 1990's will be more successful than those of the last decade. But in some important respects they are different. The deals of this decade seem to be aimed more at strategic partnerships than at making a fat and fast return on an investment, which was the goal of many done in the late 1980's. And the deals may be resting on a better financial foundation because more of them are being financed with cash or stock than with borrowed money.
Many factors have led to the record-setting pace of deals, but one of the key ones has been the rally in the stock market, which has sent the Standard & Poor's index of 500 stocks up 27 percent this year.
This jump has given many corporations a powerful currency to spend -- their own high-priced shares. Wells Fargo, for example, was able to offer to pay roughly three times the $3.5 billion book value, or $10 billion, to buy First Interstate at least in part because its stock at the time it made its hostile bid in mid-October had appreciated almost 50 percent since the beginning of the year.
In addition, competition is putting pressure on pricing and costs. Thus, the prospect of cost savings -- often corporate America's euphemism for layoffs -- has spurred a lot of deals. Chemical and Chase Manhattan plan to shed 12,000 employees worldwide, while the chief executive of Kimberly Clark, which paid $7 billion for Scott Paper, has promised Wall Street a "significant staff reduction" in the companies' combined work force of 57,000.
Also, aggressive investors are increasingly focusing managements' attention on enhancing shareholder value. Michael Price, head of Heine Securities and a major owner of Chase's stock, was instrumental in forcing the Chemical-Chase merger by demanding that Chase move quickly to improve the value of its shares, which had been languishing. "When there is inadequate performance, their investors are quick to let their unhappiness be unknown," said Bruce D. Fiedorek, head of the worldwide mergers and acquisitions group at Morgan Stanley, which has been the No. 1 deal maker so far this year.
Plus, investors seem more willing to recognize the potential cost savings and synergies of a deal when it is announced. In the past, the price of an acquiring company's shares fell while the shares of the target company jumped. But in many of the deals this year, both the acquirer's stock and the stock of the target gained after the announcement. The stock of Wells Fargo jumped 7 percent, to $229 a share, when it announced on Oct. 18 its bid to buy First Interstate, whose stock also rose, climbing 32 percent to close at $140.25.
This more favorable reception may be one reason blue-chip companies have adopted the hostile takeover, once the exclusive province of buyout firms and corporate raiders, and its less controversial cousin, the "bear hug," or an offer too good to resist, as an acquisition strategy. I.B.M.'s hostile offer for Lotus Development seems to have legitimized the practice, paving the way for Johnson & Johnson, that gentle corporate giant famous for making baby shampoo, to make an unsolicited bid for Cordis.
According to Securities Data, the percentage of hostile deals, in terms of dollar volume, has risen to 14 percent this year from just under 6 percent in 1993. The dollar total of about $49 billion is second to 1988, when $127 billion, or 38 percent, of the deals were hostile.
And Mr. Fiedorek said American companies were increasingly exporting the hostile takeover to the European market, where International Paper made an unwanted offer for Holvis, a Swiss paper and medical materials manufacturer; ARCO went after Aran Energy of Dublin, and Amoco tried to buy Home Oil of Alberta, Canada.
As corporate America has become more aggressive, corporate raiders have begun to resurface. Kirk Kerkorian is goading Chrysler to sell out to him, while Carl C. Icahn has teamed up with Bennett S. LeBow to prod RJR Nabisco into selling its tobacco holdings.
In the entertainment business, regulatory changes that broke the barriers separating the producers and distributors of programming led to the biggest deal of the year so far, Walt Disney's $19 billion acquisition of Capital Cities/ABC. That spawned a host of similar deals, the last of which was Time Warner's proposed $7.5 billion purchase of Turner Broadcasting.
Slow economic growth has encouraged companies to combine in hopes of increasing revenue growth. Glaxo's $14 billion acquisition of Wellcome was an effort to increase its growth rate by combining two fairly stagnant streams of revenue and eliminating overlapping jobs and functions to bolster the combined company's growth.
While the lawyers and investment bankers who orchestrate mergers and acquisitions have definitely benefited from the elevated level of activity in the market, the business is not as lucrative as it once was. In part this is because the percentage a firm gets on a deal of more than $1 billion is lower than on smaller deals. Plus on larger deals, many companies are hiring more than one investment bank to represent them, which pushes fees down.
And since most deals these days lack the combativeness that marked the takeovers of the 1980's, lawyers are also making less. "The business today is a lot better than 1991 and 1992, but it isn't the bonanza that it was in the 1980's," Mr. Butler, the Cravath, Swaine partner, said.
To be sure, deal brokers are quick to add that the mergers and acquisitions business is difficult to predict. But they contend that the business has reached a new level of magnitude that is here to stay. "Worldwide, the dollar volume of deals done in the early 1980's was between $20 billion and $40 billion," Mr. Lindsay of CS First Boston said. "My sense is that, globally, the market today is somewhere between $400 billion and $800 billion."

HEADLINE: Mexican Markets Applaud Plan for Economy

BYLINE: By ANTHONY DePALMA 

DATELINE: MEXICO CITY, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Mexico's economic managers got the initial reaction they wanted today as financial markets responded favorably to a new recovery plan to revive the battered economy.
The Mexican stock exchange, which lost ground through most of October, roared back, gaining 4.57 percent, its biggest one-day gain since July. The market's main index shot up 102.27 points, to close at 2,348.64, as investors expressed confidence that the broad package of tax cuts, business incentives and fiscal restraints announced on Sunday would help Mexico break the hold of a recession that has turned out to be far deeper and more durable than expected. 
 The peso also rebounded after dropping last week. In late trading today, it sold at 6.925 to the dollar, or 14.44 cents, a gain of about 2 percent over Friday's rate.
Mexico customarily announces such significant economic policies on Sundays to leave some time for officials to shape reaction before the markets reopen for business. But they cannot control the longer view of investors and analysts.
Thus, while initial reaction was positive, there was a clear sense today that by itself the new package of incentives would not be sufficient to restore the investor confidence Mexico lost after a disastrous devaluation of the peso last December.
"The incentives are not a magic solution," said Mario Dehesa, director of Macro Asesoria Economica, an economic consulting firm here. "The stimuli will have a significant effect not in the short term, but more likely in the midterm."
Mexican business leaders were reluctant last week to sign another of the periodic pacts through which the Government has shaped its economic policies since 1987. Today, they wholeheartedly endorsed its business tax cuts and investment tax write-offs. That enthusiasm also brought a favorable reaction among investors and analysts in the United States, as well. American depository receipts of Telefonos de Mexico rose 87.5 cents, to $28.50, on the New York Stock Exchange. Prices of Mexican closed-end funds were up, too.
John F. H. Purcell, managing director of emerging markets research at Salomon Brothers in New York, said, "The apparent broadness of the agreement is seen as very positive," adding that the tax breaks on corporate investment and the elimination of the sales tax on new cars would help stimulate Mexico's economy.
But he was worried about the lack of a firm exchange-rate policy and said investors would need to see signs of growth by the middle of next year. "They will only wait so long before throwing up their hands and saying that Mexico is simply a no-growth country," Mr. Purcell said.
At a news conference today, Mexican officials said the exchange rate would continue to float freely, but they left unclear what level they expected it to reach next year. Finance Minister Guillermo Ortiz refused to say what target rate was used to calculate assumptions for the 20 percent inflation and 3 percent growth in the gross domestic product that the plan projects for 1996.
Mr. Ortiz said such a reference rate would be made public next month when the Government presented its budget to Congress.
He also said futures contracts on pesos now being sold in the Chicago Mercantile Exchange were poor indicators of what would happen to the exchange rate. Contracts now being sold for delivery in September 1996 call for an exchange rate of 10 pesos to the dollar.
But Alejandro Valenzuela, the Government's chief spokesman for the recovery programs, acknowledged that estimates of export growth for 1996 were based on what officials hoped would be a strengthening of the peso next year over this year. Exports, excluding services, are expected to grow 12 percent in peso terms, but 18 to 20 percent in dollar terms -- which, if true, would indicate that the peso will be stronger than it is today.

HEADLINE: Republican Bills on Health Bow to Wishes of Doctors

BYLINE: By MARTIN GOTTLIEB 

 BODY:
For years, many doctors practiced their profession in a cut-and-dried way: they hung out shingles and tended to their patients.
But the Medicare and Medicaid measures working their way through Congress could remold the role of many doctors, turning them into medical entrepreneurs and permitting them to engage in business enterprises that are now forbidden. The legislation is an important victory for the American Medical Association after years in which doctors have complained about losses of income and authority in the age of managed care. 
 The House version of the legislation would allow doctors to start physician-run health groups without the financial and regulatory requirements that states impose on similar organizations. Instead, the House bill would authorize development of new Federal regulations to police the doctors.
The bill could also make it easier for doctors to set prices in ways that now violate antitrust rules. It, along with the Senate version, would let doctors charge some Medicare recipients higher fees then they can now. And it would permit local medical groups to define standards in ways that Government officials say would curb competition and raise prices to patients.
President Clinton has threatened to veto the bills, making the future of these provisions uncertain. Before the President gets a bill, the House and Senate also have to compromise on their differences.
The House bill, more than the Senate version, reflects the lobbying efforts of the A.M.A. But both bills would help doctors do battle with managed care organizations and earn money in new ways.
"This is not about dollars; it's about control," Dr. Lonnie R. Bristow, president of the A.M.A., said in an interview, referring to many provisions. "It will give physicians an opportunity to come together and compete with the large insurance companies and corporate managed care entities."
Patient care will improve, he said, because decisions will be made by doctors and patients unencumbered by restrictions from private managed care groups. Those groups pay doctors a fixed fee for the care of a patient and often prohibit or discourage treatments deemed to be ineffective or too expensive.
Republicans argue that in place of fee restrictions and other Government regulations, a hotly competitive market for Medicare and Medicaid dollars will hold down prices and improve benefits.
But consumer groups and top antitrust officials say many changes would be disastrous for patients, potentially leading to higher fees, poorer care and a fraying of trust, with patients questioning whether doctors are making decisions for economic rather than medical reasons.
"One of the fundamental elements of health care is a personal relationship, a human relationship, which can't be quantified," said Gene Kimmelman, co-director of the Washington office of Consumers Union, the advocacy group. "This represents the corporatization or commodification of medical care, if you will."
The Republican legislation would, over seven years, cut more than $440 billion in anticipated growth from the budgets of Medicare, which serves 37 million elderly people, and Medicaid, which covers almost as many poor and disabled people, and nursing home residents.
Doctors would absorb billions in losses in projected income, according to Congressional estimates.
For the A.M.A., which endorsed the House Republican plan days before its passage, the lobbying effort centered not only on trying to limit the losses, but also on extending the ability of doctors to operate as entrepreneurs and curtailing their liability in cases of malpractice and suspected fraud and abuse.
The group succeeded most strikingly in the House legislation, which relaxes restrictions on doctors' ability to operate laboratories and own companies that they can refer patients to, heightens the Government's burden of proof in fraud and abuse cases and limits punitive malpractice awards.
 
Doctors Push Insurers Back
 Most significant, many experts say, the House legislation gives doctors the opportunity to start their own health plans, physician service networks, without having to put up the capital reserves or meet the detailed mandates often required by states of managed care companies.
These networks would be exempt from state regulations on quality standards, appeals processes for patients and monetary reserves, which are intended to insure that plans have enough money to cover expensive treatment.
The House bill would have these groups regulated by the Federal Government rather than by states, where restrictions by insurance departments have often limited the ability of doctors to compete. The Senate version would require the doctors' groups to apply for licenses from the states, but gives them the right to go to the Federal level if they do not receive approval in 90 days or if they are rejected under regulations deemed to be at variance with the Federal ones.
"I think that's the Normandy Invasion by the providers to try to roll back the insurance industry's growing role in health care," said Lynn Etheredge, head of the health care staff at the Office of Management and Budget in the Jimmy Carter and Ronald Reagan Administrations.
Uwe E. Reinhardt, a health care economist at Princeton University, said this provision, as well as others, would allow doctors to reassert themselves after humbling years in which the advent of cost-cutting managed care companies led to a loss of autonomy and often of income, as well.
By operating their own managed care groups and benefiting from the relaxation of many regulations, doctors, he said, "will move to the head of the food chain."
 
Making a Business Out of Healing
 Under the plans, doctors would start a network, typically through a community hospital or county or state medical society, and offer services to Medicare recipients. The networks would receive a fixed rate for each member from the Federal Government. From this, it would administer the patients' care, keeping any money left over, but also running the risk of having to pay expenses that run beyond the Government contributions.
Several doctors health plans along these lines have already been started around the country.
In addition, under both the House and Senate bills, doctors would be free to set their fees for certain patients -- those who receive care outside their networks or who pay for care under the traditional fee-for-service arrangement. Currently, doctors are allowed to charge a maximum of 115 percent of what Medicare deems a fair price in such situations.
This provision was vehemently attacked by consumer groups, but Republicans argued that the increased competition fostered by the legislation would help hold down fees and make price controls unnecessary.
Howard B. Shapiro, director of public policy for the American College of Physicians, the professional organization of internists that strongly opposed the broad legislation, said the network proposal would help encourage preventive medicine. And he added that unlike standard managed care plans, the doctor-run plans would not "take out of the system the marketing and shareholder percentages that are lost to health care."
Insurers and managed care companies oppose the legislation, fearing that it will give doctors an unfair advantage by exempting them from regulations they say should apply to everyone.
Consumer groups warn that if the doctors' networks do not have to adhere to strict standards, like putting up substantial capital reserves, they will invite scandals similar to ones that afflicted California 20 years ago. There, many health maintenance organizations with meager capital reserves went bankrupt and left patients stranded.
The groups also argue that the new networks will make doctors responsible for care and put them on the line for expensive procedures that could damage them financially.
"All the concern about overtreatment when doctors were paid a fee for every service they performed is matched by a concern for undertreatment here," said Cathy L. Hurwit, legislative director of Citizens Action, an advocacy group.
Predictions vary widely about the financial potential of the networks.
"They have an enormous marketing advantage," Professor Reinhardt said of doctors. "They can literally market their plans in the examination room."
But Kenneth S. Abramowitz, a health securities analyst with Sanford S. Bernstein & Company, noted that good doctors were often not good businessmen. "They have no chance of making it, but they want to have a chance," he said dryly.
Gail Wilensky, who ran the Medicare and Medicaid programs in the George Bush Administration, said that after years of doctors complaining about H.M.O.'s, "this is an attempt to say, 'All right, guys, put your money where your mouths have been.' "
 
Regulations Are Wiped Away
 Doctors would also be helped by two changes in antitrust laws in the House bill.
One would allow medical societies and other professional groups to write standards "designed to promote the quality of health care services provided to patients." Many doctors maintain such standards are necessary for patient safety.
But in a letter to Representative Pete Stark, Democrat of California, Robert Pitofsky, chairman of the Federal Trade Commission, and Anne K. Bingaman, who heads the Justice Department's antitrust division, warned that under the guise of protecting quality care, local medical societies have in the past -- and could again in the future -- write standards that in reality are intended to squelch competition.
Such standards, they wrote, have been used to enforce "broad restraints on price competition, policies that inhibited the development of managed care organizations, and concerted refusals to deal with providers or organizations that represented a competitive threat to physicians."
The second provision would exempt doctors in networks who set fees together from a rule that would hold such activity on its face to be illegal price-fixing, unless the doctors have a financial relationship.
Instead, the Government would have to consider each case independently. Dr. Bristow of the A.M.A. said the change would simply require a review of individual situations. "If it is anticompetitive, that's what they should say," he said.
But consumer groups fear that the Government would not have the time or personnel to consider every case. Ms. Bingaman and Mr. Pitofsky described such price setting as a "naked restraint on competition."
Dr. Bristow said that in the end the various provisions would allow the doctor-patient relationship to flourish. Professor Reinhardt, who has worked with the A.M.A. in the past, disagreed. "This thing of physicians becoming corporate entrepreneurs may very well tarnish their image," he said, referring to the many changes that would help doctors.
"When they pass around the cookie plate and you take two cookies, no one will say anything, but it will hurt your image."

HEADLINE: Washington Journal;
For City's Strays, a New Life of Sorts

BYLINE: By MICHAEL JANOFSKY 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30

 BODY:
The District of Columbia is still going to the dogs, and for once in this financially strapped city, that's good news.
Hours after the Washington Humane Society shut down the capital's largest animal shelter today rather than allow the city to run up a tab for animal control services that has reached nearly $400,000, another private concern, Animal Link Inc., agreed to take over the shelter. 
 That not only assured that as many as 80 stray dogs and 30 cats could be housed at one time in the safety, if not comfort, of steel-fence cages, it also meant that workers would continue picking up stray animals deemed to be too dangerous or too aggressive to leave roaming the streets.
Still, the sudden change in management underscores what has happened to many city contractors in Washington because the city cannot pay its bills. After hanging on for months, looking for the city's check in the mail, the Humane Society finally gave up. Its officials spent the last few days relocating the animals to other shelters before closing their doors this morning and turning people away.
John Allem, a spokesman for Mayor Marion S. Barry Jr., said the District of Columbia still owed private vendors about $140 million, after paying off part of the $322 million of debt the Barry Administration says it inherited from the previous Mayor, Sharon Pratt Kelly.
Mary Healy, executive director of the Humane Society, said problems for the shelter began earlier this year after city officials stopped signing yearlong contracts in favor of short-term deals, some for as brief as five days.
"It became increasingly difficult to hire and maintain a staff," said Ms. Healy, who once had a staff of 30 full-time and part-time workers. "But how can you hire someone if you can't tell them they'll have a job five days from now?"
The change is, at least, an encouraging development for abandoned pets. Ms. Healy said that about 60 percent of the dogs and cats that reached the shelter were returned to their owners, and 20 percent were adopted by others. The remainder are put to death.
 
These Mean Streets
 Among businesses large and small hurt by the city's inability to pay its bills, Ebone Inc. provides a bleak example of trickle-down economics as it works these days in Washington.
Ebone, a minority-owned construction company that has repaired city streets, gutters and sidewalks for 17 years -- and once grossed $9 million in annual revenue -- is nearly out of business. Belle Vee Gentry, the owner, said the unpaid bills had caused her to fall behind in her taxes and to dismiss all but four of the company's 75 employees.
The Internal Revenue Service has notified her that on Wednesday it plans to seize her fleet of trucks and other heavy equipment, worth about $500,000. Some of her former employees, she said, have turned to illegal activities because they cannot find other jobs to support their families.
She blamed the District government for withholding more than $2.4 million for work completed and the Federal Government, particularly President Clinton, for not doing more to protect local businesses from poor management by the city.
"I'm losing everything I worked for, and people's lives have been destroyed," Ms. Gentry said, citing $460,000 owed her for work completed this year and another $2 million for previous projects.
"We have gone from solvency to total insolvency," she continued. "My family has been devastated. It's terrible, and I just can't understand how the Government can let the nation's capital fall."
Ms. Gentry said she would sue the city if she had the money to hire a lawyer.
 
From Darkness to Light
 When the Potomac Electric Power Company sold the city's street-light system to the District government 10 years ago, it signed a maintenance contract promising to keep the city properly lighted.
But with the contract expired and the city this month paying off only $8 million of an $11.8 million debt, the utility stopped replacing burned-out bulbs along Washington's streets and alleys. That caught the attention of Speaker Newt Gingrich, whose interest in helping the city recover stems in part from his concern over public safety.
An aide to Mr. Gingrich called John Derrick, president of the utility, and prevailed on the company to replace the dead bulbs. As a result, the utility dispatched work crews last Friday night, and they worked through the weekend, making dark streets light. Nancy Moses, a spokeswoman for the company, said crews would be sent out another three nights this week to finish the job.
In return, Ms. Moses said, the city has promised to pay off its debt this week and enter into a new maintenance contract.
