HEADLINE: Peace in the Balkans Now Depends On the Man Who Fanned Its Wars

BYLINE: By ROGER COHEN 

DATELINE: BELGRADE, Serbia

 BODY:
Slobodan Milosevic, the Serbian President, is often to be found padding through the cavernous presidency building in Belgrade, alone except for a single faithful aide.
"He's the only person you ever see there," said an American official who has conversed a great deal with Mr. Milosevic of late. "It's eerie the way he rattles around the building. Once he wandered into a side room and muttered about never having been in it before. Sometimes you ask yourself: Is there anyone else in this country?" 
 It is a pertinent question. Eight years after he seized power by exhorting Serbs to rise against perceived past injustices and create a "Greater Serbia," and four years after the wars of Yugoslavia's destruction began, Mr. Milosevic stands alone. He seems close to the end of a labyrinthine political journey that has left his ragtag band of former nationalist allies and his former country devastated.
What remains is one man's almost uncontested personal power in Serbia, and his remorseless quest for the renewed international respectability that would vindicate him.
Mr. Milosevic's visit to the United States for the peace talks that are to start on Wednesday in Dayton, Ohio, brings him closer than ever to that goal. For a long time, he has yearned to set foot in America. It is the closest thing to the fulfillment of an obsession: He is given to reminiscences of the "wonderful smells of New York" and the charms of the Drake Hotel, a regular haunt during his stint as president of a major Belgrade bank.
But even as he emerges from isolation, cast in the role of peacemaker, this man of jutting jaw and shifting tactics seems haunted by his past.
Now seen by western powers as a key figure in the quest for peace in the Balkans, Mr. Milosevic is also the man most identified with the descent of Yugoslavia into war.
Like shadows, the wars of Croatia and Bosnia, their bloodshed and their atrocities, keep darkening his path, even as he charms dignitary after dignitary with good humor, good English and apparent good will.
This month the dignitary was Richard C. Holbrooke, Assistant Secretary of State who is chief American envoy to the Balkans; the atrocities were those reportedly carried out in northwest Bosnia by Zeljko Raznatovic, the Serbian paramilitary leader known as Arkan.
Between Oct. 6 and 12, half of an estimated 2,000 Muslim men who had been detained in the area were killed. Mr. Holbrooke's question was simple: Can't you control Arkan?
The response, American officials said, was outraged protestation. Not only could he not control Arkan, Mr. Milosevic insisted, but the paramilitary leader would probably kill him if he had a chance. "Arkan is my sworn enemy," an official quoted Mr. Milosevic as saying.
The reply was consistent. Through 42 months of war in Bosnia he has held fast to his position: Bosnia is another country. To link him with killing or genocide there, Mr. Milosevic told BBC television recently, "is really out of all consideration."
 
America Good; Russian Meat Bad
 The case of Arkan is, however, difficult to reconcile with Mr. Milosevic's claims. An intelligence report carried by Mr. Holbrooke, for example, confirmed that Arkan was already in the employ of the Serbian security services in the late 1980's.
But Mr. Milosevic is a persuasive man. He exudes power; he wants peace now; he likes America. He can also be witty. When an American official recently asked about Russian "humanitarian aid" for Serbia, Mr. Milosevic scoffed: "Humanitarian aid? You mean some meat we had to bury because it was radioactive and some biscuits manufactured in 1969?" His pronunciation of "biscuits" is "bis-kweets."
This bonhomie alternates with a frozen stoniness. When 170,000 Serbs were chased from the Krajina in August, he remained silent and distant. He is apt to set his jaw, narrow his shrewd eyes and wave away the carnage of Bosnia. The basic message is: These Bosnian Serbs are crazy, so what can I do?
Robert Frasure, the American diplomat who died in an accident near Sarajevo last August and dealt extensively with the Serbian President, once said he was reminded of "a Mafia boss who desperately wants to get out of the business."
Mr. Milosevic has been trying since mid-1993 to put the tidal wave of Serbian nationalism he orchestrated and the accompanying destruction of Yugoslavia behind him. His conclusion that diplomacy could achieve his ends -- described by one American official as "Greater Serbia Lite" -- is what now makes him central to American diplomacy.
"Forced to choose between Greater Serbia and Greater Slobo, there is no doubt where Milosevic has come down," said one western diplomat. "He now sees Serbia as a prosperous little eastern gateway to Europe, and he needs peace to secure that."
Over the last several months, moreover, Mr. Milosevic has delivered. He has forced the Bosnian Serbs to line up behind him and begin serious peace negotiations. And American officials say he has promised that, in the event of a peace, elections would oust the Bosnian Serb leader, Radovan Karadzic, and the Serbs' military leader, Ratko Mladic, would be removed.
The removal of these men, closely identified with the massacre of Muslims, is almost certainly vital if any Bosnian peace is to last and any hope remains of patching Bosnia together again.
Thus Mr. Milosevic has an important role, if he can be taken at his word. But if General Mladic can be removed, why could his savage assault on Srebrenica not be stopped?
Moreover, Mr. Milosevic's recent compliancy, like many other tactical switches in his career, begs the question of what he believes in and to what extent he is believable.
 
A Son of Suicides Adopted by Marxism
 He was born in the provincial town of Pozarevac, about 50 miles from Belgrade, the son of an Orthodox priest who committed suicide when his son was 4. His mother was a schoolteacher who committed suicide several years later.
Mr. Milosevic, earnest and energetic, embraced Communism early and rose through the ranks, hitched to the coat-tails of Ivan Stambolic, the leader of the Serbian Communist party, whom he ultimately humiliated and deposed in 1987.
The vehicle for this humiliation, and for Mr. Milosevic's own apotheosis, was Serbian nationalism. He discovered its force, and potential political rewards, in Kosovo in April 1987.
In a stand-off between the Serbs of the then-autonomous region and the ethnic Albanians who account for 90 percent of Kosovo's population, Mr. Milosevic abruptly shed the dour garb of the apparatchik to side with the Serbs against the mainly Albanian authorities of his own party.
Film of this critical moment shows Mr. Milosevic hesitating before the Serbian crowd, then ditching his past, thundering to the ecstatic crowd of Serbs, "Nobody has the right to beat you!"
Having sensed his opportunity, Mr. Milosevic moved fast. Medieval battles, the relics of Serbian kings, the sacrifices of Serbian soldiers in two world wars, the alleged injustices endured by Serbs in Tito's Yugoslavia, all suddenly became grist for the nationalist mill.
When tens of thousands of Serbs, appalled by the increasingly hate-filled propaganda on state-controlled media, tried to protest in the streets of Belgrade in 1991, Mr. Milosevic crushed them with tanks.
 
'Serbia Does Not Kneel' Is the Party's Motto
 Baying supporters, bused through Serbia, chanted the name of "Slobo" as he eliminated the autonomous status of Kosovo and Vojvodina and brought them under direct Serbian rule. Serbia, the refrain went, would no longer be humiliated. The motto of his Communist-turned-Socialist party was, "Serbia does not kneel."
"The national and historic being of Serbs," Mr. Milosevic declared, "is a liberating one."
But the rest of Yugoslavia did not want to be "liberated;" on the contrary, it took fright. Indeed, perhaps the deepest irony of the Serbs' argument that they have only fought in the last four years because they wanted to remain in Yugoslavia is that Mr. Milosevic almost certainly did more than anyone to destabilize the country and set it on its course to implosion.
Another irony is that the very right the Serbs have claimed -- that of their people in Bosnia and Croatia to govern themselves -- was precisely the one Mr. Milosevic denied to the ethnic Albanians of Kosovo.
Moreover, it has become clear that Mr. Milosevic's real interest in preserving Yugoslavia was slight. When Slovenia seceded on June 26, 1991, it was allowed to go with scarcely a fight: there was no Serbian minority there. The real aim, as a senior presidential aide, Borislav Jovic, recently explained, was to use wars in Croatia and Bosnia to take the Serbian-occupied areas and so forge a Greater Serbia.
In extensive interviews with the BBC for a documentary on the destruction of Yugoslavia, Mr. Jovic, now vice president of Mr. Milosevic's Serbian Socialist party, said that in April 1991, more than two months before war broke out in Croatia: "We decided to change tactics. We would deploy troops in Serb areas of Croatia, the Croats would provoke war, and we would then take those territories."
The same tactics were used in Bosnia, where war broke out in April 1992.
"We knew that when Bosnia was recognized, we'd be seen as aggressors because our army was there," Mr. Jovic said. "So Milosevic and I talked it over, and we realized we'd have to pull a fast one. We transferred all the Bosnian Serbs in our Yugoslav Army to their forces and promised to pay all their costs." Thus was an extremely well-armed Bosnian Serb force created.
 
What Goes Around Comes Around
 The results, in the end, have been disappointing. The war in Croatia has effectively been lost and the war in Bosnia is going against the Serbs. Serbia lies in economic ruin and hundreds of thousands of Serbs are refugees. Moreover, because Mr. Milosevic has resorted to irregular forces to disguise his hand in the wars, Arkan and other paramilitary groups have been given a freer hand.
"Any assessment of Milosevic must conclude that he has been immensely destructive, to his own people and to others," said Zarko Korac, an opposition politician. "The world now wants to see him as a pragmatist, but it is destructiveness that, up to now, seems to distinguish him."
The Serbian President is also a survivor. His overwhelming control of the police and press has insured that, as has his ability to convince foreign diplomats that he constitutes the ineluctable road to peace.
"He's so easy to deal with compared to the Bosnian Government," said one American official. "He's charming and decisive. Sometimes we have to remind ourselves that with Milosevic we are dealing with a compliant dictator, whereas in Bosnia we are dealing with a muddled, fledgling democracy."

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Canadian Dollar Surges in Asia After Quebec Rejects Secession

BYLINE: By Reuters 

 BODY:
The Canadian dollar rallied last night after Quebec voters narrowly rejected a proposal to secede from Canada.
Initial returns after polls closed had showed a sharp separatist lead, leading to a steep selloff of the Canadian dollar against the United States dollar in Asian trading. 
 But as later results came in, showing a swing in votes against an independent Quebec, the Canadian dollar recovered and shot up more than 2 United States cents in less than an hour.
"The Canadian dollar was really getting whacked on the early return," John Lilley, a currency analyst with MMS International in Singapore, told Bloomberg Business News. With the late returns giving the opponents of independence a decisive lead, "the currency and bond markets have taken off," he said.
By midday in Tokyo today, the Canadian dollar was quoted at 74.71 United States cents, up from its late New York quote of 73.34 United States cents.
Earlier yesterday, the United States dollar was little changed in thin trading as currency traders moved cautiously while awaiting the results of the vote in Quebec.
"People are sitting on the sidelines watching to see what happens in Canada and how it will affect the dollar," said David Ogg, foreign-exchange manager at Midland Bank.
The possibility of an independent Quebec raised questions about the effect on Canada's heavy debt load and on the North American trade agreement.
In New York trading late yesterday, the United States dollar edged up to 101.95 Japanese yen from 101.93 yen on Friday, but it slipped to 1.4089 German marks from 1.4090. The British pound rose to $1.5779 from $1.5765.
The United States dollar was locked in narrow ranges during the day.
 "There are some good reasons to buy dollars and some good reasons to sell dollars, and I'm not sure the market knows which way to go," said James Kemp, chief dealer at Citibank.
 Mr. Kemp said the perception of instability in Canada and Mexico, coupled with tensions over the Federal budget negotiations in the United States, could weigh on the dollar. But political uncertainty in Russia was bolstering it.
 The Mexican peso strengthened yesterday after investors felt reassured by a weekend economic pact among the Government, labor and business. The accord inspired more confidence in the peso, which tumbled last week on worries over Mexico's economy. The dollar traded at 6.925 pesos yesterday, down from 7.0650 on Friday.

HEADLINE: Europeans Pledge Aid for the Balkans

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: LUXEMBOURG, Oct. 30

 BODY:
The European Union will provide $2 billion in reconstruction aid for the countries that made up the former Yugoslavia, a third of the total package, if coming peace talks lead to an overall settlement, officials said today.
Foreign Minister Herve de Charette of France said that European Union foreign ministers would like to see $2 billion contributed by the United States, with the final $2 billion provided by other countries, probably wealthy Gulf states and other Muslim nations. 
 The money would be disbursed jointly by the European Union and the international lending organizations, the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund.
The European Commission, the European executive body, plans to hold a pledging meeting for donors in early December with the aim of raising the money.
An outline plan by the commission would divide aid into three categories: relief efforts, rehabilitation money and reconstruction.

HEADLINE: Chilly Scenes for Winter;
Retailers Brace for a Gray Christmas Selling Season

BYLINE: By JENNIFER STEINHAUER 

 BODY:
Mimi Cassin's Christmas list is not the stuff of traditional fantasy. It seems she would be happy with a salad bowl.
"This year, I would like something like kitchenware," said Ms. Cassin, who lives in Kansas City, Mo., where she is an assistant in a retail leasing firm. As for giving, she plans to spend less money on her friends and family this holiday than she did last year, and will not be dropping into clothing stores, preferring art supplies for her 5-year-old. 
 Ms. Cassin is not the only one planning for a modest Christmas this year, bringing scant joy to retailers, who have grown weary of cautious consumers spoiling the industry's most crucial season.
A high level of consumer debt, a low savings rate and a new wave of mergers and acquisitions that has many people edgy about their jobs are all expected to contribute to reined-in spending, economists say. Many analysts expect retail sales for the season, which traditionally begins the day after Thanksgiving, to rise by about 5 percent, compared with 7 percent last year.
"I am expecting a decent, but not spectacular, Christmas," said Stanton J. Bluestone, the chief executive of Carson Pirie Scott & Company, a department store chain in the Midwest. "The consumer is somewhat restricted."
Not everyone is hemmed in, of course. Many investors are quite flush, given the sharp rise in the stock markets this year, though how much of that paper wealth shows up in retailers' registers is uncertain. Still, while much of working America is feeling the pinch, affluent shoppers are not expected to head for the sidelines.
Nor is the outlook the same for all retailers. To hear some analysts and shoppers tell it, Christmas morning may feature cozy scenes like these: consumers turning on the new computer, unwrapping some jewelry, perhaps even trying out the new vacuum cleaner -- all while wearing last year's shabby bathrobes.
Indeed, the predictions for apparel are far worse than for any other category, a continuation of that group's decline. Computer products and household goods are picked as winners, reflecting the current trends for nearly everything high-tech and nest-feathering. And while cheap bracelets and low-end paisley scarves may suffer, upscale merchandise like expensive jewelry and furniture should fare better.
"I see this as being a very selective Christmas," Janet Kloppenburg, an analyst in New York at Robertson, Stephens & Company, said. "It won't be a total doomsday, because certain retailers have positioned themselves very intimately with their customers and they know what an aging baby boomer wants."
To be sure, predicting any Christmas season is dicey. In 1992, for example, analysts forecast clouds and misery, and the season ended nicely. And at least a few experts are bucking the consensus view, saying many people who managed to keep costs in check all year are now ready to splurge.
Still, whatever the sales numbers turn out to be, most agree that profit margins will be slimmer across the board, if only because retailers will be forced to begin the season with big sales to lure shoppers who have been spoiled by the discounting in recent Christmases.
Clearly, many retailers are already reaching for their Tums.
Same-store sales, the clearest indicator of how retail operations are faring, have been poor in the clothing business for months. More broadly, declining profits quarter after quarter have been harbingers of bad things for general discounters, off-price chains and the specialty stores. Some, like the Caldor Corporation, filed for bankruptcy protection even before waiting to see how the latest quarter turns out. Department stores, too, will have to work overtime to turn profits.
Even catalogue retailers, who rely heavily on the holiday season to lift them through the rest of the year, are worried, because they have been hurt badly by big increases in paper costs and, to a lesser degree, postal rates. The Christmas catalogue by Lands' End, for instance, is 20 pages thinner than last year, and is offering incentives like free postage and monogramming for early orders.
In recent years, retailers have been in the difficult position of needing to unload a huge overstock of goods during the holiday season any way they could. As a result, shoppers have come to expect the pre-Christmas sale, a promotion almost unheard of a decade ago.
"Retailers have trained customers to be value-conscious," said Karen Sack, an analyst in New York at the Standard & Poor's Corporation. "Sales were up last year at Christmas, around 7 percent, but it was because stores were very promotional. This year it will be more like 5 percent, but it is going to be very promotional again."
Rosemary Bregande is on to this, and snoops around the Galleria Shopping Center in Houston for bargains. Mrs. Bregande, who is 55, said that she had already bought her grandchild's Christmas present -- a winter wardrobe -- at half the price she was used to paying years ago.
Recent Government figures on rising levels of consumer debt help to explain why many shoppers are expected to be so careful. Personal debts are reaching a level close to the most recent peak of the late 1980's, a point at which most consumers started to pull back on spending.
Other factors are at work as well. "The savings rate is fairly low in historical context," Donald H. Straszheim, the chief economist at Merrill Lynch & Company, said. "It is 4 percent now, and has been more like 5 in recent years."
Mr. Straszheim also said that people are increasingly nervous about Washington's plans to cut back on social spending, particularly for Medicare, which further tightens their purse strings.
Despite all the concerns, some retailers expect a fine season. Almost all analysts said that a surge in interest in technology will translate into long lines at the checkout counter for sellers of computers, facsimile machines and other electronic equipment.
Some analysts picked Best Buy and Circuit City Stores as consumer-electronics chains likely to do well, along with the Compaq Computer Corporation and other manufacturers that are introducing new products.
"I was at the Mall of America last week and all the entertainment stores were very busy," said Alan G. Millstein, publisher of the Fashion Network Report, an industry newsletter, referring to the Bloomington, Minn., shopping mall, the country's largest. "You could have rolled a bowling ball through the apparel stores."
Paul Ewert, vice president of merchandising at CompUSA Inc., a national chain of computer stores, is already licking his lips. "We expect to do extremely well this Christmas season," he said, ticking off a series of products that shoppers are enamored of, like multimedia personal computers, the latest generation of high-speed modems and video enhancers. He declined, though, to discuss projections about profits.
"Electronics will have an outstanding Christmas," Mr. Millstein said. "But I think their margins will be hurt simply because of price wars, and because computers don't self-destruct like clothes, so they are not replaced often."
Lisa Davis, a 35-year-old lawyer in Manhattan, said that while she had cut back on her general spending, she intended to allow for frills, like an upgrade for her wedding ring. "I want a diamond band," she said with a firmness that should send her husband walking briskly to Fortunoff. "I don't like utilitarian gifts."
She represents a small but significant group of spenders.
"Luxury items are likely to do quite well for a strange reason that we call the wealth effect," said Kurt Barnard, the president of Barnard's Retail Marketing Report, a trade publication. "The stock market has soared and a lot of people are feeling extremely wealthy."
Ms. Kloppenburg, the analyst at Robertson, Stephens, also expects a lift from this group, forecasting an 8 percent increase in fine jewelry sales for the coming season.
"Jewelry is perceived as having value and being durable," said Alan M. Zimmer, the chief executive of Reeds Jewelers Inc., which has 100 stores in the East. His company saw a 7 percent increase in same-store sales last year, and he expects to hit that range again.
And for housewares, Bed Bath and Beyond, a chain based in Springfield, N.J., seems to be the analysts' darling this year, with consumers expressing a strong desire for items for the home. A spokeswoman for the chain said it expected to do well in everything from waffle makers to flannel sheets to Christmas-decorated toilet paper.
Within apparel, Mr. Barnard said he expected Sears, Roebuck & Company to be the anomaly, largely because he thinks the company's new marketing push for reasonably priced women's fashions may well bump out rivals that are offering similar products for less value.
Over all, retailers may find the blows softened by earlier experience. Third-quarter earnings were so weak in many areas that the Christmas numbers may not look that bad. Also, retailers have learned from previous bad holiday seasons to keep inventories in check.
And there are those retail bulls who predict that at the last minute, consumers are going to throw their money about.
"I'm thinking that a lot of analysts are just wrong," said Audrey Gusky, a professor of marketing at Duquesne University in Pittsburgh. "A lot of people have been postponing their purchases all year and now feel they are due."

HEADLINE: Real Politics: Why Suharto Is In and Castro Is Out

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Fidel Castro never got so much as a smile or a handshake when he stepped into the same reception with President Clinton last week; the Secret Service deftly kept them from coming face to face.
President Jiang Zemin of China did a bit better. The Administration quashed his hopes for a state visit, but insisted that a largely empty two-hour meeting in New York had advanced the cause of "comprehensive engagement" with the Chinese, the kind of engagement Mr. Castro is desperate to win. 
 And then came Suharto, the aging, military-backed leader of Indonesia, and a man who also knows a good deal about how to keep dissenters under control. When he arrived at the White House on Friday for a "private" visit with the President, the Cabinet room was jammed with top officials ready to welcome him. Vice President Gore was there, along with Secretary of State Warren Christopher; the Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff, Gen. John Shalikashvili; Commerce Secretary Ronald H. Brown; the United States trade representative, Mickey Kantor; the national security adviser, Anthony Lake, and many others.
"There wasn't an empty chair in the room," one participant said. "No one used to treat the Indonesians like this, and it said a lot about how our priorities in the world have changed."
For a quick understanding of how the Clinton Administration balances American economic interests abroad, Presidential election politics at home and human rights concerns around the world these days, simply look at how President Clinton dealt with three very different, very authoritarian foreign visitors.
For years Washington has embraced dictatorial leaders, particularly anti-Communist ones, when it served the national interest. But in interviews in recent days, Administration officials said the treatment of Mr. Castro, Mr. Jiang and Mr. Suharto was driven by very different litmus tests, a potent mix of power politics and emerging markets.
Of all the three leaders, Mr. Castro probably evokes the strongest emotional response, especially in the Cuban community in Florida, where anti-Castro sentiment remains at a fever pitch and a crucial state in the coming election. "There are no votes riding on how we deal with Indonesia, and not many on how we deal with China," one of Mr. Clinton's foreign policy advisers said the other day. "Castro is still political dynamite."
And of the three, he has the fewest strategic and economic cards to play. Russia no longer cares about Mr. Castro's fate. And he has few other sources of capital to reach for. The Europeans, the Japanese and the overseas Chinese, who are vying for manufacturing footholds in Indonesia and China, could care less about his little island.
That allows the United States the luxury of continuing a policy of isolating Cuba while proclaiming that "engaging" other hard-line Communist countries, including China, Vietnam and North Korea, is the best way to bring about change in their Governments.
"You use your leverage where you can find it, and we are in a unique position to isolate Castro," a senior Administration official said on Friday when asked what it would have cost Mr. Clinton to shake Mr. Castro's hand. "It is a real power politics approach, but why would we want to give him the status and respectability that would come from a meeting with the President until he has given way? And if he doesn't deal with us, who else can he turn to?"
Fred Bergsten, who heads the Institute for International Economics in Washington, noted recently: "Cuba is the anomaly. Not only are its relations with the U.S. driven by domestic politics here, it has nothing to offer. Vietnam at least has 90 million people in a terrific neighborhood, a place where everyone wants a plant." And those who want to deal with Mr. Castro -- including most of the nations in Latin America -- do not have the billions of dollars in capital available to build highways, plants and factories there, even if they were willing to take the political risk.
Mr. Jiang presented a far more complex case for the White House. Since the beginning of the summer, when China was outraged by the Clinton Administration's decision to allow Taiwan's President, Lee Teng-hui, to make a so-called private visit to Cornell University, the State Department has been consumed with getting back on speaking terms with Beijing. The rebuilding process began with Mr. Christopher, who met his Chinese counterpart in Brunei, and then New York. The Chinese responded by releasing Harry Wu, an American who had been seized and charged with espionage during one of his human rights investigations in China.
But Mr. Clinton is having a harder and harder time making the case that his decision two years ago to "delink" the issue of human rights from the annual extension of preferential trade treatment is paying off. Nor is there much evidence that "commercial engagement," a phrase coined by Mr. Brown, has resulted in significantly better treatment for dissidents.
Moreover, he can hardly boast too loudly about the economic benefits reaped by his new approach. American exports to China have risen slowly -- they now stand at about $9 billion -- but the trade deficit has exploded to Japan-like levels.
Certainly there is huge pressure from American businesses to keep all channels to the Government open, but privately American business executives tell tale after tale of frustration. Of the $6 billion in deals signed with great fanfare by Mr. Brown in Beijing last year, two-thirds have barely made progress.
But unlike Cuba, China is all about huge potential, upside and downside. Failure to get into the market is seen as tantamount to surrender to Japanese, Taiwanese and European business interests. And everyone in Asia expects the United States to continue acting as the counterweight to Chinese military power -- without, of course, feeding China's fear that a containment policy is under way.
"This is a case where you are dealing with a great power, and you have no choice but to make sure that your contacts stay open," one of Mr. Clinton's top foreign policy advisers said last week. "But bring Jiang to a state dinner, with trumpets and everything? That would be a bit much for everyone to take."
That leaves Mr. Suharto, who is sitting on the ultimate emerging market: some 13,000 islands, a population of 193 million and an economy growing at more than 7 percent a year. The country remains wildly corrupt and Mr. Suharto's family controls leading businesses that competitors in Jakarta would be unwise to challenge. But Mr. Suharto, unlike the Chinese, has been savvy in keeping Washington happy. He has deregulated the economy, opened Indonesia to foreign investors and kept the Japanese, Indonesia's largest supplier of foreign aid, from grabbing more than a quarter of the market for goods imported into the country.
So Mr. Clinton made the requisite complaints about Indonesia's repressive tactics in East Timor, where anti-Government protests continue, and moved right on to business, getting Mr. Suharto's support for market-opening progress during the annual Asian Pacific Economic Cooperation meeting in Osaka in mid-November.
"He's our kind of guy," a senior Administration official who deals often on Asian policy, said the other day. "The message of his visit was clear: this is the kind of relationship we want to have with China."

HEADLINE: Guggenheim Foundation, Long a Donor, Is Now Having to Seek Donations

BYLINE: By KAREN W. ARENSON 

 BODY:
For 70 years, the John Simon Guggenheim Memorial Foundation has bestowed grants on promising scholars, scientists and artists -- from Aaron Copland, Ansel Adams and James Baldwin to Henry A. Kissinger, Linus Pauling and Paul Samuelson -- 13,500 in all.
But now, cup in hand, the foundation, set up with proceeds from the Guggenheim mining fortune, is asking others for contributions so it can continue to give money to a new generation of artists and researchers. 
 Pleas from the American Cancer Society, the American Red Cross and CARE come by the armful. But a request from the Guggenheim Foundation seems about as likely as the computer magnate William H. Gates asking for a loan.
"People look at us," said Joel O. Conarroe, a literary scholar who is president of the Guggenheim Foundation. "They say: 'You're a foundation. You have the Guggenheim name. Why do you need money?' "
But years of generous spending, coupled with erratic investment earnings resulting from an overly conservative investment strategy, eroded the wealth of the foundation in the 1970's and 1980's. Now the Guggenheim Foundation is on a quest to add some $10 million to $20 million to its $150 million endowment.
While it is not unusual for foundations to have difficulty earning high enough investment returns year after year to replace what they spend and to keep pace with inflation, it is highly unusual for a private foundation like the Guggenheim to resort to raising money.
Although some argue that foundations ought to spend themselves out of existence rather than being self-perpetuating fonts of grant money, the Guggenheim's directors say they do not think that closing down makes sense for them.
"There was discussion of the board about whether we should exist in perpetuity or go out of business, but no real debate," said Robert V. Lindsay, the former president of the Morgan Guaranty Trust Company and the chairman of the foundation; he leads its fund-raising campaign. "Everyone felt we would be more and more needed."
The Guggenheim Foundation was set up by Senator Simon Guggenheim and his wife, Olga, after the death of their 17-year-old son at Phillips Exeter Academy in 1922, shortly before he was to enter Harvard. The Senator's idea, developed after consultations with several Rhodes scholars, was to provide grubstakes for exceptional scholars, scientists and artists, or, in his own words, "to continue the influence of the young life of eager aspiration."
In the years since then, the Guggenheim Foundation has acquired a luster that rubs off on its recipients.
"Guggenheim invented the prestige fellowship," said Stanley N. Katz, president of the American Council of Learned Societies. "It's not just the money. For young academics, it was the Good Housekeeping Seal of Approval. With its peer review process, it came to have a cachet that was invaluable."
Other foundations grant fellowships, as do Government agencies, like the National Science Foundation, the National Endowment for the Arts and the National Endowment for the Humanities, and a growing number of research centers at universities. But many of these have a narrower vision. The Guggenheim Foundation offers fellowships in 79 fields; computer studies is one of the newest. And the National Endowments, the biggest sources of financing in their fields, face deep cuts from Congress and an uncertain future.
"In terms of what's available, the Guggenheims are critical," said Mr. Katz, whose own organization gives about $1.1 million in fellowships in the humanities and social sciences annually, about half of what the Guggenheim Foundation spends in those areas each year.
The grants are not large. The average fellowship last year was $27,031, and the highest was $31,000; the amount is based on need. But for professors with a half-year's salary for a year's sabbatical leave, or for writers and artists used to living frugally, they often make an important financial difference.
The realization that the foundation needed to tighten its belt if it wanted to stay in business began to take shape in the mid-1980's, about the time that its second president, Gordon Ray, was preparing to retire.
With an endowment hovering between about $100 million and $120 million, the foundation had been awarding, since the 1950's, about 300 fellowships each year, including about 25 to 30 awards each year in Latin America, where Guggenheim mining activities brought in huge profits.
While foundations are required by law to spend an amount equal to at least 5 percent of their assets annually, the Guggenheim had spent more than that in many years to maintain the number of grants and to try to adjust the value of the grants to account for inflation.
But, like numerous other foundations, its investment returns have been erratic, with big gains in some years and losses in others. In some years, its investment earnings did not even cover its grants. Like many foundations and other investors in the 1980's, the Guggenheim had left all its assets in the hands of one bank money manager, rather than trying for more diversification and more aggressive money managers who might have brought in higher returns. The trustees began to realize that those results, combined with inflation, meant that if the foundation continued along the path it was on, it faced a gradual erosion of its spending power in coming decades.
With the help of a planning grant from the Andrew W. Mellon Foundation, the Guggenheim Foundation called in a consulting firm that confirmed the worst fears of the trustees: they could not continue to hand out grants at the rate they had been if they wanted the foundation to be around indefinitely.
Under the new president, Mr. Conarroe, who had been the dean of the School of Arts and Sciences at the University of Pennsylvania and a Guggenheim fellow himself, the foundation adopted in 1989 a four-step plan that involved cutting costs, awarding fewer fellowships, following a more aggressive investment strategy for the endowment and raising money.
The foundation handles some 3,500 applications a year, but it is modestly run. Its staff members opt for subways over taxis, and they bring up food from the delicatessen downstairs. Its flashiest assets are its Park Avenue address, its prestigious name and the copious works by fellows that adorn the offices: shelves and shelves of books by well-known authors like Edna St. Vincent Millay, Hart Crane, Saul Bellow and John Updike, and walls lined with the work of big-name photographers like Adams and Diane Arbus.
Still, it trimmed its staff and cut the number of grants by almost half. "The cuts were really draconian," Mr. Connaroe said. "Where before we might have had 12 composers or 12 poets, now we had only six or seven."
The foundation also added two new investment experts -- Leon Levy of Odyssey Partners and S. Parker Gilbert of Morgan Stanley -- to its board of trustees and finance committee. They have helped reshape the investment strategy, which will take the foundation into some less conventional investments, like specialized hedge funds. And, for the first time ever, the foundation took on a part-time development officer to help raise money.
So far, the fund raising, now in its fourth year of formal solicitations, has been low-key. Efforts to raise money from other foundations proved difficult, so the Guggenheim Foundation is focusing on its own body of fellows and friends, calling on former fellows like Wendy Wasserstein, the playwright; E. Annie Proulx, the novelist, and Theodore R. Sizer, the educator, to write artful letters to other fellows in their fields.
"Lord knows, fund raising is not my thing," Ms. Proulx said. But she agreed to do it. "The foundation is a wonderful place to put your money if you have some -- the awards are so important," Ms. Proulx said. But she added: "Guggenheim or not, a lot of writers are not exactly rolling in the chips. So, as a group to appeal to, they have shallow pockets."
While the trustees recognize that, they still say it is the appropriate place to start.
"We are taking a rather quiet approach -- with a rifle, not a shotgun," said Mr. Lindsay, the foundation chairman. "It's going to be a slow process. It may seem too deliberate, but that doesn't worry me."
Mr. Lindsay said the goal was to be able to back 20 to 40 more fellowships by the end of the century, or enough to bring the total to about 200 per year. That would still be fewer fellowships than the foundation had been awarding each year through 1989.
So far, the campaign has taken in about $2 million of its $10 million to $20 million goal, not counting bequests or a recent pledge of $500,000 by Mr. Levy. It is also working out final details on a $1 million challenge grant from William T. Golden, a philanthropist who was a trustee.
So far, 1,776 fellows have made donations, and 97 more have designated the foundation as a recipient after their deaths. The average gift last year was $225.
But even among fellows who talk of the Guggenheim program as one of the highlights of their lives, it is a hard sell.
"They're as worthy as anything else I give to," said Sondra Perl, a professor of English at Lehman College of the City University of New York. "The Guggenheim was invaluable, and their fund-raising letter made me think. But I certainly didn't get the feeling that their existence was in any way threatened."
But there are others who appear to be more inclined to help. John Kenneth Galbraith, the Harvard University economist, said that he would be more than willing to contribute.
"For better or worse, I would have not written "The Affluent Society" without my Guggenheim," he said. "It gave me a year off in Switzerland to write with no interruptions. I'm eternally grateful."

HEADLINE: High-Technology Stocks Lead the Market's Broad Advance

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
Stocks advanced broadly yesterday, buoyed by gains in Novell and other technology companies.
The technology sector, which has led the market all year, was helped yesterday by the news of several multimillion-dollar deals and by a survey of portfolio managers by Barron's magazine this week that highlighted a number of technology issues favored by managers. 
 In addition, reports last week of unexpectedly strong orders for durable goods and strong economic growth in the third quarter brightened the business outlook for technology companies, analysts said.
"The earnings estimates and the profit figures that are being discussed for 1996 are too low if you have that much growth," said Tracy Herrick, chief investment strategist at Jefferies & Company in Palo Alto, Calif.
The Dow Jones industrial average rose 14.82 points, to 4,756.57. The broader Standard & Poor's 500-stock index increased 3.55 points, to 583.25. Of the 90 industry groups in the S.& P. 500, computer, software and semiconductor stocks added the most to the index's gain.
The Morgan Stanley High Tech Index of 35 stocks surged 10.2 points, to a record of 348.1, surpassing its Sept. 11 high, while the technology-laden Nasdaq composite index climbed 14.14 points, to 1039.69.
More than six stocks rose for every five that fell on the New York Stock Exchange, where trading volume fell to 319.2 million shares from 379.5 million on Friday.
Novell's move to sell its Wordperfect software business and buy back as much as 10 percent of its own stock showed that technology companies are "taking positive steps to boost profitability and lower costs," said Peter DaPuzzo, managing director for equities at Cantor, Fitzgerald & Company.
Shares of Novell jumped 1 1/2, to 16 3/8, even though the company is widely expected to sell Wordperfect for far less than the $855 million it paid for it just 18 months ago.
Dozens of other technology stocks rallied. I.B.M. rose 1 3/4, to 98 1/4; Microsoft climbed 2 3/4, to 102 3/4; Intel rose 2, to 69 3/4, and Hewlett-Packard surged 3 5/8, to a record 96.
"These technology stocks should continue to do well from an order and earnings standpoint," said Christopher Fleming, money manager at the Burridge Group.
Friday's report that the economy grew by a 4.2 percent annual rate in the third quarter "was higher than expected, and people are viewing that as a sign orders are going to continue to be strong well into the fourth and first quarters," Mr. Fleming said.
Netscape Communications soared 7, to 88 1/2. The company, which first sold shares at 28 each in August, cut prices by as much as 57 percent on some of its Internet software, a move aimed at gaining customers in an increasingly competitive market.
Minnesota Educational Computing vaulted 10, to 31 1/4. Softkey International, which was down 6 at 32 1/8, agreed to buy the maker of educational software for $370 million at the same time as Softkey made a tender offer for the Learning Company, which was up 4 7/8, to 60 3/8.
Fore Systems climbed 5 1/4, to 55 3/4, after analysts at Needham & Company and Robertson Stephens & Company raised their investment opinions on the maker of computer network equipment.
Macromedia rose 6, to 36 1/4, after the software publisher linked up with Sun Microsystems, which was up 2 1/8 at 78 3/4, to build graphics and multimedia standards for the Internet.
A $2.1 billion buyout offer for CBI Industries, a maker of industrial gases, gave a boost to so-called cyclical stocks, whose business is closely tied to economic booms and busts.
Praxair Inc. offered on Sunday to buy CBI for $32 a share, driving up CBI's stock 10 5/8, to 30 3/4, yesterday. Praxair fell 1/2, to 26 3/8. Priced at a 60 percent premium to the closing price of CBI's shares Friday, the offer shows that cyclical stocks are not too expensive, analysts said.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasuries Unchanged In Optimism On Economy

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Treasury prices were steady yesterday as optimism that the economy was expanding at a moderate pace returned to the market.
Bond prices fell sharply on Friday morning after a report on the gross domestic product was stronger than expected. But prices ended mixed that day, buoyed in part by optimism that a Federal budget agreement would be reached. And many in the market still expect that the Federal Reserve will again lower short-term interest rates. 
 Still, market participants are expected to avoid making any major moves ahead of a number of economic reports this week, in particular, the October employment report, which is due on Friday.
And the market yesterday was also watching the outcome of a referendum in Quebec on whether the province should secede from Canada. A vote for independence would be expected to generate a flight out of Canadian securities to the relative safety and stability of short maturities in the United States Treasury market.
The price of the 30-year Treasury bond was unchanged from Friday at 107 to yield 6.35 percent. Rates were mixed at the Treasury's regular weekly bill auction yesterday.
Bond prices bounced a bit early in the session after the Commerce Department released its report on consumer spending and income in September. The data were in line with analysts' expectations, and traders said the bounce was probably because there were no surprises in the numbers.
But Anthony Chan, vice president and chief economist at the Banc One Investment Advisers Corporation in Columbus, Ohio, warned of the potential inflationary pressures in the higher savings rate in the month. The savings rate rose to 4.2 percent in September, from 4 percent, which was revised from 3.6 percent.
"Given the fairly robust expenditures," Mr. Chan said, "the savings rate does suggest that consumers have effectively reloaded their guns for greater expenditures during the fourth quarter if consumer sentiment improves in response to a healthier labor market."
He said the credit markets were continuing to struggle with two major themes. "One camp fears that last Friday's real gross domestic product report suggests that the economic momentum going into the fourth quarter might actually be stronger than previously anticipated, which clearly points to higher yields," Mr. Chan said. "On the other hand, the market's tone still appears to be extremely healthy. Who would have believed that the bond market would have closed higher after learning that growth was almost twice as large as the consensus estimate. Consequently, if you can't bring this market to its knees on such robust growth, then what if anything can push this market lower?"
In the corporate market, the supply of new debt will increase significantly today when the Tennessee Valley Authority prices $1.6 billion of global power bonds through underwriters led by Lehman Brothers International and Merrill Lynch International. About $1 billion of the five-year global notes are expected to be priced to yield 19 to 21 basis points more than the five-year Treasury note, while $600 million of 30-year securities will be priced to yield about 37 to 39 basis points more than the Treasury bond. A basis point is one-hundredth of a percentage point.
Traders report that good investor demand on the T.V.A. issue prompted a $50 million increase in the size of the 30-year noncallable security. The healthy demand was encouraging amid the flood of corporate debt now coming to market to take advantage of the lowest interest rates in 20 years. Last June, when the T.V.A. sold global bonds, the offering was so popular that it was increased to $2 billion from $1 billion.
Following are the results of yesterday's Treasury auction of new three- and six-month bills:

HEADLINE: Consumer Spending Shows A Slower Rate of Growth

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Consumer spending rose in September at half the rate that incomes grew, the Government said today in reports that pointed to slower growth ahead.
Spending rose just two-tenths of 1 percent, to a rate of $4.92 trillion, after jumping a revised eight-tenths of 1 percent in August, the Commerce Department said. Consumer incomes from all sources grew four-tenths of 1 percent, to a $6.1 trillion annual rate, after edging up one-tenth of 1 percent in August. 
 Americans owe so much in credit card and other debt that they are slowly being forced to spend less, which could dampen holiday season sales and be a drag on the economy in the fourth quarter, analysts said. Consumer spending accounts for two-thirds of the nation's economic activity.
"Given the debt situation, it's good to see income growth coming in at higher rates than spending," said Daryl Delano, an economist at Cahners Economics Inc. in Newton, Mass.
A spurt in spending helped drive gross domestic product -- the Government's broadest measure of the economy -- up at a brisk 4.2 percent rate in the third quarter, the Government reported on Friday.
The data on spending and income, analysts said, confirm their belief that the brisk pace of expansion is likely to slow.
"Consumer installment credit has been creeping up steadily for a couple of years now and there are people who think it could be our downfall, could put an end to this expansion," Mr. Delano said. He said he expected more modest growth in the current quarter.
Norm Williams, an economist with First Interstate Bancorp in Los Angeles, said the likelihood of more moderate economic growth in the fourth quarter meant that Federal Reserve policy makers would probably hold short-term interest rates steady when they meet Nov. 15.
Most of the gain in income last month came from higher wages and salaries, which rose at a $14.8 billion annual rate after falling $6.2 billion in August. Interest and dividend income rose four-tenths of 1 percent, which also increased earnings.
But Americans saved more, with the savings rate rising to 4.2 cents of each dollar earned from 4 cents in August, the department said. Personal savings rose to a rate of $219.5 billion from $208 billion.
The Commerce Department said spending on durable goods fell a sharp 1.4 percent last month, to a $635.4 billion annual rate, hurt mainly by sluggish demand for cars.
Spending on nondurable goods including food rose six-tenths of 1 percent, to a $1.45 trillion rate, and spending for services edged up three-tenths of 1 percent, to a $2.83 trillion rate.
Consumer incomes had risen six-tenths of 1 percent in July and five-tenths of 1 percent in June. The department's figures are seasonally adjusted.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Merger Mania Nears Record-Setting Pace
The wave of corporate consolidations is likely to set a record this year. The pace is as feverish as it was in the last three years of the 1980's, the decade of the takeover. 
 Securities Data said that American merger activity so far this year was nearly $346 billion, just shy of the record $347.1 billion completed during all of last year. Global deals, at $653.4 billion so far, are at a record pace. [Page A1.]
 
Computer Stocks Buoy the Market
Stocks advanced broadly, helped by a big rise in technology issues, notably by Novell. I.B.M., Microsoft and a number of other technology companies also gained. The Dow Jones industrials rose 14.82 points, to 4,756.57, while the technology-laden Nasdaq composite index climbed 14.14, to 1,039.69. [D10.]
 
Fruit of the Loom Cuts Jobs
Fruit of the Loom plans to shut six plants and cut back two others, laying off 3,200 workers, 12 percent of its U.S. work force. The underwear maker blamed trade pacts. [D6.]
 
Nabisco Spinoff Is Sought
Bennett S. LeBow and Carl C. Icahn announced that they jointly owned 4.8 percent of RJR Nabisco Holdings and called on shareholders to separate food and tobacco operations. [D4.]
 
2 Big Moves by Softkey
Softkey began a hostile takeover bid valued at $606 million for the Learning Company, which Broderbund has agreed to buy, and agreed to buy Minnesota Educational Computing for stock valued at $370 million. [D5.]
 
Leeson Plea Bargain Expected
Nicholas W. Leeson, whose trading losses brought down Barings, appeared to be seeking a plea bargain in exchange for testimony against top Barings executives. [D7.]
 
Dow Loses $10 Million Verdict
A Nevada jury ordered Dow Chemical to pay $10 million in punitive damages to a woman who said she was severely injured by the company's silicone breast implants. [A14.]
 
Rockefeller Center Bid Lapses
A $1.16 billion bid for Rockefeller Center's mortgage was allowed to expire yesterday by the group led by Sam Zell. [D5.]
 
A Tight Grip on Consumer Wallets
Consumer spending rose just two-tenths of 1 percent in September -- half the rate that incomes grew, the Government said. [D2.]
 
New Optimism on Mexican Markets
Mexican financial markets responded favorably to the Government's new recovery plan to revive the battered economy, with the main stock index up 4.57 percent. [D6.]
 
Shareholder Activism Growing
When disgruntled shareholders take on corporate management, only a few, like the California Public Employees' Retirement System or individual investors, seem to lead the charge. But a new survey of nearly 240 large investors shows that shareholder activism is much more widespread. [D2.]
 
A Holiday For Advertisers
Halloween has become such a popular holiday for adults that advertisers are spending a lot more on creepy advertisements. Anheuser-Busch features Crypt Keeper in promotions for its Budweiser beers, and Pepsi-Cola is spending heavily on its Halloween "Monster Smash 1995." Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D8.]
 
Skepticism Greets Kmart Chief
Kmart's chief executive, Floyd Hall, meeting separately with analysts and investors, was greeted with skepticism as he tried to dispel nagging rumors of a credit crunch. [D22.]

LENGTH: 1054 words

HEADLINE: New York Outlines a New Policy On Seizure of Buildings for Taxes

BYLINE: By SHAWN G. KENNEDY 

 BODY:
In the first major revision of New York City's housing policy in almost two decades, the Giuliani administration announced yesterday that it was ending the city's role as the landlord of last resort.
Under the change, which makes official a practice that has been in effect informally for two years, the city will no longer take over ownership and management of all buildings whose owners fall far behind on property taxes. Instead, it will give the existing owners advice and even direct financial help in paying their tax debt. 
 If that does not work, new owners, possibly including nonprofit organizations, will be found for the properties.
In addition, the city will eventually turn over to new owners the nearly 5,000 homes and apartment buildings it already owns and manages.
City officials described the new policy in interviews yesterday, and Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani plans to discuss it in a news conference today.
New York City now pays $220 million a year to manage and maintain residential properties it owns. While the new policy will have its own costs, city officials expect them to be much lower, through savings on property-management expenses and liability claims for problems like personal injuries and lead-paint contamination.
One reason the city feels pressed to reduce its housing management burden is that much of the cost is paid by the Federal Government through community development block grants -- a source of money that may soon dry up.
"The money we use is in serious jeopardy," John S. Dyson, Deputy Mayor for Finance and Economic Development, said, referring the budget-cutting mood in Washington. "And with the city's finances being what they are, there is little chance that we could make up the difference even if we wanted to."
The city imposed an informal moratorium on the seizing of buildings two years ago. The Giuliani administration also said it would work to dispose of the several thousand buildings it already owns.
"We are changing our role," Deborah Wright, commissioner of the Department of Housing Preservation and Development, said in an interview. Her office serves as the city's housing manager.
"We are putting in money early in the process," she said, "when the money can do the most good, rather than putting money in the back end, when buildings are in a state of crisis and are close to being unsalvageable."
The new strategy follows other Giuliani administration proposals that look for private-sector solutions to government problems. As an example, the administration is trying to sell or lease several of the city's hospitals to private companies.
"We have spent the better part of 18 months looking at the current policies," Ms. Wright said. "We now have a system that is not working for tenants, neighborhoods, taxpayers or landlords."
The city first became the landlord for thousands of New Yorkers during the administration of Mayor Edward I. Koch. Before then, properties with delinquent tax bills were sold at public auction. But many of those new owners were no more able to pay taxes than the original owners, and so the buildings were auctioned again and again -- until finally many were abandoned.
Mayor Koch changed the policy and began taking over ownership and management of these properties, and eventually City Hall became the largest landlord in all five boroughs. But soon the city government was overwhelmed.
The city took over fewer buildings during the real-estate boom of the 1980's. But in recent years, as the city's real-estate market has slumped, more owners have been found in default, and the city's inventory has grown.
As of July, the Department of Housing Preservation and Development, the agency responsible for these buildings, owned and managed 47,025 apartments in 4,406 buildings across the city. The city has always intended to sell properties it acquires, and has been more or less successful in doing so, depending on the prevailing economic circumstances.
In the last year, the Giuliani administration has been able to sell off about 19 percent of its properties. Still, an independent consultant, Arthur Andersen Consulting, working for city without charge, estimated last summer that with no change in the current policy, the cost of owning and managing just the properties the city already owns would be $10.6 billion over the next two decades.
Under the new policy, the city will offer several kinds of help to building owners, including low-interest loans and management assistance.
If the owners still cannot pay the taxes, the city will deed the properties directly to private owners without passing them through the city's hands.
The new policy requires the approval of the City Council. Yesterday, one council member whose district would be significantly affected said she would support any change that would improve conditions for tenants.
"I have not seen the new policy proposal," said C. Virginia Fields, whose district include most of Harlem, where the city owns a large number of buildings. "I am delighted to see the city at least move forward on this issue. As long as the new program sees to it that buildings get necessary assistance, I think that we are talking about an improvement over the status quo."
Frank P. Braconi, executive director of the Citizens Housing and Planning Council, a housing-advocacy organization that has long criticized the city's existing policy, also expressed tentative support for the changes. But he also said he would reserve judgment until the city revealed more details about the program.
"From our point of view this is a step in the right direction," he said. "But what is missing are the details of how much they are willing to spend on the assistance, and whether the program will recognize the fact that many low-income buildings have been excessively taxed."
Under the current system, the city begins a foreclosure when owners fall behind in taxes for more than one year. At this point, owners are offered the opportunity to pay off their debt in installments. If taxes remain unpaid into the second year, the city seizes the property.
Under the new plan, the city would take over buildings only when all other measures fail. And in those instances management would be by outside contractors, not a city agency.

HEADLINE: NEW JERSEY DAILY BRIEFING;
A Plan for the Hospitalized Poor

BYLINE: By SUSAN JO KELLER 

DATELINE: TRENTON

 BODY:
The Senate President, Donald T. DiFrancesco, thinks there is a better way to pay for hospital care for the working poor. 
 The state now gives $400 million a year to hospitals to subsidize the care of patients who cannot pay; the money comes from a Charity Care Trust Fund supported by unemployment benefit reserves. That financing expires on Dec. 31, and Mr. DiFrancesco said yesterday that he would block any new arrangement that did not include his plan to divert $200 million from the fund to help people buy hospitalization insurance from private companies.

HEADLINE: THE AD CAMPAIGN;
Pequots' Foxwood Casino

 BODY:

PLAYER: The Mashantucket Pequot Indian Tribe, Ledyard, Conn.
 
PRODUCER: Robinson & Cole, Hartford, and Direct Response, Tolland, Conn.
 
SCRIPT: Foxwoods -- the world's most successful casino. But that's only part of the story. The Mashantucket Pequots' Foxwoods Resort and Casino has become one of Connecticut's largest employers, providing jobs to over 10,000 people, strengthening thousands of additional jobs throughout Connecticut, while purchasing over $1 billion in construction, goods and services from large and small businesses and contributing over $300 million to the state. A success story born in the woods of Eastern Connecticut. Coming soon to the the city of Bridgeport: a new world-class casino, bringing over 9,000 new jobs to Connecticut. Mashantucket Pequots, committed to Connecticut's future and Connecticut's people.
 
VISUAL: The commercial opens with an aerial view of Foxwoods, then cuts to 17 images of workers. After another scenic shot of Foxwoods, it cuts to a bleak shot of the Bridgeport skyline, followed by an artist's rendition of the proposed casino. It ends with a construction worker on a steel beam, accompanied by the tribe's logo.
 
ACCURACY: The commercial accurately states the economic contributions Foxwoods has made. Its assumptions about the future in Bridgeport, however, are subject to debate. The ad says flatly that a casino will open soon, without acknowledging that the plan faces an uphill battle in the Legislature and that construction would take more than three years. Some legislators have questioned the claim that the casino will mean 9,000 jobs.
 
SCORECARD: Facing intense opposition in the legislature, the tribe is trying to take the debate outside the Capitol and use Foxwoods's accomplishments to encourage support for the Bridgeport proposal. Such image-building advs are common at the start of electoral and legislative campaigns. But it may be too late to mobilize support to influence the Legislature.
